# Spoonbill Weather

# Chapter 1

From the water, nothing about Rookery Key looked finished. The camp stood on pilings above blackwater and mangrove roots, sun-whitened boards lifted just high enough to pretend the tide and rot had been accounted for. As the skiff nosed in, Mina's shirt pasted itself to her spine and the one-page contract in her pocket went soft at the folds. The screens along the walkways shivered in the wind. A generator thudded somewhere behind the main house. Pink flashes moved above the trees where spoonbills crossed from one pocket of shade to another, and the whole place smelled like wet rope, old diesel, mangrove mud, and onions already browning for somebody else's dinner.

It was the first place in eleven months that had offered her wages without requiring a board vote, a letter of contrition, or the kind of cheerful public humility that felt worse than being turned down. Six hundred and forty a week, a narrow bed, and the possibility of keeping the Gulf Wildlife Board payment plan current another month. The last of her savings had gone three weeks earlier into the civil penalty and the transmission on her truck. She had sold the truck yesterday. There had not been enough left over to feel noble about it.

The skiff driver killed the motor and let the boat slap gently against a bumper made from old tire halves. A skinny kid in a camp shirt stepped onto the rail before the skiff had fully settled. He moved with the easy bad judgment of somebody too used to not falling. His clothes hung a little large on him, and a pale burn crescent showed on the inside of one wrist when he bent for Mina's duffel. A grease-soft photocopy stuck from his back pocket, some diagram of a small engine with the title half erased by sweat.

"You made the low tide," he said. "That counts as a professional start around here."

"Do I get a certificate?"

"Only if you drown later. Then we put your name on something."

He grinned fast so she would know he was kidding, or mostly kidding, and swung her duffel up to the dock before she could tell him she had carried heavier things for worse reasons.

Imogen Price came down the walkway as if she had been informed a shipment had arrived that might be useful and might also explode. She was trim and sun-creased, silver hair pinned up with a yellow carpenter's pencil, field shirt faded into a respectable green by years of washing and force of posture. There was a clipboard in one hand and a seam ripper hooked into the shirt pocket with her pen, the kind of tiny practical weapon a person carried only if she had spent too much of her life rescuing linens and mosquito netting from the cheap decisions of donors.

For a second Mina saw the earlier version of her, the woman from conferences and field camps, the one who could identify twelve species by call in a windstorm and then drink everybody under a folding table. That woman had once taken Mina's side in a meeting that mattered. This one stopped three feet short and took inventory.

"Mina."

"Imogen."

The kid shifted the duffel onto his shoulder and looked between them with interest he was trying not to wear openly.

Imogen said, "Beto, put her things in the west bunk. Not the guest side. The west bunk."

"I know where we hide the unmarketable staff," Beto said.

"That is a useful skill. Go."

He went, but he did it backwards for two steps, balancing the duffel on one shoulder and his own body on the edge of the dock rail just to prove he could. Mina watched him turn the corner toward the generator shed and the low row of staff rooms beyond it.

Imogen tipped her head toward the walkway. "Come with me before the kitchen needs something else from me and this turns into another day of not saying what needs to be said."

The camp sounded built rather than settled. Boards gave underfoot with little damp complaints. Screens chattered. The generator thrum came and went as the wind shifted. Through one section of mesh Mina caught a glimpse of a side building with battery chargers lined on a table and a tall woman inside threading a cable tie through a torn mist net, dark braid tight down her back. The room smelled faintly of rubbing alcohol even out here on the walkway.

Mina looked away before the woman turned. Imogen noticed that too. Imogen noticed most things.

Her office had once been a porch. The windows were jalousies painted shut in two places, and the desk held three neat stacks of paper that would have been four anywhere else: repair invoices, guest packets, donor letters, and a smaller stack of field permits weighted by a horseshoe crab shell. A standing fan pushed heat from one side of the room to the other.

Imogen stayed on her feet. She did not offer Mina a chair.

"I am going to spare us the hopeful version," she said. "I hired you because spring guests still want a guide who can actually find birds, because the camp is short one competent adult, and because you were willing to come for temporary money on short notice. That is all true. Some less flattering things are also true."

"I can supply a few if you're running low."

"Do not start with charm. It did not save you last time."

There it was, clean and early, with no pretense that they were two colleagues meeting again under unfortunate weather.

Mina put her hands in her pockets so she would stop touching the damp edge of the contract. "What exactly are we doing here, then?"

Imogen laid the clipboard on the desk without looking at it. "You are on day work only. Dawn platform, boat guests, unloading, kitchen overflow if Beto gets buried, laundry if we lose one more volunteer to heat rash. You smile when guests require smiling. You keep your language fit for donors. You do not improvise your own role."

"I know how camp jobs work."

"Then I can be brief. You are not on any night permits. You are not on Rhea Acosta's surveys. You are not to request county access from Tomas. You are not to conduct independent research, photography, sampling, or rumor-chasing of any kind while you are under my roof."

Mina said, too quickly, "I'm here to work."

Imogen's eyes moved once across her face, sharp and almost tired. "That would have landed better if your email had not contained three questions about rookery closures after dark."

"I like knowing the boundaries."

"No." Imogen's voice stayed even, which was worse than if she had raised it. "You like boundaries right up until they interfere with desire, curiosity, or the mistaken conviction that you are the special exception. I do not have room for that here."

Heat gathered under Mina's shirt. It was not the heat outside. "You think I came all the way out here to steal marsh time between guest breakfasts?"

"I think you came because nobody else with a bed and a payroll would touch your name. I think you also came because Rookery Key sits beside exactly the kind of water you cannot leave alone."

That landed cleanly because it was half true and therefore harder to argue with.

Imogen reached behind her for a manila folder and slid it across the desk. Inside were Mina's temporary contract, a meal schedule, the week's guest list, and a typed page titled REPRESENTATION EXPECTATIONS. The first bullet instructed staff not to speculate about local wildlife phenomena in front of paying visitors.

"One week," Imogen said. "Possibly two if occupancy holds and if you make yourself more useful than risky. I am not offering you a season. I am offering you a narrow bridge and asking you not to set it on fire while you are standing on it."

Mina thumbed the edge of the guest list. The names ran from Naples and Sarasota to one Atlanta donor couple and two bird-club retirees from Michigan, the kind of people who bought binocular harnesses with climate-control opinions.

"You've gotten very managerial."

"I have gotten poor," Imogen said. "There is a difference."

The words came out flatter than complaint. Mina looked up.

For one beat the room lost its office shape and became what it was under the paperwork: deferred repairs, labor shortages, one more storm season coming. A water stain had spread along the ceiling in the corner above the permits. Somebody had painted over it twice. The stain had come through both times.

Imogen saw her see it and shut that opening at once.

"I also need you to understand this," she said. "The BrightCurrent business did not only damage you. It damaged every person who vouched for you in public. Some of us are less interested in doing that a second time."

Mina let the folder close. The paper slapped the desk harder than she intended.

"I didn't ask you to vouch for me."

"No. You asked for work. That is already more grace than most people would extend."

Silence held for a moment with the fan clicking every third turn.

Mina thought of the old photo before she meant to: blue under black water, a curve of something impossible, her own credit line under it for six bright weeks before the arguments started. Then came the hearings, and then came the word fabrication, used by people who had once begged her for field notes. Fabrication was clean language. It kept everybody's hands dry. It did not mention the cooler handle burning into her palm or the motel ice machine where she had hidden the thing one night because Jonah said they only needed twenty-four more hours.

She said, "You'll get your guide voice. You'll get clean shirts. I can do all of that."

Imogen nodded once. "Good. Because if you cause trouble here, it will not read as romantic failure or scientific ambition. It will read as me being foolish enough to hire the eel woman after everyone else learned better."

There was no use flinching at that. The guides on the mainland said worse.

"Understood."

Imogen slid the clipboard back toward herself. "Do you have any questions that are actually about the job I am paying you to do?"

Mina almost asked how close the spoonbill channels came to the western edge of camp, whether the county ran after midnight this week, whether the low water exposed the old service cuts. Instead she said, "Where do I put my laundry if it comes back smelling like the generator?"

Something almost like approval crossed Imogen's face. "Blue bin behind the kitchen. Do not overload it. The machine walks."

That was as close to welcome as she was going to get.

Beto had left Mina's duffel in a room behind the generator shed, barely wide enough for the cot, a narrow dresser, and a nail hammered into the wall at shoulder height. A box fan in the window shook like it wanted out. The pillowcase smelled of bleach on top of old humidity. Through the slats she could see a strip of blackwater and one length of the boardwalk that led away toward the county dock.

Beto leaned in the doorway while she set the duffel down.

"If the fan stops, kick the wall once near the floor. Not hard. It takes that personally."

"Good to know."

"If you kick twice, it thinks you're starting a fight and it quits for the night."

Mina smiled despite herself. "You've trained the appliances."

"I'm training everything till I can leave for trade school." He caught himself there, glanced down as if he had not meant to say that much, and hooked a thumb into his pocket over the grease-soft engine diagram. "Anyway. Dinner's at six unless a guest starts talking and then it becomes whenever mercy arrives."

"What are you studying?"

His expression changed fast, all the joking stacked aside for a second. He pulled the folded photocopy out and looked at it like it had become embarrassing in daylight. It showed carburetor parts, exploded in black-and-white and annotated in somebody else's pen.

"Nothing glamorous," he said. "I just like knowing why stuff dies."

Then he folded it down again and the quick grin came back over the top. "You need anything before Imogen decides you can carry coolers with the rest of us?"

"A shower and a fake passport."

"Shower's end of the hall. Passport's probably in donor relations."

After he left, Mina sat on the cot and let the room hold her weight. The boards under the thin mattress gave with a damp little complaint. Somewhere in the main house a screen door snapped shut. Somebody laughed. Somebody cursed in the kitchen. The camp settled around her not into calm but into its own kind of constant low-grade vibration, architecture and insects and distant bird noise making one long suspended note.

She unpacked three shirts, field pants, socks, a flashlight, the battered binoculars she had not yet been forced to pawn, and the hard case that held a camera body with no lens worth bragging about. She put the camera in the dresser drawer and shut it there. Imogen had not banned cameras by accident.

At the sink she washed the salt from her face and looked at herself in the spotted mirror. She was thirty-four and leaner than she used to be, with sleeplessness already visible under skin that otherwise took sun well and dark hair making a frizzy argument with the damp. The old prop scar along her left calf had gone white against the rest of her. She had started to look like a field version of herself somebody had left out too long.

When she came back to the room, the air outside had shifted. It was not cooler, just thicker, as if the afternoon had chosen a different grip. Mina stood by the window and listened. Beyond the generator hum and spoonbill racket she caught another engine, higher and harder, coming and going through distance. It was not a skiff. It was fan-driven, county work probably, the kind that meant night channels, fuel cages, and authorized access. She could not see the boat from here, only one turn of the boardwalk disappearing into mangroves and the idea of a dock beyond it.

She shut the dresser drawer harder than necessary and told herself again what the job was for: wages, a bed, and time to keep breathing in public until something else opened.

The blackwater below the pilings held its own face and gave nothing back.

* * *

By two in the afternoon Imogen had put Mina in a camp polo with a stitched spoonbill over the breast and sent her up to the observation platform with three guests and a plastic tub of loaner binoculars. The platform faced the guest side of the rookery, which meant the trees had been trimmed just enough to frame birds without admitting what the camp had done to the mangroves to buy that view. Heat shimmered off the railings. The boards were dusty with feather grit and a whitening crust of guano that Beto had clearly attacked with a hose and clearly lost to.

Mina slipped into the old public voice because it still came when she called for it. She talked them through the difference between the white ibis lifting off in a line and the spoonbills feeding with their bills scything the shallows. She named a tricolored heron for the woman from Michigan before the woman had fully raised her binoculars. She made the Atlanta donor laugh by describing juvenile pelicans as overconfident furniture. When the man from Sarasota asked whether the pink got brighter in mating season, she said yes, if the diet held and the birds were healthy, and kept herself from adding if the county didn't poison everything first.

It was work she could still do almost without pain. That was the problem. If she had become bad at it, the shame might have simplified. Instead the old muscle held. She could read wind on feathers and know where the birds would turn. She could hear a spoonbill's rough bill-clatter through guest chatter and know a pair was about to settle nearer the open pocket of water on the left. She had once believed that competency protected people. Then she had met institutions.

The donors were pleased enough to stay longer than scheduled and cheap enough to leave an insult in the tip jar. When they finally went back down the walkway toward iced tea and air-conditioning, Mina looked into the jar and found seven dollars, two quarters, and a sugar packet somebody had apparently set there by mistake and then decided not to correct. The Michigan woman had also left a mint wrapped in gold foil.

Mina took the money and the mint both. Money was money, and sugar became dinner often enough when weeks went wrong.

Beto came up the steps carrying a coil of hose over one shoulder and a case of canned seltzer balanced against his ribs.

"How'd the bird aristocracy treat you?"

Mina dropped the damp bills into her pocket. "I can now afford half a gallon of gas or a very small moral crisis."

"Don't spend it all on one thing."

He set the seltzer down in the patch of shade nearest the stairs and hooked one foot under the lowest rail, half sitting, half hanging over nothing. Imogen shouted from below for him to quit trying to die theatrically where guests could see. He shouted back that if he died on schedule maybe somebody else would have to unload supplies. He was talking more than he needed to. Stress did that to some people; youth did it to others. He had both working for him.

Below them, a flat-bottomed supply skiff nudged into the service side of the dock with ice, produce, cleaning fluid, a box of donor wine, and two propane cylinders lashed side by side like unwilling dance partners.

"Mercy has not arrived," Beto said. "Welcome to the glamorous portion of coastal ecology."

They worked through the dock load in the worst of the heat. The ice went first, then the wine because Imogen valued perishability in her own specific order, then sacks of rice and onions and freezer bait wrapped so badly the box leaked fish stink down Mina's thigh. A mosquito found the soft part under her wrist even in full daylight. Another got her through the camp polo at the spine.

Beto kept up a stream of commentary as if silence might let the weather win.

"Those onions are for staff chili, which means Imogen is trying to make us believe beans count as meat if the paprika's aggressive enough."

"I like paprika."

"You'll like it less on day four."

He took a propane cylinder from her before she asked for help and rolled it against his shin with practiced care. Up close the burn mark on his wrist looked older than a kitchen accident, puckered in a neat pale half-moon. He caught her looking.

"Steam tray," he said. "I was fourteen and trying to show off."

"How'd that go?"

"It cured me of showing off in food service. On docks I still think I'm immortal."

He tipped the cylinder into place against the kitchen wall and wiped his face with the bottom hem of his shirt. The engine photocopy had worked loose again from his pocket. This time Mina could read one of the handwritten notes in the margin: FLOAT HEIGHT OFF = FLOODING.

"Trade school?" she said.

He shrugged, suddenly shy under the shrug. "Marine systems if I can get off the islands. If not, air-conditioning. Everybody's desperate for air-conditioning."

"Ambitious and realistic."

"That's my brand. Don't tell anybody. I've got a reputation as an idiot to maintain."

The next box held cheap rosé and white wine packed in melting foam. Beto lifted one bottle, squinted at the label, and said, "This tastes like divorce. Put it on the guest shelf."

Mina laughed before she could stop herself. The sound surprised both of them.

He looked pleased by that and then, because people liked to give away stories when a laugh loosened them, said, "You got the spoonbills doing neck porn up there?"

"Pairing displays," Mina said.

"Sure. Scientific neck porn."

He hauled the wine box onto his shoulder. "Another week and it'll get louder. They start making those dry toy-truck sounds all night, everybody gets mean from the heat, and then some old guy on the dock swears the water under them lit up again."

Mina shifted the leaking bait box to her other hip. "Lit up how?"

Beto glanced over. The question must have come out harder than she meant it to.

"Blue," he said. "Like somebody dropped a busted motel sign into the roots."

The fish water from the box ran down her forearm. She did not feel it.

"Who says that?"

"Half the people around here if they've had enough beer, but my abuelo said it sober, so I give him extra points. He used to pole mullet traps on the back side of the rookery when he was younger than I am. Said you keep away from the spoonbills when they start breeding hard because the birds go stupid and the water under them goes weirder."

Mina set the bait box down on the dock with more care than it deserved. "He saw it himself?"

"He said his brother saw it. Then later he said he saw it once too, which means either it happened or he got old enough to steal other people's stories and make eye contact while doing it. But Tomas won't run that cut this time of year unless the county makes him, and that man will drive through weather that makes church ladies start confessing in parking lots."

"What cut?"

Beto's grin came back, but slower now. He had noticed the hook was in.

"Back channels west of the nesting trees. The side with all the roots standing up like elbows. You can hear the spoonbills before you see them. Everybody local's got some name for it. My family calls it the wedding ditch because the birds act insane."

Mina could feel the old part of her waking up, the part that turned rumor into map and map into route before anybody sensible could intervene. She started naming the variables to herself: breeding concentration, blackwater, something blue visible only under heavy biological disturbance. There were stupid explanations: dinoflagellates where they did not belong, reflected dock light, somebody's myth built on bad sleep. There were also explanations that did not sit politely in public.

She heard herself ask, "What time?"

"At night, obviously."

"No, what part of the night?"

Beto whistled very softly. "There she is."

"There who is?"

"The reason Imogen said I wasn't supposed to get you started."

Mina straightened. "I didn't ask for folklore."

"No, you asked for timestamp and habitat." He bumped the bait box lightly with his knee. "Relax. I was going to tell you anyway. My abuelo said hot nights, low water, birds making a racket like they're throwing dishes. He also said if you get close enough to see the blue you're already too close to the nests, so I guess the real lesson is old men like stories where they get to be right and mysterious at the same time."

From beyond the mangroves came the rising fan-whine Mina had heard from her room. It thickened fast, mechanical and intimate in the air, then an airboat slid into view at the end of the boardwalk beyond the service dock, county stencil on the side filmed with salt. The prop cage ticked as the engine came down. A man stepped off and tied up one-handed without looking at the cleat.

He was broad-shouldered under a sweat-dark shirt, with forearms marked by old prop nicks and fresh mosquito bites and a crooked nose that looked broken years ago and healed without much sympathy. Before he did anything else he set a paper cup on the dock post, checked that it was level, and only then unclipped a brass fuel key from his belt and knocked it once against his thumbnail as if verifying the day had not stolen it.

Beto tipped his chin. "And there is your county mystery priest now."

Tomas looked over at them with the tired amusement of a man coming off heat and noise and finding more people waiting on the dock.

"If you've broken something expensive," he said, "hide it before Imogen smells fear."

"We only broke the donor economy," Beto said. "Mina got seven dollars and a mint."

Tomas came farther up the dock. Closer, Mina could see the pale stripe a wedding band left when his hand opened off the rope. He smelled like diesel, river water, and mosquito repellent that had already lost the argument.

"Seven and a mint?" he said. "That means they found you educational but not transformative."

"I'm crushed."

His glance touched her, quick but not careless. It was a workingman's look, measuring weight, mood, whether help would actually be accepted. Then it moved to the bait box leaking onto the boards.

"You want a hand or just witnesses?"

"We've got it," Mina said.

"She says that because she's new," Beto told him. "Give her till Thursday. She'll be asking the rice sacks for legal representation."

Tomas picked up one end of the bait box anyway. "What's Imogen got you doing first day, Cordero? Smiling at retirees and carrying fish?"

The sound of her name in his mouth tightened her belly so abruptly she nearly lost her grip on the box. She reset her hand against the wet cardboard and kept her face flat. He knew who she was, then, or enough of it to say Cordero like the rumor came with a person attached.

"Apparently I'm multitalented."

"Apparently," he said.

Beto, who had the instincts of a person forever poking bruises just to see what stories leaked out, said, "I was telling her about the blue under the spoonbills."

Tomas did not pause, but he did not answer right away either. He walked the bait box up the ramp with Mina, set it by the freezer room, and wiped his hands on his shorts. The fuel key flashed once at his belt when he turned.

"Were you," he said.

"You don't have to sound so grateful."

"I'm saving gratitude for a larger emergency."

Beto folded his arms. "Tell her I'm not making it up."

Tomas looked past him toward the rookery, where the spoonbills were loud even now in the heat, rough wooden clatter carrying over the blackwater.

"People make all sorts of things up less specifically than that," he said.

"That's not an answer," Mina said.

One corner of his mouth moved. "No. It isn't."

She held his gaze. "Have you seen it?"

He leaned his hip against the freezer-room wall, suddenly still in the way that careful men got still before deciding how much trouble to invite.

"I've seen mullet turn silver under lights that weren't there a second before," he said. "I've seen phosphorescence in places it shouldn't be, runoff doing ugly little miracles, and one deputy out by Graveyard Cut swear a manatee was glowing because he wanted a story bad enough to earn one. Blue isn't hard to invent in black water."

"That still isn't an answer."

"Here is the answer part." He tipped his head toward the mangroves west of camp. "When the spoonbills are breeding hard, that side gets loud and stupid. Fish get pushed up. Birds drop lower. The county doesn't love us there after dark because every extra wake turns into paperwork and every flashlight becomes somebody's niece with a wildlife lawsuit. So if you're asking whether people talk about strange light under the birds, yes. If you're asking whether I sign my name under that on a county form, no."

Beto pointed at Mina as if presenting evidence to a jury. "See? Actionable mystery."

"You need a hobby," Tomas told him.

"I've got six. They're just all underfunded."

Mina said, "How close do county routes get?"

Tomas's eyes came back to her face and stayed there long enough to leave heat behind. Mina's throat went dry. She saw the brass fuel key at his pocket and the pale wedding-band stripe on his hand in the same glance, and the thought landed ugly and clear: county access came attached to a married body.

"Closer than camp permits do," he said.

"That's not an answer either."

"It is if you're smart."

Beto let out a laugh sharp enough to nick. "He talks like this when he thinks somebody's either trouble or pretty."

"Beto," Tomas said.

"What? Married men can still identify categories."

The wedding-ring stripe flashed again when Tomas shoved the fuel key deeper into his pocket. He did not look embarrassed. He looked like a man who had heard worse from younger coworkers and lived.

"Go stack the wine before Imogen catches it cooking on the dock," he said.

"Deflection noted."

Beto went, but he dragged the box slowly enough to keep listening.

Mina should have let the subject go. Imogen's instructions were still practically warm. Instead she said, "What makes that side so different?"

Tomas scrubbed a hand over the back of his neck. Salt had dried in a white line there. "Low water. Root tangles. Noise bounces wrong. Spoonbills get thick through there this time of year. You're always half a second from flushing something protected and getting yelled at by three agencies that hate each other. Pick a reason."

"And the light?"

"Some nights water does odd things when enough life is shoving through it."

"That's almost poetry."

"No, that's me not getting trapped by a person I met ten minutes ago."

The line should have shut it down. Instead it made her more aware of the laugh he was withholding and of the fact that he had seen exactly what she was asking for and had not walked away from it.

Imogen's voice cut across the yard from the kitchen door. "If the two of you are trading bad decisions on my freezer ramp, do it while moving."

Tomas straightened. "See? Management."

Imogen came down the steps wiping her hands on a dish towel she had already ruined beyond washing. Her donor expression was gone. In its place was the stripped-down impatience Mina had seen in the office.

"Tomas, if you have the invoice for the extra ice, I need it before dinner. Mina, the onions are still on the dock. Unless the rumor has become load-bearing, pick them up."

"Yes, ma'am," Beto said from behind the wine box.

Imogen ignored him and looked at Mina directly. "Daylight."

Only one word. It held the whole office conversation inside it.

Mina bent for the onion sack. "I heard you the first time."

"Good. I dislike reciting myself."

Tomas handed Imogen a folded invoice from his pocket. "You still owe the county for last week's extra fuel cans."

"The county can send a priest or a collections notice. I will disappoint either one."

"That's the spirit."

He stepped back to let her past, and as she lifted the onion sack again he took the bottom for one second, just enough to spare her the worst of the awkward shift. The touch was practical, but his hand came through the burlap rough and hot enough to make her breath snag. She nearly missed the split seam he was pointing at. Then it was gone.

"Careful with that left side," he said. "The seam's blown."

Mina looked down. He was right. One wrong lift and she'd have dumped twenty pounds of onions through the cracks in the dock.

"Thanks."

"Don't sound so surprised."

He went back down the boardwalk toward the county side, paper cup still waiting on the post where he'd left it. As he passed, Mina saw what was inside: mango slices dusted red with chile powder, sweating sugar into the cardboard. For one filthy second she wanted the salt and heat of it on her own tongue. Then she saw the pale ring stripe again and heard the brass key tap at his belt, and the wanting turned cheap inside her. It was not only him. It was the routes and fuel and after-dark permission clipped to his body.

Beto reappeared at her elbow the instant Tomas was out of easy hearing.

"See?"

"See what?"

"That you're thinking about it too hard already."

"I'm thinking about onions."

"You're thinking about the west channels and whether county men come with keys or just attitude."

She hefted the sack and started for the kitchen. "For a person maintaining an idiot reputation, you're observant."

"I need a side hustle."

Inside, the kitchen was wet heat and knife noise. Imogen had a stockpot going and one reading glass perched low on her nose while she checked a donor seating chart with her free hand. The room smelled of cumin, bleach, and fish thawing in defeat. Nobody spoke about the rumor again because dinner service arrived and then dishes and then guest questions about sunrise departure times and whether the mosquitoes were always this bad and whether the spoonbills ever nested closer to the platform for photographs.

Mina gave them all the safe answers. She carried bowls. She wiped down tables. She laughed in the right places. She did not ask Tomas another thing because Tomas had gone back to the county dock before dinner, and because Imogen had only needed the one word.

After dark the camp changed shape without becoming quiet. Guest lamps threw screened gold squares onto the walkways. The generator deepened. Spoonbills argued somewhere beyond the mangroves with a sound like dry branches knocking together. The air got no cooler. It only pressed closer, a wet palm over the mouth of the place.

Mina took her plate to the back steps after the guests were fed and the staff finally ate. Beto sat two stairs below her with a second helping of chili and a marine-systems school website open on his phone, application fees glowing on the cracked screen until he locked it and pretended she had not seen.

"How bad's the smell in the west bunk?" he asked.

"Like hot socks in a machine shop."

"Then the fan's working."

He ate in quick hungry shovels. The kitchen burn on his wrist shone pale when he lifted the spoon.

"Your abuelo still around?" Mina asked.

"No." He looked out into the dark while he swallowed. "He'd hate the donor wine and love stealing the donor wine, so I keep him alive the normal way."

It was the first thing he'd said all day without a joke built into the frame.

"Sorry."

He shrugged. "He'd say don't be. He had three bypasses and the manners of a pirate."

Then voices rose from the main house and the softer moment closed up, not lost exactly, just filed for later. Beto stood, rinsed his bowl, and said Imogen would have them sorting linens at dawn if the laundry volunteer did not come back from town.

When Mina finally went to her room, the west bunk held the day's heat like a grudge. She stripped off the camp polo, wrung sweat from the hem into the sink, and counted what was in her pocket: the seven dollars, the quarters, the mint flattened almost silver against the bills. She laid the money on the dresser beside the contract and did the arithmetic she hated. She set the weekly wage against the board payment, then against food if she needed anything off camp. Gas was no longer relevant because the truck was gone. She kept going until the numbers became one question: how many weeks before one missed installment turned into a notice, then a hearing, then another public room where people used the word ethics like it belonged only to them.

Outside, from far enough away to feel illicit before it even resolved into sound, the county airboat started up again.

She went to the window barefoot and looked through the slats toward the unseen dock. The fan whine rose, then steadied. Somewhere beyond the trees Tomas Acosta was taking county fuel and county permission into channels the camp could not touch after dark. Somewhere west of the nesting trees the water ran low around root tangles and birds loud enough to orient by. Somewhere in that black water people had been telling the same ridiculous blue story long enough for a dead grandfather to inherit it and pass it on.

Mina put one hand on the sill. The paint had bubbled and gone tacky in the damp.

She could have left after a week with enough money to delay disaster and no new damage done. That had been the sensible shape of the job when she took it: she would smile for guests, keep her head down, let the camp use her competence, and let that be the whole transaction.

Then Beto had said blue under the spoonbills, and Tomas had not laughed in the right place.

The old memory came back in a piece she did not invite: motel ice melting under a cheap cooler, Jonah's voice saying hold it level, hold it level, as if level could save anything. She shut her eyes until the memory thinned. What had ruined her once did not stop wanting her. That was the ugliest part. The penalty and the gossip hurt, but what shamed her more was wanting it still.

Out in the dark, the airboat moved off. She could hear it by its changing pressure on the night, the engine swallowed and returned by bends in the creek.

Mina opened her eyes.

Imogen could have the daylight version. She could have the clean shirts, the donor voice, and the woman on the platform who knew where to point binoculars and how to make retirees feel chosen by a bird. Mina would carry bait, fold linens, smile through the heat, and make herself worth the bunk.

But she was not leaving after a week if the rumor held even half the weight it had taken on in her body. She would stay. She would learn the camp's rhythms, the boardwalk to the county dock, the timing of tides and guest dinners, and who locked what where. If Tomas Acosta was the nearest route into the night channels, then he was still a route, and the thought hit her low and hot enough to make her grip the sill harder. The pale stripe on his ring finger should have closed the subject. Instead it made the whole thing meaner, because it was not only the line of his mouth or the heat of his hands she kept thinking about. It was the fuel key, the unlocked cages, and the night channels waiting on the other side of him.

The fan rattled once in the window and nearly stopped. Mina kicked the wall near the floor. It caught again and lurched back into motion.

When the airboat noise had thinned to almost nothing, she was still standing there, listening like she could hear blue through wood and water both. By the time she lay down, the decision had settled in her with the hot, mean steadiness of a hook set clean. Morning could worry about the paperwork.


# Chapter 2

Another page crawled out of the printer and curled hot in Mina's hand: NIGHT ACCESS REQUEST, COLONIAL WADING BIRD PROTECTION ZONE, DRY-SEASON BREEDING RESTRICTIONS. By nine the camp office already smelled like hot dust, sunscreen, and the sweet burnt stink of a machine old enough to have retired before the last hurricane. She sat with her knees against a metal filing cabinet and looked at the title with its heavy confidence, the kind designed by people who never had to carry it through mud.

She read the same paragraph three times because the language kept changing shape on her. Night entry into the protected channels required one of three things: county authorization for mosquito control or emergency response, a research permit filed thirty days in advance with institutional sponsor attached, or an active wildlife-management credential unencumbered by prior compliance action. The phrase sat there with its dry little teeth. Unencumbered.

Her name would not survive the third line.

Outside the open louvered window, somebody dragged a cooler across the boardwalk. The sound came up through the pilings and into her shins. The main house gave one of its long tidal creaks. Heat was already collecting in the corners. She had pinned her curls up with a pencil and the pencil was losing the fight.

She filled the easy boxes anyway. Date. Site. Target waterway. Purpose of entry.

For purpose she tried behavioral observation, then changed it to nocturnal survey support, then deleted both and wrote species-confirmation reconnaissance before admitting that sounded like the exact kind of phrase a person invented when she wanted a protected place at night for reasons no form had a box for.

She let the cursor blink and thought of the rumor the way she had all night, the way she had pretended not to on the bunk that smelled of bleach and old damp plywood: blue rising under the spoonbills when the birds got filthy enough with breeding. Not a legend broad enough to travel. A local story. A channel story. The kind that attached to one bend of water because somebody had seen something there and had not managed to shut up about it.

Beto flew past the office door with two sacks of ice braced against his hip, his shirt hanging off one shoulder and his whole body tilted like he still believed gravity was a rumor for older people. He caught her looking and backpedaled three steps without spilling a cube.

"You murdering that printer on purpose?" he asked.

His left wrist showed the pale shiny half-moons of old kitchen burns. He wore one red shrimp boot and one ordinary sneaker, as if he'd dressed mid-emergency and never bothered to correct it.

"It's murdering itself," Mina said.

"Fair." He shifted the ice, grinned once, and was gone toward the kitchen.

The office settled back into fan noise and page noise. Mina looked at the form until the letters blurred, then set it aside and reached for the camp phone.

The regional permit desk sent her through four menus before a woman answered with the sort of exhausted courtesy that came from years of explaining the same restrictions to wealthy men in expensive hats. Mina could hear the woman's desk fan knocking once each rotation, a tiny plastic click beneath every sentence.

"Colonial bird access, South Gulf district."

"Hi," Mina said, and heard herself snap too bright, too fast. "I'm calling about nocturnal rookery access from the Rookery Key side. Protected channels, active breeding, but observational only. No collection."

"Sponsor?"

"Temporary camp contract."

"Institutional sponsor?"

"Rookery Key Bird Camp."

The keyboard clicked. The woman cleared her throat. "If the director is submitting on behalf of a managed program, the committee can review at the next meeting."

"When's the next meeting?"

"Four weeks."

Mina looked at the heat shaking over the mangroves beyond the dock, at a sky so white it had stopped pretending to be blue. "That won't work."

"Then county authorization."

"For observation?"

"For county business." Another click from the fan. "Mosquito, disease, incident response, something with a badge attached to it."

"What if the county pilot agreed to take an observer?"

"Then the county would have to request it. Name?"

The lie gathered behind her teeth and died there. "Mina Cordero," she said.

The pause lengthened by exactly the amount of time it took someone to pull up an old mess.

"Ms. Cordero," the woman said, and her voice got cleaner, more vertical. "I am seeing a prior Gulf Wildlife Board compliance matter. In that case any night-access request tied to your name would require board review regardless of sponsor."

"You just said committee."

"Board review in addition to committee."

"So more than four weeks."

"Yes."

Mina pressed two fingers against the bridge of her nose until the cartilage hurt. The printer spat another page with bureaucratic cheer. "Is there any shorter path?"

"Not a legal one I could tell you about on a recorded line."

For a second Mina liked her. Then the woman coughed, apologized, and repeated the access language one more time as if kindness and refusal were the same public service. Mina thanked her because she had not yet deteriorated enough to start cursing strangers who answered phones for the state, and hung up with the fan click still turning inside her ear.

She tried the respectable route next. She opened a blank email and stared at the white field until the old professional version of herself came limping back, the one that knew how to sound collegial and harmless and worth attaching your name to.

Dear Sonia,

I hope field season is treating you well. I am currently on a short-term contract at Rookery Key Bird Camp and hoped to ask whether you might be willing to advise on expedited observational access during breeding window...

She stopped there. Advised sounded innocent. Expedited sounded guilty. She deleted the line, wrote it again, and spent ten mean minutes sanding the message into something that looked less like begging than it was. She did not mention the rumor. She did not mention Tomas. She did not mention that the water had already narrowed in her head to one specific creek and one specific dark.

Sonia Vale had once spent an entire conference dinner rearranging lemon wedges around her plate by size because she said disorder made her scalp itch. Her email signature still carried a line about patience in estuarine work, which Mina had mocked privately for years and wanted now with a hunger that embarrassed her. She hit send.

Then she crossed to the fuel ledger clipped beside the office door and looked at numbers she already knew by smell. Generator reserve. Guest skiff. Kitchen propane delivery overdue. No line for private obsession. No line for disgraced guide wants to chase blue nonsense into protected water after dark.

Imogen found her there.

The director had managed, as always, to make a faded field shirt look deliberate. One cuff had been mended in white thread so small and exact it read less like repair than reproach. Her silver hair was pinned up with two pencils, one real and one campaign pen from some donor gala she probably hated.

"If you are trying to invent fuel," Imogen said, "do it in larger quantities."

"I'm trying to see whether there's any I can borrow."

"There isn't."

Mina kept her hand on the ledger clip. "For a survey window. One night. I wouldn't need much."

"You do not have a survey permit."

"I'm working on that."

Imogen looked at the stack of forms on the desk, then back at Mina's face. "No. You are practicing the emotional theater of working on that."

Mina's skin got hotter. The fan in the ceiling moved the air from one damp place to another. "I could go by paddle."

"Into a protected rookery at night during breeding season? On whose authority?"

"Yours, if you felt reckless."

"I do not."

There it was, the lower contraction rate, the manager's clean edges. Imogen only went fully formal when she was either furious or very tired. Today she looked too dry-eyed for fury.

"I need a look," Mina said. "Not a whole operation."

"Everybody who gets in trouble in wildlife work starts by needing a look." Imogen stepped closer and touched the ledger with one blunt fingernail. "This camp has guest runs booked through the weekend. The generator already coughed twice last night. If I spare fuel for anything, it will be for light or refrigeration, not for whatever private compulsion brought that expression onto your face."

Private compulsion. Donor-safe language for obsession.

Mina said, "You hired me because I know water and species behavior."

"I hired you because you needed work and because I needed a guide who could smile through bad optics for three weeks without setting the place on fire." Imogen's tone stayed almost pleasant. That was worse. "Do not mistake temporary usefulness for trust."

The words landed with the neatness of forms stamped denied. Outside, a gull hit the fish-cleaning table hard enough to rattle silverware. Neither of them looked.

"If I brought you something solid," Mina said, "not rumor, not camp folklore, would that change anything?"

Imogen's mouth tightened by one degree. "If you brought me something solid, I would first ask how you obtained it. That is not a threat. It is the condition of living in the world."

She took the ledger from under Mina's hand and rehung it on its nail. Conversation over. Managerial mercy. Mina hated how much it resembled professionalism.

The email from Sonia came thirteen minutes later.

Mina was back at the desk pretending she had moved on to guest check-in forms when the phone buzzed with the camp's cheap notification chime. Sonia had replied from a state truck somewhere inland, judging by the signature block and the little thumbnail photo of a wet dog in a life vest she used instead of a headshot.

Mina,

I can't touch this. Off the record: your name on a nocturnal rookery request would trigger review all the way up, and nobody is going to help you fast-track anything in breeding season. If Price sponsors it, she gets watched too. Let it go.

S

No greeting, no false warmth, no prayerful estuary quote. Just that.

Mina read the message twice, then a third time because she disliked how much the sentence let it go felt like old friends talking when it was really a professional door being closed with the heel.

She deleted the draft permit instead of saving it. The state printer wheezed once more and shut itself down as if offended by waste.

By noon the office had become a box of hot paper and insect noise. Mina stepped out onto the screened walkway and the heat hit with full body weight. The camp sat over blackwater on weathered pilings, each boardwalk joint speaking its own creak. Somewhere under the kitchen Beto was singing to himself in a language made mostly of curses and spoon names. The smell of fish scales traveled farther in heat than seemed fair.

Across the shell path and down toward the county dock, an airboat engine coughed, caught, and settled into a low rude confidence. Tomas, then.

She stood with Sonia's email still lit on the phone in her hand and watched a pair of white ibises tip through the shallows below. Official routes had not failed her by accident. They had worked exactly as designed. They had sorted her into the category that waited, apologized, submitted, disappeared.

***

The county dock sat a ten-minute walk away if you moved like an honest employee and seven if you moved like a thief. Mina made it in eight, sweating through the waistband of her shorts, stepping around oyster shell, mangrove roots, and the little sunstruck lizards that refused to yield the boardwalk until the last second.

County property announced itself with reflective signage and chain-link confidence. MOSQUITO CONTROL DISTRICT. AUTHORIZED ACCESS ONLY. FUEL LOG REQUIRED. Somewhere down the line, taxpayers had paid for the right kind of lock and the right kind of warning, and Tomas used both to push a flat aluminum world through water after dark.

He was on the shaded side of the dock with the engine cowling open. His forearms were dark with grease and old sun, the prop nicks on them thin as pencil marks. A chipped glass jar sweated on the plank beside him, full of coffee the color of engine oil. He drank it iced because, as Beto had already volunteered yesterday, Tomas claimed hot coffee before a night route made him seasick on land.

He looked up before she called his name. "You walk down here to arrest me or flirt?"

Mina stopped two boards short of the grease stain spreading under the airboat. "How lucky are you feeling?"

"Not very. County sent me two boxes of forms and half a can of spray. That's a bad sign." He tightened something with a socket wrench, listened, and tightened it again a quarter turn because apparently the first answer had not satisfied him. "You look like the state took a bite out of you."

"The state doesn't bite. It sends you to a different phone number until you die."

"That too."

He set the wrench down in a line with three others, smallest to largest, and wiped his hand on a rag that had given up being clean years ago. It was a careful habit, the lined tools, almost dainty if the hands had not been cut to hell.

Mina said, "How hard is it to get into the rookery channels after dark on county authority?"

Tomas leaned one hip against the airboat rail and looked at her properly now. Not at her chest, not at her legs, though he had done both often enough in passing. At her face. At the want she had apparently failed to hide.

"For county authority?" he said. "Easy as a uniform."

"For an observer."

"Depends who's observing and whether I enjoy unemployment."

"You'd enjoy it badly."

"I'd be talented at it, though."

He took a swallow from the jar. Coffee, sugar, maybe condensed milk. His wedding band flashed once in the dull light and disappeared again under grease. There was nothing pretty about the ring; it had the plain battered look of something worn through fuel, rope, dishwater, storms. The sight of it did not stop anything in her. It just changed the flavor.

"Permit desk told me county access is the only fast path," she said.

"Permit desk was right. That won't happen often. Mark the date." He hooked a thumb toward the water. "Protected channels at night mean one of three things. Mosquitoes. Fire. Dead body. You don't look like any of those."

"I could."

"No, you look like paperwork failed you."

She laughed despite herself. It came out meaner than amusement. "I need one run."

"Everybody says one."

"I mean it."

"So do they."

He did not move, and somehow the lack of movement had more leverage in it than stepping closer would have. Behind him the county fuel cage stood locked. Beyond that, the channel opened toward mangroves that turned black as old tea once the sun went down. She could map half the route from memory just by smell.

"You know where I want to go," she said.

"Yeah."

"You know why."

"Mostly." He tipped the jar at her phone. "How many people told you no before you walked down here?"

"Enough."

"Specific number."

"Two calls, one email, one boss."

"That's efficient."

"I've got a gift."

Tomas smiled with one side of his mouth only. "No, your gift is making trouble sound like fieldwork."

The line scraped at her pride. Lower down, something tightened anyway in the same place hunger and bad decision-making shared real estate.

"Can you do it?" she asked.

He looked back into the engine for a second as if the carburetor had earned a say. Then he lowered the cowling carefully instead of slamming it.

"I can do lots of things," he said. "Question is which part you think is free."

There it was. Not flirtation exactly. Not yet. The dry shift in the water before a gator moved.

Mina crossed her arms because otherwise her hands were going to start answering him. "I didn't ask for free."

"You asked like you hoped you'd get charity from a county man in mosquito season. That's cute."

"I'm not cute."

"No." He took another swallow of coffee. "You're expensive in a whole different way."

Heat crawled up her chest. Part shame, part anger, part the annoying fact that he was not wrong about the existence of a price. She said, "If you want cash, say cash."

He snorted. "For what, gas? I don't need your bird-camp wages. I need my job." He set the jar down. "Listen plain. I can shave a fuel log. I can say I burned more on drift and tide than I did. I can put you in the channels and keep my mouth shut after. I know every turn in there when the moon's useless. But if I do that, I'm not lending you binoculars. I'm putting county numbers and my own ass under your story."

"It's not my story yet."

"Then it's worse. It's a maybe." He pushed off the rail and came a little closer, enough that she could smell coffee, sweat, and the ghost of whatever solvent had lived on his hands all week. "I know what you're asking me to steal. Fuel's the easy part."

Mina held his gaze. "What do you want, then?"

He let the silence sit. Somewhere behind the dock, something metallic banged twice in the storage shed and stopped. The insects whined up out of the reeds as the day thickened toward evening.

"I want you not to turn me into a favor," he said at last. "I want you in this on purpose."

She almost said something neat and evasive. Some version of you think I'm that cold, or you don't know me, or don't flatter yourself. But she did know what he meant. A free ride let her keep the story clean in her own head: useful local man, necessary compromise, regrettable but impersonal. He was asking for the part that stained.

"You could've just said you wanted to fuck me," she said.

"I want to fuck you." His tone stayed almost conversational. "That isn't the whole point."

The sweat between her shoulder blades went cold under the shirt.

Tomas glanced once toward the road, then back. "If I help you for nothing, later you get to tell yourself I was just transport. Some airboat attached to a pulse. I don't feel like being that for you."

The line landed because it was too close to true. Mina had already done it in her head, filed him under access, fuel, channels, married complication. She had felt desire, yes, but she had also converted him into logistics so she would not have to examine what kind of woman kept reaching for locked places through men with keys.

"You're making yourself sound noble," she said.

"No, I'm making myself sound selfish. Keep up."

That dragged a laugh out of her, unwilling and hot. He saw it. He saw too much.

"Tonight," he said. "After lights-out. You come back here. If you still want the creek, you stop pretending this is just work. Then tomorrow I put gas where it doesn't belong and take you where the county says you weren't."

"And discretion?"

"I already offered that." He shrugged one shoulder. "Unless you plan to get loud."

She stayed where she was. The improved version she kept trying to sponsor into existence with clean emails and careful language lived in some other body, already halfway up the dock and calling this what it was: old damage finding a familiar door.

"You do this often?" she said.

"No."

"You expect me to believe that?"

"Believe whatever keeps you moving. Just be here or don't."

His phone buzzed in the pocket of his work shorts. He pulled it out, looked, and exhaled through his nose in something like amusement. For a second the screen showed a message from Rhea over a lock screen photo of a juvenile heron standing in a bait bucket with one offended yellow eye. Bring cumin if you're passing the market. The ordinary intimacy of it hit Mina harder than if the text had been explicit.

Tomas typed back with his thumb and pocketed the phone.

"You ought to go before one of the district idiots sees you and decides he's a witness," he said.

Mina did not move.

"That all?" he asked.

"No."

"Then what's left?"

She hated how little time it took to answer. "I'm deciding whether I mind you being right."

He laughed once, low. "Mind it tonight. Or don't."

When she walked back to camp the shell path felt steeper than it had on the way down. The heat had shifted from white to copper. Mangrove shadows stretched long and the insects had stopped pretending they would wait for dark.

Every board under the main house seemed to know where she had been.

She tried to work through dinner prep and failed at it. Tried to clean binocular lenses and nearly dropped one into a bucket. Tried to tell herself the only thing he had done was make plain what she already knew about access in a place run on keys, permits, marriages, and favors. That was true. It was also too bloodless to help.

By dusk the camp became a machine of small noises: pans, shower pipes, someone laughing at the far guest cabins, Beto swearing at a cooler latch as if it had insulted his mother. The sky went bruised peach over the mangroves. Mina took a shower that ran warm before it ran cold and stood under both temperatures without feeling cleaner.

When she dried off she checked her bank balance out of habit and instantly regretted it. Her last installment to the Gulf Wildlife Board had cleared two days earlier. The number left in her account looked like a dare. She put the phone face down on the bunk.

This was the stupid part, she told herself. Not the sex, exactly. Not even the theft. The stupid part was the old geometry of it, the one she had already paid for once and kept paying: a locked place, a man who moved easily through it, her own certainty that what waited on the other side might finally justify the mess. Jonah with the cooler. Tomas with the fuel key. Same shape in a harsher light.

But the memory would not stay Jonah's. It kept slipping back to water under spoonbills, to that specific rumor with its ugly little hook in her mouth.

***

After full dark she waited another fifteen minutes so she would not look eager, then hated the pettiness of the gesture because eagerness was exactly what had her by the throat. She changed into her least damp shirt, laced her boots, and stepped onto the boardwalk.

The camp at night was never quiet. It only rearranged its noise. Railing creaks. The generator's blunt hum. Fish smacking under the pilings. Insects loud enough to alter thought. She took the shell path without a light and let the mangrove silhouettes absorb her. Once she stopped because something big moved in the shallows and she did not feel like stepping on a stingray for principle.

The county dock kept one sodium lamp burning over the fuel cage and another over the sign-in station. Everything else went soft at the edges. Tomas was already there.

He had changed shirts but not jobs. Fresh cotton clung dark to the center of his back. The logbook lay open on the station counter beside a flashlight, two five-gallon cans, and the county key. The key had a yellow float the size of a fist attached to it, childish-looking until you imagined dropping it in black water.

He did not look surprised.

"Thought maybe you'd got smart," he said.

"I did. Late, though."

He pushed the logbook toward her with two fingers. "Read line seven."

She stepped in close enough to see the entries under the sodium glare. Date. Route block. Gallons issued. Vehicle number. Employee initials. The handwriting changed every line. Some neat, some drunk, some trying not to look drunk. Line seven was blank except for the date already written in Tomas's square mechanical hand.

"Why?" she asked.

"Because this is the part people like to forget. The boring line. The one that turns sexy bad decisions into a paper trail." He tapped the blank space. "If I fill those cans, I write fiction here. Not good fiction either. County fiction. Full of decimals."

Mina looked from the page to his face. "Trying to scare me?"

"Trying to make sure we're both awake."

She set her palms on the counter. It was warm from the day still leaking out of the metal. "I'm awake."

"Good." He held her there with his eyes. "Then say what you want."

There were easier versions. I want a ride. I want a window in the channels. I want a look. He kept waiting through all of them.

"I want into the rookery at night," she said.

"That's the permit answer."

"You asked for the plain one."

"I did."

Her throat felt dry and oddly metallic, like she'd licked a battery without meaning to. The sodium light flattened everything except the shadows under his cheekbones.

"I want the creek," she said. "I want the channel under the spoonbills before somebody else talks me out of it or the water changes. I want you to get me there."

He was still for a beat. "And?"

Mina hated him a little for not rescuing her with speed. She hated herself more for wanting rescue in the middle of this specific choice.

"And I want this too," she said. "You. Happy?"

Tomas's mouth shifted. Not a smile. Something more dangerous because it had relief in it.

"Closer," he said.

She came around the sign-in station and stood in front of him. The lamp brought every insect in the district to their orbit. One landed on his throat and he flicked it off without looking away from her.

"Last chance," he said. "If you walk now, I call you stubborn and we both sleep."

"Do we?"

"Probably not, but we'd stay dressed."

That did it. The last sliver of distance burned off under the line. Mina took his shirt in both fists and kissed him first because she refused, on some useless point of pride, to be invited into her own compromise.

His mouth tasted like sugarless coffee and salt. He made a rough sound low in his chest, one hand coming up under her jaw, the other bracing on the counter behind her hard enough to ring the metal. There was no soft testing to it. He kissed like a man who had spent all afternoon doing arithmetic around desire and had reached the number he wanted.

The county key knocked once against the counter as his hand found her waist. The float bumped her hip, absurdly buoyant. She laughed into his mouth and he bit her lower lip in answer, not gentle, not cruel, just enough to make the whole thing stop masquerading as banter.

"Inside," he muttered.

The storage shed smelled of rope, old plywood, bait plastic, gasoline, and the permanent warm rust of coastal work. Tomas shut the door with his heel but did not latch it. A slit of sodium light came through the warped boards and laid orange bars over stacked crates, spare life vests, a coil of hose.

He looked at her in the half dark and let the moment widen again. "Still yes?"

"Yes."

"You don't get to say later you did this for the fish."

"It's not a fish."

"See, that's how I know you're already gone."

His hand went under her shirt, palm rough, stopping flat against the skin of her stomach as if he wanted the answer there too. Mina sucked in breath. His hands were still faintly cool from the glass jar outside. Work smell lived in his knuckles: metal, fuel, mosquito repellent, channel mud. Her body answered before her conscience had finished forming objections. She was wet already. That offended her just enough to deepen it.

Tomas kissed her again, slower now, learning the angle. He slid his hand up her ribs and cupped her breast through the bra, thumb dragging once over the nipple until the fabric caught. Mina felt the old shame rise, not maiden shame, nothing that clean. Trade shame. The humiliating fact of wanting what you were also using.

She put both hands on his shoulders and felt muscle under sweat and thin cotton, the braced balance of somebody more used to moving against water than standing still on land. "You always run a negotiation like this?" she asked.

"No," he said against her throat. "Usually there's more paperwork."

She laughed once and then lost the laugh when his teeth found the tendon below her ear. The shed had no moving air. Heat stuck to everything. Somewhere beyond the wall a mullet flipped in the channel with a slap like a wet rag against wood.

His wedding band touched her side when he reached for the button of her shorts. The small cold line of it made her flinch.

Tomas stopped at once. "What?"

She could have lied. Could have said nothing, bug bite, splinter, wrong angle. Instead she said, "The ring."

He held her gaze in the half dark, fingers still resting at her waistband. "You knew about the ring this afternoon."

"I know."

"And?"

The clean answer was that it made this worse. The truer answer was that worse had become part of the draw the minute the deal took shape. Rhea existed out there in the dark beyond the dock, not as a prop, not as a sentence-ending moral lesson, but as a real woman who texted him about cumin and probably came home smelling like bats and alcohol swabs. That reality didn't stop the machinery. It lit every gear.

"And I know," Mina said.

Something unreadable moved through his face. Not guilt exactly. Not dismissal. More like tired recognition.

"All right," he said, and opened her shorts.

She stepped out of them herself because that felt less like being handled. Tomas watched, jaw tight. When she kicked the fabric aside, he let one hand travel down the inside of her thigh and pause there, testing nothing except maybe her nerve.

"You want me to tell you to stop?" he asked.

"Do you?"

"No."

"Then don't."

He pushed two fingers under the elastic of her underwear and found how wet she was. The breath he let out had laughter in it, bitter and pleased. "Jesus."

"Don't be smug."

"I'm not smug. I'm encouraged."

His fingers slid through her and her body went sharp and immediate around the touch. There was no romance left in the room after that, only pressure and the humiliating ease of response. She gripped a shelf bracket behind her head to keep balance. The metal was greasy and warm.

Tomas kept looking at her face as he touched her, as if reading instruments. He did not perform tenderness. He did not perform roughness either. He paid attention. That was worse. That made it intimate before either of them deserved intimacy.

"Tell me if you want different," he said.

# Chapter 3

The following night, by the time Mina left the screened walkway, the camp had gone from evening noise to the thinner sounds people made when they were pretending to sleep in heat. A box fan knocked somewhere in the guest rooms, once per rotation, the casing loose enough to complain but not loose enough to stop. The generator kept its low metal headache behind the shed. Beyond the pilings, the dark had weight. It held the channels flat and hidden, and it held the rookery too, spoonbills muttering in the mangroves with the ugly intimate sound of birds settling on eggs and each other.

She had changed into the least reflective clothes she owned, black shorts, dark long-sleeved shirt despite the heat, old deck shoes she did not mind ruining. Bug dope slicked the backs of her knees. She had a red-filter flashlight in one pocket, a small notebook in a zip bag in the other, and no camera worth the shame of carrying. If she came back with nothing, a camera would only turn the whole night into a better joke. If she came back with something, a camera phone clip through mangrove dark would not save her name.

The shell path to the county dock kept snagging at her ankles with roots and broken oyster. Mosquitoes found the strip of skin above her collar and needled there until she slapped one and felt her own blood wet on her fingertip. She kept walking.

Tomas was at the fuel cage under the dock light, half in yellow glare and half out of it. He had a penlight clenched in his teeth and a pair of cheap reading glasses low on his nose while he copied a number from the pump into the district ledger line he had left blank the night before. The county shirt hung open over a sweat-dark tank. His forearms were already striped with bites, and every time a mosquito landed he pinched it dead without interrupting the line of figures. He heard her shoes on the planks, looked up, and pulled the glasses off so fast the motion had embarrassment in it.

She pretended not to notice. He pretended she had not.

"You made it," he said, folding the glasses one-handed and shoving them into his shirt pocket. The penlight clicked off. "I was about to log you as a no-show and save myself the felony."

"You were not."

"No." He tapped the ledger with the pencil. "I was about to make up a route code that sounds boring enough nobody asks questions tomorrow."

He said it dry, but the page in front of him changed the night. Flirtation had felt deniable when it lived in doorways and half-jokes. His pencil moving over county paper did not feel deniable. Her name was nowhere on the sheet, which made it worse. If something went wrong, he could say he had been working. She would be the thing he had smuggled aboard.

The airboat sat tied off below them, fan cage ghostly in the dock light, hull nosed against pilings scarred by old paint and harder collisions. It smelled of mud, hot metal, fuel, and the sweet chemical bite of mosquito district work. A coil of hose lay on the planks. Lock cages for gear glinted farther down. The whole place looked practical enough to belong to men who got called out for weather and illness and dead fish, not to rumor.

Tomas tore the page from the carbon pad, tucked the duplicate under the clip, and glanced toward the road though nothing moved there except bugs under the lamp. "You want the last chance to back out, take it now."

"You offering a refund?"

His mouth shifted, not quite a smile. "No refunds. I said a chance."

She looked at the boat instead of him. If she met his eyes now she would have to decide whether she was admitting that she was here for the creek, for him, or for the possibility that the whole thing would reduce her to an old pattern with mosquitoes on it.

"I'm here," she said.

"Yeah," he said. "I noticed."

He went down the ladder first. She followed with one hand on the rail and one on the notebook in her pocket, absurdly protective of blank paper. When she stepped into the hull it rocked harder than she expected, and his hand came to her waist fast and certain, fingers spread wide through the damp shirt. He steadied her, then left his hand there half a beat too long before moving away to untie the line.

It had been like that since yesterday evening, every exchange carrying the memory of the fuel cage behind them and her back against the chain link, his thumb hooked under the waistband of her shorts while he told her what he could get her and what it would cost. She had done so much waiting in the years since BrightCurrent cracked her open in public. The eel existed anyway.

She leaned forward to be heard without shouting. "If you hadn't thought there was a chance, you wouldn't have done this."

"I might've done it just to see that look on your face when the creek paid out."

"Asshole."

"Maybe."

The word came back through the engine wash with no defensiveness in it. He took the next turn and then another, not toward open water yet. Mina felt the change in direction before she mapped it. The noise of the rookery dropped behind them. Mangroves closed up again, but lower here, more scrub than cathedral. The channel dead-ended in a pocket of slick mud and knee roots.

"This isn't the dock," she said.

"No."

He cut the engine.

Silence rushed back, thinner here, mostly insects and the slow click of cooling metal. The hull rocked once and settled into mud. Tomas did not look at her immediately. He sat with both hands still on the controls as if making sure the machine had truly stopped.

"What are you doing?" she asked.

"If I take you back like this you'll walk right through camp looking like a woman who got religion in a ditch."

"That's not an answer."

He turned then. In the dark his expression was mostly voice. "I'm giving us five minutes."

The sentence opened the space between them so fast it felt physical. Mina could have said no. She knew that. She could have told him to start the engine, could have climbed over the bench, could have taken the shell path back to the stilts and sat awake with the blue replaying by itself. Instead she looked at the mud bank, the hooked roots, the mosquitoes stitching in the air, and knew with humiliating clarity that five minutes was not what she wanted.

"That's not enough," she said.

He exhaled once through his nose. "Good."

She moved first because if she gave herself one more thought the old machinery of judgment might start up and she was sick of its voice. She crossed the narrow space of the boat, caught his shirt in both fists, and kissed him hard enough that their teeth clicked. Tomas made a low sound against her mouth, surprise and triumph both in it, then got one hand to the back of her neck and kissed her back with the stored force of several days' restraint and several years of not being young enough to mistake restraint for virtue.

He tasted of coffee gone stale hours ago and the mint he chewed to keep from smelling county chemicals all night. She felt the edge of his reading glasses in the shirt pocket when her hand flattened there, ridiculous and intimate. He laughed into her mouth when she found them.

"You hid them like contraband."

"They are contraband if you tell anybody."

Something bit the back of her calf and she ignored it.

This was the contract, or part of it, except no contract she had ever signed had her this hungry. The danger was that it did not feel purely transactional. If it had, she could have filed it with the other compromises. Instead the wanting ran on its own current. She wanted the way his hands stayed practical even while they got rougher, checking her balance before dragging her closer. She wanted the smell of fuel on his wrist when he pushed her shirt up. She wanted to stop hearing the word cost in both their mouths.

"Yes."

"Say it like you mean it."

"I'm sure," she said, and then because she wanted the leverage of honesty more than the dignity of pretending otherwise, "I saw it and I want you and I'm not sorting those out tonight."

That got him. Something open and ugly crossed his face, want with the manners knocked off it. "Good," he said again, softer now. "Because I'm not sorting shit either."

"I'm thirty-eight, not an idiot."

"Debatable."

"Keep talking."

He tore the foil with his teeth when his thumb slipped on the edge. The motion was clumsy enough to be human. The clumsiness undid her more than practiced smoothness would have. "I won't."

"That's not the same."

He obeyed, and still made obedience feel like pressure. The bench edge dug into the back of her thigh. He swore softly into her neck. She bit his shoulder through the tank top because the sound rising in her would have carried if she let it loose.

He held her at the hip and moved with the same contained force he used on the boat, no wasted flourish, no pretending they were somewhere softer. The hull gave a little under each thrust, nudging mud, tapping root. Mosquitoes whined around their heads. Fuel and mangrove rot and sex mixed so thoroughly she would smell this place on herself until morning.

She was glad the joke died. She had not come here for sweetness. She had come because the creek had shown its impossible blue spine and because desire made as much sense as anything else when the world briefly proved it still contained miracles contaminated by human hands.

Tomas got one hand under the back of her knee and hitched her leg higher. The shift opened her enough that she saw white for an instant, not metaphor, just actual pressure behind her eyes. She pressed her forehead into the damp side of his neck and felt his pulse hammering there. He kept one hand broad between her shoulder blades when the boat rocked, a worker's reflex that did not vanish just because he was inside her.

"Shut up."

"Make me."

It was the second time tonight, and the question had changed both times.

This time his mouth softened. "Yeah."

She rested a hand on the rail and stared at the black water beyond the bow. Nothing glowed there. Nothing had to. The night had already split itself into before and after.

"I wasn't paying you," she said.

He took that without flinching. "I know."

"And you weren't collecting."

"I know that too."

She nodded once, not because the matter was settled but because naming the false versions mattered. Payment would have been simpler. Innocence would have been simpler too. What they had instead was something that could slide between hunger and leverage without ever holding still long enough to cleanly indict or absolve either of them.

Tomas reached past her, turned the key halfway, and then stopped before the engine caught. "Walk me through what you saw," he said.

She did. Not the whole mythology, not the wetlab memory, not Jonah. "That's all?"

"That's plenty."

That one word should not have steadied her, but it did. Not for the future or for proof anybody else would take. Only for the next few hours, which was all the night could be asked to carry.

He started the engine. The roar came back brutal and familiar. They backed out of the pocket and turned toward open water. Mina sat beside him this time instead of on the rear bench, one hand braced on the dash and the other on the seat between them. The fan wash shoved damp air over both of them indiscriminately. The dark widened. Far off, the camp lights rose on their stilts like something smaller than it had looked from shore.

When he skipped the cut that would have taken them back toward the county road light, she glanced at him.

"You're missing your dock," she said.

"I'm not dropping you there and making you walk back smelling like diesel and bad decisions."

"Only bad decisions?"

He kept his eyes on the channel. "Don't push it."

The answer should have sounded like a joke. It landed closer to care, which was worse. Mina faced forward again and watched the camp grow out of the dark. The screened walkway showed a faint stripe of dim yellow. The generator muttered on the service side. Under the main house, pilings stood up from the blackwater like wet posts in a ruined barn.

By the time he eased the airboat in under the camp stilts beside the service ladder, the first edge of exhaustion had arrived, not enough to dull anything, just enough to make every movement feel scored into her body. When she stepped onto the ladder bolted to the camp's underside, he followed close enough that his hand hovered near the back of her leg but did not touch.
