"You need to wipe your neck," he said.

"Why?"

He rubbed at his own skin with two fingers. She reached up and came away with a smear of mud or grease or his sweat, impossible to separate. She laughed once, tired and not sorry.

"See you tomorrow?" he asked.

He said it lightly, but there were three different questions inside it: whether she would act strange, whether she would ask for more, whether she would pretend tonight had given her ownership.

"Speak for yourself."

"You're the one with the government pen."

She climbed the rest of the ladder with the notebook still blank in her pocket and the blue turn alive in her head: bend around root, pale twig, dead tank-room blue under tannin. The stilts creaked under her weight. When she reached the walkway, the engine coughed back to life below her and then thinned into distance as Tomas took the boat back out toward the county dock.

The camp smelled of old frying oil gone cold, damp wood, bird shit on the railings, and the thin medicinal trace of bug spray everybody wore this time of year. She moved quietly past the screened walkway. Behind one door somebody snored. Behind another, a radio murmured low enough that only the rhythm got through. Ordinary life had kept going while the creek briefly opened and showed her one lit muscle of impossible history.

At the shower stall first and then the sink of the outdoor wash station, she tried twice to get clean and watched black grit and a faint sheen of fuel swirl toward the drain. The water never got the smell fully off. Diesel stayed in her cuticles, and the corrugated bench still ached in the insides of her thighs. She was glad of that. The residue kept the sighting from drifting too fast into self-doubt.

When she finally lay down on her narrow camp bed, the sheet rough against her salt-stiff legs, she knew how little she had. She had no photo, no sample, no witness steady enough to take into a hearing and win. She had only one blue coil under blackwater, Tomas's hands on her waist in a hidden cut, and the knowledge that the rumor had moved from local story to private fact inside her body.

Sleep came in scraps.


# Chapter 4

By dusk, even after two showers, diesel still sat in Mina's cuticles. She had been lying on the cot in her underwear with a towel under her back, trying not to map the previous night too accurately, since Tomas dropped her at the stilts. The insides of her thighs still felt the corrugated airboat bench. Sleep had come in scraps and left her gummy-eyed and mean. When Rhea knocked on the screen door, Mina spent one hard second thinking Tomas had talked, that this was a summons disguised as politeness.

At the door, Rhea stood with a coil of mist net over one shoulder and one of her shaved-down pencil stubs tucked behind her ear. The pencil was so short it looked useless, but Mina had already noticed that Rhea distrusted mechanical ones on principle. She said they jammed when the air got wet and people trusted them anyway.

"You awake," Rhea said, not asking, "or just hiding from useful labor?"

Mina dragged shorts up her legs and opened the door. "Awake."

Too quick. She heard it herself.

Rhea did not bother pretending not to. Her face stayed still, narrow and tired in the lowering light, braid pulled tight enough to sharpen her cheekbones. Guano and rubbing alcohol rode under the clean salt of her skin. "Good. I need another set of hands for net checks. The glamorous assistant I was supposed to have developed an urgent romance with diarrhea."

Mina leaned on the doorframe because it kept her from fidgeting. "You always recruit people like that?"

"Only the ones who look as if they know how not to scream when something with wings touches them." Rhea shifted the net on her shoulder. "Have you ever untangled anything small and angry without making it worse?"

"Birds, mostly. Pelicans, gulls, one tern that tried to blind me over a sardine carcass."

"Close enough." A dry half-smile passed and was gone. "Bats are less theatrical than pelicans. Usually."

The screened walkway behind Rhea carried the camp's evening sounds in fragments: a pan knocked in the kitchen, Beto laughing somewhere near the generator shed with the crack in the middle that always made it sound as if he had surprised himself, a guest coughing with the martyrdom of someone who wanted the whole house to know he had allergies. The building turned every ordinary noise into evidence. Mina felt that all at once.

"Why me?" she asked.

"Because you're here, and because Imogen says you can keep your hands steady when people are watching." Rhea glanced down at Mina's bare feet. "Put on shoes. We'll be in the mangroves and I'm not dragging you to urgent care over a nail."

There were a dozen better answers Mina could have made. Something light. Something that made it plain she did not feel like prey. Instead she said, "Give me a minute," like a subordinate, and shut the door before Rhea could read anything else off her face.

By the time Mina came out with her boots unlaced and still damp from the afternoon rinse, Rhea had laid the mist net coil on the walkway rail and was checking a black hard case no bigger than a lunch pail. A row of green lights blinked on its face.

"What's that?"

"Ultrasonic recorder." Rhea snapped the case shut and handed Mina two canvas bags, rolled poles, and a bundle of cloth holding sacks clipped together with carabiners. "Grant-funded proof that I can still turn batteries into footnotes."

"Sounds expensive."

"That's why I resent it." Rhea took the net again. "Come on."

They crossed the short screened run to the bat lab. Mina had passed the place every day without going in. Up close it smelled like a hot attic soaked in ammonia: guano under old wood, rubbing alcohol, damp mesh, battery heat, the faint sweet rot of fruit someone had left too long in a field cooler. Cloth bags hung on a line above a stainless sink. A milk crate held labeled vials and a tangle of cables. Rhea's data table was pinned under maps, permit paperwork, a hand lens, and three more of the pencil stubs lined in a tobacco tin.

Rhea caught Mina looking at them. "Pens die. Pencils at least fail honestly."

"That's bleak."

"It's field science."

She said it with enough flat amusement that Mina laughed before she could stop it. The sound startled her hard enough that she felt it in the back of her teeth. She had spent the whole day with the blue coil in her head, the turn of it under black water, and every conversation since dawn had felt one inch off because of it. In the hot ammonia reek of the bat lab, the laugh came out clean anyway and left her breathing easier than she liked.

Rhea was already moving, efficient without hurry. She checked the recorder's memory card, tore strips of tape with her teeth, wrote tiny block letters on the cloth bags, then tucked the pencil back behind her ear. Her hands were narrow and scarred across the knuckles, not from one clean incident but from years of wire, rock, rust, and animals small enough to make people careless.

"Where are we setting?" Mina asked.

"Edge of the shell path near the freshwater seep first. Then the cut behind the lab if traffic is good." Rhea slung the recorder strap across her chest. "The mosquitoes lift there. The bats follow."

"It's rarer than people think." Rhea took the bag from her, weighing it in her palm for a second. "A lot of perfectly decent field people get scared and become clumsy. You get scared and get more exact."

"I get exact when I need something," Mina said.

"That also sounds specific."

It was. She could have named permits, gate keys, borrowed fuel, the damp black turn of an animal under the creek. She could have named Jonah years ago, or Tomas last night, or the whole gross old hunger of her life: access first, cost second, then the cheap surprise when the cost turned out to have a face. Instead she said, "Birders with hooks in pelican bills. Rich retirees who decide nesting terns need selfies. The usual."

Rhea's eyes stayed on her a moment longer than the answer deserved. "The usual is a violent category."

They worked through the next hour in that rhythm, silence and brief talk, talk and the soft snap back into silence when a net trembled. Rhea showed her how to check the tail membrane for wear without manhandling, how to keep cloth bags from twisting on themselves, how to listen past the mosquitoes to the recorder's dry chatter. She explained the difference between what donors called data and what actually ended up mattering. Wind. Water level. Which edge had been mowed. Which trees had been lit all week by guests who could not understand that floodlamps counted as habitat destruction even when they ran on good intentions.

"I spend half my time proving obvious things in expensive language," she said at one point, crouched over the recorder. "If I write 'artificial night-light encroachment alters roost use,' someone in an office nods solemnly and releases a few hundred dollars. If I write 'people leave lights on and then act surprised when animals stop coming,' I sound unscientific."

"You sound right."

"That's not the same category."

Mina looked at her profile bent over the machine, the clean line of nose and mouth gone blunt under headlamp shadow, the braid dark against the back of her field shirt. There was wear in her, not fragility but use: a shine at the cuff where fabric had rubbed thin, a healed split on one thumb, the tired precision of someone whose body had been made to serve grants, seasons, and low-budget expertise for so long that she no longer bothered pretending the work was noble all the time. Mina had expected intelligence. She had not expected that kind of damage. It made Rhea harder to dismiss and, therefore, more dangerous.

Rhea had been handed every opening: where Mina had been the night before, why Tomas had come in smelling of creek and sex and fuel. She had used none of them. She had needed competent hands, and Mina had given them. The plainness of that exchange stripped away a defense Mina had been leaning on without admitting it.

Back at the lab, Rhea hung the used bags and opened the recorder case on the data table. The green lights blinked like a patient insult. Mina washed her hands at the sink. The water from the tap came out warm already, tasting faintly of the pipes.

"How many?" Mina asked.

"Enough to justify losing sleep." Rhea keyed notes into her laptop with two fingers, not because she could not type faster but because she hated mistakes more than delay. "I'll know whether the recorder agrees after coffee."

Mina dried her hands on her shorts again. Decency would have sent her off with a good night, or a good morning, and left Rhea to her notes. She stayed anyway, looking at the line of damp cloth bags and the tobacco tin full of pencil stubs and the slumped shoulders Rhea let herself have now that no animal required anything from her.

Rhea shut the laptop halfway. "You can go collapse if you want."

"Do I look that bad?"

"You look like somebody who has been awake for two nights for two different reasons."

Heat touched Mina's face again. "That's generous."

Rhea slid the recorder case off the table. "Come get coffee before I regret speaking kindly."

She said it over her shoulder, already out the lab door.

Mina followed.

* * *

The main house held dawn the way a screened thing held water: not securely, but enough to change the air. Gray light came through the mesh and laid a weak pattern over the kitchen table, over the drying dish rack, over the stack of guest mugs with hand-painted spoonbills on them. The generator had dropped to a lower grumble. Somewhere in the walls a gecko clicked like a cheap switch.

Tomas was asleep in the room off the hall. Mina knew it before she saw the boot outside the door, toppled on its side with the lace still knotted because he always gave up on the first one and trusted sleep to strip the rest of him later. His snore came thin and whistling on the inhale through the nose he'd broken in a teenage fight and never fixed. The sound carried plainly enough that it might as well have been in the kitchen with them.

Mina stopped at the sink with the old familiar urge to become smaller than her outline. Last night or the night before, depending on where exhaustion had blurred the date, that same sound had been under her palms after Tomas turned on his side in her bunk and said he had to go before the house woke. She had not let herself think about him as a husband then. It had been easier to think of him as access wearing a body. Standing in Rhea's kitchen with his boot in view, that lie lost some of its efficiency.

Rhea set the kettle on the stove and turned the burner with the economy of long practice. She moved barefoot on the scarred wood, one heel blackened faintly from never remembering shoes soon enough. From the icebox she took eggs, yesterday's tortillas wrapped in a towel, half a tomato, and a jar of coffee grounds kept in an old pickle jar because the lid sealed better than the pretty tin Imogen used for guests.

"Sit," she said. "If you hover like that, I'll start assigning chores."

Mina pulled out a chair. It gave a long complaint against the floorboards. The whole building seemed to speak in dry old joints: the pilings down below, the screen door springs, the warped threshold to the pantry. Nothing here did silence properly.

Rhea poured water into the kettle and set two mugs on the counter, then, on reflex, set out a third. The motion had the weight of habit. Mina watched it and hated how much it moved her. Rhea cracked eggs one-handed against the pan rim, then warmed tortillas directly over the flame, flipping them with her fingertips because, apparently, she did not believe in tongs either.

"You're going to burn yourself," Mina said before she could stop herself.

"I do this every morning."

"That doesn't make it smart."

"No. It makes it repetitive."

She said it with the same dry plainness she used for everything, but a strand of hair had escaped her braid and stuck damply to the side of her neck, and there was something indecently intimate in seeing a person only partly assembled for the day. Not beautiful in any polished sense. Better than that. Human enough to want coffee before language. Human enough to stand in a kitchen in a field shirt that smelled of mangrove mud and cook eggs with the focused annoyance of someone who considered breakfast another maintenance task.

Mina realized, with a kind of dread, that this was exactly the sort of detail desire liked to anchor to.

Rhea slid a mug across to her. "Black?"

"Yeah."

"That's the only civilized answer before six."

The coffee was brutal and a little burned. Mina drank anyway. Her body met it with relief so immediate it felt crude. Rhea folded the eggs into the tortillas, added tomato slices, salt, and a splash of whatever vinegar-pepper concoction lived in an unlabeled bottle by the stove. She put one plate in front of Mina and kept the other, then carried the third mug to the edge of the counter nearest Tomas's room without comment.

"He'll find it if he wakes before it goes cold," she said.

There was no performance in it. No domestic sweetness either. Just knowledge accumulated by repetition. Mina took a bite to keep from saying something stupid. The tortilla was scorched in spots and still cold in the middle. The eggs were oversalted. It tasted better than anything she had eaten all week.

For a minute the only sounds were chewing, the kettle ticking as it cooled, Tomas's thin snore, and a spoonbill starting up outside with a rusty, offended racket that another answered farther off. Through the screen the dawn smelled of salt, wet wood, and the kitchen can outside beginning to warm toward fish rot.

Rhea drank half her coffee in one go and looked toward the hall as if taking the measure of the house. "For what it's worth," she said, "this place hears everything."

Mina set her mug down carefully. "That so."

"It's a design flaw if you enjoy privacy. It's a useful diagnostic tool if you don't."

Mina forced a small shrug. "Camp's old."

"Camp is acoustically indiscreet." Rhea tore her tortilla in half. "Cabin Two had a couple here last week who whispered all their arguments and somehow made them louder. I know which guest unwraps cough drops at three in the morning. Beto eats cereal over the sink when he thinks Imogen is asleep. Tomas believes he comes up the walkway quietly after county runs, which is optimistic." She took another bite, chewed, swallowed. "You see my point."

Every board in Mina's body went rigid. It would have been easier if Rhea had come at her hot. Easier if there were tears, accusation, territory. Something dumb and obvious she could manage with apology or denial or cruelty. This measured plainness left nowhere useful to stand.

"If this is about Tomas," Mina said, too fast again, "I didn't ask him to report to you, and I didn't ask for commentary over breakfast."

Rhea looked at her over the rim of the mug. Not cold. Not warm. Exact. "No. You asked him for other things."

The blood rose hard under Mina's skin. She stared back because looking away would have been worse. The hall suddenly felt too narrow, the kitchen too near every room in the house.

"You wanted a scene?" she said. "You could've had it sooner."

"I don't want a scene." Rhea's voice stayed level enough that the words sounded less emotional than final. "If I wanted theater, I wouldn't have spent six hours handing you cloth bags in mosquito weather."

That checked Mina more cleanly than shouting would have. She heard the truth in it. Rhea had not used the shift to humiliate her. She had used the shift for the shift. The rest was this: eggs, coffee, daylight, and nowhere to hide.

Mina looked down at the table. The painted spoonbill on her mug had a chip through one wing. "Then what do you want?"

"At the moment? More sleep." Rhea set the mug down. "Beyond that, I want you to understand where you're standing."

Mina said nothing.

Rhea leaned back in her chair, listening for a beat as Tomas turned over in the other room. The mattress springs gave a tired small cry. "You're on stilts with three staff rooms, six guest rooms, and screens thin enough to pass gossip through by osmosis. Imogen misses very little that touches payroll or reputation. Beto misses nothing at all and pretends not to because he has manners. The guests are bored. The walls are cheap. If you think anything here belongs only to the people doing it, you're making a category error."

It was such a Rhea way to say it that Mina almost laughed, except nothing in her was loose enough for laughter now.

"So this is a warning."

"Obviously."

"For me?"

Rhea considered that too, which felt worse than if she had answered fast. "Mostly, yes."

Mina let out air through her nose. "That's generous of you."

"It's practical." Rhea's eyes stayed on her. "Tomas has county work. I've got a contract and a room tied to it. You've got a temporary bed and Imogen's patience, both of which can disappear in the same afternoon. If this turns into camp knowledge, the consequences won't distribute evenly."

There it was, stripped clean. No plea for loyalty. No claim of injury. A power map laid on the table next to breakfast.

Mina hated that the part of her that answered most quickly was the part relieved to be understood. "You think I don't know I'm the easiest person here to throw overboard?"

"I think knowing a fact and behaving as if you know it are different skills."

Mina almost snapped something useless back. Instead she heard Tomas's snore catch in the hall, heard a guest toilet flush two rooms away, heard the screen slap once in the breeze. The building was doing exactly what Rhea said it did: making private life public by inches.

"This wasn't supposed to be..." Mina started, then stopped because every ending to the sentence sounded cheap.

Rhea spared her by taking a bite of tortilla. After she swallowed, she said, "Nothing ever is."

The mercy of that nearly undid Mina more than the warning had.

She picked at the edge of her plate. "You seem very calm."

"I'm tired," Rhea said. "That can resemble calm from a distance."

Mina looked up.

Rhea's mouth shifted, not quite smiling. "Also, I'm a scientist on seasonal funding. If I reacted to every stupid thing people did with their bodies, I'd have no time left for data."

Despite everything, Mina laughed once, sharp and involuntary. Rhea's expression loosened a fraction in answer.

"That isn't the same as saying I don't care," she added.

The sentence altered the room. Mina felt the skin at the back of her neck tighten, as if a draft had found its way through the screens. Rhea had left the caring undefined, unsheltering, and that made it more real than anything kinder would have.

Mina's fingers tightened on the mug. The ceramic had gone slick with condensation where the kitchen air hit the last of the cooler coffee. "Are you asking me to stop?"

Rhea shook her head once. "I'm not issuing permits."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer I've got." She pushed the plate away and stood to rinse it. "What you do with Tomas is your business until the building, the camp, and everybody's employment make it mine. We're closer to that line than you think. That's all."

She ran water over the plate, then over her own fingers, as if the conversation required the same practical clean-up as egg grease. Mina watched the tendons move in the back of her wrist. Watched the escaped strand of hair darken further where the sink mist caught it. The wanting that rose in her stacked itself too fast: wrist, neck, mouth, and the mind that had laid out the danger without dressing it as virtue, the work-night competence, the refusal to turn herself into either martyr or fool.

Mina said, because her pride never knew when to stay buried, "Most people would've screamed."

Rhea dried her hands on a dish towel. "Most people should not work with bats."

"That can't be your only standard."

"No." She hung the towel back over the oven handle with unnecessary precision. "I also dislike melodrama before sunrise."

There was humor in it, but not enough to make the thing light. Mina felt the laugh come and did not give it sound. Outside, the spoonbills had properly started. Their noise rolled in ragged bursts from the rookery, half newborn trumpet, half rusty hinge.

"Why ask me tonight?" Mina said. "For the shift, I mean. If you already knew."

Rhea leaned against the counter and folded her arms. In the daylight, with the edge gone off the field dark, Mina could see how badly the night had used her too: grit stuck at one cuff, a faint bruise of exhaustion under the eyes, the red welt of a mosquito bite she had missed behind one ear. "Because I needed competent help," she said. "Because Beto had kitchen duty. Because Tomas would have tried to make himself useful and ruined at least one net. Because I was curious whether you were actually as good with your hands as you look when you're pretending not to be nervous."

Mina stared.

Rhea let that sit for a beat, then added, "You are."

The kitchen, already hot, seemed to tighten another notch around Mina's skin. Safety would have meant a joke or silence. She said, quieter than she meant to, "You make that sound dangerous."

"Most competence is." Rhea picked up her mug again. "Especially in a place that runs on scarcity and proximity."

The line settled in her like a hook finding hold. Flirting would have been easy to waste. This had structure; it gave the wanting room to develop a spine.

Tomas coughed in the other room and rolled over again. The mug Rhea had left for him waited untouched on the counter, steam gone thin from the top. Mina followed Rhea's glance to it and felt, not jealousy exactly, but the dense fact of another life: mornings repeated often enough to become gesture, knowledge of each other's boots and coffee habits and broken sleep. Rhea had not been standing still in some wife-shaped holding pattern waiting for Mina to complicate her. She had a marriage, a survey contract, a tobacco tin full of pencil stubs, a body full of bites and work, a way of turning tortillas with bare fingers because she hated tongs. Wanting her would mean wanting a whole arrangement of habits, loyalties, intelligence, and material conditions. Mina recognized the scale of that too late to stop.

Rhea looked back at her. "I'm running the nets again tomorrow night if the weather holds."

The invitation was plain enough to make Mina mistrust it.

"After this speech?"

"It wasn't a speech. It was site information."

"You always wrap warnings in field language?"

"Only when the field deserves accuracy." Rhea took the last swallow of coffee and set the mug in the sink. "Can you come or not?"

Mina answered before caution could organize itself. "Yeah."

Rhea nodded as if the answer had been expected and therefore not worth dramatizing. "Good. Try to sleep before then."

She gathered the plates, Tomas's untouched mug, and the pepper-vinegar bottle into one capable arm. At the hall she paused and looked back with the same unreadable steadiness she had brought to the nets.

"For the record," she said, "the walls aren't the only problem. People get sloppy when they think secrecy is the same thing as privacy. It isn't."

Then she went down the hall toward the sink and Tomas's boot and the room beyond, carrying the ordinary debris of breakfast as if nothing in the house had shifted. Maybe nothing had, from her point of view. Maybe this was only one more maintenance task before sleep.

Mina stayed at the table with her ruined appetite and the last bitter inch of coffee. The dawn light had brightened enough to show every scratch in the wood. Outside, a skiff engine started somewhere far off in the channels and flattened into distance. The camp woke in pieces around her: a shower pipe rattling, Beto swearing softly when something metal hit the floor in the kitchen annex, one guest beginning a forced-cheerful conversation with nobody yet visible.

Rhea had meant to warn her. She had done that. Mina understood the geometry now more clearly than she had yesterday: the thin walls, the payroll, the marriage, the fact that Tomas was not some private compromise she could keep sealed away from the rest of the camp. The warning had teeth. What stayed nearest the skin was the six hours in the mosquitoes, the quiet competence, the story about the crying graduate student, the tobacco tin full of pencil stubs, the way Rhea had seen her nerves and named the better thing underneath them instead.

Mina had spent the last day treating Rhea as a structure to get around. By the time she finally stood and took her plate to the sink, that lie was gone. In its place stood a hunger with no practical use. She wanted another night beside Rhea under the mangroves, another hour of clipped instructions and dry jokes and exact hands on wet net line. She wanted the woman's mind, which was the real trouble. She wanted to be looked at that way again and not found sloppy.

With Tomas, wanting had been a bargain and a door. With Rhea it already felt like a measurement.


# Chapter 5

Rhea had written the low tide time on the inside of her wrist in blunt pencil, and every few minutes she looked at it as if skin might revise itself out of spite. The digits were already smudging with sweat. In the bat lab's yellow light they looked like a second pulse under her bone. She was on her knees in front of an open equipment case, sorting cables into two piles with the tidy impatience she brought to anything that had once worked and no longer did. The room smelled of rubbing alcohol, warm plastic, and the sweet rot of old guano baked into plywood.

"You still have your boots on," she said without looking up. "That gives you a chance at earning your keep. Come with me."

Mina leaned in the doorway with a flashlight tucked under one arm and pretended she had not been watching the back of Rhea's neck for a full minute. Rhea's braid was pulled so tight it changed the shape of her face, all those narrow, severe lines made sharper by the heat. A crescent of white zinc clung to one ear where she had missed it that afternoon. She had changed out of her donor-safe shirt and into an old gray tank with a university logo almost scrubbed away by bleach. That should not have made her look less official. It did.

"Is that an invitation," Mina said, "or a conscription?"

"Field labor doesn't improve if you flirt at it." Rhea snapped a battery door closed and finally glanced up. Her mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "The west recorder died again. I want the card before the tide comes back over the route."

"Where's west, exactly?"

"Under the old intake line."

The phrase hit Mina a half second late, not because she recognized it yet, but because anything under the camp sounded better than another evening in the donor-facing half of the place, hauling coolers for guests who called every roseate spoonbill pretty in the same lacquered tone they used for cocktails. She pushed off the frame.

"You could've led with that."

"I assumed curiosity was already your strongest muscle."

She said it dryly, as if she had measured Mina and found appetite easier to trust than virtue. Mina liked that about her. It was cleaner than niceness. She crossed the room and took the second pack. One recorder, one hand net, spare batteries, calipers, sample bags, gloves, two headlamps wrapped in orange tape so nobody borrowed them and forgot where they came from. Rhea slipped a peppermint into her mouth from a dented tin she kept in the side pocket and offered Mina one without ceremony. Mina took it. The sugar cracked between her teeth, cold and medicinal.

"Guano?" Mina asked.

"And because the mosquitoes are less impressed by sweetness than the guests are."

They went out through the screened walkway, the hinges giving their nightly complaint. The camp at low water always looked indecent to Mina, as if the tide had lifted the hem and showed too much of its legs. Barnacled pilings sweated in the late light. The blackwater under the house had drawn down enough to expose slick roots, beer-bottle glass buried in marl, a length of pipe furred with oyster shells. Above them someone laughed in one of the guest rooms, one bright donor laugh cut off by the door sliding shut. The generator thudded from the shed in patient, expensive intervals.

Rhea moved ahead of her with that unnerving economy she had in the field, never rushing, never hesitating, as if the ground declared itself properly beneath her feet. She used a cheap telescoping pointer to nudge ferns aside rather than her hands. A habit, probably. Also a preference. Mina watched the wet graphite of the tide time disappear and reappear on her wrist as she swung her arm.

They took the shell path past the kitchen cistern, then dropped off the boardwalk where the camp kept pretending no route existed. The mud accepted Mina to the ankle on the first step and to mid-calf on the second. It was warm on top and cool underneath, the layers distinct as if the earth had been storing separate weather. Mosquitoes found the backs of her knees at once.

"You do this every survey?" Mina asked.

"Only the ones the permit office forgot to make impossible."

"That sounds almost cheerful."

"Don't make me repeat myself and ruin it."

There was enough of a path for one person if you believed in paths. Mangrove knees rose from the muck like clenched knuckles. The roots made cages and false doors and abrupt little chambers of shadow full of fiddler crabs waving their one ridiculous hand. Rhea used her pointer, the beam of her headlamp, and nothing else. Mina used her boots, her palms, and a vocabulary that had improved a lot since she started sleeping with Tomas and spending her afternoons with guests. The mud sucked at her left calf where the old prop scar ran, as if it recognized the groove and wanted back in.

They kept talking because the route invited silence too easily, and silence with Rhea did not feel empty. It felt selected.

"What killed the recorder?" Mina asked.

"Humidity. Salt. A bad seal. An iguana with opinions. Pick any three."

"I like the iguana. Gives the failure some dignity."

"The failure is a county reimbursement form. It has no dignity."

Mina laughed. Rhea glanced back once, brief and satisfied, the way a person checked whether a knot had held. She ducked under a low branch and stopped at a rusted stake with a square of survey tape tied to it. On the tape somebody had written R4 in waterproof marker, the letters bled thick by rain and years.

"From here," Rhea said, "watch your right side. The marl shears off."

"You tell everybody that."

"No. Most people I let slide."

That should have been an insult. It wasn't. It landed with the strange lightness of being chosen for extra trouble.

The air changed before the ground did. The mangrove heat thinned. Something cooler breathed through it, mineral and stale, not the living swamp exhale of rot and tannin but a sealed smell let out after a long sentence. Mina stopped smelling tide and started smelling old concrete, wet metal, the dead-cold memory of air-conditioned hallways where people in logo shirts talked about resilience over catered shrimp.

Ahead, roots gave way to a dark cut in the bank. Concrete shoulders, half swallowed by mangroves. A service opening high enough to crouch through, low enough to feel like trespass before you even bent. Emergency light housings, long dead, clung to the walls like blind fish eyes. Black mildew veined the ceiling. One section of hazard stripe still held under the slime: yellow, black, yellow.

"Jesus," Mina said.

"That was roughly my note on the original site map."

"You knew this was here."

"I knew there was abandoned infrastructure under the west berm. The camp deed calls it a drainage easement because people get nervous around nouns." Rhea squinted at the recorder in her hand. "I didn't know the bats liked it until last week."

She crouched and went in. Mina followed because the alternative was standing outside with her own pulse making a fool of her. The temperature dropped another few degrees inside, enough to pebble the sweat on her forearms. The beam of her headlamp picked out pipes cut flush to walls, cable trays with their guts ripped out, barnacle scars where old waterlines had once sweated salt. The floor pitched gently down. Tidal seep had left a slick line of silver mud through the middle. Overhead, tiny dry scratches multiplied into the nervous leather whisper of roosting bats.

The tunnel bent left. On the wall at shoulder height, under a rash of rust, an enamel sign still clung on with three bolts. Most of the lettering had gone brown and illegible. The logo had not.

BrightCurrent.

This was not the polished public version with the stylized wave in blue glass. It was the service mark, the one stamped on staff doors and tank fittings and loading cases that were not meant for visitors. The wave reduced to a hard white arc. Under it, just enough words survived to be worse than none.

ANNEX C

INTAKE

SENTINEL

Mina's body did a stupid thing and believed she had stepped backward in time. It wasn't metaphor, or the grand tragic language people used when they wanted the past to sound artistic. Her stomach simply dropped as if an elevator had started moving. For a beat she tasted refrigeration and latex gloves and the powdered coffee BrightCurrent kept in conference rooms no contractor was supposed to linger in. Jonah's hand at the small of her back came and went like a bad smell. So did the shine of tank glass. So did the lie she had once told herself that service corridors did not count as inside.

She reached out before she meant to and put two fingers on the rusted edge of the sign. The enamel was colder than the air.

"Mina."

Rhea had stopped ten feet ahead, one boot planted on a cracked curb that had once directed runoff. The recorder hung slack in her hand. She was not startled. That would have been easier. She was watching with the flat, deliberate attention she gave data that had turned troublesome.

"You know this place," she said.

The bats above them shifted, a dry million-fold clicking like fingernails on paper.

Mina took her hand off the sign. Rust reddened her fingertips. "I know the company."

"That wasn't my question."

The tunnel seemed narrower all at once, not because it had changed but because Rhea had put language in it and language took up room.

"Let's get your recorder," Mina said. "Then you can interrogate me on stable footing."

Rhea kept looking at her for another breath, then nodded once. "Fine. But don't confuse postponement with escape."

***

She turned and kept walking. Mina followed because she had already chosen the worse path several times in her life and this at least was honest about the mud.

The tunnel opened into a wider chamber where concrete met root and lost. Mangroves had found hairline cracks years ago and widened them into a patient vandalism. Roots as thick as wrists punched through the back wall and drank the dim green light leaking from the far end. Half the ceiling had been lined once with acoustic insulation; now it hung in wet rotting flaps. Bats nested in the pockets behind it. The recorder sat zip-tied to a conduit run above a barred drainage grate, its casing salted white.

"There," Rhea said.

Mina shouldered off the pack and handed up the spare batteries. Rhea climbed the conduit support with more contempt than caution, the recorder between her teeth for a second while she freed the knot. It should not have been erotic. Mina did not bother lying to herself that it wasn't. Everything about Rhea was cleaner in motion and dirtier in implication. The tendons moved in her bare arm when she braced. A streak of mud ran along one shin. Her braid had started to fray at the nape and the loosened hairs caught the light like black fishing line.

When she dropped back down, she landed close enough that their boots touched. The chamber smelled of bat piss, tidewater, and old machinery giving up slowly in the dark.

"Card's good," Rhea said. "The housing isn't. Salt got under the gasket again."

Mina took the dead recorder, more grateful for the object than she should have been. It gave her hands a reason to move. She unscrewed the back and peered in at the green bloom around the contacts.

"You could dry-brush this, but it'll corrode again."

"I know."

"I mean tonight. With alcohol first, then brush."

"I heard you the first time."

She said it mildly. Mina looked up. Rhea had leaned one shoulder against the wall under a cluster of roosting bats and folded her arms. She was not going to help her out of this with work.

"You went white back there," Rhea said. "That's not easy in this light."

Mina kept her eyes on the battery compartment. "It's a company sign. I've been publicly flayed over the company sign. Some reaction isn't weird."

"No." Rhea tapped the wall once beside her shoulder. "This is weird."

The concrete gave back a hollow sound. Behind it water moved, slow and hidden. Mina felt it rather than heard it, the way she sometimes felt a big fish shift under blackwater before the surface said anything. She looked at the cracks webbing the chamber, at the roots feeding themselves through poured concrete, at the low pipe mouths cut into the far wall. Intake. The word was no metaphor now, no rumor either. Water had come through here from somewhere and gone back out somewhere else. There were only so many somewhere elses in a wetland with this tide.

Her mouth filled with peppermint and metal.

"I worked around BrightCurrent," she said.

Rhea did not move.

"That still isn't specific enough."

"I know."

Mina shut the recorder harder than necessary. The sound slapped around the chamber. A few bats shivered overhead but held fast.

"I did donor interpretation for them sometimes," she said. "Guided groups. Translating habitat talk into language rich people could mistake for ethics. They liked having a field face in the room when they talked about what their fish were going to save."

"Fish."

"Eels. Whatever version sold best that quarter."

Rhea's head tilted slightly. It was the smallest movement, but on her it meant a door had opened somewhere behind the eyes. "The articles said you photographed a specimen and lied about where it came from."

"The articles said a lot of tidy things."

"Were any of them true?"

Mina laughed once, no humor in it. "Enough to ruin me."

Rhea let the answer sit there and spoil. She was good at that. Tomas filled silence with heat or jokes or blunt hands. Rhea let it hold its shape until the person across from her either respected it or panicked.

Mina panicked a little.

"I had access I wasn't supposed to have," she said. "Not employee access. Not exactly. But more than a tour guide should. Enough that I know their service markings. Enough that seeing this under camp makes me want to peel my own skin off."

"Because?"

"Because public-side BrightCurrent liked glass walls and educational plaques. Back-side BrightCurrent looked like this. Pipes, intake runs, staff doors with boiled-off paint. This isn't a random culvert they happened to slap a logo on. This is theirs."

Rhea's gaze went past Mina to the cut pipe mouths, the cracked floor, the root-thickened wall. Her face did not change much, but her breathing did. Slower. More measured. That was how strain showed up on her, not as spectacle but as calibration.

"If this fed into the west channels," she said, "my contamination baselines are garbage."

"Maybe not garbage."

"Do not offer me comfort you haven't earned."

There it was. Not loud. Worse than loud. Mina felt it along her ribs like a line of cold nails.

"Fair," she said.

Rhea stepped off the wall. "How much more is there than the magazine version of your disgrace?"

"Enough."

"That's not a quantity."

"It's the one I have on me."

Rhea looked at her so steadily Mina had to turn and aim her light at the far wall, just to break the line. The beam found more of the old chamber. A heavy steel door warped on its hinges where the concrete narrowed again. The stencil on it had peeled down to three surviving letters. NEX. To the right, a conduit pipe vanished into marl and root, heading east under the camp.

This wasn't camp-adjacent, or the same watershed in the abstract. It ran under the camp and straight through it. Whatever BrightCurrent had done here had not stopped at its own neat parcel lines. The water under the guest rooms, under the kitchen, under the boards where donors set wine glasses during sunset talks, had once been organized to move through this place. Mina knew enough hydraulics to feel sick with it. The creek she had been treating like wild witness was plumbed straight through the edges of her old shame.

She heard herself say, "Fuck."

Rhea followed the beam. "You recognized more than the logo."

"Yeah."

"Talk."

Mina dragged the back of her wrist across her mouth. The peppermint was gone. The guano smell had won. "Service annex. Intake side. They used satellite rooms for holding systems, filtration, quarantine, whatever they didn't want donors near. I wasn't supposed to see most of it. But I saw enough. Enough to know they wouldn't build dead-end concrete for decoration."

"So where does this tunnel go?"

"If the maps in my head still mean anything, it ties the old annex to the channels east of camp. Maybe as exchange water, maybe outflow during storms, maybe both." She swallowed. "I don't know if it still functions. I know it existed on purpose."

Rhea's jaw tightened, not with pretty theatrical clenching but with a precise little shift that made one side of her mouth flatter. "And you were going to tell me this when?"

"When I stopped feeling like I was going to throw up on your recorder."

"That's an answer about timing, not intent."

Mina set the recorder down on the curb between them because she needed both hands empty. "I didn't know this was here."

"I'm not accusing you of pouring the concrete personally."

"Good. Because I'd have done a worse job."

For the first time since the sign, Rhea almost smiled. It vanished fast. "I'm accusing you of carrying around a bigger story than you've given anyone at camp."

Mina looked at the steel door with its peeled letters. The memory came wrong and sharp: Jonah leaning against a loading dock in a shirt too expensive to be called rough, telling her no one ever remembered which cooler had moved through which door if the paperwork smiled hard enough. She had been younger then, not stupid exactly, but hungry in the way that makes stupid feel like strategy. She could say none of that now without saying too much. The thought of naming the cooler tightened the back of her throat. If she said it tonight, the word would not stay buried in wet concrete. It would climb the pilings with them, get into Tomas's face somehow, get into the kitchen, get into every room with a screen door that couldn't quite shut.

"There is more," she said. "You were right about that before you asked. But I'm not giving you the whole thing in a hole under camp with bat shit dripping in my hair."

Rhea absorbed it. No flinch, no offended wife act, no righteous speech. Mina had wanted her, from the first time she'd watched Rhea wash a net with methodical, angry care, because wanting her felt like wanting an instrument sharpened for work. This was part of it too. The refusal to overperform the wound.

"All right," Rhea said.

Mina blinked. "That's it?"

"No." Rhea bent, picked up the recorder, and slid it into the pack. "That's me deciding the next question gets me farther than the last one."

She took two steps toward Mina. The chamber made them feel closer than they were. Bats shifted above, their tiny bodies making heat in the insulation. Somewhere behind the wall the hidden water moved again with a soft suck through pipe or root.

"You and Tomas are not nearly as covert as either of you think," Rhea said.

Mina's face went hot in a way the tunnel did not deserve. "Rhea."

"Don't. I'm not asking for a recital and I'm not offering you a scene." She spoke with that same controlled syntax she used on guests right before cutting them off at the knees. "I'm saying I already live with one man who assumes omission counts as kindness. I don't need a second person trying it on me."

Mina stared at her.

Rhea's eyes dropped, not coy, not shy, just measuring, down Mina's throat, over the mud on her shirt, to the place where the recorder pack strap had rubbed a wet diagonal across her chest. Then back up.

"And before you decide to be noble," she said, "don't. It isn't kinder if you flatten me into the wife who doesn't know what room she's in. It's just lazier."

The chamber had gone very quiet except for the bats and the buried water. Mina could feel the space between them the way she felt a storm front before it showed on radar, pressure first, then static, then the first rearranged smell. She wanted to say Tomas mattered, Tomas wasn't a prop, Tomas had not been tricked so much as entered. All of that was true and none of it was the point.

"You want me to apologize," Mina said finally.

"No." Rhea's voice dropped a register. "I want you to stop editing reality in ways that require me to play innocent."

That landed harder than accusation would have. Innocent. As if that were the humiliation she actually feared for Rhea: not being wronged, but being handled.

Mina nodded once. It felt insufficient and therefore honest.

"Okay," she said.

Rhea held her gaze a second longer, then turned toward the tunnel mouth. "Good. The emergence count will start in ten minutes. Bring the pack."

Mina shouldered it and followed her out, the BrightCurrent sign at her back like a hand she had once mistaken for luck.

***

By the time they reached the tunnel mouth again the outside light had thinned to mangrove dusk, that dirty gold minute when every mosquito in the county filed its claim. Rhea chose a concrete lip just outside the opening where the bank widened enough for two people to crouch without sliding into the mud. From there they could watch the emergence and still hear the recorder if it decided to die theatrically in public. The tide was turning. Water licked at exposed roots with a slow, deliberate sound, like someone cleaning a knife.

Rhea set the spare recorder on an overturned crate embedded in marl and took out her field notebook. Its cover was bubbled from years of humidity. She wrote the time, weather, route condition, probable equipment failure, then underlined probable once so hard the pen nearly tore through. Mina sat beside her and held the red-lensed flashlight where she needed it.

The bats came out in hesitant drips at first, one, three, five, then a whole shaken length of them, small bodies slipping through the concrete throat and up into the dark over the trees. Rhea counted under her breath. Her voice changed while she worked. It lost whatever edge camp life put on it and grew barer, almost intimate, the syllables pared down to what observation required. No softness in it, only concentration. Mina had heard singers warm up with less private focus.

"Forty-seven, fifty-eight, seventy-one..." Rhea's pencil moved fast. "Gap. Start again."

"I thought you said this wasn't a choir audition."

"Do not sabotage the data because you're sexually inconvenient."

Mina choked on a laugh. "That's one way to phrase it."

"It's the one that fits."

The line should have broken the charge. It only changed its flavor. There was a way Rhea said explicit things without making them cheap. She laid them on the table like instruments, sterile and ready. Mina wanted to put her mouth on that calm just to see whether it would stay calm.

The last heavy burst of bats streamed out and vanished toward the channel. Rhea finished the count, shut the notebook, and flexed her writing hand. She had a callus where the pen sat. Mina watched her thumb rub it once.

"You've done this route alone?" Mina asked.

"Twice. I preferred tonight."

"Because of the extra hands."

"Because if I slipped on that rebar and cracked my head open, you'd be arrogant enough to drag me back before the tide covered me."

"That is almost romantic."

"Don't push your luck."

The dusk gathered around them. Camp was hidden now by root and bank, but Mina could still picture the lights coming on along the walkway, the guest rooms breathing cooled air through bad seals, Tomas somewhere on county shift or on his way back from it. His absence felt present the way county signage felt present even when you could not see the dock: a structure shaping movement. She wondered if Rhea had timed this route partly because Tomas was gone. She wondered how long Rhea had been deciding anything at all.

Rhea unscrewed the cap of her canteen and drank. A little water escaped and ran down the side of her throat into the hollow at her collarbone. Mina looked away too late.

"All right," Rhea said.

That tone again. Not lab, not donor, not wife. Decision.

Mina braced one hand on the crate. "All right, what?"

"You were about to make this harder than it needed to be. I'd rather save us the extra labor."

Rhea recapped the canteen, set it aside, and turned fully toward her. Her knee touched Mina's through damp field pants, a deliberate contact brief enough to preserve the lie if anybody later needed one. Mina felt it like current.

"I know you want me," Rhea said.

Mina could have insulted them both by pretending otherwise. "Yeah."

"And I know you've been sleeping with Tomas."

There it was, plain as weather. Mina opened her mouth, shut it again, then said, "Yeah."

"Good. That saves time." Rhea rested her forearms on her knees and looked out toward the dark water for a moment before continuing. "Here's what I'm not available for."

Mina waited.

"I'm not available to be the corrective detail in somebody else's story. Not the broad-minded wife. Not the scientist who makes bad behavior sound field-progressive. Not the person you fold in later so you can tell yourself there was honesty all along."

Each sentence came laid down cleanly, no wasted breath, no drama. Mina listened harder because of that. The roots below them clicked softly as the tide began to lift them.

"And I'm not interested," Rhea said, "in being handled like fragile collateral because you feel guilty. If you want distance, choose distance. If you want me, use the noun. But don't offer me etiquette and call it respect."

Mina stared at the mud on her own boots. It looked like another substance entirely in the red flashlight glow, black and arterial. "You make a person feel very cheap in unusually elegant syntax."

"That's because cheapness sharpens when you name it plain."

Mina laughed once, helplessly. It hurt her pride and made her want Rhea more. That was probably not a healthy development. It was also not remotely optional.

"All right," she said. "My turn."

Rhea nodded.

"I don't want you because Tomas got me in the habit of your household. I don't want you because this would make me look less filthy. It doesn't." Mina glanced back at the tunnel. "Especially not here. I want you because every time you look at a room you make it tell the truth. I want you because you don't waste words pretending anybody in this place is cleaner than they are. I want you because when you're doing fieldwork you look like the only person on the island who's exactly the same species as her own body."

She had not meant to say that much. The words came anyway, quick and unsanded. Stress made her overname. Shame made her blunt. There it was.

Rhea's expression did not soften. It deepened. Different thing.

"That last sentence was almost indecently flattering," she said.

"I can do worse."

"I know."

The answer slipped under Mina's skin and stayed there as recognition, stripped of boast and tease.

Rhea reached out and took Mina's left wrist. Her fingers were dry and cool despite the heat, as if she stored a private climate in her hands. She turned the wrist over, looking at the rust still caught in the fine lines near the heel of Mina's palm.

"One more condition," she said.

"You have a list."

"A sensible one." Her thumb pressed lightly against the rust stain. "At some point, not tonight if tonight is too stupid for it, you tell me what BrightCurrent did to you and what you did back. Not the public version. Not the version designed to survive lawyers. The one I can work with."

The tide made another sound under them, fuller now. Water was reentering the narrow places.

Mina swallowed. "You really know how to ruin foreplay with future obligations."

"I know how to keep desire from becoming anesthesia."

It was the kind of sentence nobody else at camp would have said, and if someone else had said it Mina would have rolled her eyes. From Rhea it landed as pure instrument. A lever. A fact about how she intended to live.

"Okay," Mina said quietly.

"Okay," Rhea repeated.

She still had Mina's wrist in her hand. Instead of releasing it, she drew Mina closer by that grip alone. The movement was small and completely unambiguous. Mina came because not coming would have been theatrical. Their knees met first, then their shoulders. Rhea smelled like sweat dried to salt, guano dust, and the peppermint she had eaten an hour ago fighting for survival under everything else.

Rhea touched Mina's face with her free hand, not cupping it, not tender in any way meant to spare her, just laying two fingers along her jaw as if checking alignment before making a cut. Then she kissed her.

There was nothing hesitant in it. Rhea kissed the way she wrote notes: decisively, with no decorative preamble. Her mouth was cooler than Mina expected. Peppermint, salt, a trace of canteen aluminum. She bit once at Mina's lower lip, just enough to make it plain she had chosen pressure on purpose. Mina made a noise she would have denied under oath and leaned in hard enough that the crate shifted under her boot.

Rhea broke the kiss only far enough to say, against Mina's mouth, "Hold still."

It should not have worked as well as it did. It worked obscenely well.

Mina held still for less than a second before her hands betrayed her and went to Rhea's waist. The tank was damp at the spine. Mud had dried in a flake along the hem. Under it, Rhea's skin was warmer than her hands, hard with breath and muscle, no softness offered where none existed. She let Mina have the waist, the ribs, one palm flattening between her shoulder blades. Then she shifted, turning Mina so her back met the rough concrete lip of the tunnel mouth.

Concrete grit bit through the thin field shirt. A mosquito whined near Mina's ear and got lost in the rush of blood and bats and water. Rhea kissed her again, slower this time and somehow more ruthless for it, opening Mina's mouth with patient certainty until Mina forgot what dignity had ever bought her. She spread her knees because the space demanded it and because Rhea's thigh had already worked between them.

"You see," Rhea said softly, her mouth brushing the corner of Mina's, "this is why I object to innocence theater."

Mina laughed breathlessly. "Christ."

"No. Keep up."

Then Rhea slid her hand under Mina's shirt, palm flattening over her stomach first, feeling the jump there, the involuntary tightening. Mina's body answered before she could pretend to have principles. The tunnel wall at her back was cool and sweating. The front of her was all heat. Rhea's thumb traced once over the waistband of her field shorts and paused, giving Mina exactly enough time to know she could stop it and exactly none to construct a lie about wanting to.

"Tell me to stop if you want me to stop," Rhea said.

"Don't."

Rhea nodded once, as if confirming a field variable, and put her hand inside Mina's shorts.

The first touch was too precise to be called tentative. Mud had dried in powder on Mina's thigh. Rhea's fingers came away damp anyway. Mina's head knocked lightly against the concrete when she exhaled. It was not a sound she could pretty up afterward, not with the concrete at her back and Rhea's hand inside her shorts. It was ugly and immediate and dragged up from low in the body, the sound of something being recognized by force.

Rhea watched her while she touched her. That was the worst and best of it. Her gaze did not flicker away in courtesy. She seemed to consider the whole event part of the same reality as the tunnel, the tide, the BrightCurrent sign under rust. Mina understood with humiliating clarity that Rhea had no interest in separating appetite from context just because separation might feel safer.

"You like being looked at," Rhea murmured.

"Not always."

"No. Just when the look tells the truth."

Mina would have argued if Rhea's thumb had not just changed angle. Instead she made a sound into Rhea's shoulder and grabbed at the concrete edge hard enough to skin two knuckles. The pain was bright and clarifying. It kept the moment from floating off into any of the lies she'd once used to make sex mean less than it did.

The tide under the bank moved louder now, pushing water back through root and pipe. Behind them in the tunnel, the last of the bats dripped out late, their wings brushing the air over Mina's head. Rhea kept working her with infuriating composure, slow enough to force attention, firm enough to make attention impossible anywhere else. Mina could feel the recorder strap caught under her own shoulder blade, could smell mud baking off her calves, could taste old peppermint and iron from the split on her lip. The whole scene stayed ugly in the ways that mattered. Nothing blurred. Nothing let her off.

"Rhea," she said, and heard how wrecked the name sounded.

"I know."

That nearly finished her by itself.

Rhea kissed her once more, brief and hard, as Mina came against her hand with the concrete at her back and the wet intake breath of BrightCurrent's old tunnel on one side and the living swamp on the other. There was no room in it for transcendence. Thank God. It was better than that. It was the body insisting on itself in a place that had once been built to organize other bodies for profit.

When it passed, Mina stood there with her forehead against Rhea's shoulder, breathing in little shamed pulls. Rhea's hand left her slowly, not coy, not apologetic. She wiped her fingers on a sample bag, folded the bag over once, and put it in the trash pocket of the pack with the ordinary efficiency of someone refusing to let even desire escape the category of consequence.

"Still with me?" she asked.

Mina laughed weakly into the fabric at Rhea's shoulder. "Unfortunately."

"Good."

Rhea rested her chin briefly against Mina's temple. The touch was so light it might have been balance. It was also the gentlest thing she had done. That made it worse.

After a moment Mina straightened. The concrete had printed grit into her shirt. Her knees felt unreliable. Rhea looked at her with the same unsparing calm as before, except now Mina knew what that calm could contain.

"You make a terrifying argument," Mina said.

"For what?"

"For not underestimating you."

"That should not have required demonstration."

Mina wiped her mouth with the heel of her hand and found herself smiling, which was ridiculous considering the location and the BrightCurrent sign and the fact that her whole past had just developed plumbing under the camp. "No. But I appreciate instructional materials."

Rhea actually smiled then, brief and wicked enough to alter the whole temperature of her face. She picked up the notebook, the canteen, the recorder pack. Back to work. That too was part of her terms. No scene discarded the labor around it.

"We need to move," she said. "The route will be underwater in twenty minutes."

Mina pushed off the wall carefully. "Right."

"And Mina."

"Yeah."

Rhea waited until she was looking at her. "Do not mistake what just happened for absolution. I'm interested in you. That is not the same thing."

There was the blade again, sharp and necessary.

"I know," Mina said.

This time she did.

They took the mud path back at a faster clip because the water was already reoccupying the low places. The marl edge Rhea had warned about earlier was now a black shine under a skin of moving tide. Twice Mina caught Rhea's elbow. Once Rhea caught the back of Mina's shirt and hauled her clear of a sudden drop where the bank had slumped off. Their bodies had changed category and not changed function. Working hands, charged hands, the same hands.

The camp lights appeared through the mangroves in pieces, rectangles caught between roots. Someone had opened the kitchen louvers. Frying oil and garlic came down the path in a hot line. Human life again. Boards, screens, jokes, unpaid labor, and the observation platform waiting to be turned donor-ready by morning, all of it perched over the same water that had once been directed through a biotech intake. Mina could not unknow that now. The channels under the pilings felt less like habitat and more like evidence. Not less alive. More implicated.

At the edge of the boardwalk Rhea stopped and took the pointer from where she had tucked it through a strap.

"Tomorrow," she said, "I need to strip the salt off the recorder and file a route note that doesn't start a permit war. After that, we can decide how much of this tunnel exists on paper."

"You say the sexiest things."

"And yet you've already been rewarded."

Mina grinned despite herself. It faded when she looked past Rhea toward the underside of the main house. Tidewater moved between the pilings with a patient suck and release, touching wood, root, pipe, wood again. The water wasn't innocent, and it wasn't guilty either. It was connected. That was worse. Guilt wanted neat ownership. Connection spread.

"Rhea," she said, before the moment could flatten into wit again.

Rhea waited.

"I meant it. About wanting you for you."

Rhea's face changed so slightly Mina might have imagined it if she hadn't just had Rhea's fingers inside her. A small settling. Belief postponed, maybe, but not refused.

"Good," she said. "Because if you use me to make your own story prettier, I'll know."

"You probably will."

"I definitely will."

Then she stepped up onto the boardwalk and started toward the bat lab without looking back to see whether Mina followed. Mina did, carrying the weight of the pack, the taste of peppermint still ghosting under the swamp, and the new ugly fact under everything else: the creek that had been gleaming toward her like a chance at vindication did not begin untouched and wild on the far side of her life. It ran straight through the machinery of the old disgrace. Under the camp, under the marriage, under the wanting. The whole wetland had a memory in it, and tonight she had put her mouth on one of the people who intended to make her answer to it.


# Chapter 6

Mina had been on the observation platform since dawn, scrubbing yesterday's guano dust out of the deck grooves and mostly redistributing it. By eight-thirty the rail burned the heel of her hand. Sun came off the bay white and flat, then broke pink where it caught the spoonbills lifting over the mangroves. One wet breeze turned the place into hot pine, bird shit, lemon cleaner, and the bait shrimp somebody had forgotten in the ice chest under the stairs.

Beto was on the outside of the rail again, balancing on the beam in his half-size-too-big camp polo while he looped fresh bunting around a post. His sneakers knocked wood in a careless rhythm. One wrist showed the pale crescent burns he had collected on the kitchen flat-top before he had learned to stop grabbing hot pans like they insulted him personally.

"If you fall in front of donors, I will tell them you are an interpretive exhibit," Imogen said.

"Then tell them to tip," Beto said, not climbing back inside until he saw the look on her face and decided he preferred employment.

Imogen had forced her silver hair into a pinned knot that made her look almost ceremonial until the wind lifted one strand loose over her cheek. She wore the camp's faded green field shirt with a clean scarf tucked at the throat, the closest thing Rookery Key had to formal wear. Behind her the main house screens rattled and the generator thrummed through the planks. The camp always sounded temporary on mornings when it most needed to look permanent.

"Preview weekend is not difficult," she said, which meant it would be. "These people would like to feel rugged while remaining hydrated. They would also like to imagine they are the reason the spoonbills still exist. Give them enough truth to keep them useful."

Mina adjusted the spotting scope and kept her face arranged in something capable and donor-safe. "I can do that."

"I know you can. That is why I am irritated already." Imogen looked past her at the channel, at the heat beginning to tremble over the blackwater. "No freelancing. No folklore. No mention of the mangrove annex, no matter what anyone thinks they saw in the brochure's background photo. Keep it to breeding plumage, nesting pressure, hydrology at a gentleman's level, and how much deferred maintenance a generous check might relieve."

"Hydrology at a gentleman's level," Mina said.

"Do not improve the phrase."

Rhea came up the stairs carrying a clipboard and a soft case of optics against one hip. Her dark hair was braided tight enough to pull the planes of her face sharper than usual. She smelled faintly of alcohol wipes and old guano, because she had already been in the survey room changing batteries before breakfast. She did not look at Mina first. She looked at the trees beyond the platform, counted under her breath, then glanced at the donors' reserved seats as if assessing whether any of them might be stupid enough to clap at a bird.

"The males are active on the east edge," she said. "If the group gets loud, they will move deeper."

"You hear that?" Imogen said. "Even the bats want professionalism."

Rhea's mouth twitched. It was the kind of almost-smile that made Mina's skin feel too tight under her shirt. Last night that same mouth had said her name into the wet dark of the intake tunnel like it was something she had noticed and not yet decided whether to keep. This morning Rhea's face gave her nothing public at all.

That blankness should have made things easier, but it only left Mina standing in the bright with nowhere to set what still burned.

Below the platform, at the shell path by the dock cart, Tomas was unloading coolers of bottled water for the guests. He moved in a county work shirt gone dark between the shoulder blades with sweat, broad back bent over the cheap plastic handles. He looked up once, enough to find Mina through the slats, then away again, the glance quick and familiar and dangerous in its own separate way. Even from here she could smell the diesel and river rot he brought around with him from the dock.

Imogen checked her watch. "Board-adjacent people in nine minutes. Actual board people in twenty, if the ferry captain is lying in the usual direction. Mina, you have the platform. Rhea, stay visible and technical. Beto, if I hear you tell the osprey mating joke again, I will mail you to trade school in pieces."

"That seems expensive," Beto said.

"Only in postage."

He grinned and took the lemonade bucket toward the shade table. Rhea set up a second pair of binoculars. Tomas thumped the last cooler into place and disappeared toward the kitchen. Mina stood for one second in the bright before the guests came, her palm flat on the hot rail, and tried to settle into the version of herself she could still perform without thinking. Species first. Weather second. Give people the shape of awe they already expected to buy. Make the wild feel exact and expensive and just out of reach.

It worked, at first.

The donors came up in polished little clusters, breathing harder than they wanted anyone to see. Linen hats. Good binoculars. Shirt collars the color of cloud. One man wore a bird club patch from Connecticut and shook Mina's hand with the solemnity of a treaty signing. A woman in a sleeveless white blouse asked whether the spoonbills were "always this pink" as if they might have dressed for her. Another guest, a narrow retired dentist named Hal who clicked his tongue every time he focused his lens, kept a pencil tucked behind one ear and wrote every species in a pocket notebook with tiny furious block letters.

The one who recognized Mina came up last. She had the hard tanned knees of somebody who still gardened despite a doctor advising otherwise, and a strip of floral kinesiology tape disappearing under one sock. A tiny silver tin rode in the pocket of her fishing vest. Every few minutes she tipped fennel seeds from it into her palm and ate them like medicine. Her name tag said `Lydia Fane`, though Mina would not know her actual name for another hour because rich birders loved a shortened version of themselves. She adjusted her hearing aids before lifting her binoculars, turquoise shells glinting in the sun.

"You can smell the nests from here," Lydia said, almost cheerfully.

"That means they're doing well," Mina said. "Or badly for any fish stupid enough to swim under them."

That bought the right laugh. Mina took them through the rookery in the clean order she had learned years ago and never entirely lost, not even after the fines, not even after hearing rooms and clipped voices and the deadened way old colleagues had begun introducing her without the adjectives she had once earned. She showed them the spoonbill pairs working sticks into the mangroves, the white ibis moving in underneath to steal whatever they could, the black skimmers slashing the shallows farther out where the channel widened. She talked about low water concentrating prey, about how the adults' pink deepened with carotenoid-rich feeding, about why the rookery sounded like a machine shop if you listened long enough. The group leaned where she wanted them to lean. They forgot their knees. They forgot to perform one another for a little while.

Then Lydia lowered her binoculars and stared directly at Mina's face with the unblinking concentration of someone trying to place a song she hated.

"I know you," she said.

Mina kept her smile where it was. "You may have been on one of my Gulf coast trips a while back."

"No." Lydia rubbed one finger along the arm of her glasses. "No, that is not it."

Hal the retired dentist kept writing without looking up. A breeze shifted. Feather dust lifted from the rail and caught in the sweat on Mina's forearm.

"Lydia," the woman in white said softly, a warning or an encouragement, Mina could not tell which.

But Lydia was already reaching for her phone, dragging the beat out because she enjoyed the little snap between suspicion and proof.

"BrightCurrent," she said. "That was it. You were the guide in the eel mess."

The platform altered around the words without shifting an inch. The boards still creaked. The spoonbills still barked and clattered in the trees. A bottle of sunscreen rolled an inch across the table because somebody had not set it flat. But every lens on the deck came down from the birds.

Mina heard herself answer too quickly. "I was a guide involved in the reporting around that case, yes."

"Involved," Lydia said. She had found something on her screen and turned it toward the woman in white, then toward Hal, who finally stopped writing. "My God, it is you. I read every miserable article when I could not sleep during chemo. The photo was everywhere."

Chemo ought to have roughened the sentence. Instead it made the appetite in it feel tidier.

The man with the Connecticut patch held out a hand as if Lydia might pass him the phone. "BrightCurrent the biotech people?"

"The glowing eel people," Lydia said. "The woman who got cited."

Mina's scalp felt tight. The sun had found the back of her neck where her hair tie had slipped. "The Gulf Wildlife Board fined me over documentation and handling violations. That matter is closed."

"Closed legally?" Hal asked. "Or scientifically?"

There it was, the neat little pleasure of blood in the water.

The woman in white made a face that suggested she disapproved of rudeness in the abstract, provided someone else did it first. "Were you not the one who claimed the animal was wild?"

Mina could have lied cleaner than this. She could have said she was not discussing old litigation on a bird platform. She could have stepped off the deck and let Imogen handle the donors. Instead she felt the old reflex come up: answer fast, answer exact, drown the room in nouns before anybody noticed the blood.

"I produced a photograph and field notes that were later judged compromised in chain of custody," she said. "That's not the same as fabricating an animal."

"Compromised by whom?" Hal asked.

"BrightCurrent?" the Connecticut man said at the same time.

"Does Imogen know?" the woman in white asked, which was the first truly dangerous question.

Below the platform somebody dropped a tray in the kitchen and cursed. Out in the rookery, a spoonbill landed badly and slapped the tops of the mangroves with its wings before settling. Lydia's phone glowed in her hand. Mina did not need to see the picture to know which one it was: her eight years younger, damp hair coming loose at the temples, one hand on the rim of a cooler, looking at a thing outside the frame with such stupid animal hope it still made her want to hit that woman in the throat.

"Imogen hired an experienced guide for a temporary contract," Mina said. "My permits here are the camp's and fully in order."

Lydia's brows lifted. "That is not quite an answer."

"It's the one you're getting."

Silence snapped over the group, quick and live.

From the edge of the platform, Rhea spoke without raising her voice. "If anyone came here for spoonbills rather than archived scandal, the east cluster is courtship-feeding right now. You will miss it if you keep looking at your phones."

It was efficient, dry, technically kind. It gave the guests a ramp down if they wanted one.

The invitation hung there, and every one of them let it drop.

Lydia kept her eyes on Mina. "I only ask because donor camps have become very casual about vetting in the last few years, and I have written checks to places that turned out to have extraordinary judgment problems."

There was the real sentence, dressed at last.

Mina's mouth filled with the taste of hot pennies from nowhere. She set her palm on the scope tripod so hard the metal bit. "If you're concerned about the camp's hiring, you can address that with the director after the walk."

"I just did," Lydia said, looking past Mina.

Imogen had come onto the platform without Mina hearing her. That was how angry she was: every part of her under control at once. She took in the lowered binoculars, Lydia's lit phone, Mina's face, Rhea's stillness. Only then did her gaze cut toward the channel below, where Tomas had stopped halfway up the stairs with a case of canned seltzer braced against his thigh.

"Is there a problem?" Imogen asked.

Lydia smiled at her with practiced pain. "Only a question of disclosure."

"There is a question of timing," Rhea said. "The birds are flushing."

Imogen ignored that. "What exactly is being disclosed?"

Lydia turned the phone. Imogen looked at the screen for one beat too long. Long enough for Mina to know she recognized the photo immediately. Long enough for Tomas to start climbing.

"It seems," Lydia said, "that your guide this morning is Mina Cordero. I remember the BrightCurrent hearing rather vividly."

Tomas reached the top stair and set the seltzer case square against the post before he spoke. "Then maybe remember she's working and you're on camp time, not in a hearing."

Imogen's head snapped toward him. "Not now."

"It's exactly now," Tomas said. His voice stayed low enough that the guests had to lean for it. "She came up here for birds. She doesn't get to turn staff time into her own little hearing for free."

The Connecticut man stiffened. Lydia's expression sharpened, not frightened, not exactly offended. Pleased, almost. She had turned a donor preview into a scene and now a working man had joined the cast.

"Tomas," Mina said. "Leave it."

"Why?" he said. "So she can keep taking bites out of staff?"

Rhea did not look at him. She looked at the birds, then at the guests, then at Imogen. "This platform is above disturbance threshold. Either continue the tour or clear it."

It was such a Rhea sentence that Mina almost laughed, which would have been unforgivable. Tomas muttered something low and sharp in Spanish. Beto had appeared at the stairs behind him, frozen with a tub of sweating lemonade in both hands, eyes wide in a face still too young for all the ways adults could rot in public.

Imogen stepped forward, taking the space back by force of posture. "Mrs. Fane," she said. "Mr. Pelham. Everyone. Mina is here under a temporary employment arrangement that complies with camp policy and permit obligations. If you have concerns about an unrelated past matter, you may bring them to me after the walk."

"Unrelated?" Lydia said. "A biotech scandal involving a supposed wildlife discovery seems rather related to how one markets ecological expertise."

"This camp is not a biotech firm," Imogen said.

"No," Lydia said. "It is a camp trying to raise money."

Every person on the platform heard the money in that sentence. That was the true knife: Lydia had named the camp's hunger in front of the whole deck.

Imogen's voice cooled another degree. "Then let us not waste your morning."

She gestured toward the mangroves as if this had always been a planned transition.
