The guests obeyed because wealthy people often did obey when given the chance to pretend they had not been feeding minutes earlier. Hal put his notebook away but not before Mina saw him write one last line. Lydia slipped her phone back into her vest and tipped a few fennel seeds into her mouth. She had won exactly as much as she wanted.

Mina continued the tour because there was nothing else to do. She talked about nesting chronology with her face burning in the open light. She explained how spoonbills clacked their bills in threat displays while the deck smelled of sunscreen and judgment. The group watched the birds again, but not like before. Now and then somebody looked sideways at her with the furtive concentration people reserved for accident scenes and old lovers. Tomas went back down the stairs in a violence of silence. Rhea wrote something on her clipboard with the same neat hand she used for bat counts, not once lifting her head in Mina's direction.

By the time the walk ended, the story had outrun the platform.

Beto proved it without meaning to. He was pouring melting ice into a drain by the kitchen when Mina came down the boardwalk with the scope over one shoulder. He kept his voice low, but the lowness did not help.

"That lady's showing your picture around in the breakfast room," he said. "The one with the eels. I mean, maybe not to be evil. But also definitely to be evil."

Mina set the scope case against the wall. "What picture?"

Beto winced because they both knew that was a stupid question. "The article one. You're holding a cooler. Mr. Pelham zoomed in on it so much the face got all blocky."

There was a fry-cook towel hanging from his back pocket, damp at one corner, and an anatomy flash card stuck halfway out beside it. Radius, ulna, clean blue print. He had probably been quizzing himself between ferry loads again. Mina looked at the card because it was easier than looking at his pity.

"Did Imogen ask for me?" she said.

"Not yet. She asked for the good coffee, which is how you know it's bad."

"Helpful."

"I try."

He shifted the ice bucket from one hand to the other. The joking dropped out of him in a way it had not before. "For what it's worth, I didn't know all that. I just knew there was some kind of board mess. Everybody in the kitchen knows now."

Everybody. The word sat in the heat like a nailed thing.

On the far side of the yard, Tomas was smoking beside the generator shed, even though Imogen hated it when anyone smoked near the fuel cans. He drew on the cigarette like it had insulted his family. Rhea crossed from the lab to the main house with her clipboard tucked flat to her chest, not looking left or right. She moved like a person choosing the exact amount of herself the day would be allowed to touch. Neither of them looked toward Mina.

The ferry whistle sounded from the bay. More guests.

Imogen opened the breakfast room screen and said, without raising her voice, "Mina. Inside."

***

The breakfast room at Rookery Key pretended to be casual while sweating through every sign of strain. The wicker chairs had been painted over so many times their woven joints looked clogged. One section of floor sagged near the coffee station, and a braided rug had been laid over it as if aesthetics could replace carpentry. On donor weekends Imogen put hibiscus in mason jars and folded cloth napkins around the cheap silverware, which only made the cracks in the windowsills more obvious.

Lydia Fane sat at the far end of the room under the paddle fan, eating papaya with a seriousness usually reserved for prayer. Her phone lay faceup beside the plate. Hal Pelham was bent over it from the next table, reading. When Mina entered, neither of them looked embarrassed. That was the humiliation of this sort of thing. Nobody in the room had to act cruel because the structure had already done the work for them.

Imogen did not take Mina into the office. She stopped beside the coffee station, where staff came for refills and guests passed through on the way to the platform stairs. Public enough that no one could miss it, casual enough to deny it was a spectacle. Worse than theatrical.

"You should have told me the scale of it," she said.

Mina glanced once toward Lydia's table. "You knew there'd been a board citation."

"I knew there had been fallout. I did not know your face was still a hobby for rich insomniacs."

Across the room somebody laughed too loudly at a different conversation. Silverware clicked. The fan pulled warm air over the buffet and redistributed the smell of eggs, coffee grounds, and ripe fruit. Mina could feel sweat collecting under her bra band and nowhere to wipe it.

"I wasn't hiding that I was Mina Cordero."

"No," Imogen said. "You were allowing me to imagine that mattered less than it does."

Lydia's turquoise hearing aids flashed when she turned her head. She was not trying to overhear. She was simply hearing.

Mina kept her voice low. "What do you want me to say?"

"For the moment? Nothing." Imogen folded her arms. "I want to know whether there is anything about that BrightCurrent disgrace that can still touch this camp in a regulatory or donor-facing way beyond gossip."

Rhea came in through the side screen before Mina could answer. She must have been summoned or had simply read the current in the room and followed it. She set her clipboard on a chair and stood near the coffee urn, posture straight, face unreadable.

"Donor-facing, yes," Rhea said. "Regulatory, not on present facts."

Imogen gave her a flat look. "That is a careful distinction."

"It's the useful one."

Mina looked at Rhea then, because the sentence had landed with the clean coldness of forceps: accurate, bloodless, and not remotely kind.

"There aren't active restrictions on me guiding guests here," Mina said. "The penalty was civil. It's paid on a plan. I don't have a current wildlife ban."

Imogen's eyes sharpened at the monthly-payment part, maybe because she had not known it, maybe because debt always made people look less salvageable in the wrong rooms. "You did not think to mention that your name would invite exactly this kind of scrutiny on donor weekend?"

"I thought if I did my job well enough people would look at the birds."

"That is charmingly naive for a woman with your history."

The side door struck the frame once behind Tomas as he came in from the yard, sun and cigarette smoke still on him. He kept his voice flat. "If this is about the woman from the platform, don't hang the bill on Mina. Lydia's the one who ran it up."

Imogen squared the stack of paper napkins by the coffee urn into a hard white block before she looked at him. "Why are you in my breakfast room?"

"Because everybody can hear you from the boardwalk."

"Then everybody can hear me ask you to leave."

He did not. He planted himself by the drink cooler, forearms marked with old prop nicks and new mosquito welts, jaw set around anger like he did not trust himself to spend it all at once. "She didn't drag her own name into this room. Lydia did, and now breakfast's buying everybody a turn."

"Ran it up," Imogen repeated. "An excellent phrase. I shall put it on the annual appeal."

"Don't do that thing," Tomas said. "You know exactly what I mean."

"I do know exactly what you mean. That is why I am tired already."

Rhea touched two fingers to the edge of the coffee table, not fidgeting, grounding. "This isn't helped by making it a second scene."

Tomas gave her a look sharp enough to strip bark. "And it's helped by what, exactly? You talking like a permit memo?"

Something flickered in her face then vanished. "It's helped by deciding which risk is real."

"The risk is they eat her alive and you all help set the table."

There were six words in that sentence Mina wished he had not said in public and one she could not stop hearing. Her. He had skipped Mina, skipped guide, and the pronoun exposed more than a hand on the waist.

Imogen heard it too. Her eyes moved once from Tomas to Mina and then to Rhea, calculating faster than most people could flinch. Mina watched the possibility of a second scandal take shape and, by some miracle of self-preservation or exhaustion, fail to fully bloom.

"Enough," Imogen said. It was quiet. Quiet from her was worse than shouting. "Mina, collect your things after lunch."

The room changed again. Even Lydia looked up fully at that.

Mina felt the words in her stomach before she understood them, the way a rotten floor gives an extra inch underfoot before the drop registers.

"Imogen," she said.

"No. I am not staging a debate." Imogen's chin lifted toward the guests, toward the open room, toward the platform beyond. "You have turned my preview into a reputational event."

Tomas took one step forward. His voice stayed clipped. "You cut your best dawn guide at lunch and by four you've got refunds, gossip, and nobody steady on that platform."

"Keep talking about refunds in front of my guests," Imogen said. "I would like the grant people in Naples to hear that too."

He did not stop. "They're going to hear it anyway. Lydia leaves here and it's checks, calls, all of it."

"Ability is not the only criterion for public trust."

"Public trust," Tomas said, and laughed once without humor. "No. Money."

Rhea's voice came in level and harder than before. "The issue isn't whether the guest is contemptible. The issue is whether she can cost the camp money."

Mina turned on her. "Is that what I am now? A line item?"

Rhea met her eyes at last. There it was, the full look, and because it came so late it hurt more. "Right now you're a labor category with a search trail."

It should have enraged Mina. Instead the sentence landed like cold hardware: useful, ugly, impossible to argue with in front of witnesses. Rhea's thumb stayed hooked under the metal clip of her board.

Tomas looked from Rhea to Imogen. "You're both talking like she's freight."

"No," Rhea said, still looking at Mina. "He's making it worse because he thinks leverage only counts if it happens out loud."

"And you're making it better by acting like she's already a memo."

"I'm making it legible to the person with the power to throw her out today."

That finally shut him up, not because he agreed, but because the sentence hit the exact machinery of the room. Employer. Housing. Guests. Schedules. Need.

Imogen exhaled through her nose, almost a laugh, almost a surrender to arithmetic. She turned to the wall calendar beside the pantry door where Beto had marked ferry arrivals in blue grease pencil and somebody else had ringed next week's spoonbill walks in red. Mina watched her eyes move over the schedule. Full dawn platform. Two skiff runs. Evening lecture. One sick volunteer. No spare guide worth putting in front of donors.

The camp's finances had hands around every throat in the place.

"No," Imogen said finally. "Not after lunch."

Nobody moved.

She kept looking at the calendar. "If I remove you today, I create a second story: camp in chaos, guests abandoned, staff instability. I do not have the margin for that. You will work through spoonbill week. Every dawn shift. Every donor-facing obligation already assigned. You will be so competent and so dull that these people get tired of saying your name."

Mina could hear the fan, the forks, the distant creak of the platform. "And after that?"

Imogen looked back at her. "After the last spoonbill run, you are finished here."

Lydia Fane took a sip of coffee three tables away. The cup clicked back into the saucer. Mina had never wanted so badly to throw something breakable.

"That is a week," Mina said.

"Yes." Imogen's voice gentled only enough to become more brutal. "That is the amount of time your utility currently exceeds your damage."

Tomas said one word, loud enough to carry to Lydia's table. "Fuck."

"You may spare me the masculine outrage," Imogen said. "If any of you had warned me this might happen, I would have structured the weekend differently."

Mina nearly said, I did warn you, but I said it in the skimpy language of somebody hoping shame might stay abstract. The sentence died before it got to her mouth. Imogen was not wrong. That was the part that made anger feel thin.

Rhea picked up her clipboard. "There is one more question."

Imogen turned to her.

"If another guest raises it publicly, what line do you want staff using?"

Rhea already had her pen uncapped. Tomas stared at her like she had priced Mina by the pound. Mina wanted somebody to say she was worth more than her usefulness, but the question gave Imogen something to grab besides the spectacle.

Imogen answered without hesitation. "Staff say that Mina is a qualified temporary guide employed under current camp policy, and that any historical regulatory matter is closed and not relevant to guest safety. If pressed further, direct them to me and only to me."

"Good," Rhea said.

Tomas barked out a laugh. "Good?"

Rhea looked at him then, finally allowing some temper to show. "Yes. Good. Because now there is a line. You don't survive public pressure by telling the truth louder than the people with money. You survive it by making them repeat themselves until they sound ugly."

For the first time since Lydia had spoken Mina's name on the platform, somebody in the room said something that belonged to neither appetite nor management. It did not comfort her. It did not try to. But it gave shape to the day.

Imogen straightened a stack of napkins that did not need straightening. "Mina, return to the platform at four for the estuary light talk. Wear the clean shirt this time."

Mina looked down at herself. Guano at the hem. A thumbprint of rust from the scope clamp. Nothing any guest had noticed before the phone came out.

"Yes," she said.

"Tomas, back to whatever county fiction is paying you today."

"You can't seriously expect her to smile through this afternoon."

"Of course I can. That is what I am paying her for."

The word paying hit Tomas hard enough that he looked down at his taped thumb and squeezed it until the knuckle blanched. When he looked back up, bitterness and helplessness had sharpened his face into something older. The sight of it steadied her anyway.

Rhea was already moving toward the door. On her way past Mina she stopped just long enough to adjust the fallen collar on Mina's camp shirt, two fingers, efficient as fieldwork. No one watching would have called it intimate. That was the point.

"Don't answer fast this afternoon," she said quietly. "It makes them smell blood."

Then she was gone into the glare.

Tomas saw the touch. His jaw tightened. "That's your advice?"

Mina did not look at him. "Don't start."

"I'm not starting anything. I'm saying this place is using you."

"This place was using me before breakfast."

"And you're fine with that?"

She finally faced him. Up close the salt had dried pale on his shirt and there was a nick on his thumb wrapped in electrical tape where he had caught it on a prop housing. He looked like work, anger, and bad decisions made flesh. God help her, there was comfort in that.

"I'm fine with one week," she said. "After that, you can have whatever speech you want."

"One week until she boots you."

"One week until spoonbill week."

He understood then. The channel, the low water, the breeding peak, the last bright chance before the season shifted. His face changed around the knowledge, settling into the same hard alignment she felt in her own ribs.

"You still think the creek is worth this?"

Mina glanced across the room. Lydia laughed at something Hal said and touched the silver fennel tin in her pocket like a rosary. Beto hovered in the kitchen door with a stack of plates and the solemn expression he wore only when adults were bleeding in ways he could not bandage. Outside, the mangroves flashed once with pink wings, then a chain of irritated clacks traveled deeper into the rookery and one spoonbill lifted off the outer branches to settle farther back in the leaves.

"I think the creek was worth this before I was any good at regretting things," she said.

Imogen heard that much. "Then regret on your own time. Four o'clock, platform."

Mina picked up the scope case and left before the room could take anything else off her.

On the boardwalk, the heat had thickened enough to make the screws in the planks smell metallic. Somebody had spilled sweet tea near the steps and a seam of ants was working it. The camp had gone about its business with the extra attention of a body touching a sore tooth. Kitchen staff looked up and looked down again. A volunteer carrying laundry watched her one beat too long. From an open guest room window came Lydia's voice, delighted now, retelling the story with the briskness of a woman who believed bad luck became anecdote the moment it belonged to someone else.

Mina took the back path toward the utility shed because she could not bear the main house for one more minute. The shed was barely more than a roof, a table, and a wall of tools salted into permanent decay. It smelled of rope, bleach, and old bait. She set the scope down and braced both hands on the workbench until the splintered plywood pressed crescents into her palms.

She did not cry. She was too hot for that, too angry, too publicly skinned. What came instead was a bright mean clarity. One week. Seven dawns, maybe six if weather turned. Enough time for the spoonbills to go fully feral, enough time for donors to keep repeating her name, enough time for the blackwater under the rookery to give up another blue turn if it meant to.

Somewhere behind her a board creaked, a tread too measured for Beto and too quiet for Imogen's anger.

"I wasn't going to defend you to Lydia Fane," Rhea said from the doorway. "She feeds on resistance. It would've helped her."

Mina kept her hands on the bench. "I noticed."

Rhea came one step inside. In the shed's shade her face looked less cut from stone and more tired, which was worse in a different way. She lifted Mina's right hand off the bench, worried a splinter loose from the heel of her palm with her thumbnail, and let the hand go. "If I'd stepped between you and Lydia, she'd have stayed for blood. If I'd argued fairness with Imogen, she'd have heard liability and fired you anyway. So I gave her a sentence she could use. That wasn't the part where I wanted my hand on the back of your neck and your feet off that platform."

The sentence moved through Mina slowly. Desire offered without softness. Typical Rhea. Cruel only because it was exact.

"You called me a labor category."

"In front of Imogen, yes."

"That felt wonderful."

"I know." Rhea leaned a shoulder against the doorframe. "It also kept her from firing you on the spot."

Mina turned then. The shed cut the light into hard bars over Rhea's shirt and braid. "You always know the correct thing to do once it has made everybody miserable."

"That's not always true." A pause. "Often true."

Against her will, Mina laughed once. It hurt her face. Rhea's gaze dropped to that laugh as if cataloging a rare animal.

"You have the week," Rhea said. "Use it."

"That sounds like an order."

"No. Orders are clearer."

Outside, Tomas shouted for Beto to bring him the fuel key for the dock cart, and Beto shouted back that if Tomas wanted it he could come get it with the rest of his emotional maturity. The noise of them carried through the stilts and screens, absurd and alive. Mina looked at Rhea and thought of how thin every wall here was, how impossible privacy had become, how humiliation did not end when the witnesses left. It soaked into structures. It made a camp sound different.

"Does everybody know now?" she asked.

Rhea did not lie. "Enough people."

That was the day's true sentence, though no one said it aloud again. Enough people. Enough money. Enough damage. Enough time left to be useful before she got thrown back into whatever came next.

Rhea pushed off the frame. At the door she paused. "For what it's worth, Lydia Fane puts Sweet'N Low on papaya. That alone should disqualify her from moral authority."

It was such a small, dry, unnecessary cruelty that Mina nearly loved her for it on the spot.

"Get out," Mina said.

"Four o'clock," Rhea said, and went.

Mina stayed in the shed until the heat there became as bad as the room she had left. Then she picked up the scope and went to change into the clean shirt. On the hook by her bunk hung the camp name badge she wore for donor-facing shifts. Somebody had left it turned over, so that only the clip showed. When she flipped it, her own name stared back at her in waterproof black type. `MINA CORDERO`.

The letters looked newly insufficient, a waterproof label clipped over a history the badge could not hold.

She pinned the badge on anyway. If the weather held till four, she would climb back onto the bright deck and talk about estuary light as if light were not the whole problem. If it broke early, Imogen would put her wherever the camp needed another pair of hands. At dawn tomorrow she would do it again. One week. Then gone.

Under the floorboards, through all the hot timber and human noise, the tide began at last to turn and the air over the bay thickened toward weather.


# Chapter 7

The screened walkway to the bat lab rattled on one hinge hard enough to sound like somebody trying the place for weakness.

By four the sky had gone the color of old pewter. A board under the kitchen window gave a dry tick every time the wind shifted east. Somewhere above Mina's room a loose strip of metal flashed and slapped and flashed again. The spoonbills had gone low and restless in the mangroves, their noise flattened by air so heavy it felt already wet. Even before the first drop hit, the whole place sounded built rather than finished.

Beto stood on a folding ladder in the west hall with a caulk gun in one hand and a coffee can of mismatched screws hooked over the other wrist. He had on an oversized camp T-shirt from some donor weekend years ago, the hem dragging against the back pockets of his shorts, and he was chewing the inside of his cheek while he stared at a water stain shaped like South America.

"That one'll open first," he said without looking down. "I know the family."

Rhea sat at the common-room table with six batteries lined in military neatness on a bath towel, testing charge with a little meter and writing percentages on painter's tape in handwriting so small and even it looked typeset. Her braid, dark with damp already, had left a wet stripe down the back of her shirt. Tomas had come in ten minutes earlier through the kitchen door carrying two mangrove snapper by the gills and dripping storm sweat onto the worn linoleum. He had set them in the sink, washed his hands, and immediately dirtied them again pulling the old hurricane shutters out from behind the pantry.

Mina stood in the middle of all of it with a roll of paper towels and nothing assigned to her yet, which was close enough to humiliation that her skin knew the route.

Yesterday's donor scene still lived in her body as a kind of false heat. If she held still too long, she could feel the exact moment the woman on the platform had smiled with recognition and made Mina's old disgrace into entertainment with iced tea and binoculars in hand. The storm at least gave everyone a better reason to watch the walls.

"Tell me what to do," Mina said.

Tomas glanced over with the quick sideways look he used when he was trying not to make anything obvious. Grease still darkened the creases around his nails no matter how hard he scrubbed. "Chop onion. Slice the limes. If the lights go, grab the kerosene lamp before Beto trips over his own comedy routine."

"I'm carrying morale here," Beto said. "Nobody respects craft."

Rhea did not look up from the batteries. "Use the shorter screws on the west frame. The long ones split the beam last time."

"See," Beto said to Mina, climbing down two rungs. "This is why I never get to freeload in peace. She has evidence."

He was nineteen and fast enough to make every older person at camp nervous. Even on the ladder he balanced like railings had been built for him personally. A burn scar ran white and shiny across the inside of one wrist where kitchen oil had jumped him last winter, and he kept flexing that hand when thunder moved somewhere far out over the Gulf. At the end of the hall, a gallon freezer bag sat on top of the linen shelf with a diesel-engine workbook and a trade-school brochure sealed inside it like contraband against weather.

Mina took the onions and a dull knife from the drawer. "You want them thin?"

"I want them edible," Tomas said. "Anything beyond that is luxury."

"That's a confession of character, not a recipe," Rhea said.

It pulled a laugh out of Mina before she had decided she was available for one.

Rain started in fat exploratory drops against the screens. They sounded separate at first, a scatter of thrown beads. Then the line came in hard from the bay and the whole roof took it at once.

Beto said, "There it is," with the satisfied dread of a man whose prediction had just become labor.

He dragged the ladder farther down the hall. Tomas shouldered into the pantry for the shutters. Rhea capped the battery marker, rose, and moved to the kitchen window to help set the first panel into place. Mina followed Tomas to the sink and laid one of the snapper on the cutting board.

The fish was still cool and slick from the ice chest. Its scales flashed old-rose and pewter under the fluorescent light, and when Mina ran the back of the knife from tail to head, scales popped loose and stuck to her forearm and throat. Tomas came in at her shoulder with the other fish, close enough that she could smell wet cotton, diesel, and whatever cheap soap he used when he remembered.

"You don't have to stay useful every second," he said quietly.

"I'd rather not stand around while the building makes its choices."

"Fair."

He slit the belly open with more confidence than finesse. His forearms, nicked with old prop scars and fresh mosquito bites, flexed under the wet hair. He had a cook's indifference to splatter and a mechanic's respect for a sharp edge; the sink collected blood and scales and onion skin together and smelled briefly like low tide at a fish-cleaning dock.

Mina gutted, rinsed, and salted. Tomas found cornmeal, a bottle of hot sauce, and a box of instant rice so old the cardboard had gone soft at the corners. On the other side of the room Rhea brought over two lanterns and set them by the stove, then resumed her battery triage while the rain thickened and the light beyond the screens went green enough to make the mangroves look submarine.

"How bad?" Mina asked.

Rhea clicked the meter onto another battery. "For the weather or for camp?"

"Dealer's choice."

"For weather, I would not choose a boat tonight unless someone was bleeding. For camp, the west roof remains a piece of fiction, the generator coughs on startup, and one of the headlamp batteries is pretending to be charged."

"That's anthropomorphic," Tomas said.

"It's accurate."

The lights flickered once, held, and then decided to keep going a little longer.

Mina sliced the limes and let their oil sting the tiny cuts the fish scales had made on her knuckles. It was easier to work than to think about how normal this looked if you took away the facts: a wife at the table, a husband at the stove, a kid on a ladder swearing at a leak by the men's room, Mina at the counter cutting citrus as if she had been folded into the evening on purpose and not in the aftermath of a public shaming and two separate bad decisions that had developed bodies and names.

Rhea said, "Beto, move your study bag away from the hall if you want a future in engines."

He leaned around the corner. "Already did. You think I don't know my own weather system?"

"I think you know yours and not the building's."

"Same thing at this point."

He hauled the bag into the common room and set it on top of the bookcase, then, with the easy embarrassment of someone caught caring too plainly, he wiped his hand on his shorts and added, "Test's in two weeks. If the roof eats the workbook, I'm going to have to learn carburetors by prayer."

Tomas snorted. "Prayer's free, at least."

"You mock because you fear my upward mobility."

"I mock because you don't know what a carburetor does."

"It makes dads feel superior."

That got Tomas laughing for real, head tipped back, crooked nose more obvious in profile. Mina had seen him meaner than this and hungrier than this; it still startled her sometimes, the boy under the wear, the way pleasure made him briefly look less employed by everything.

Rhea went to the pantry and came back with a roll of contractor bags. "Before anybody gets philosophical, line the hall table. The leak there stains."

"Yes, Doctor Doom," Beto said.

"That title required more schooling than I completed."

"You still earned it in spirit."

Mina cut the fish into pan-sized halves while Tomas poured oil into the cast-iron skillet. It was the old heavy one with the wobble in the handle and a history of getting hotter than anyone expected on the right side. The first fish hit with a hard angry spit. Tomas stepped back, swore, and then leaned in again like a man taking insult from a machine.

"You always crowd the pan," Rhea said.

"I'm not taking technical criticism from a person who alphabetizes batteries."

"They're not alphabetized. They're grouped by reliability."

"There you go." He pointed a fork at Mina. "That tone right there is why I married her."

Rhea's mouth shifted one degree. "My administrative rigor seduced you?"

"No. Your contempt did."

Beto called from the hall, "That's dark, man."

The fish crisped too fast. Mina could smell the point where good browned edges turned into dryness that would need hot sauce and lies. Tomas lifted one piece, frowned at its hardness, and lowered it back as if force might reverse chemistry.

"You can admit defeat," Mina said.

"Defeat's for people with backup plans."

"Dinner is not a county budget hearing," Rhea said.

"Everything's a county budget hearing if you listen close enough."

The second batch went worse because Tomas got distracted hammering the shutter latch with the heel of his hand while the rice boiled over on the back burner. Mina took over the rice, shoved the fish onto a platter lined with paper towels, and squeezed lime over all of it while the flesh was still hot enough to drink the acid. Rhea produced a jar of pickled jalapenos from somewhere behind the tea and set it on the table without comment, which felt to Mina like the purest form of kindness in the room.

When they finally sat, the storm had settled into seriousness. Rain sheeted past the windows so steadily the mangroves looked painted over. # Chapter 8

Mina lay on top of the sheet because underneath her the bed was soaked through with sweat, storm damp, and what the three of them had done to it. The thin blanket Tomas had dragged over all three of them in the night had kicked down around her shins, damp at the edge. Tomas's forearm lay heavy across her waist, engine-rough and hot. Rhea was behind her with one bare shin hooked over Mina's calf, the loosened tail of her braid rubbing damply at Mina's shoulder blade. The room smelled like fish grease left over from the meal, wet wood, mosquito repellent gone sour on skin, and sex cooling into something less glamorous and more human. It should have felt like consequence already. Instead, for one foolish minute, it felt like shelter.

Rain had quit sometime before dawn, but the camp was still leaking from every seam. Water ticked from the screen frame onto the warped sill. The pilings answered each little wind shift with their old throat-clearing groan. Somewhere out in the mangroves a spoonbill let out a cracked, offended bark, and another one answered as if the dark itself had stepped on its foot.

That was the dangerous part. Bargains had edges. You could bruise yourself cleanly against them. Tenderness spread out. It got into corners. It turned a room into a witness.

"Tell me what happened at BrightCurrent," she said.

The words were quiet enough that the rain ticks around them almost swallowed them. That made them worse.

Mina kept looking at the screen, where a fat bead of water gathered, trembled, and let go. "You want the newspaper version or the one the donors use when they act brave?"

"Neither." Rhea's voice still carried sleep in it, but only there. The rest was clean. "I want the one that made you look sick in that tunnel."

"Rae," he said, rough with sleep, "it's not even light."

"I know it's early." She moved a little closer behind Mina, warm and direct, no apology in it. "Ask later and she'll have her boots on. I don't want her boots on."

Mina gave a breath that almost passed for a laugh. "That's romantic."

"It isn't." Rhea's thumb rested over the scar in Mina's calf, holding it there as if it were a line on a page. "I don't want the polished version. I don't want the one where you were young and stupid and a corporation happened to you. I want the one with your hands in it."

The room held. Tomas did not tell his wife to stop. He did not tell Mina to answer. He just lay there with the weight of his hand no longer touching her, and the absence of it was somehow more intimate than the hand had been.

Mina stared at the water-dark screen and tried, out of reflex, to build a smaller story. There had been bad management. There had been Jonah. There had been a series of choices that only looked separate if you spread them far enough apart. None of that was false. None of it was the thing.

"There was a cooler," she said.

Rhea did not move. Tomas swore once, not loudly.

"Start before that," Rhea said.

So Mina did, because lying there between the two of them with Tomas's heat still in her skin and Rhea's mouth mark on her shoulder had made any graceful omission impossible. The shame of it was not only what she had done. The shame of it was how badly she had wanted to be chosen for it.

"Jonah Pike was senior guide there then. You know his name?"

"I know the type," Tomas said.

Mina almost told him he did not, because Tomas's contempt ran practical and Jonah's had always run curated, wrapped in field charm and older-man patience and the kind of attention that felt expensive when nobody had ever spent it on you before. But Tomas would hear the defense in that, and it would be a defense, so she let it go.

"He was older," Mina said. "Not old. Just old enough to make every room tilt toward him if he wanted it. Sunburned neck, always peeling there, and he kept fennel seeds in an Altoids tin because he said marsh water got in your mouth through your own teeth. He had one pair of sunglasses he refused to throw out even after one arm snapped. He fixed them with orange fly-tying thread and made it look like a field trick instead of being cheap."

Rhea's fingers shifted once against Mina's calf. Keep going.

"I was twenty-six and freelancing. BrightCurrent wasn't paying me scientist money. They were paying me smile-for-the-board money and take-the-journalists-out money and sometimes translate-for-the-guys-who-actually touched the tanks money. Jonah was the one who knew which donors mattered, which researchers drank too much, which doors stayed propped open if you wanted to feel like the real work had brushed past you. I was sleeping with him."

Tomas made a sound through his nose.

"You can say it," Mina said.

"Why? You already said it."

That was Tomas when he was hurt enough to get quieter instead of louder. Mina felt it like weather pressure. She kept going before she could start arranging herself around it.

"There was a board visit that week. A storm sitting offshore, one of those ugly April ones that can't decide if it wants to be a rehearsal or a real thing. The wetlab was locked down because some compliance team from upstate had started asking where every live specimen sat at every hour. Everybody was tense and pretending not to be tense, which is a BrightCurrent specialty." She swallowed. "Jonah found me after the donor boat came in. I was still in the guide polo. My shirt was stuck to me under the arms and I smelled like sunscreen and bait shrimp. He said he needed a favor."

Rhea said, "What kind?"

"A carrying kind." Mina shut her eyes once. The memory came back cold first. That was what she had forgotten for years on purpose, the way the loading corridor behind the wetlab had felt like stepping into a grocery case after a whole day in Florida heat. "He had the cooler already by the service door. White marine cooler, chest-high on me if I hugged it wrong, blue cord cinched over the lid because the latch was busted. There were two little vent holes near the handle. Sloppy drill job. He'd done them himself."

"You remember that," Tomas said.

"I remember too much when I'm scared." Mina opened her eyes. "I picked it up and whatever was inside hit the side. Hard enough I almost dropped it. The plastic body of the cooler knocked my hip bone."

Nobody spoke.

"Did you know it was alive?" Rhea asked.

"I knew there was something alive in there. I knew he didn't want it on a log. I knew he said if the compliance people got a clean count they would bleach a whole line and call it disposal." Mina turned her face into the damp pillow for a second, then back to the room. "I told myself I was helping save something. That's the version I used for a while."

"And the less flattering version?" Rhea asked.

Mina laughed once, with no humor in it. "The less flattering version is that he asked me because I was useful and eager and already in his bed. And I liked being useful to him. I liked that he was letting me carry the dirty thing instead of leaving me out with the donors."

Tomas sat up. The sheet slid down his stomach. Mosquito bites rose over his shoulder in small red welts, and a bruise-dark bite mark Mina had left on the muscle above his chest looked almost black in the storm-gray room. He scrubbed a hand over his face and stared at the wall, not at her.

"Where'd you take it?" he asked.

She could hear the question changing shape in him. He was not asking for absolution yet. He was already sorting route, vehicle, distance, exposure.

"To his skiff behind the service dock. He told me to keep my head down because the loading camera covered the north corner and not the south. He knew the blind spots." Mina could feel the slick handle digging into both palms again, the meltwater running down her forearms. "I set it in the bow. He threw a tarp over it like that made it invisible. Then we went out."

"Out where?"

"Not far. South intake side. There were feeder cuts off the annex then before the mangroves swallowed half of them. He killed the motor and let us drift because he liked drama." She tasted rot at the back of her tongue, memory making its own weather. "When he took the tarp off, the whole inside of the cooler had a blue leak to it. Not bright like a sign. More like a live bruise under plastic."

Tomas looked at her now. "Jesus."

Mina nodded because there was nothing else to do with that word.

"He opened the lid and there was a bag inside, tied off, water slapping hard enough that it sounded like somebody trapped a hand in there. One eel. At least one by then. Maybe he'd moved the others earlier, maybe there had only ever been one, I don't know. It was longer than my forearm and thinner than it looked in the tanks, all muscle and glass and that wrong blue light under the skin." She could see it too clearly: the animal twisting against the clear bag, light slipping up her wrists, Jonah grinning because he knew awe made people easier. "He said it had been in a monitoring line they were about to kill."

"Did you believe him?" Rhea asked.

"Enough." Mina wiped the heel of her hand over her mouth. "Not because it made sense. Because I wanted him to be the kind of man who did rescues instead of theft."

Rhea's fingers left Mina's calf at last. The loss of that touch felt earned.

"What happened next?" she asked.

Mina turned onto her back so she did not have to speak into the mattress. The ceiling above them wore a water stain shaped like a gull with a broken neck. Tomas sat at the edge of the bed, elbows on his knees. Rhea propped herself on one forearm, braid come half loose, eyes dry and exact.

"He told me to hold the bag open under the water." Mina's voice flattened on its own. That happened when she got close to panic. "Said if we just dumped it on the surface it'd bash itself stupid on the hull. So I knelt in the bow and put my hands in. The creek water was warm. The bag wasn't. I cut the tie with his knife because my hands were shaking too hard to untwist it. He laughed at that."

She could feel the knife again, the cheap serrated edge sawing plastic, Jonah's knee against her ass to steady her or control her, she had never decided which. The eel had hit her wrist on the way out, all panic and muscle, then it was in blackwater, light smearing into tannin, then gone.

"So yes," Mina said. "If you need the clean sentence, I helped put one into the creek."

The mosquito near the ceiling found its pitch and held it. Tomas kept his elbows on his knees but stared harder at the floorboards, thumb grinding once over his palm. Rhea's hand flattened on the sheet instead of coming back to Mina's leg.

Rhea asked, "And the first eel you built your name on?"

There it was. The question under the other questions, the one Mina had been dodging since the day she stepped onto the stilts and heard Beto tell the spoonbill story with that half-mocking grin.

"I don't know," she said.

Tomas looked at her hard enough that she felt stripped by it. "That's not an answer."

"It's the only one I've got." Anger came up fast because shame had opened the door for it. "Months later Jonah took me back out near the same system, different cut, more wild cover. He'd heard rumors from one of the maintenance guys, or he said he had. We saw blue in the water and I got the photo everybody knows. Maybe it was the same animal. Maybe it was another one from the same tank line. Maybe the creek already had something running loose through it before I touched that cooler. I don't fucking know. I told the story like discovery because discovery was the only version that let me keep my job."

Rhea's face did not harden exactly. It became more still. "Did Jonah tell you it was wild?"

"He used the word feral."

That earned one small, ugly laugh from Rhea. "Of course he did."

"He said that like it made him honest. Like he was splitting hairs instead of moving the whole argument." Mina stared at the ceiling stain. "I let him. Then I let myself. By the time people wanted panels and quotes and footage and some clean little miracle rising out of contaminated water, I was already in too deep to say maybe the first one came through a cooler I carried for a man I was sleeping with."

Tomas got off the bed. He did it slowly, as if speed would make him stupid. He found his shorts on the floor, shook a condom wrapper loose from one leg, and stepped into them without bothering to look embarrassed. Mina had seen him naked enough now that shame should have shifted elsewhere, but this was different. This was clothing as retreat.

"You had me stealing county fuel for that?" he asked.

"For what's here now," Mina said too fast. "For the creek now. For what I've seen under the spoonbills."

He looked over his shoulder. "Don't answer the question I didn't ask."

She shut her mouth.

Rhea sat up and pulled the sheet across her chest, not modest, just cold now that sweat had started drying. "You used him for access at the start," she said. It was not accusation for performance. It was a tool laid on the table. "Did you decide not to tell him because you thought it would end that?"

Mina could have lied with more finesse if they had asked her in clothes, in daylight, across a kitchen table with coffee in front of everybody. Here the truth had less distance to cross.

"At first? Yes." The word came out like a splinter. "After that it got worse, because then I wanted him for himself and I still didn't say it. Then I wanted you and I still didn't say it. That isn't nobler. It's just messier."

Tomas looked away first. That hurt more than if he had called her a liar to her face.

"I'm gonna make coffee," he said, and left the room.

They heard him go down the short hall, the old boards giving him away one complaint at a time. The kitchen cabinet banged once. Then again, gentler, because even angry Tomas knew the walls at camp were basically gossip with nails in it.

Mina sat up. The sheet fell to her waist. Her skin cooled too fast in the damp air. "You asked," she said to Rhea. "You got it."

"Not all of it." Rhea retied the loose end of her braid with the black elastic she'd kept on her wrist through the whole night. It had left a pale groove in her skin. She pulled the braid tight enough to make the elastic snap. "But enough."

"If you want me out, say it plain."

Rhea slid off the bed and bent for her underwear, one controlled movement after another, like she was packing delicate equipment instead of dressing after sex. Mina watched her pull Tomas's shirt over her head. The hem landed high on her thighs. Guano and rubbing alcohol still haunted the fabric she'd shed on the floor last night. Rhea always smelled faintly of work, even naked.

"I don't want you out," she said. "I want you to stop editing yourself after people are already implicated."

She left the room with the shirt hanging crooked over one shoulder. Mina stayed on the edge of the bed long enough for the silence to become humiliating. Then she found her shorts under the chair, wiped herself with the least-damp corner of the sheet because the room offered nothing cleaner, and followed.

***

Day had come up mean and colorless after the storm. The screened walkway threw a gray lattice over the boards. Water dripped from the roof into the bucket Beto had shoved under the bad seam last week, each hit carrying that hollow aluminum bong that made the whole place sound makeshift even on quiet mornings. Farther off, from the generator shed, came one brief scrape of metal and Beto's irritated voice telling something inanimate to quit acting possessed. He had the particular talent of scolding machinery as if it were a younger cousin who had embarrassed the family.

In the kitchen Tomas stood at the counter in cutoff work pants, barefoot, measuring coffee with the same attention he gave carburetors. He knocked the spoon twice against the tin to level it, then once more because his hands were not steady enough on the second measure. His phone lay faceup by the sink. The cracked corner of the screen flashed and went dark, flashed and went dark. Mina did not need to read the district notice banner to know it was work.

Rhea sat at the table with one knee up, Tomas's shirt riding higher on her thigh. She had rebraided her hair, but too fast; the plait ran slightly crooked down her back. Beside her was the bat recorder case with a strip of silver duct tape peeling off one corner. She worried it with a thumbnail while she watched Tomas pour water into the kettle.

Nobody told Mina not to come in. That made the room harder than welcome would've.

The coffee smell arrived before the kettle got hot, bitter and dark enough to cut through wet wood and old fish. Tomas did not look at Mina when he said, "Door."

She turned and pushed the screen shut behind her. Mosquitoes battered against it from the outside, frantic with the post-rain hatch.

"Thank you," he said. Still not looking at her.

Mina leaned her hip against the counter by the icebox and instantly regretted it because the wood was damp and cold. Her body had gone alert in that stupid animal way shame produced, reading every object as if it might be used in evidence. Three mugs drying by the sink. Fuel key lanyard hanging from the wall hook. The grease-smudged county map rolled tight in the umbrella stand. Rhea's recorder batteries lined up like blunt silver teeth. Things people used. Things people paid for. Things she had entered through appetite and need and now saw as collateral.

Tomas killed the flame under the kettle before it whistled. "Walk me through it again," he said. "Only this time do it like I'm the part that matters."

Rhea said, "That's fair."

Mina let out air through her nose. "All right."

He finally turned. He had put on his reading glasses, the cheap pair he only used for small print and map edges. They made his anger look almost administrative, which was somehow worse. "From the point where you knew there was a live animal in the cooler."

"I already told you."

"No. You told me the part that hurts you." He set three mugs on the counter harder than necessary. "I'm asking for the part that would've been useful to me before I started burning county gas and signing my name under it."

Rhea's eyes flicked to Mina once. Answer him.

So Mina did. She kept her hands flat against the counter because they wanted to start moving, and movement would only turn into defense.

"He had me carry it from the service door to the skiff because I was less visible than he was in that moment. That's one. He knew the camera blind spot. That's two. He took me through the south intake cuts because it was close and because those channels already touched BrightCurrent drainage if you knew where the concrete still held under the roots. That's three." She swallowed. "And when it was in the creek, I didn't report it. Not to BrightCurrent, not to wildlife, not to anybody."

Tomas poured the water. "Why not?"

"Because I was in bed with him and because I wanted the animal to exist outside the lab and because if I reported it, I was in it too." Mina kept going because the uglier sentence was usually the real one. "And because part of me liked having seen something nobody else had. I liked that more than I liked being clean."

He nodded once, tight. "There you go."

"That's not the full story either," Rhea said.

"No," Mina said. "It isn't."

The kettle had steamed up Tomas's glasses. He took them off, wiped them with the hem of a dish towel, and leaned both hands on the counter.

"The photo," he said. "The one everybody knows. Was it the same eel?"

"Maybe." Mina hated the word so much she almost bit it in half. "I know that's useless."

"It's not useless," Rhea said. "It's unstable. There is a difference."

Tomas gave her a quick look that said he appreciated the distinction and hated the situation that required it.

Mina said, "The light looked different in the creek than it did in the cooler. In the cooler it was too clean. Hard blue. It made the plastic glow like a cheap toy. In the creek the tannin smeared it out, made it look older, more animal. I told myself that meant wild. It could just as easily have meant water." She rubbed her thumb over a notch in the damp wood counter. "When I got the photo later, I told the story that way. That I'd found something impossible in blackwater and documented it. Which I had. I just couldn't say what else I might also have done."

Rhea asked, "Did Jonah push you to go public?"

"Yes."

"Because he wanted credit?"

"Because he wanted deniability. A young guide saying she'd seen something uncanny in the creek played better than him saying he'd moved biotech inventory around after hours."

"And you let it play."

"Yes."

There was relief in the plainness of that one. She did not wrap it in weather or a little sermon. She just said yes.

Tomas picked up the sugar jar, set it down again without using it, then laughed once under his breath like a man who had found mold in a place he already knew was wet.

"I don't give a shit that you were screwing him," he said. "That part isn't the news. The news is you knew from the start this wasn't just some bad old article hanging around your neck. It was a live piece of the story. And you let me ferry you into it blind."

Mina made herself stay still. "I didn't know it was the same creek under this camp then."

"That's not what I said."

"I know." She pushed off the counter because leaning had started to feel like cowardice. "I knew enough to tell you there was a cooler and a man and a maybe-not-wild eel at the root of all this. I didn't. That's true."

"Why?" Tomas asked.

The simplicity of the question scraped her because it was not simple at all.

"At first because I needed your boat and your fuel key and I thought telling you would make me look exactly like what I was." She forced herself not to look away. "Then because you kept being kind to me in ways I hadn't earned, and that made it harder. Then because Rhea came into it, and once she did I wanted one room in my life where the story wasn't already poisoning the floor."

Rhea's mouth tightened, not with surprise. With recognition.

"That room was made of us," she said. "You don't get to call it separate just because that would have been convenient."

"I know."

"Do you?" Tomas said.

Mina turned to him. He had gone very calm. That was the sharpest version of him. The county fuel key hung six inches from his elbow, brass cut with a little half-moon bite near the head where years of use had worn it. Mina had kissed that wrist once on a dock after he'd unlocked the pump cage for her, thinking at the time that she understood the texture of risk. She had understood almost none of it.

"I know now," she said.

"No, you knew then. You just preferred the version where it stayed expensive and erotic instead of stupid and possibly criminal." He pulled the key off the hook and let it swing from one finger. "You know what part makes me feel like an idiot? Not the sex. Not even the lying, exactly. It's that every gallon I moved for you went on a county sheet with my badge number next to it, and you were standing there already connected to a biotech mess big enough to sink people."

"Tomas," Rhea said.

"No, let me have it right." He turned to her, but he was still talking to Mina. "If the county comes asking later what I knew, what do I say? That I thought I was sneaking a disgraced guide into protected water for a look at a weird fish? That I didn't know there was a stolen specimen and some charismatic swamp bastard in the foundation? That my wife was in bed with her too, so it all felt more modern and less illegal?" He shook his head once. "That doesn't get funnier if I say it slower."

Mina felt heat come into her face despite the damp cool of the kitchen, and it was not the accusation doing it. It was hearing how little dignity any of them would be allowed if it ever reached official ears. The room had no language for what they were that could survive paperwork.

"You can say I kept it from you," she said.

"I'd love to. Sounds great. It also sounds like I was too dumb to ask the right questions before I started siphoning public fuel." He hooked the key back onto the wall so hard it slapped wood. "I've got a skiff note that hits whether I sleep or not. County doesn't care if I was horny. County doesn't care if I believed in your miracle fish. County cares if the numbers line up."

There it was, the piece beneath the anger that had nothing to do with jealousy. Money. Schedule. The humiliating fact of labor being measurable in ways desire never was.

Mina said, "I know what your job costs you."

"Do you?" Tomas's smile was brief and nasty. "You know what it bought you."

The key he'd slapped back on the hook still trembled against the wall. Mina watched the brass half-moon flash and had to swallow before she answered. At the beginning, that was exactly what it had been: access bought with a body, with his and hers both. The trouble was that the truth had not stayed pinned to the beginning. It had gone on breathing after that, which made it harder, not easier.

Rhea peeled the strip of duct tape all the way off the recorder case and folded it into a tight gray square between her fingers. "Stop flattening it," she said.

Tomas looked at her. "I'm not."

"You are, a little. She used you. You used her. So did I, if we're being honest enough to bother." Her voice stayed even, but that was not softness. It was discipline. "The difference is that she withheld information that changed the risk profile for everybody else in the room."

Tomas gave a short nod. "Yes. That's the part."

Rhea turned to Mina. "Is there more?"

It would have been easy to think she meant another lover, another eel, another beautiful half-ruined story. Rhea did not mean easy things.

"No more coolers," Mina said. "No other secret release I know about. No paper trail in my drawer. Just the old photo, the old story, and Jonah telling me feral like that made him a philosopher instead of a thief."

"Have you talked to him recently?" Rhea asked.

"No."

"Would he contradict you?"

Mina thought of Jonah's easy grin, the way he could make a lie sound like shared memory. "He'd reframe me. Same difference."

Tomas snorted. "That kind always thinks wording is a life raft."

"Sometimes it works," Mina said.

"Not for me."

Silence moved through the kitchen, not empty this time but occupied. The kettle ticked as it cooled. Outside, one of the spoonbills started up again, harsh and ridiculous and unmistakably alive. Beto dropped something metal in the shed and swore at it in two languages. A gust pushed mangrove stink through the screen. The whole camp smelled rinsed and not cleaner for it.

Rhea stood and came to the counter for her mug. Tomas poured for her first without being asked. Then he poured for Mina and left the mug near the edge of the counter, close enough for steam to reach her wrist and far enough that he did not have to hand it to her.

The coffee was black and violent. Mina drank anyway. It burned the roof of her mouth and gave her somewhere specific to put the feeling.

Rhea leaned against the sink and held her mug in both hands. "Here's what changes," she said. "No more delayed truth because the timing feels bad or the room feels good. If there's another layer, I get it before you get to decide whether telling me would cost you something."

"You both do," Tomas said.

Mina nodded.

"Say it," he said.

She looked at him. He was tired. More than angry, maybe. The crooked nose, the grease that never fully left the cuticles, the mosquito bites stippling both forearms, all of it made him look not like the man who had ferried her into secrecy but like the county worker who would still be expected at a dock in an hour with wet boots and signed logs and no useful category for what had happened in his house last night.

"You both do," she said.

He accepted that with the smallest movement of his chin.

Rhea asked, "When you saw the animal in the cooler, did it look like what you've been seeing here?"

This was the kind of question Rhea asked when she had already felt the emotional part and was now turning the hurt into inquiry, because inquiry at least had handholds.

Mina considered. "Yes and no."

Rhea waited.

"The body, yes. The movement, yes. That same way of looking like it belonged to root shadow even when there wasn't any. But the cooler light felt wrong. Too clean, like it had never touched mud. Here the glow comes through tannin and silt and whatever the creek has done to it. Here it looks lived in."

"Selection pressure," Rhea murmured, almost to herself.

Tomas rolled his eyes without heat. "Can you two maybe not do science pillow talk right now?"

The joke died in the coffee steam. Rhea gave him a look that would have cut most people. Tomas took a drink of coffee instead of grinning back, and the silence carried all the old shorthand with none of the relief.

Mina said, "I don't know if I'm describing the animal or my guilt."

"Both are data," Rhea said.

"Christ," Tomas muttered, but not viciously.

The phone on the counter buzzed again. He looked at the screen this time. Mina watched his eyes move once, twice.

"Standing water checks," he said. "Whole district's gonna be out bitching about mosquitoes acting like mosquitoes."

He set the phone down and rubbed at the bridge of his nose with thumb and forefinger. Mina noticed the pale dent there from the reading glasses, the small human mark of a thing he probably would not remember doing by lunch.

"I need one more answer," he said.

She braced.

"If you'd told me before last night, before any of that in there" - he jerked his chin toward the bedroom hall without looking at it - "would I still have been the man with the boat?"

It was such a naked question that for a second she could not protect herself from it with intelligence. That was Rhea's gift and Tomas's too, in different dialects. Between them they could cut past every version Mina liked to perform.

"At the very start?" she said. "Yes, mostly."

He looked down into his mug and laughed once, because the truth had at least spared him the insult of politeness.

"After?" she said before he could move away from the first answer. "No. After, you were Tomas. Which is worse for me, not better."

His mouth shifted, but the expression never softened into anything she could work with. It left him looking resolved more than wounded, which was worse.

"That doesn't refund the gas," he said.

"I know."

"And it doesn't make me feel smarter."

"I know."

Rhea set her mug down. "Nobody here gets to come out looking smart."

The mug clicked once against the sink and settled. Mina opened her mouth to argue, found nothing but heat there, and closed it again.

Tomas picked up the map tube from the umbrella stand and knocked it once against his palm. "Next time," he said, and Mina looked up because the phrase itself felt like a reprieve she had not earned, "if we're going back into those cuts, I want every rotten piece of history on the table first. I don't care if it makes you look filthier. I don't care if it makes me sound stupid for being here. I want the whole damn creek, not the brochure."

Rhea said, "Before the next boat."

Tomas looked at her. "Before the next gallon."

Mina closed her hand around the hot mug until it hurt. Outside, the post-storm light was finally gathering into actual morning, turning the mangroves from one black mass into many separate black things. The camp creaked. The bucket under the roof seam rang again. Somewhere along the walkway Beto started whistling a song he never remembered all the words to, only the tune, and the tune kept losing arguments with a bent hinge. Farther out, beyond the mangroves, the rookery was getting loud again, all stolen sticks and bill-clatter.

No one reached for her. No one told her to go.

Rhea stayed at the sink in Tomas's shirt with her braid crooked from haste, her questions no softer for having wanted Mina an hour ago. Tomas held the county map like it was both work and warning. Mina stood between them with the mug heating one hand and the handle of a stolen cooler biting the other across eight years, and understood that innocence had not been what she was about to lose.

It was privacy.

And privacy, she saw now, had been the cheapest part of the lie.


# Chapter 9

The rookery had gone past beautiful and into something sticky, loud, and faintly indecent. The spoonbills did not so much occupy the mangroves as overtake them. Pink bodies flashed from branch to branch. Bills clattered like someone shaking a crate of wet bones. Every exposed limb of mangrove seemed to hold a nest in progress, a nest collapsing, or a pair of birds trying to fuck on top of materials they had not finished stealing yet.

From the observation platform Mina could smell the colony before she could sort any individual bird from the mass. Late afternoon had cooked everything down to guano, sun-baked fish, green mangrove sap torn fresh from twigs, the sugary donor wine sweating in its metal tub behind her. The wind kept changing its mind and bringing all of it at once. Guests had paid extra for spoonbill week and the birds were delivering exactly the sort of spectacle rich people liked to pretend they had discovered with their own moral seriousness. Every few minutes somebody on the platform made a delighted little noise that was really embarrassment wearing a visor.

One male spoonbill lurched onto a nest already occupied by a female and three stolen sticks, flapped pink and white over her head, lost his footing, recovered it with a furious clack of the bill, then mounted her anyway while she stayed braced and practical beneath him. Two donors laughed, then looked around to see whether laughing at bird sex counted as vulgar. Another bird came in crosswise with a mangrove twig longer than its own body and got into a shoving match with the pair before the male finished. A shower of leaves and droppings came down through the branches. Somebody behind Mina said, "Oh my God," with the round, pleased horror of a woman seeing a bartender crack an egg into a whiskey on purpose.

Mina kept the binocular strap from sticking to the back of her neck and gave them the cleaned-up version.

"Peak breeding does this," she said. "They get territorial, sloppy, loud. Everybody wants the same branch at once."

"That one is really committed," a man in a linen fishing shirt said, pointing with a champagne flute.

"He's," Mina said, because there was no honest donor-safe synonym for what the bird was doing.

She heard Beto snort behind the beverage table. He had changed into a camp polo that still hung off him like it belonged to an older cousin, and the shiny burn mark on his right wrist showed every time he reached into the ice. He was supposed to be topping off glasses and keeping people from leaning too hard on the rail. Instead he was watching the colony with the bright, hungry concentration he usually gave a broken motor, head tipped, lips keeping a silent count.

"You want me to tell them that's the family values package?" he murmured when he passed Mina a sweating pitcher of water.

"I want you not to get fired in front of canapes."

"Then I need hazard pay."

He slid away before she could answer, grinning at one of the older donors with a courtesy that became almost formal whenever he was actually nervous. The kid had talked himself hoarse through the storm two nights ago and then gone quiet this morning, carrying trays and toolboxes with the carefulness of somebody trying not to think too hard. Mina had seen the anatomy notes folded in his back pocket at lunch, the corner softened white from rereading. He wanted trade school so badly he studied tendon diagrams between dish shifts. He still stopped for birds when they made a noise nobody else heard.

Imogen Price moved through the crowd with a tray of paper napkins and the sort of posture that made the whole weather-beaten platform feel like a formal institution for as long as she was standing on it. Her silver hair had mostly pulled loose from the pins she had forced into it at noon, but she kept smoothing it back, practicing order on the one thing she could still control. Every donor got the right blend of Latin name, soft crisis, and manageable hope from her. She could make deferred maintenance sound like intimacy with place. She could make a leaking roof sound like authenticity.

"Mina," she said without looking at her, still smiling at a board couple from Naples. "Stay visible until dinner service starts."

It was not a request. In Imogen's mouth, even the low pulse of panic came out with clause control.

"I'm visible now."

Imogen's eyes flicked to her, brief and dry. "Do not experiment with me in front of people who write checks."

Then she drifted onward, all gracious edges, to rescue a man who had mistaken a juvenile ibis for a spoonbill and was about to say so loudly.

Mina watched her go and felt the old mix of gratitude and irritation scrape together in her ribs. Spoonbill week. The deadline had narrowed to that phrase until it sounded like a sentence and not a period on a calendar. Through spoonbill week, Imogen had said after the donor-platform humiliation. After that, one more problem and Mina could take her debts and her scandal and her sleeping bag somewhere else. The camp was an argument built on pilings, and Mina was the loosest board in it.

Below the platform, the guest-facing boardwalk buckled with traffic. Another skiff idled out past the mangroves, not close enough to break the protected line but close enough to throw wake where it did not belong. A guide in a white performance shirt lifted a hand in bland apology toward the camp. Tomas, down at the county dock, answered with the kind of wave that looked friendly from a distance and obscene up close.

Even from here Mina could tell he was angry by how precise he went. He stood beside the airboat with one boot on the rub rail and the fuel key clipped to his belt, broad shoulders held square as lumber. He had changed out of county khaki into a thin gray T-shirt darkened at the spine with sweat, but he still wore the work radio at his hip. His left thumb was wrapped in black electrical tape where he had split the nail lifting a battery last week, and every time he flexed his hand he rubbed that taped thumb against his forefinger, checking for feeling by reflex. When he looked up and found Mina on the platform he did not smile. He only touched two fingers to the side of the hull, then to the mangroves to the west.

Fifteen minutes, if she read him right.

Rhea came up the stairs carrying a hard case and the soft-sided dry bag that held her recorder. Sweat had darkened the braid down her back to almost black, and there was a strip of silver gaffer tape stuck to the side of her shorts from some earlier repair she had not bothered to peel off. She always wrote battery charge percentages on masking tape flags instead of trusting the factory labels, and three of those little white flags fluttered now from the case handle like surrendered thoughts. She crossed to the shady side of the platform, not wasting any breath on the donors, and set the case down with both hands, easing her fingers off the latches one at a time.

"You're still working the room," she said.

"Imogen's idea of penance."

Rhea looked out over the birds. "The colony is doing the work for you."

One of the donors, a woman with enamel warblers pinned all around the brim of her visor, edged close enough to hear. "Doctor Acosta, is this normal?"

Rhea gave the woman a level look that was not rude only because it was too exact to qualify as social. "For spoonbills in breeding season, yes. For people on platforms, probably not."

The woman laughed harder than the line deserved, relieved to have been given permission.

Rhea crouched to unlatch the case. Inside lay the hydrophone cable coiled in patient loops, a little handheld recorder fitted with an adapter that had clearly lived too many field seasons, two desiccant packs, and a clamp arm with one rubber foot missing. Mina crouched beside her. On the platform the wood planks carried donor footsteps, Beto's quick balancing run, the steady tap of Imogen's ring against a glass as she redirected attention from bird sex to fundraising. Under all of it came the colony itself, a wet constant uproar that made every human voice sound performative.

"How bad?" Mina asked.

"Good enough to embarrass us later." Rhea checked the recorder input, then the cable. "I had to cannibalize the clean adapter from the cave unit. The seal on this housing isn't trustworthy. If it gets swamped, the file will survive only out of spite."

"Encouraging."

"I don't do encouragement. I do equipment triage."

Mina watched Rhea's hands. Long fingers, blunt nails, no rings. Clean movements, never hurried, even now. The morning after the confession Rhea had asked questions the way some people laid out scalpels, one beside another, each more useful than kind. Since then she had not withdrawn from Mina, but she had stopped making room for any fantasy that hunger and honesty were the same thing. Mina had earned that. The fact that Rhea was here anyway, repurposing grant gear for an illegal eel run on a donor night, felt less like absolution than a second, sharper chance.

"You think he can get us far enough in?" Mina asked.

"Tomas thinks he can. That usually means he can or he dies annoyed."

From below came the service bell from the kitchen, clanged twice by somebody who liked the theatrical version of every task. The donors turned as a group toward the main house. Wineglasses flashed. Imogen raised her voice, all hospitality and managed scarcity.

"If you would like a closer look after plates are out, Mina can take a small group back to the rail."

Mina almost laughed. Of course. Use the birds twice. Once before dinner, once after. Turn the feral noise into a premium tier.

Rhea snapped the hard case shut. "That's our opening. The second seating buys us forty minutes before somebody starts looking for a narrative."

"You make that sound like a criminal window."

"It's a criminal window."

They stood. Rhea slung the dry bag crosswise over her chest and tucked one of the white tape flags down so it would not show. Her shoulder brushed Mina's forearm on the turn. It was a tiny thing, accidental enough from a distance. Mina felt it all the way to the back of her teeth.

At the stairs Beto intercepted them with a tray of shrimp toasts and a look sharpened by curiosity.

"If anybody asks," he said softly, not quite meeting Mina's eyes, "you're both in the bat lab because the charger started sparking again."

Mina blinked. "Why would anybody ask you?"

He shrugged one narrow shoulder. "Because nobody tells me anything, so everyone assumes I know everything."

Rhea studied him for a second. "Don't let anyone touch the west rail. The birds are already flushing from that side wake."

"Yeah, I saw." He set the tray down on the platform rail and wiped his damp palms on his oversized shirt. "That charter guy's running too close because his clients paid for indecency. Same as ours."

He said it with the offhand meanness of someone too young to bother coating the truth. Then he lifted the tray again and transformed back into staff, smiling at a donor who wanted another napkin and pretending the spoonbills were the strangest thing on the platform.

Mina followed Rhea down the screened walkway. The whole camp sounded overstressed. Silverware in the kitchen. Screens ticking in a crosswind. Somebody starting the generator for the lights that only worked when kicked. The birds on top of all that, relentless and pink and filthy. By the time they reached the service side of the main house, Tomas had the airboat tucked behind the county shed where the mangroves swallowed most of the view from the guest path.

"Move," he said when he saw them, low and without prelude. "That board couple from Naples is finishing dessert faster than God intended."

He reached for the hard case before Rhea could answer. She let him take the weight but not the responsibility, keeping one hand on the latch as she stepped down into the boat. Tomas hated being corrected in public. In private he hated it too, but he could at least enjoy the fight. Tonight he had no spare room for either pleasure. His jaw had gone tight under the crooked nose, and a mosquito had burst dark on the side of his neck where he had slapped it flat and not bothered to wipe the blood.

"You radioed in?" Rhea asked.

"Larval check and trap pull on the west fingers. Boring enough nobody'll follow up unless I crash."

"Comforting theme tonight," Mina said.

Tomas looked at her then, finally, and the look had heat in it even through the strain. "If I wanted comforting, I would've stayed on the platform and watched rich people pretend not to enjoy bird porn."

Mina got into the bow beside the dry bag. The aluminum floor was still hot from the day. Tomas shoved off with one boot, brought the fan up just enough to clear the mud seam, and the camp began to slide away behind them. On the guest side somebody laughed too loud at something Imogen had arranged for them to find charming. The colony answered with a rising rattle so dense it felt like weather bearing down on the back of Mina's neck.

* * *

The first stretch of channel ran black and flat under the mangroves, but even there the breeding week had altered the air. Feathers clung to the roots. White crusts of guano marked the higher branches like bad paint. Small silver fish kept flicking at the surface; the water had gone all nerves. Tomas steered by habit more than sight, easing the boat through turns that looked to Mina like solid wall until the last second opened a seam in the leaves. He knew where the creeks made room for a hull by the color of the dark.

Once they were clear of camp lights he cut the fan down and let the engine settle into a lower growl. The sound went from public machine to intimate threat. Rhea knelt on the center bench and clipped the recorder to the dry bag strap. She had taped a square of red film over the screen so the glow would not carry. Her braid swung forward over one shoulder when she bent to feed out cable, and Mina had the irrational urge to touch the damp nape of her neck with her mouth. Desire had not become cleaner since the confession. If anything it had lost the easy alibi of secrecy and turned more exact.

Tomas saw where she was looking. He gave one short breath that might have been a laugh and might have been exhaustion.

"You two planning to help me or just think complicated thoughts at each other?"

"Both," Mina said.

"Good. Hold this."

He passed her the push pole he kept for the tighter channels. The wood was slick with old river varnish and new spray. She braced it along the gunwale while he adjusted course around a floating mat of leaves and a dead mullet white at the belly. Ahead, the noise from the colony thickened until it seemed impossible that human donors on platforms could hear it and still imagine themselves near anything genteel.

The channel opened without warning.

One moment they were under mangrove shade and the next the rookery spread out in front of them, a low flooded bowl of roots and narrow islands and birds everywhere. The last of the sun had gone syrup-colored. It hit the spoonbills and turned them too saturated to be believable, all rose and dirty salmon and the pale flash of undersides when wings opened. Nests packed the mangroves so densely that from a distance the trees looked upholstered. Adults came in carrying sticks, or left with them, because half the work of breeding seemed to be theft. Pairs jostled. Bills crossed. Wings lifted. Birds mounted and dismounted with a crude urgency that had nothing romantic in it. One female stayed planted on her nest while the male on top of her beat his wings for balance and shat over the side without interrupting himself.

"Jesus," Mina said, not bothering to make it donor-safe for anyone.

"Yes," Rhea said, and lowered the hydrophone into the blackwater.

The cable slipped through her hand in measured increments. She watched the tension in the line the way she watched mist nets. Equipment had a language to her, and she refused to miss a syllable. Tomas brought the boat sideways to the current and set the prop just enough to counter drift. On the far side of the colony another skiff ghosted past at the legal perimeter, two passengers lifting phones that flashed white every time they caught sky. Each flash sent a pulse of irritation through Mina's body as specific as grit under a contact lens.

"That asshole is going to flush the whole west shoulder," Tomas muttered.

"Can you hold here?" Rhea asked.

"If nobody throws wake and if these birds don't decide my face looks like nesting material."
