"I am not saying more right now than the form says," she told him. "Go home. Do not contact the pump contractor. Do not attempt to access district property after today without written notice. If there is an explanation that helps you, counsel will want it clean."

Tomas's mouth moved like he was lining up six responses and killing them all. He signed the form. His signature looked meaner than usual, each letter pressed through the page. When he straightened, the room seemed to tip around the absence at his belt.

Outside, the sun had climbed high enough to turn the dock planks tacky. Vega stayed in the office. She did not escort them out. The county knew how to do humiliation cheaply.

Tomas walked to the truck and stood with one hand on the open door, staring into the cab as if he had forgotten why he owned it. The mangroves beyond the lot clicked with insects. Somewhere under the dock a mullet slapped once at the surface.

Mina came up beside him. "Tom."

He flinched at the short form, not in tenderness.

"Do not," he said, then corrected himself as if even that much open form wasted energy. "Don't start with sorry unless you've got another eleven-eighty every two weeks and the overtime on top."

His voice stayed low. That was the cruelty of it. If he had shouted, she could have taken the blast and called it weather. Quiet made every word chosen.

"I can cover some of it," she said, though even as she said it she heard how ridiculous it was. Six hundred and forty a week from the camp, less after groceries and her penalty payment. "Not all. Some."

"With what."

"I've got a little put back."

"For your Board payment."

She hated that he knew the exact shape of her poverty. Hated more that she had let him know because intimacy had made accounting feel like honesty.

"I can miss one month."

"And then what."

The truck cab smelled of vinyl and sun and the fried sweetness of the gas-station pastries he kept buying when sleep got thin. On the floorboard lay a spool of line, two socket extensions, and the envelope from the bank about the skiff loan. Open already, crumpled once, smoothed back out. Mina did not need to read it again to know the number. Nine thousand one hundred still hanging from a boat that only made sense if Tomas kept county work steady enough to keep feeding it.

"I said I'd help," she said.

"Help with what, Mina? Groceries for a week? One auto-debit? Great. Then the next one comes. Then the one after that. Rhea's stipend lands once a month and the camp likes to remember housing is a favor when anybody gets inconvenient. This wasn't some sexy risk anymore the second they put my key in a tray."

He shut the truck door harder than he needed to. The sound slapped over the lot and came back from the metal shed.

She looked at his hands because looking higher would have meant seeing too much. Grease still lived in the half-moons of his nails no matter how often he scrubbed. A pale groove showed around his wrist where he usually wore the district lanyard on rough nights.

"I didn't ask you to borrow the thermal rig," she said.

"No. You asked for proof. The rest was just me being useful, right?"

"That's not fair."

"It is exactly fair."

He rubbed the bridge of his nose with thumb and forefinger, the motion abrupt and tired. When he dropped his hand he sounded flatter, which meant he was closer to the truth than either of them liked. "I knew what I was doing. That's on me. But don't stand here acting shocked at the bill."

The dock office door opened again behind them. The young county employee came out carrying a bucket of absorbent compound. He kept his eyes politely away from them and whistled one bar of some old ranchera under his breath as he crossed toward the pumps, the tune breaking because he could not remember the next line. That, more than anything Vega had said, made Mina want to put her fist through something. Ordinary work continuing around the ruin. Somebody else's shy little song.

"What are you going to tell Rhea," she asked.

He gave her a look from the side. "You think I haven't already."

Of course he had. He had texted from the office when Vega stepped out or before Mina arrived. Or maybe he had sent it at dawn the second the district called him in. Rhea would have read the message in the bat lab with the recorder batteries warming beside her and her mouth gone thin.

The heat on the lot thickened. The coffee on Vega's desk would go to skin soon.

"Night channels are done for me," Tomas said. "Maybe for two weeks. Maybe for good. Even if I get back on days, the key isn't mine. The cage isn't mine. Every gallon's going to glow now."

Glow was the wrong word, too close to what had made this possible.

He heard it too and looked away toward the mangroves. "Jesus."

For a second she thought he might touch her. Instead he opened the driver-side door and climbed in.

"Tom."

He leaned across and shoved the passenger door open without meeting her eyes. "I can't drive back with you walking."

Habit opened the door. Weathered tenderness followed. So did the old rule that anger rode home with you instead of leaving somebody to cook on the shell path. Mina got in.

They drove to camp with the windows down because the truck air conditioner had been dying for months and nobody had money to pretend otherwise. Tomas kept one hand high on the wheel, the other braced against the door. Every time the tires struck a pothole the tools under the seat clinked together. He did not put on the radio.

At the turnoff to Rookery Key he said, "Do not ask Rhea for a workaround today."

The formality snagged in the air between them. He knew it too. He corrected nothing.

"I wasn't going to."

He looked at her then, long enough to show he did not believe her or did not trust her not to start believing it in the next hour.

"If you want the creek now," he said, "you find a way that doesn't come out of my hide."

He turned into camp without waiting for an answer.

* * *

By noon the bat lab smelled hotter than the day outside. It smelled of battery heat, guano, old plywood, and the sour medicinal bite of rubbing alcohol. The little window unit had given up keeping pace and now mostly snarled. Mina stood in the screened walkway with one hand on the frame, listening to Rhea move around inside among hard-plastic cases and mesh bags and the clicking playback of bat calls slowed into human range.

Rhea did not tell her to come in. She did not tell her to stay out either.

She was at the central table cleaning one of the ultrasonic microphones with a child's toothbrush. She had changed out of field clothes into an old gray university T-shirt with the sleeves cut off, and her wet hair was braided so tight it made the bones of her face look even cleaner. Reading glasses sat low on her nose, one arm wrapped in white veterinary tape where it had snapped months ago. Beside her elbow lay a legal pad divided into columns so neat they looked almost sarcastic in this room.

Mina stepped inside. The screen door slapped and bounced once behind her.

"He told you," she said.

"Yes."

Rhea kept brushing dried mud from the seam of the microphone casing. Her voice did not rise. The lack of drama in it made Mina feel more exposed than if Rhea had been crying or furious.

"You saw him?"

"For two minutes before he went to shower. He smelled like county office and bad coffee."

The phrase carried an intimacy that did not include Mina. It marked Tomas as husband, not co-conspirator, and the plainness of it stung.

On the table, under the legal pad, Mina saw a yellow envelope from the university and a folded camp housing agreement with Imogen's signature at the bottom. Rhea followed her glance and laid the legal pad over both without hurry.

"If you're here to tell me this isn't your fault," Rhea said, "save the effort. I'm not in a mood for clean causality."

"I'm not."

"Good."

Rhea set the microphone aside and picked up the next piece of equipment. Her hands were careful even now. That had always been part of what Mina wanted from her, the way she touched fragile things without making a show of gentleness.

"Tom said not to ask you for a workaround today," Mina said.

One side of Rhea's mouth twitched with bleak recognition, the kind reserved for a stupidity so predictable it almost counted as weather. "And yet here you are."

Mina crossed the room because standing in the doorway made her feel like a teenager at a principal's office. The floor flexed under her bare feet. Through the open inner window came the camp's layered noise: a pot banging in the kitchen, somebody laughing too hard out on the guest deck, screens ticking as the wind shifted.

"I'm here because if you step away now, the whole thing changes."

"It already changed."

"You know what I mean."

Rhea looked up at last. Her eyes were tired enough to flatten the room. "Do I."

Mina put both hands on the table, careful not to touch the gear. She could smell the soap she had used after the dock, cheap coconut over diesel residue that would not come fully off. "We still have the recorder. We still have the partial file. Low tide's early this week. The west root path is passable if the rain holds. There are ways in that don't use his route."

Rhea let out a breath through her nose and removed the glasses. She folded them with the deliberate, dangerous calm Mina had started learning over the last few weeks and still hated every time it came toward her.

"You came here to say that today."

"I came here because the window is closing."

"The window for what. Proof? Redemption? Another night where we all pretend desire and logistics are separate departments?"

"Don't do that."

"Do what."

"Make this uglier than it is."

Rhea gave a small, almost incredulous laugh. "Mina, it's ugly. That's not my embellishment. My husband lost his route this morning. If the district doesn't clear him, we lose the biweekly pay that has been making the engine loan look survivable. If Imogen decides my permit work now smells like trespass and biotech scandal, we lose housing at the same time. The camp isn't some neutral motel. The room is part of the job. So yes, I'm going to be exact."

She stood while she said it. Tall, narrow, still wet at the hairline from the shower she must have taken after handling Tomas's shirt or touching his back. On her collarbone, above the T-shirt edge, a fading bruise showed yellow at the center. Mina had put it there days ago with her mouth. It sat now above all this language like a stupid little relic from another economy.

"I know what the room is," Mina said quietly.

"Do you."

"Don't talk to me like I'm a tourist."

"Then stop speaking as if the only vanishing resource in this camp is your access to a creek."

The words landed with clinical force. Rhea did not raise her voice because she did not need to. The lab was too small for sound to do the work.

Mina straightened. Every part of her wanted to answer too fast, to start naming dates and routes and biological windows until precision became a shield again. She made herself hold still. Outside the screen a mosquito whined against the mesh and found no entry.

"I know he got hurt because of this," she said. "Because of me, some of it."

"Some."

"Fine. A lot."

"That's closer."

Rhea rubbed the spot between her eyebrows where the glasses had pressed. "I'm not interested in making you confess for sport. I know Tom made choices. I know he likes being necessary. I know you didn't put a gun to his head and make him falsify county fuel. But if you walk in here an hour after suspension and start reconfiguring my grant equipment into your next chance, you're telling me something about the scale you use. I need you to hear yourself."

Mina looked past her to the foam equipment cases stacked by the wall, the batteries charging, the labeled bags of guano samples. Work everywhere. Real work with protocols and dead grants and tiny exact data points nobody would ever call miraculous. The creek always made her feel that anything not luminous was secondary. Here the opposite pressure held.

"What do you want me to say?" she asked.

"Nothing polished." Rhea's voice lowered. "I want you to know that I'm deciding three things at once and none of them match cleanly. Marriage. Housing. You."

The last word did not sound romantic. It sounded like a specimen she was refusing to mislabel.

Mina said, "And the eel."

"No." Rhea shook her head once. "That's the point. Not right now."

Silence widened between them, filled by insect noise and the slowed-down chatter of bat calls from the speaker, tiny machine screams stretched into something almost plaintive. Mina remembered Rhea telling her once that the software ruined the bats' elegance but made patterns legible to human ears. That felt like the whole mess in miniature. There was nothing noble about translation, just damage turned readable.

"Do you want me to leave you alone," Mina asked.

Rhea's expression loosened a fraction. Fatigue showed first, then the smaller risk beneath it: a brief unguardedness. She set both palms on the table behind her and leaned her weight there.

"Not entirely," she said.

The answer went through Mina like fresh water and then hurt just as fast because it was not enough to live on.

"I still want you," Rhea said. "That's not the useful fact today, but it's true. I'm also furious with you in a very specific way. I'm furious that you can see this much wreckage and still come in speaking in route alternatives. That doesn't cancel the wanting. It sits beside it."

Mina nodded once because any sentence she attempted would have come out defensive.

Rhea continued, more plainly now, as if anger had burned the formal edges off her syntax. "I'm not helping you get back out there this week. I'm not loaning gear, and I'm not using my permit as camouflage. If Tom is under review, I'm not adding field lies to the pile. If Imogen asks me where I stand, I need to be able to answer without tasting bullshit."

"And us."

"Us is not a route category."

Despite herself Mina almost smiled. Rhea saw it and did not permit it to become relief.

"Don't mistake language for softness," she said.

"I know better."

"Good."

Mina moved around the table before she had fully decided to. She stopped close enough to smell Rhea's damp braid and the alcohol on her hands. Up close, the bruise on the collarbone was smaller than it had looked across the room, an old bite already migrating toward ordinary skin.

"I wasn't trying to use you," Mina said.

Rhea's eyes lifted to hers. "I believe that," she said after a moment. "You were trying not to stop."

That was worse because it was truer.

Mina looked down. The legal pad partly covered the housing agreement, but one line still showed: OCCUPANCY CONTINGENT UPON ACTIVE PROGRAM PARTICIPATION. Imogen's neat signature below it. A whole domestic life reduced to conditional paperwork on a folding table that smelled like bat shit.

"He hates me right now," Mina said.

"He's angry. Hate is lazier."

"Are you protecting him from me or me from him."

"At the moment I'm protecting my own capacity to think."

The answer was so Rhea that Mina nearly laughed, then did not. She put one hand on the table beside Rhea's and left it there without touching. Rhea looked at the hand, at the scar on Mina's left calf visible through the frayed hem of shorts, at the dried salt on Mina's shin from sweat. Tiny field notes, all of them.

"If you need groceries," Mina said, hating the inadequacy while saying it, "I can cover some until his check clears. I'm not pretending that fixes it."

Rhea's mouth tightened. "That's kind. It's also humiliating, and I'm too honest to pretend otherwise."

"I know."

"I may still say yes later."

Again, Rhea handed her a rationed mercy, slight enough to ache and solid enough to matter.

Rhea reached out then, finally, and touched two fingers to the inside of Mina's wrist where her pulse sat close to the skin. The contact was brief, intentional, almost clinical in its exact placement, which made it more intimate than a grab would have been.

"Listen to me carefully," she said. "I'm not done with you. But if you ask me for access again before you ask what Tom needs, I'll be."

Mina swallowed. The room tasted like battery metal and old heat.

"All right."

"And don't come to our room tonight."

There it was: marriage, plain and singular.

"All right."

Rhea dropped her hand. "Go find Imogen before she finds you. She has been in donor voice since breakfast, which usually means the books are bad."

"She told you that."

"She did not have to."

Rhea picked up the glasses and set them back on her nose. The conversation was over with the same economy she used to close sample bags. Mina stood another second anyway, stupidly hoping the room might reverse itself if she waited. It did not.

When she opened the screen door, Rhea said without looking up, "Eat something."

It was the gentlest thing either of them had managed.

Mina stepped back into the walkway and let the heat hit her full in the face.

* * *

Imogen's office had once been a storage room, and it still admitted the truth of that in every line. Shelves meant for cleaning supplies now held donor binders, bird-checklist pamphlets, spare flashlight batteries, three hurricane lanterns polished for ambiance but too dented to trust in a storm, and a box labeled LINENS that contained nothing but extension cords. The desk sat under a patched ceiling stain shaped like Cuba. The room always smelled faintly of mildew, printer toner, and whatever citrus oil Imogen had decided would keep guests from identifying the first two.

By late afternoon the office door stood open because the fan had stopped oscillating and was now aimed permanently at the opposite wall. Mina found Beto on a step stool in the hall outside, replacing a blown bulb in the light over the framed spoonbill photographs. He had a ladder of compressor part numbers written up his left forearm in blue marker and a folded trade-school brochure sticking out of the back pocket of his too-large shorts. One of his kitchen burns, glossy and old on the wrist, flashed when he reached overhead.

"If this thing pops again," he said without looking down, "I'm declaring the house haunted by cheap wiring and emotional repression."

His voice carried the usual quick humor, but it was stretched thinner than usual.

"How bad's the wiring," Mina asked.

"Depends whether you want the guest answer or the human answer."

"Human."

He screwed the bulb in and flicked the switch. Light came on, yellow and weak. "Human answer is the west wall hates us."

He climbed down, not stepping so much as bouncing off the stool in a way that always made every adult around him tense. He held up the dead bulb. The glass was smoked black at the neck.

"Imogen in there?" Mina asked.

"Where else would she stage-manage disaster from."

He lowered his voice and leaned in. "Don't mention the ice machine first. She already lost a battle with it and I think the machine won on points."

There was a clatter inside the office and Imogen's voice, sharper than donor-safe but not yet stripped bare. "If you are whispering about me in my own corridor, at least make it worth hearing."

Beto winced theatrically and grinned at Mina with a crack of real nerves under it. "See?"

He passed her the dead bulb like it meant something and ducked into the kitchen before he could be assigned another task. Through the swinging door Mina saw him stop at the prep counter and pull a small spiral notebook from his pocket. He glanced once at the blue marker on his forearm and copied the numbers over in neat block print before washing his hands. Saving information before sweat took it off his skin. Saving himself inch by inch.

Imogen sat behind the desk in a faded field shirt buttoned all the way up despite the heat. One silver hairpin had come loose, and a coil of hair kept dropping toward her cheek while she worked. She ignored it. Around her lay open ledgers, a laptop propped on two birding guides to get it above the dead angle of the charger cord, and a petty-cash tin with its mouth open like a joke too tired to tell itself.

Without preamble she said, "Shut the door if you are staying. The guests think paper sounds are unwelcoming."

Mina did. The room lost the hall noise and became all fan, paper, and the generator thrum under the floorboards.

"Rhea told me you were looking for me," Imogen said.

"She said the books were bad."

"They are not bad." Imogen took off her glasses, pinched the bridge of her nose, then put the glasses back on. "Bad is a mood. This is arithmetic."

Mina stayed standing. The office had no chair that was not already under paper. "You heard about Tomas."

"Of course I heard."

Imogen's gaze moved over Mina's face, taking measurements. Not sympathy. Inventory again, campwide this time.

"Sit," Imogen said.

Mina cleared invoices off the spare chair and sat. The top page she moved was a repair estimate for the main walkway pilings, the total circled twice in red. The total was twenty-seven thousand four hundred. She knew the number from nothing and then from everything all at once. It belonged to a deferred structural repair invoice carried by a camp that marketed sunrise stillness and artisanal field lunches to rich birders in linen.

Imogen saw where she was looking. "Yes," she said. "That one."

Mina set the page down carefully. "I didn't know it was that deep."

"Nobody ever does when the coffee is good and the roseate spoonbills arrive on schedule."

Outside, the guest deck gave up a burst of polite laughter. Somebody was probably being handed a sweating glass of wine and told a joke about mangrove crabs. Donor theater, Mina thought, then hated the neatness of the phrase because it made the thing sound cleaner than it was. Theater implied budget and rehearsal. This was triage in decent lighting.

"Why are you showing me this," she asked.

Imogen leaned back. The chair creaked under her in the same familiar note the camp made in crosswind. "Because this morning I had to smile through breakfast with two couples from Naples while knowing the west cistern pump is running on a seal Tomas cut out of sheet rubber for me last month, the roof over cabin three still has a soft patch I cannot pay to replace before storm inspection, and the county has just suspended the one man on this island who has been able to get me fuel, parts, and practical answers faster than requisition forms."

She tapped another stack. Payroll. Utilities. A vendor statement with PAST DUE in a block so aggressive it might as well have been profanity.

"And because you," Imogen continued, "have spent the last several weeks treating the eel as private obsession with collateral inconvenience, while I have been trying not to say out loud that the camp has crossed from genteel scarcity into actual exposure."

Mina felt herself brace as if for scolding, then realized this was worse than scolding. Scolding was personal. This was structural.

"I know the eel stopped being just mine once the footage existed," she said. "I know you started seeing money in it."

"Started?" Imogen's expression thinned. "Mina, I have been seeing money in everything for fourteen years. Birds, board members, bat data, celebrity chef fundraisers, tasteful kayaks, memorial benches, children with binoculars. The question is never whether I can see money. The question is whether the thing in front of me can survive being translated into appeal copy before it is ruined."

She opened the petty-cash tin and turned it toward Mina. Inside lay three twenties, a five, and some silver sticking to the bottom in damp air.

"This," Imogen said, "is tonight's margin before the fish delivery clears. We are full for dinner. The ice machine is failing. The board treasurer thinks unrestricted gifts are a myth spread by socialists. Beto wants trade school in the fall and has been taking every extra hour I can invent for him because his mother quite reasonably prefers that he not spend the rest of his life gutting mullet for tips. Rhea's survey contract makes her housing justifiable to the university and barely justifiable to my accountant. Tomas has been quietly fixing things that would otherwise have become line items I could not meet. Your weekly wage is not large, but it is still a wage in a house where every wage comes from somewhere."

The fan pushed hot paper smell against Mina's face. She thought of Tomas setting the fuel key on the desk, of Rhea's housing agreement under the legal pad, of Beto writing compressor numbers off his own arm before soap took them away.

"You could have told us," she said, though even as she said it she heard the childishness.

Imogen laughed without humor. "And when precisely would that have been useful. During donor cocktails. During the morning kayak orientation. In the middle of your sex-fueled illegal wildlife pursuit."

Mina looked down. On the desk corner sat a stack of handwritten place cards for tomorrow night's sponsor supper, each one done in Imogen's severe neat print on thick cream stock. The names belonged to people wealthy enough to mistake a stilt house with patched screens for rustic charm if the napkins were folded correctly.

"I wasn't asking for cocktails," Mina said.

"No. You were asking for night access, county risk, staff attention, and equipment improvisation. Those all invoice somewhere."

Imogen was one of the few people alive who could make the word invoice sound like an accusation with moral depth.

Mina said, "You wanted the footage too."

"Of course I did."

There it was, no donor tone on it at all. The old field-biologist ferocity broke through the manager.

"I wanted it because miracles alter funding behavior. Scandals alter it too, but less predictably. If I had clean proof of a surviving engineered line in protected blackwater under active rookery conditions, then yes, I could turn that into board panic, restricted emergency appeals, maybe enough unrestricted embarrassment money to shore up the pilings and keep the kitchen from eating itself. I did not start treating your fish as an asset because I had become mystical. I started because maintenance does not seduce anybody with a checkbook."

She reached for another paper and slid it over.

The generator service estimate had been deferred.

The kitchen refrigeration quote had been deferred.

The septic pump inspection was overdue.

Mina read the numbers until they blurred into one continuous demand. It was all so ordinary that it became obscene. There was no single catastrophe, just a hundred small mechanical truths waiting their turn to become an emergency.

"And donor theater?" Mina asked.

Imogen gave her a sharp look, amused despite herself. "Rhea's phrase, I assume."

"Mine now."

"Fine. Donor theater." Imogen stood and went to the shelf, where she lifted a woven basket full of citronella votives, linen napkin rings, and laminated species cards tied with ribbon. She set it on the desk between them with more force than the basket deserved. "When the east walkway floods, we route guests around it and call the detour a mangrove immersion path. When the guest-room air conditioners start leaking, we stage more time on the observation platform and speak fondly about evening breezes. When the board comes, I move the prettier scopes to the front room and close the door on the storage side. When wealthy people want authenticity, I give them enough creak and guano smell to feel adventurous, not enough to make them ask how close we are to closure."

She touched the basket's ribbon, then let go. "That is not fraud. That is hospitality under capitalism."

The phrase should have been funny. It was almost funny. Mina could not get there.

There was a knock on the doorjamb and Beto leaned in with a tray of chipped wineglasses nested in a towel.

"Do you want these out before the Naples people start looking thirsty in a punitive way," he asked, "or are we embracing austerity chic."

Imogen did not turn. "Out in ten. Use the uncracked ones first."

"That narrows the field."

"Beto."

"I'm going."

He shifted the tray against his hip but did not move yet. His eyes flicked to the open payroll sheet on the desk, then away. Quick as a bird, impossible to miss once seen.

"Also," he said, trying for light and landing near it, "if my hours are weird next week, just tell me straight. I've got that school call Friday and I don't want to sound like a liar again."

Imogen's shoulders changed. Just enough.

"Come back after dinner service," she said. "We will look at the schedule."

"That sounds like a maybe."

"It is a maybe."

He nodded once. The joke washed out of his face so completely Mina felt it in her stomach. Nineteen and already fluent in managerial weather.

"Okay," he said. Then, because he could not help himself, "If the rich people ask, I know exactly two things about wine and both of them are lies."

Imogen made a small shooing motion with one hand. Beto left.

The room held the afterimage of him a moment longer than his body did.

Mina said, "You were going to cut his hours."

"I was going to avoid promising hours I cannot fund."

"Same thing if you're nineteen."

Imogen looked at her over the lenses. "Yes."

Nothing in the answer tried to spare either of them.

Rain smell moved through the cracked window then, not rain yet, only the iron-dark warning that a storm front had started building out beyond the bay. The office light flickered. Somewhere down the hall Beto swore at a drawer that had stuck again.

Mina stared at the repair estimate still under her hand. Twenty-seven thousand four hundred. More than four times what she owed the Gulf Wildlife Board and somehow less shameful because it belonged to wood and pipes instead of a body carrying an old disgrace.

"What do you want from me," she asked.

Imogen considered her long enough for the generator to miss once and catch again.

"Today?" she said. "I want you to understand scale. Tomas's suspension is not unfortunate color around your story. It is economic injury. Rhea's reluctance is not emotional complexity for your convenience. It is housing and marriage and professional legitimacy. This camp's interest in the eel is not greed in the abstract. It is the knowledge that wonder can sometimes be traded for repairs, while maintenance, however moral, cannot."

She sat again and folded her hands atop the ledger. "I am not telling you to stop caring about the animal. I am telling you the cost is no longer hiding in flirtation and late-night glamour. It is on paper now."

Mina let that sit. The fan moved hot air over the back of her neck. On the corkboard beside the desk hung next week's tide table, a donor watercolor of a spoonbill chick, and a handwritten reminder to move the good binoculars before board weekend. The whole camp sat there in miniature, beauty thumbtacked over procedure over need.

"If the eel paid for the pilings," Mina said slowly, "would you take that deal."

Imogen's face altered with the weariness of being asked a moral question that money had already made practical.

"Depends what else the deal took," she said. "If it brings regulators who shut us for the season, no. If it turns the channels into a harvest site, no. If it buys time without selling the animal to men who think naming a thing is ownership, maybe. I do not have the luxury of pretending purity keeps roofs on."

Mina thought of the annex's concrete throat beneath the mangroves, of the BrightCurrent signage half swallowed by rot. She had been treating the creek like something accessible only by boat, by fuel, by Tomas's county magic. The tide table on the wall suggested otherwise in its own indifferent way. Low tide would hit at 3:17 a.m. A mud route might be possible if the rain stayed north.

Imogen was still speaking. "I realize there is a chance you hear all of this as emotional blackmail."

"No."

"Good. I do not have the energy for elegant manipulation today."

That almost made Mina smile. Almost.

"What happens if Tomas can't get back on," she asked.

Imogen's eyes moved briefly to the payroll sheet, then to the ceiling stain, as if the answer might be written in either place. "Then he finds day work where he can, the district bleeds him by process, Rhea carries more of the visible income than is fair, and everybody's resentments become more expensive. The camp loses a useful ally. The county keeps functioning badly. The mosquitoes do not care. That is usually how these stories go."

The bluntness relieved Mina more than comfort would have. There was no sermon, just consequence.

From the kitchen came the first clatter of dinner service truly beginning, pans set down hard, the sound of Beto laughing too brightly at something somebody richer than him had said.

Mina rose.

"I should help."

"Yes," Imogen said. Then, as Mina reached the door: "For what it is worth, I do not think you were wrong to want the thing. I think you were wrong to imagine wanting it exempted you from accounting."

Mina looked back.

Imogen had already put her glasses lower on her nose and returned to the ledger, but some older, rawer self still showed through the management. Maybe it was field anger. Maybe it was grief in an administrative blouse.

"Neither did I," Mina said. "Not really."

"No," Imogen said without looking up. "You simply kept hoping the bill would arrive later."

Outside the office the corridor felt almost cool by comparison, though the storm heat had thickened and the screens were ticking with the first hard scouts of rain. Beto crossed from kitchen to deck with the wineglasses on his tray and gave Mina a quick look that asked three things at once: Are my hours gone, is the house sinking, do adults ever tell the truth before they must. She had no answer fit to hand him in passing.

She went instead to the corkboard by the office door and looked again at the tide sheet. Low tide would hit at 3:17 a.m. The route through the mangrove roots would be mud, cuts, mosquitoes, and probably chest-deep water in one section if the front broke wrong. There would be no engine, no county key, no overtime burning in somebody else's paycheck. There would be only the old BrightCurrent approach on foot if she had the nerve to take her own weight there.

Behind her the camp kept performing solvency as glass settled onto trays, citronella wicks caught, and a guest called the storm smell romantic. The whole place balanced on invoices and posture.

Mina put two fingers on the tide table until the paper dented under her skin, then let go.

If she went again, it would be through mud when the tide let go of the roots. Nobody else got to pay for the motor.


# Chapter 12

The 3:17 low had come and gone in darkness. Mina had let it. By early afternoon the daylight low tide gave the route back a little at a time, as if the mangroves were reconsidering a bad decision. First the blackwater slipped off the prop roots and left them shining to their elbows. Then the surface broke into tannin puddles and narrow runs that showed every trapped mullet and drowned leaf. The mud path under the camp was visible in sections now, a dark braid through the roots toward the old concrete mouth of the BrightCurrent intake. Mina stood on the screened walkway outside the kitchen with the taste of bad coffee gone metallic in her mouth and watched the path appear.

She had spent the morning pretending to help Beto inventory lantern batteries and cracked dock lines, his burn-marked wrist flashing every time he turned the clipboard over to study the anatomy terms he kept copying there for trade-school prep. Imogen took a donor call in the office with one silver hairpin already working loose from her bun and spoke in that careful bright voice she used when money was somewhere on the line. Tomas, who usually came up by noon smelling of two-stroke and mangrove mud, had not come up from the county dock. Suspended men did not have much reason to visit other people who had helped suspend them. Rhea had gone to the bat lab with her braid still damp at the nape from washing guano out of it and shut the door with the soft finality that meant work, or distance, or both. The camp creaked in the heat. Screens tapped. Somewhere under the stilts a spoonbill chick made the rusty hinge noise they made when hungry enough to sound furious.

If she told Imogen where she was going, the annex would become an asset before sunset. Imogen would not even have to lie to herself. She would say evidence, chain of custody, institutional responsibility, and under all of it Mina would hear twenty-seven thousand dollars in repairs, leaking guest rooms, and the electric bill the camp was always somehow one week away from not paying. If she told Tomas, he would hear one more thing she needed after his pay and pride had already gone under for her. If she told Rhea, Rhea would ask the exact question Mina least wanted to answer before she had anything in her hands: what outcome are you going down there to serve?

So she said nothing. She took a headlamp from the utility shelf, a pair of nitrile gloves from the bat lab cabinet where Rhea kept them by the alcohol wipes, a screwdriver, her phone wrapped in a freezer bag, and a roll-top dry sack that still smelled faintly of diesel from the months when Tomas had shoved it at her before night runs. She added two gallon zipper bags and her field notebook. The gear looked absurdly small for the amount of history she was walking toward.

The path off the boardwalk started in shell grit and ended in suction. Mud took her boots to the ankle on the second step and to the calf on the fifth. She worked through it with the patience of somebody who had spent enough years in wet ground to know anger only wasted oxygen. Red mangrove roots knitted the air low and close. Oyster shells caught at her pants. Mosquitoes found the strip of skin above her collar and lit there as if she had rung a dinner bell. When she shoved through a stand of black mangrove saplings the leaves painted salt and dust across her forearms. The camp sounds dropped away behind her until she could hear only insects, the occasional plunk of something small abandoning one puddle for another, and her own boots unsticking with a noise too intimate to ignore.

She had come this way once with Rhea, at night and with enough wanting in the air to make the tunnel feel charged instead of condemned. Then the old BrightCurrent sign had risen out of the dark like a threat she had already deserved. Now, in dead low tide and flat daylight, the whole route looked less haunted than administrative, like a forgotten service access and a piece of infrastructure the wetland had spent years trying to digest but had not yet managed to soften into innocence.

That was worse.

The intake mouth sat where the roots thinned and the mud steepened, a concrete spillway half buried in mangrove litter. The old sign above it was almost unreadable under mildew and salt bloom, but the corporate font survived. BrightCurrent Aquatic Systems. Annex Intake. Authorized Personnel Only. Somebody had once believed the warning would last longer than the company.

Mina stood there long enough to listen. There were no voices, no engine from the county channel, and no donor laughter leaking over the boardwalk. There was only a drop somewhere inside, steady enough to count against, and the dry rasp of crabs moving under debris.

She switched on the headlamp even though daylight still reached the first yards. The beam turned the damp concrete white and mean. Rusted emergency-light housings jutted from the wall. Cable straps hung empty where conduit had been torn free years ago. A stain on one side marked the highest flood line, chest high on her and ridged with old silt. She had the stupid thought that the place had kept that mark for her, the way some people kept notches in a doorframe for a child's growth.

The route narrowed fast. She had to turn sideways past a buckled section where the wall had shifted and peeled old paint into curls. Water stood in the floor channel no higher than her boot soles, thick with mosquito larvae and floating gray skin from rotted insulation. The air changed after ten steps. Outside had been heat with movement in it, swamp breath and wind slipping past leaves. Inside was stale and fixed, like the place had been holding one wet exhale since the storm and would never bother taking another.

Her body knew the smell before her mind named it. It was brackish water, mold, battery acid gone sweet, dead filters, and a faint medicinal note under everything, as if disinfectant had lost a long fight and never been removed.

She did not belong to the annex in any official way. That was one of the lies she had built her ruin around. She had been guide-adjacent, contract labor adjacent, the pretty bilingual field interpreter they put in front of visitors when BrightCurrent wanted mangroves translated into grant language and donor awe. She had worn pressed shirts with the company logo for weekends at a time and then gone back to hauling bait coolers and wiping down skiffs for people who made three times what she did to talk about ecosystem futures in air-conditioning.

But she knew this architecture. She did not know this exact tunnel, maybe, or every branch of it, but she knew the grammar of it. Lab annexes loved washable floors and corners too sharp to trust. They loved door windows wired with mesh, yellowing safety placards, and sinks mounted at heights meant for compliance more than comfort. They loved making working water look cleaner than water ever was. Jonah had loved the backstage feel of places like this, the way a badge and a grin could make a laborer believe she had been invited into importance.

She reached the first steel door and found it half latched, swollen in its frame. The badge reader beside it was a dead black oval furred with salt. She put the screwdriver against the jamb and leaned until metal shrieked and the seal gave. The sound went down the corridor in both directions. She froze and listened again.

Nothing came back except the delayed drip and the soft scratch of something small in the wall.

The room beyond had once been a receiving vestibule. A desk still crouched against the wall under a layer of collapsed ceiling tile. A whiteboard leaned facedown in silt. Plastic bins lay cracked open, their contents fused into unidentifiable knots: visitor booties, cable ties, laminated placards, a handful of dead markers. The waterline here had reached the top drawer of the desk and spared the upper cabinets. That spared section felt deliberate, obscene in its efficiency, as if the flood had been careful to ruin the things easiest to ruin and leave the things capable of accusing.

She started with the cabinets. Most held only warped binders and maintenance manuals puffed thick with moisture. She flipped through one until the pages tore under her glove and released a smell like wet cardboard left under a porch for a year. Another contained safety goggles, all clouded. Another had a row of empty lanyard hooks and a plastic bin labeled TEMP BADGES in black marker. The bin had split. Its contents were gone.

She set it down too hard. The crack of plastic against metal made her flinch.

The annex was bigger than the tunnel suggested. A corridor ran left toward what had once been wet rooms and right toward offices. On the wet side she found cylindrical tank housings with their fiberglass skins split and peeled back, manifolds furred with salt crystals, lengths of hose coiled like pale snakes in the corners. Someone had stenciled numbers above the tanks. 1A through 3F. The floor drains were wide. Wider than ornamental fish needed. Wider than anything a donor tour had ever been told about.

In one tank a rind of dried scale shimmered under the headlamp, not blue, not magical, just fish matter flattened by time into mica. On the concrete beside it lay a tangle of mesh sleeves and a length of restraint netting stiff enough to stand. She crouched and touched the net with the back of a gloved finger. It had the rasp of old teeth.

This was not a miracle, she thought. It was husbandry, containment, and failure.

The sounds in here were wrong for abandonment. Rooms with this much plumbing were supposed to mutter. Pumps, trickles, fans, the busy courtesy noise of systems keeping life organized. The annex answered her with absence. That made every small sound louder. Her sleeve against the wall. The little crackle when she flexed a dry patch of mold. Her own breathing trapped and returned by the corridor.

She went to the offices because paper kept better on the dry side and because she did not want, not yet, to stand any longer in front of the tanks and imagine bodies turning there in tight, fluorescent circles while men with degrees called them units.

The first office held a collapsed file cabinet and a bulletin board quilted with old pushpins. The second still had a desk chair on its side and two metal shelves bolted high on the wall. One shelf had slumped under a pile of forms, but the top shelf held a black zip case streaked with mildew and a spiral notebook too thick to be ordinary office inventory. Mina dragged a chair under it, climbed up, and almost lost her footing when the seat shifted in the silt.

The zip case came down in her hands with a weight that made her stop. Tools were usually heavier than paperwork. She set it on the desk and unzipped it carefully.

Inside was a hardbound maintenance log wrapped in a clear contractor bag and two thinner folders paper-clipped together. Somebody had tried, at the last minute or in some earlier cleanup, to keep these dry. The contractor bag had done its work badly but not uselessly. The cover of the logbook was swollen and salt-stained, yet when she opened it the inner pages were only feathered at the edges. Enough ink remained.

She sat on the desk because the chair legs had rusted through, balanced the book on her thighs, and aimed the headlamp down.

The first pages were routine. They logged pump pressure checks, filter changes, salinity readings, and valve repairs. Whoever kept the book printed in compact all-caps that leaned slightly right and pressed hard enough to leave an emboss on the pages beneath. He had a habit of circling numbers twice when they mattered. She could feel her own pulse wanting plot, confession, some single spectacular sentence that would make the whole question easy. The book refused. It gave her maintenance.

July. Intake screen fouled with mangrove litter.

July. Lot 2C feed reduction due to crowding.

August. Generator switchover lag during noon storm.

August. Wet Room 3 dissolved oxygen alarms intermittent. Sensor replaced. Monitor sentinel line overnight.

The words sentinel line snagged in her head.

She kept going.

The entries thickened as the storm approached, handwriting driven harder, margins full of arrows and addenda. Transport was delayed. The mainland route was uncertain. Backup batteries had been pulled from the admin wing. Wet-side staff had been reduced. Someone had switched pens twice and then finished a page in carpenter's pencil when the ink failed. A faint brown thumbprint stained one corner. It could have been grease, or dried blood, or enough years of Florida humidity to make the distinction academic.

By the time she reached the page marked with the evacuation date, her shirt had stuck to the small of her back. Sweat slid down between her shoulder blades and pooled against her waistband. She did not wipe it. She turned the page with both hands, careful, as if roughness could change what the words had already done.

Mandatory evacuation in effect 1400. Nonessential personnel offsite.

Mainland transfer canceled after road closure.

Sentinel stock exceeds portable live-haul capacity.

Counsel advises no mortality event in place if power loss likely.

The next line was written darker than the rest, the letters cut into the paper.

Authorize emergency drawdown to receiving channel via east sluice if generator fails.

She read it again because her mind tried to slide past the grammar. It said receiving channel, not creek or marsh or outside. A receiving channel was something infrastructure had permission to use. It sounded pre-disposed to take whatever a building could no longer keep. Then she looked back through the office door toward the wet room and the euphemism broke apart. Tank 3A's rind of scales was no longer abstract residue. It was what the walls kept after animals had come up beating for air and rasped themselves raw against fiberglass. The restraint netting on the floor was not storage. It was what somebody had grabbed when the water came over the tank lips and living weight started knotting over the concrete.

The following page had water damage across the top third and a streak of black where ink had run, but the lower half remained legible.

1840 - Generator transfer unstable. Tank 3A oxygen crash.

1855 - Wet-side overflow began during surge pulse.

1905 - Opened east sluice and bypass under storm-loss protocol. Live material entered outgoing flow.

Counts impossible.

Bagged remaining mortality from 1A and 2B.

Report external as flood breach / research fish loss.

Do not specify luminescent line in county incident form.

She could read the sequence in the room now. Oxygen crash first: mouths breaking the surface, bodies climbing over one another in a tank gone suddenly useless. Overflow next: storm water, tank water, and animal panic slapping over the floor toward those overbuilt drains. Then the sluice. Somebody had opened steel and let the current do the work, sweeping the ones still strong enough to thrash and the half-stunned ones alike toward the outgoing channel. Counts impossible because no one was stopping to peel them off the grates one by one. Bagged remaining mortality meant the ones that did not wash clear, the ones left heaped against 1A and 2B or tangled in net so stiff now it could stand by itself.

She sat very still, the book open across her knees, while a mosquito whined inside the room and failed to find a way out. The sound drilled at her ear until she slapped it without thinking and left a smear of blood and insect on her glove.

Live material entered outgoing flow.

The phrase had none of the decency of even a cowardly lie. It was worse. It was true in the language of people who believed truth could be made harmless by being stripped to systems. They had opened a sluice to an outgoing flow and called living bodies material, as if the animals had become manageable the second nobody called them animals, as if the creek had been part of the cleanup crew all along.

She looked up from the page toward the dark wet corridor and saw the tank numbers again, 1A through 3F, enough holding capacity for more bodies than she had let herself picture. Her old story with Jonah, the cooler against her thigh, the single slippery weight she had helped move because she had been stupid and hungry and wanted backstage access badly enough to confuse it with love, had always felt singular. It was damning, but it had felt singular: one theft, one lie that widened until it swallowed her name.

Here the lie had scale, a line item widening into a population.

The creek had not simply received an escapee. It had been used as disposal, weather cover, off-book habitat, a place to empty corporate risk when the pumps failed and the roads closed and nobody wanted the smell of dead engineered animals in a lab by morning.

She turned to the clipped folders.

The first held emergency cleanup notes on BrightCurrent letterhead. Most of the top pages had fused together, but the back half separated with care. There were checklists for bleach concentration, mold remediation, disposal bins, equipment losses. One page listed tank lots with boxes beside them: recovered, mortalities, presumed environmental loss. Beside the lot numbers corresponding to the wet room she'd just walked through, the handwriting changed. Not neat all-caps now but a tired cursive slant, written by someone who had stopped pretending the paperwork would return them to order.

3A - presumed environmental loss

3B - presumed environmental loss

3C - presumed environmental loss

3D - presumed environmental loss

3E - presumed environmental loss

3F - presumed environmental loss

Forty-seven total, maybe more. Tally uncertain after surge.

Forward revised language to legal before any county contact.

No public-facing animal imagery from wet side.

Mina let out a breath she had not noticed holding. It came back at her off the walls, thin and ugly.

The tally was forty-seven.

Forty-seven engineered eels or more, spread across six lots and a storm ledger. Some had rasped themselves raw in bad water. Some had gone through the east sluice with the floor runoff. Some had ended in plastic bags from 1A and 2B once the room stopped thrashing.

And still the relief that tried to rise in her chest was real. That disgusted her almost as much as the paperwork did.

Because for one split second, before she got hold of herself, the logbook seemed to offer what she had wanted from the eel since the first rumor. Proof that the corruption predated her. Proof that the creek's blue secret had not begun in her two hands and one cooler. Proof that she could point at a corporation large enough to deserve the blast radius and say there, there was the original sin, now maybe mine can shrink back to human size.

Then she read the notes again, and the relief turned rancid in her mouth.

Her stomach clenched so hard she bent over the desk. The scale of it only widened the stain. The creek had taken the company's dumping and one woman in heat for access in the same mouthful. It kept both. Whatever rolled blue under the spoonbills now carried discharge water, private lust, a wet room full of animals beating the surface, and the hands that finally quit holding them. If the thing still deserved the word miracle, it had survived bad oxygen, concrete, legal language, and those hands at all.

She forced herself to photograph every legible page. She pulled the phone from the freezer bag, turned the flash off, shifted the headlamp higher, and took one image after another, then the cleanup notes, then the cover with its title stamped in silver under salt bloom: ANNEX B WET SYSTEMS MAINTENANCE LOG. Her hands stayed steady because the alternative was losing the pages to clumsiness and having to explain that later to everyone. She slid the logbook into one zipper bag, the folders into another, and only then looked up.

The office had grown dimmer. Not because of light outside. Because she had been in here long enough for the place to close around her. Heat sat in the air without moving. The odor of old water had warmed under her lamp into something meatier, more intimate. She needed to leave.

She bent to check the lower drawer of the desk before she did, partly from instinct, partly because people who tried to save documents usually shoved other things in with them. The drawer was stuck. She knelt in the silt, set the screwdriver into the seam, and pried until rust popped and the metal screamed. The drawer came open six inches and dumped a clot of warped paper, a dead roach the size of a thumb, and three laminated cards into the mud.

One card landed faceup.

She knew the shape before she knew the face.

The laminate had bubbled at the corners. The clip was orange with rust. A strip across the top carried the faded BrightCurrent logo and beneath it, still legible under mildew, her own name.

MINA CORDERO

VISITOR INTERPRETER

ESCORT ACCESS - ANNEX B

The photo was not kind in the way young photos often were. Her hair had been down because Jonah had said donors trusted women more when they looked windblown instead of severe. Her smile was too ready. She looked like someone pleased to be near the thing that would later ruin her, someone who still believed proximity to money and research and men with security clearances meant she had stepped into a larger life instead of volunteering to decorate it.

For a moment she forgot the heat. Forgot the mud soaking through one knee of her pants. Forgot the logbook beside her.

That badge had hung from a blue lanyard that stuck to the back of her neck in the annex humidity. She remembered the exact drag of it now. She remembered Jonah hooking a finger under the strap and snapping it lightly against her skin while he talked through tank lighting and said she had a better eye than half the technicians in there. She remembered how cheap the compliment had been and how fully she had eaten it. She had been old enough to know better and broke enough to keep pretending knowledge changed hunger.

She had wanted in. That was the part shame kept trying to rewrite into victimhood or manipulation alone. Jonah had used her. Of course he had. He had liked women who mistook complicity for intimacy and he had been practiced enough to make backstage access feel erotic, almost noble. But she had wanted the door to open for her. She had wanted the badge on her chest and the little reader light turning green. She had wanted to stop being the girl outside the lab translating bird calls for rich amateurs while men inside talked patents and environmental futures over trays of catered shrimp.

The memory came back in pieces sharp enough to cut.

Storm watch was already posted. Staff were packing hard cases. Somebody was swearing over transport bins in the corridor. Jonah was sweat-slick and calm in that curated way of his, as if weather only proved he was locally born enough to deserve trust. The cooler was white and scuffed against his shin. Her badge was clipped crooked because he had put it on her himself, grinning, telling her this was what serious work looked like when the brochure cameras went home. She had carried the cooler because carrying it made her feel chosen. She had not felt innocent. She had felt chosen.

She turned the badge over. On the back, under the emergency contact number and the old liability disclaimer, somebody had written in Sharpie: RETURN FRONT DESK. The words had bled at the edges from damp. She imagined herself after the theft, after the rush and the fear and the sex and the way Jonah had made the whole thing sound temporary, tossing the badge into a pocket, then losing it here in the scramble when the storm shifted the timetable and everybody started moving too fast.

Or maybe she had dropped it earlier. Maybe it had slipped behind the desk while she leaned there with Jonah's hand between her shoulder blades, listening to him talk like the line between field and lab was a joke for smarter people to step across. The precise moment no longer mattered. The object did.

Her name was in the annex.

It was not only in the gossip version, or in the disciplinary file and the old photo caption and the ugly private story she had finally told Rhea and Tomas after too much tenderness made silence rotten. Her name was here, in the drawer, in the room with the tank numbers and the storm logs and the east sluice.

She sat back on her heels and laughed once without humor. The sound came out raw enough to startle her. If she had found only the logbook, some corner of her would have tried to use it. It would not have happened publicly, maybe not even consciously, but the temptation would have been there. BrightCurrent did this. BrightCurrent released them. BrightCurrent made the creek answer for its mistakes. All of that was true. All of it remained incomplete.

The badge made sure of that.

She was still trying to understand the exact composition of the damage when the corridor answered with a small new sound: water ticking against metal where there had been only drip before. The tide was turning. The outgoing trickle in the floor channel had begun to thicken back toward the annex.

She moved quickly then, because drowning in a condemned biotech tunnel would have been too stupid even for her. The badge went into the same zipper bag as the cleanup notes. She slung the dry sack crosswise and stood, one knee stiff with cold mud. Before leaving, she swept the headlamp once around the office to make sure she had not dropped the phone or the screwdriver.

The beam caught a row of framed safety posters on the far wall, all buckled behind cracked glass. BIOSECURITY IS EVERYONE'S JOB, one of them said over a diagram of gloved hands and sealed drains. Another showed evacuation routes in cheerful color blocks. Arrows leading people out, arrows directing materials elsewhere. Even now the posters sounded managerial, offended by circumstance.

She shut off the headlamp for a second just to see what the annex did without her beam.

Darkness took the room instantly and completely. No emergency strip came alive. No ghostly spill from another wing. Nothing luminous stirred in the tanks. She heard only the returning water in the channel and the tiny busy clicks of crabs reclaiming corners. The eel was not here to explain itself to her. The annex had already done what it needed to do.

When she switched the light back on and went into the corridor, the wet side looked changed only because she had changed. The wide floor drains no longer seemed like oversized design. They were the route. The sluice gate at the far end, half buried in silt and barnacles, was an instrument somebody had opened with a hand on purpose. They might have done it reluctantly, or under orders, or while telling themselves the storm had made the choice first. Purpose was still purpose.

She paused beside the tanks one last time, not out of sentiment but out of obligation to look.

Tank 3A still had a strip of measurement tape on its side, black marks faded but visible. Tank 3F had a maintenance tag hanging from a valve wheel, the plastic yellowed to the color of old teeth. A scoop net lay collapsed in the corner, its handle snapped. Here was the beauty's infrastructure. Here were the banal tools that had held blue bodies in circles until they stopped holding.

Outside the office the incoming water had reached the soles of her boots and was carrying fresh threads of leaf litter inward. She followed the corridor back to the intake tunnel and this time did not need to force herself not to hurry. Concrete pressed close on either side. The air seemed more used now, disturbed by her breathing and the tide's return. At the swollen door she had to shoulder it twice before it opened enough to squeeze through. A smear of rust marked her sleeve.

Then she was in the tunnel proper again, moving toward daylight while the floor channel filled around her ankles. The sound changed first. Insects returned, then the far racket of the rookery, then the structural complaints of the camp above the mangroves, all the built noises that had seemed irritating that morning and now struck her as proof of a world still willing to say what it was.

The mud outside had taken on a skin of reflected sky. She killed the headlamp and blinked against the brightness. Afternoon sun hit the blackwater pools between roots and turned them mirror-hard. For one ugly second she expected blue under the surface, some staged confirmation, some animal rising on cue to join the paperwork and complete the lesson.

Nothing moved but a stranded silverside and the minute busy mouths of fiddler crabs at the rim of a puddle.

She climbed the root path slower than she had come down because the dry sack slapped against her ribs and every step wanted careful placement. Halfway up she looked back. From this angle the intake vanished almost completely under shadow and mangrove tangle. Hidden by design when the tide was right, hidden by landscape when it was not. Easy to miss by water. Easy to miss on foot if you had not already been taught where to look.

That, too, had felt erotic once. Secret routes and locked places had seemed like intimacy instead of what they were, which was just another labor a man older than her wanted done cheaply.

By the time she reached the shell path under the camp, her shirt was pasted to her skin and the gloves were full of sweat. She peeled them off finger by finger and stuffed them into an outer pocket of the sack. The boardwalk above her threw striped shade across the mud. Somebody in the kitchen dropped a pan. Beto laughed at something, then coughed hard enough to bend double, the sound carrying through the slats. Ordinary camp life was still going above her in all its frayed particulars: lantern batteries, donor calls, audit fallout, Rhea in the bat lab with her neat hard questions, Tomas somewhere trying to imagine nine thousand dollars of debt without a county paycheck under it, and Imogen looking at a half-corrupted hydrophone file and seeing possible rescue.

Mina put one hand on the bottom rung of the ladder to the boardwalk and stopped there.

She had wanted the eel to be clean in at least one direction. Maybe not innocent, because innocence had been gone for chapters now and she was not stupid enough to ask the creek for it. But she had wanted it to be clean as evidence and clean as correction, a thing that could be real and beautiful and wild enough, or at least not provably hers enough, to lever her life a few inches away from disgrace. She had wanted discovery to do what confession never did: rearrange blame into something survivable.

The logbook killed that fantasy on the page where live material entered outgoing flow.

The badge killed the rest.

She kept her hand on the rung until the aluminum bit a ladder pattern into her palm. Then she climbed. The dry sack dragged at her shoulder, and inside it the recovered badge kept tapping the logbook, plastic on paper, her name keeping time against BrightCurrent's storm ledger. She crossed the boardwalk without looking toward Imogen's office and headed for the bat lab.


# Chapter 13

Mina came off the boardwalk and into the bat lab with the dry sack dragging at her shoulder and the recovered badge tapping softly against the logbook inside. She scrubbed her hands three times at the sink and the annex was still in the lines of her palms.

The sink in the survey room ran sulfur-warm from the rooftop tank, never cold enough to matter. It had taken the top layer off the mud and none of the smell under it. BrightCurrent had smelled different when it was alive and funded. Filtered air, citrus cleaner, the fake-quiet of expensive corridors. The annex under the mangroves smelled like wet concrete, rat droppings, old batteries, and a long argument with the tide. That smell had followed her back under her shirt. It sat in her bra, in the waistband of her shorts, in the damp strap of her watch. Even here, with guano and rubbing alcohol and battery heat pushing from the shelves, she could pick it out.

She had wrapped the logbook in two contractor bags and laid it on the metal table anyway. Beside it sat her old visitor badge, the laminate clouded from water and time. Her twenty-something face grinned out of it with a level of confidence that made her want to put her thumb through the picture. The lanyard clip had rusted into a hooked brown knot.

Rhea had arrived first, barefoot in rubber clogs, braid still wet at the nape from the quick shower she took after morning work. She had started by saying nothing. That was worse than alarm from almost anybody else. She only put on nitrile gloves, tugged each finger snug with her teeth, and stood over the bagged book while Mina talked her through the route in: low tide, root tangle, hatch half jammed with shell grit, interior steps slick enough to kill a fool. Now she was reading one page for the third time, pencil tucked behind her ear, mouth set flat. When she concentrated she lined objects into exact relation without seeming to notice. She had already squared the badge to the edge of the table and turned the logbook so the handwriting ran parallel to the metal seam.

Tomas leaned against the doorjamb, not entering far enough to count as settled. He had a county envelope folded into his shirt pocket so many times the paper had gone soft and furry at the creases. Suspension paperwork, probably, or another request that he explain himself in complete sentences to somebody who had never had to lift a fuel can in August. Mosquitoes found the backs of his calves where the work socks stopped. He killed one with his thumbnail and looked at the blood on it before wiping it down the side of his shorts.

"You couldn't wait an hour?" he said.

Mina knew what he meant. Couldn't wait until he had finished replacing the busted hinge on the skiff locker. Couldn't wait until the guest lunch cleared. Couldn't wait until there was a gentler way to say she had gone alone into the place he had lost work helping her reach.

"No," she said. "I couldn't."

He gave her a single nod that held no agreement in it.

Then Imogen came in with her reading glasses hanging on a sun-faded cord against her field shirt and a chipped camp mug in her hand. Some old sanctuary logo had worn down on the side until all that remained was a crane leg and part of a green marsh. She set the mug down near the bat-detector charger, saw the contractor bags, saw Mina's face, and stopped being a director in front of staff and became something narrower and more dangerous.

"If this is another half-formed emergency," she said, "I would prefer one that waits until I finish feeding paying people."

"It's not half-formed," Mina said.

Imogen looked at the badge first. Mina watched recognition move through her, not of the photo exactly but of the institution. BrightCurrent had touched every field station and permit office on this coast for years. Gift money, pilot partnerships, habitat language polished until it could slide past anything.

"Where did you get that?"

"Out of the annex."

Imogen's gaze came up sharply. "You went in there alone."

"Yes."

"At low tide?" Rhea asked, still looking at the page.

"Low enough. I had maybe forty minutes before the route turned stupid."

Rhea nodded once. "And you found records."

"Maintenance book. Emergency notes. Intake checks. Some of it is rotted, but enough isn't."

Tomas pushed off the jamb at last and came to the table. He smelled like old gasoline and hot metal and the cheap soap from the county dock bathroom. For a stupid second Mina wanted to lean into it the way she had on the walk back from the mosquito launch two weeks ago, when he had let her steal shade from his shoulder and called her a menace for sweating through his shirt. The wanting did not vanish because the room had gone bad. That was part of the problem. The body kept voting after the rest of it had turned.

He looked down at the badge. "Jesus."

"Yeah," Mina said.

The photo had caught her before the fines, before the public hearing, before enough bad sleep could roughen a face that fast. Hair shorter, mouth cocked like the world had been designed with her in mind and she had almost forgiven it for the trouble. She remembered the day the picture was taken. Jonah behind the camera telling her to look less like she wanted to steal the building.

Rhea flipped a page with gloved fingers. Water had swelled the paper and then dried it crooked. Whole corners had melted. But the center lines held: dates, initials, tank numbers, salinity columns, one recurring notation in a hand so neat it felt insulting. Mina had copied it into her own notebook on the walk back because she had not trusted herself to remember without making it smaller.

"'Expedited release pursuant to containment compromise,'" Rhea read. Her voice stayed level. That made the phrase uglier. "'Sentinel stock transferred through auxiliary intake under storm directive.'"

No one said anything for a beat.

The bug zapper outside the lab door cracked once. From the main house came a burst of guest laughter, cut short as if somebody remembered where they were supposed to be and took the volume back down.

Imogen took her glasses off the cord and put them on. "How many pages?"

"Forty-two that I could separate without turning them into pulp. There are more fused together."

"Any signatures?"

"Initials. Shift notations. Tank references."

"Dates?"

"Storm week." Mina swallowed. The room tasted metallic, as if the annex rust had climbed into the back of her throat. "Years back. Before the hearing. Before my photo went wide."

Imogen took that in and asked the next question exactly as herself. "Do these pages say release, or do they say loss?"

"They say release." Mina pulled the notebook from her back pocket and opened to the page where she had copied the clearest lines. Her handwriting had gone hard and narrow. "They used softer words around it because that is what paperwork does when it wants not to sound like a dump. But they moved live stock into the system. Not one animal. A line."

Tomas looked from her notes to the badge and then to her face. "So it wasn't just the cooler."

"No." She could hear how hungry that answer sounded. She hated it. "No. It wasn't."

There had been a version of the truth she had been stupid enough to want on the walk back. Not innocence; she had not been that stupid. She had wanted scale, something big enough to make her own theft feel like a splinter instead of the whole wound. The annex had killed that fantasy under a dead emergency light.

Rhea read farther down the page. "Auxiliary intake. That means the tunnel route was operational."

"At least during evacuation. Maybe longer."

"And if this stock survived." Imogen tapped the contractor bag once, a tiny contained movement, as if she would rather strike the table harder and had decided not to spend the energy where it could not invoice back. "Then the camp is not sitting on a miracle sighting. It is sitting on an abandoned biotech release under protected habitat."

"It's both," Mina said.

"For the purposes of what happens next, it is whatever people with lawyers can be forced to hear."

Mina felt her molars touch. Imogen was already feeding the mess into office language, turning blood into a briefing packet before it dried.

Tomas barked a laugh with no humor in it. "Of course."

Imogen did not look at him. "If these pages hold up, the camp becomes the reporting site. The witness site. Not the origin point."

"Publicity," Mina said.

"Survival," Imogen said. She finally turned. "Do not say that word to me as if it only means donor cocktails and brochure copy. If BrightCurrent put engineered stock into this system and the evidence surfaced here, then there are mitigation funds, legal obligations, habitat review, a reason the board cannot tell me to patch the pilings with borrowed screws for one more season and be grateful."

Her tone stayed formal for three beats and then the facade split, just slightly.

"I have repair invoices stacked in my office like kindling," she said. "If this place goes under, the birds do not get a moral victory. They get one more rotten platform in a creek full of them."

Mina knew it was true. That was the annoying thing about Imogen. Her opportunism nearly always had a real bill behind it.

Tomas said, "You already started spending it in your head."

Imogen took the hit without blinking. "The minute I understood what it might mean, yes."

"Jesus Christ."

"Do not perform surprise for me, Tomas. You knew exactly what the sighting could do for the camp."

"I knew what it could do for her." He jerked his chin at Mina. "And maybe for us if it turned real enough."

"It is real enough," Mina said.

"That isn't the fucking point."

The insect hum outside seemed to climb with his voice. Rain had not arrived yet, but the light through the lab's slatted windows had gone yellow and strained. Storm front close. The whole camp always heard weather before it got wet. Screens started whispering differently. Pilings took on a deeper creak. Spoonbills in the rookery lifted and settled in irritated bursts.

Rhea set the page down. "Say the point, then."

Tomas looked at her. Something in his face changed there, not softening exactly, but widening long enough for the hurt to show in plain daylight before he pressed it thin again.

"The point," he said, "is I lost my route because she needed nighttime and gas and somebody who could get her in without a permit. The point is I let county fuel walk and I let county gear walk and I sat there getting chewed by a supervisor half my age while he asked whether I'd been selling gas to friends or just too stupid to count." He looked back at Mina. "And now I get to hear it was all sitting there anyway. Dumped years ago. Whole secret already in the water, and I still get to be the asshole who burned his job down ferrying you to it."

Mina felt the metal lip of the table edge against the back of her hand and held there because if she moved she would start defending herself too fast.

"I didn't know about the release."

"No," he said. "You just knew enough other things."

That landed harder because he had left the sentence bare. She had nowhere soft to set it down.

Imogen said, "You also knew what you were doing, Tomas."

He rounded on her then. "Don't. Not from you."

"From me exactly. You are not some boy dragged into a ditch by witchcraft. You stole fuel because you wanted to. You took her out because you wanted to. You kept taking her out because the eel made you feel less like a county janitor with wings on his boat."

The words were cruel enough that Mina almost admired them. Tomas took the county envelope from his pocket, ran a thumb down each softened crease until the paper lay flat, and tucked it back with careful force. He was meanest when he got careful.

"And you looked away because the county route helped this place," he said.

Imogen's mouth tightened. "Yes."

"You took rides. You took info. You took the fact that I could get places after dark and pretended it was just neighborly."

"I took help where I could get it because this camp is not funded by purity."

"That's the prettier label."

"For what?"

"Everybody gets to call what they take by a prettier name."

Nobody in the room could really argue with that. Mina kept looking at the badge and the contractor bag sweating on the steel table, cheap laminate beside black plastic, two kinds of cover over the same mess.

Rhea pulled off one glove and laid her fingertips on the page as if testing for heat. "This is not a contest for cleanest motives."

"No?" Tomas said. "Could've fooled me."

"I said what I said."

He looked away from her, out toward the mangroves. That hurt, visibly. Rhea's face changed less than most people's, but Mina saw the small pause before she continued. Saw the care she took with each word when she knew anything sloppy would be used as cover by somebody else.

"The pages matter," Rhea said. "Because the second anybody says miracle in public, strangers start asking where to stand. That means redactions, locked storage, and no one freelancing this into a camp-rescue story."

Imogen gave a brief nod, but Rhea was not done.

"Tomas is right about something too. The cost didn't land evenly."

That brought Tomas's gaze back.

"You lost pay," Rhea said. "You lost county access. You got to be publicly stupid in front of men who would've liked that even without Mina. That's real. But if either of you is about to turn yourself into the only body that got used here, stop."

His jaw flexed once.

"And if you're about to tell yourself a story," she said to Mina, "where annex records make you a messenger instead of a participant, don't do that either."

Mina almost laughed because the skin at the back of her neck tightened. This was what she trusted in Rhea: the hard, unsentimental shove that kept language from wriggling into alibi.

"I know I was in it," Mina said.
