"You keep saying that like it's the end of the sentence."

Imogen crossed her arms. "Would anyone like to propose an alternative to me making copies, calling counsel, and getting in front of this before BrightCurrent's remains do?"

"Yes," Mina said. "Not turning it into a camp resurrection pitch."

"I did not say resurrection."

"You thought it."

"What I thought was that a defunct biotech company poisoning a protected wetland might finally move money in this county in the correct direction."

Tomas laughed again, low and mean. "Toward the fucking gift shop?"

Imogen's eyes flashed. That did it. Professional Imogen stepped aside completely.

"Toward keeping Beto employed and fed," she said. "Toward not failing a hurricane inspection because the east walkway still lists six unsafe boards and a handrail that flexes like boiled pasta. Toward not losing every contract scientist who needs a roof because the generator dies in July. Toward not asking me, again, to be ashamed that I can see numbers while the rest of you are busy bleeding all over abstractions."

It was so specific it shut the room for a second. Mina could picture the unsafe-board list taped inside Imogen's office cabinet. Could picture the yellow invoice folder with the corners rounded from being opened and closed and not paid. Imogen, who could still pin her silver hair up with the same pencil she used to mark bird counts and then spend an hour pretending not to notice that guests were taking extra shrimp from the staff tray because calling it out would make them feel poor in the wrong direction.

Mina said, "I get it."

"No," Imogen said. "You get pieces when they bruise you personally. That is not the same thing."

Fair enough.

Thunder rolled somewhere far enough off to sound expensive.

Tomas dragged a hand over his mouth. "Everybody wants to file it under something that pays."

Rhea's mouth twitched without humor. "Including us. Stop talking like the bed sat outside the rest of this. It didn't."

No one had anywhere to look except the table.

Imogen looked away first, to the windows, because even she had limits. "I am going to pretend I did not hear that sentence."

"You heard it," Rhea said.

"Unfortunately."

Tomas said, "You want it plain? Fine. Plain enough? She came to me because I had the boat and the fuel and the route. That was day one. Then she came to you because you knew the tunnel and the data and because sleeping with one half of a marriage is messier than sleeping with both." He was looking at Mina again, but the collateral hit everybody. "Maybe after that it got real in spots. Maybe not. I don't know what the fuck to call real anymore. I know what started it."

Mina had been bracing against exactly this for weeks, maybe since the second time Tomas pushed her against the aluminum side of the mosquito shed and laughed into her throat when she bit his shoulder to keep quiet. That did not make it easier to hear.

"At first," she said, because anything else would be another lie, "I came to you because you had the route."

The words dropped with a terrible little neatness.

Tomas looked almost relieved for one second. Then the rest of it hit.

"That's what I needed to hear."

"That wasn't all it was."

"No," he said. "Just the first thing."

"Yes."

He nodded twice, slow. "Order matters."

Mina opened her mouth and closed it. Because it did. Because every decent thing that came after had been built on top of a want she could count in fuel and hours and access points. She remembered the first night too clearly all at once: mosquito whine inside her cuffs, his hand flat between her shoulder blades as she climbed into the boat, the blue turn under blackwater, and then later his face above her in the heat with the county key still clipped to his belt, knocking against her thigh every time he moved. She had wanted the key off him almost as badly as she had wanted him. The shame of that was not abstract. It had edges, metal-cold, exactly that shape.

Rhea said, "That is not the whole order."

Tomas did not even glance at her. "It is enough of one."

Imogen took off her glasses and pinched the bridge of her nose. "We are not going to solve ourselves in the bat lab. We need three decisions before someone else stumbles onto the annex or the hydrophone file leaks from a drunk phone call: who holds the original, who holds the site, and who makes the first call."

"The original stays in my cabinet," Rhea said. "Site markers come off every copy."

"Fine. The first call still can't wait."

"No donors. No press. Not before habitat protection," Mina said.

"Donors can wait till morning," Imogen said. "The call cannot."

"Then you say contamination and habitat risk," Rhea said. "Not miracle. Not coordinates."

Imogen gave her a tight, unwilling nod.

Tomas looked at the book again as if he wished it would turn back into simple paper and stop radiating consequence. "And while you all decide who gets the careful version, what exactly am I supposed to do? Sit home and admire procedure? My night hours are gone. The skiff note still clears Friday. The county already thinks I was stealing fuel for a side hustle or a woman or both, and I don't even get the dignity of saying I burned my life down for something wild. It was corporate trash and horny idiots the whole way through."

"Not the whole way," Mina said.

He swung to her so fast the chair legs on the far side of the room rattled from where his knee hit the table.

"Don't give me scraps because you feel guilty." His voice stayed low. That was what made Rhea straighten. "Don't stand there and make me noble after the fact. I wasn't noble. I wanted you. I liked the boat with you in it. I liked you in my house. I liked watching my wife look at you and not look away. I liked all of it. And I still got used."

Mina felt that one all the way down in the places his hands had taught her body to answer before thought got there. Not because it was unfair. Because it wasn't.

"Yes," she said.

He stared at her, waiting for more, and she made herself keep going.

"Yes. I used you. You used me too. Rhea used both of us. I let myself love parts of that because loving them made it easier not to stop. That isn't a defense. It's just the shape."

Rain hit the roof then, sudden and hard, one sheet of water flattening the mosquito sound for a second before the drains took over and the whole building started talking through its pipes. Somebody in the main house let out a startled shout. A spoonbill lifted screaming from the rookery, then another.

Rhea stood very still and then said, "There. That is closer."

Imogen looked between them all, disgusted and impressed in equal parts. "You could have reached closer with your clothes on years ago."

No one answered.

She picked up her mug. "Here is what I need from you. I need copies of every page. I need the badge photographed. I need no one taking this to a donor, a county friend, an ex-lover, or a barstool between now and when we decide whether the first call is legal, regulatory, or press."

"Press?" Mina said.

"Strategic press, if necessary."

"You are unbelievable."

"I am fifty-two, underfunded, and looking at a buried corporate release under my camp," Imogen said. "Believe harder."

Rhea slid the logbook back into the contractor bag and folded the top down once. "Copies, yes. Donor theater, no."

"I said strategic."

"You mean curated."

"I mean alive next season."

Tomas pointed at the floor between them as if he were laying out a rule with chalk. "Then hear me clearly. Nobody uses my skiff, my cans, or anything with my name on it to get back out there."

"I mean done."

Mina looked up. "Tomas."

"No." He took the envelope from his pocket, crumpled it once, smoothed it again with his thumb, then shoved it back. "You want honest? Fine. Get your things out of our room before dark."

The rain hammered harder. For a second the sound was so dense it seemed to hold the whole room inside it.

"Tomas," Rhea said, not pleading, not quite warning.

"I said what I said."

He left without slamming the door. He did not need to. The storm did it for him, shaking the screen hard enough to rattle the frame after he was already out on the walkway.

Imogen watched the door swing and settle. "Well," she said. "There goes diplomacy."

"You were not exactly helping," Mina said.

Imogen gave her a cool look over the rim of the mug. "You did not call me here for help. You called me because you found a bigger version of the fire and wanted witnesses before it changed shape in your own hands."

That was true enough to sting.

Rhea stripped off the second glove and dropped both on the table. "I will photograph the pages."

"In your lab?" Imogen asked.

"Yes."

"Then do it now before the power flickers."

Imogen gathered her glasses, hesitated like she might say something human, then thought better of it and went back into the weather and the paying guests and the camp she was still, absurdly, trying to save. The room felt larger when she left and less breathable.

Rhea kept her eyes on the bagged book. "You did the right thing bringing it back."

Mina almost laughed. "That sounded painful."

"It was precise, not kind."

"Same as always."

Rhea finally looked at her then, and the look was not cold. That would have been easier. It was intimate and exhausted and impossible to stand inside for long.

"Take him seriously," she said. "And don't ask me to convert this into a cleaner story because you're frightened."

"I wasn't going to."

Rhea's expression said she knew better than that. "Photographs first," she said.

Then she picked up the contractor bag and carried it deeper into the lab, away from Mina, as carefully as if it held something alive that might still bite through plastic if handled badly.

* * *

By dark the rain had narrowed from violence to persistence, the kind that seemed built into the air rather than falling through it. Water ran off every roof edge at camp. The screened walkways dripped in straight silver cords. Somewhere under the main house a bucket kept taking overflow from a known leak with a slow, shaming plink. The generator worked harder in wet weather; you could hear the load in it, a lower note under the usual thrum.

Mina stood outside Tomas and Rhea's door with a dry bag in one hand and the annex badge in her pocket because she had not wanted to leave it anywhere else. The plastic edge pressed against her hip through damp shorts every time she breathed. She had spent the last two hours helping Beto move stacked coolers away from the east wall and pretending not to notice that he was trying not to look at her. He had whistled under his breath the whole time, the same two bars from an old norteño song he went to when he was scared and wanted his hands to seem occupied by something ordinary. He had not asked why her face looked flayed open. For nineteen, that counted as grace.

Now the walkway light outside the room threw a bug-flecked cone through the screen and turned the rain beyond it into bent wires. She could smell damp wood, diesel residue dragged in on Tomas's boots, and the faint powder-clean scent of Rhea's alcohol wipes. Her body knew this threshold too well. Nights of stepping over it barefoot, nights of stepping over it sticky and half asleep, nights of hearing Tomas laugh low from inside while Rhea said, from farther back, "If you're coming in, close the damn screen." The memory came with its own muscle, the hand already shaped for the latch.

She knocked anyway.

"Come in," Rhea said.

The room had already been rearranged by grief and practicality.

Not drastically. That would have felt theatrical. The bed was stripped, mattress bare except for the thin camp pad zipped over it. The striped sheet Mina had bought cheap in town and then left here by accident after the third night had been folded into a hard square on the chair. Her toothbrush lay dry on top of it. Tomas's work shirts still hung from the wall hooks. Rhea's recorder case sat open on the desk with batteries lined in a row by charge label, green tape tabs all facing the same direction. A mosquito had died flattened into the wall near the lamp, one dark starburst among many older paint scars.

Rhea stood by the dresser in a clean tank top and field shorts, braid redone tighter than before. She was sorting Mina's things from theirs without speed and without hesitation. Sports bra. Spare shirt. Hair tie. Paperback with the cover bent back where Tomas had dropped it in the rain last week and apologized by drying every page with the hair dryer like it was a wounded bird. The sight of that book on Rhea's palm hit Mina so hard she had to set the dry bag down before her hand started shaking and gave the cheap vinyl away.

"I could have done this," Mina said.

"I know."

"You didn't have to."

"I know that too."

Rain ticked the screen, lighter now, almost conversational. Rhea put the paperback on the folded sheet and finally faced her.

"He went down to the skiff for a while," she said. "He's back. He's in the shower."

Mina nodded. Water ran in the small bathroom behind the half-closed door, loud in the cramped space. Tomas always showered hot enough to make the mirror cry.

"I didn't come to make a scene," Mina said.

"Then we're aligned on at least one point."

Mina almost smiled. It failed halfway. "Are you furious with me?"

Rhea considered. That was one of the things Mina had fallen into with her: the real considering. No performed spontaneity. No automatic soothing.

"Yes," she said. "But not in the simple direction."

"That's useful."

"It's accurate."

Mina stepped inside and closed the screen behind her. The room immediately took her in through the skin the way it always had, through the trapped warmth of it, through the smell of the three of them laid down over weeks in accumulations too specific to fake. Sweat dried into cotton. Wet mangrove under one bed leg from boots never fully clean. Tomas's citrus soap. Rhea's skin after the bat lab, the rubbing-alcohol clean on top of guano and salt. Her own shampoo somewhere in the weave. The room had always embarrassed her by smelling married and then, somehow, by smelling like her too.

"I wasn't lying when I said it changed," she said. "About Tomas. About you."

"I know."

"That sounded like it hurt you to admit."

"No. It hurt to hear you say the first part out loud in front of him."

Mina leaned her hip against the dresser and felt the wood stick slightly to the damp back of her thigh. "What was I supposed to do? Lie prettier?"

"No." Rhea picked up Mina's razor from the windowsill and tucked it into the dry bag. "But don't confuse honesty with timing. You told the truth after he had already been skinned open."

"He asked."

"Yes."

The answer held neither mercy nor accusation. It sat there, factual and clean.

Mina looked at the bed. Without the sheet the mattress showed its camp-issue zipper, a rust bloom near one seam, and the long dip where Tomas slept heavier than Rhea. On the shelf the open condom box still carried Imogen's black-marker inventory code. The room looked less like a private country now than borrowed housing with her things sorted off to one side.

"Did you?" Mina asked. "Use me."

Rhea looked almost bored by the question, which was its own cruelty.

"Of course I did."

Mina let out a breath she had not realized she was holding. The relief in it felt sick.

"For what?"

"Company. Disruption. Pleasure. A witness who knew what contamination language sounds like before a page is halfway turned. An excuse not to spend every night speaking only in marriage." Rhea adjusted the folded sheet so the corners matched. "Take your pick."

"That doesn't make me feel better."

"I wasn't trying to make you feel better."

"No."

Rhea softened then, not much. Just enough for the next sentence to land as something other than punishment.

"It also doesn't make it false," she said.

Mina looked up.

"Wanting you was real," Rhea said. "Using you was also real. Those things aren't enemies. They're just inconvenient together."

The shower shut off. Pipes knocked once in the wall.

Mina said, "Are you asking me to stay away from you?"

Rhea's eyes held hers. "Tonight, I'm asking you to leave this room without asking me to clean it emotionally."

"And after tonight?"

"After tonight I'll know more than I know now."

Accuracy left a sliver of air in the doorway between them, no wider than it had to be.

The bathroom door opened. Steam drifted out carrying hot water, soap, and the wet-metal smell of Tomas's skin after a day around tools. He had a towel around his neck and another low on his hips. The crooked break in his nose looked whiter after heat. He stopped when he saw Mina and did not pretend surprise. Water still ran down the cords of his forearms and darkened the waistband of the towel.

"Good," he said. "You're here."

Rhea did not move between them. Mina respected her for that and hated her a little too.

"I came for my things."

"I can see that."

He crossed to the hook by the door, took down one of her shirts that had been hanging under his rain jacket, and tossed it onto the folded pile on the chair. Not hard. Accurate.

"You missed a sock under the bed," he said. "Rhea found it."

Humiliation should have felt like heat or collapse or some other dramatic system. What it felt like was the seam of the dry bag handle cutting into Mina's palm while a drop from Tomas's hair hit the floorboards between them and sat there shining stupidly in the lamp light.

"Thanks," she said.

He gave a short laugh. "Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Don't get polite like this is a checkout desk."

Mina put the dry bag on the chair and started loading it because she needed her hands doing a sequence. Toothbrush. Paperback. Razor. Shirt. The folded sheet was too much. She left it.

"I'm not trying to be polite," she said.

"Then what are you trying to be?"

Honest, she might have said if the word had not taken enough damage for one day.

"Here," she said instead.

That brought another laugh, even lower. "Fantastic."

Rhea closed the recorder case and snapped both latches. "Don't make me regret staying in the room for this."

Tomas looked at her, then away. "I'm not the one who turned it into a symposium."

"And yet you're still speaking."

Mina should not have loved that line. She did. The room was full of things that kept refusing to die when they were supposed to.

Tomas rubbed the towel hard over his hair and flung it across the desk chair. "You want me quiet, I'll be quiet. Here's quiet." He looked at Mina. "I don't want you in my bed anymore."

The directness of it was easier than the sarcasm had been.

"Okay," Mina said.

"And I don't want you waiting around for me to cool off and decide the sex was stronger than the math."

"Tomas."

"No, let me finish." He planted one hand on the dresser, close enough that the wood creaked. "You said the shape. Fine. Here's the shape from where I'm standing. You needed a route and I was the route. Then I wanted you enough that I let that stop being humiliating. Then I wanted you enough that when you started sleeping with my wife too, I told myself it meant we were all honest because nobody was pretending ownership." He shook his head once. "Turns out there are still cheaper and more expensive bodies in the room."

Mina felt the sentence strike all the way through. Rhea's face changed then, finally.

"That's not fair," Rhea said.

"No?"

"No. It's sharp, but it's not exact."

"What's exact, then?"

Rhea folded her arms. "The county key was on the nightstand with the condoms from Imogen's laundry cabinet. My tunnel maps were under the recorder. Your debt was in your pockets. Class wasn't waiting outside the bed. We just liked the sex enough not to say its name."

Tomas barked a humorless laugh. "There. Better wording. I feel healed already."

"I'm not trying to heal you," Rhea said. "I'm trying to stop you from lying because rage is easier."

He took that and did not answer.

Mina forced herself to meet his eyes. "If you want me to say you were right to be careful with me from the start, you were."

"I wasn't careful."

"No."

"I was stupid."

"No," she said. "You were greedy in some of the same ways I was greedy."

That made his mouth twitch, almost in offense, almost in recognition.

"You really think that helps."

"I think it's true."

He pushed off the dresser and paced once to the door and back because rooms never fit him right when he got this angry. He was more at ease bracing against wake than standing in rooms. Mina knew that about his body now. Knew the way he planted feet wide on land as if something under the floor might shift without warning. Knew how he slept harder after night water than after any dry day. Knowledge did not become innocent because it had been gained intimately.

"Do you know what my supervisor asked me?" he said.

Mina waited.

"He asked whether I was stealing for resale, or because I had gotten sentimental." Tomas smiled with all the wrong muscles. "Sentimental. That's the county word when they want to call you soft and stupid in one breath. I sat there with that man looking at my logs, and all I could think about was you in my truck with your feet on the dash, salt all over your legs, talking about spoonbills like they were a weather system inside your ribs. I thought, this is going to be the dumbest reason anybody ever lost a job on this coast."

Rain hissed in the dark outside the screen. Mina pictured the county office fluorescent and cold, Tomas under it in his work shirt, grease still in his knuckles because no sink ever took it all. The humiliation of being translated by somebody else into a petty kind of fool. She had done that to him too, by omission if not by intention.

"I'm sorry," she said.

He flinched as if she had thrown something weak at him.

"I know."

"No, I mean it."

"I know that too." He looked tired suddenly, deeper than anger. "The problem is sorry doesn't put overtime back in the check, and it doesn't take your first reason out of the room."

Mina slid the paperback deeper into the bag because if she kept looking at the bent cover she was going to start remembering the night Tomas had read half a page aloud in a terrible British accent until Rhea laughed wine out her nose. All those stupid domestic details. They hurt worse than the sex, maybe because they had been the proof she used on herself when she wanted to believe the arrangement had become a life.

"I don't want to bill it out," she said. "I know that's not what this is."

"Good," he said. "Because if you offer me money, I'll say something I can't take back."

That had, embarrassingly, been in the back of her mind. Not as a fix. As tribute. As if damage could be made less ugly by admitting it came with a measurable cost. Rhea's eyes flicked to her face and told her she had guessed exactly that.

"I'm not going to," Mina said.

"Smart."

Rhea picked up the folded sheet at last and held it toward Mina. "Take this."

Mina stared at it. A cheap striped thing from town, blue gone almost gray from too many wash cycles, one corner still faintly stained with the tannin smear Tomas's calf had left after he came in from the creek and forgot not to sit down first. She had bought it because the camp-issued sheets felt like hospital paper and then the sheet had migrated here because so had she.

"You keep it," Mina said.

"No."

"Rhea."

"Take it."

The command came flat and procedural. The sheet belonged in the bag, not on the chair pretending to be an exception.

Mina took the sheet. It was warm from Rhea's hands. The warmth nearly undid her.

"I came for the boat first," she said quietly, because if she left the room without trying one more true sentence, she would deserve whatever simplification followed. "It wasn't the last reason. I wanted you, Tomas. I wanted her. I wanted the room to keep being real enough that I didn't have to go back to being one woman with a rumor and a debt and a camp job that was never going to turn into anything better."

Tomas looked at her for a long time.

"I believe that," he said.

The relief came so fast it made her knees feel hollow. Then he kept going.

"I just don't know what good it does me."

Mina had no answer to that. The dry-bag strap had cut a red groove into her palm.

Rhea rested one hip against the desk. "It does not have to do him good to be true."

Mina looked at her.

Rhea held the look and did not soften it. "And don't hear that as permission to stay. It isn't."

The bug-zapper outside cracked again. From the kitchen walkway came the clang of a pot and Beto's voice saying something too cheerful to be natural, followed by Imogen telling him to stop dropping metal like he had shares in the hardware store. Life kept moving its ordinary feet over the wreck.

Tomas picked up his damp work shirt from the hook and pulled it on without drying fully. The fabric stuck to his shoulders.

"I'm sleeping out on the skiff if the rain lets up," he said, mostly to Rhea.

"You don't need to punish your back for theater," she said.

"It isn't theater."

"No," she said. "It's bad planning."

For the first time that night, the corner of his mouth moved in the direction of the old him. Not enough to call it a smile.

"See?" he said to Mina. "That's marriage. Not the glamorous parts."

Rhea closed her eyes briefly, then opened them. "Don't make me sentimental on principle."

Mina wanted, almost painfully, to step into the little shared current between them and make it three again. That was the old sheltering illusion right there, still offering itself even now: that if she could get them laughing, if she could take off her shirt, if she could say exactly the right ugly thing and let one of them answer by touching her, the rest of the system would loosen. It had worked before, or seemed to. The room itself argued for it. The mattress. The smell. Tomas barefoot on wet boards. Rhea with one hand on the recorder case and the other still open from passing over the sheet.

She saw the temptation clearly enough not to trust it.

"I'm going," she said.

Neither of them stopped her.

At the door she turned back because she did not know when she would next be allowed the chance to be exact with all three of them in one room.

"I'm not taking the pages anywhere," she said. "Not tonight."

Imogen's phrasing had gotten into her despite herself. Rhea noticed. So did Tomas.

"Good," Tomas said.

"And I'm not asking either of you to tell me what this was supposed to mean."

Rhea nodded once. "Good."

Tomas looked down at the floorboards. "Mina."

She waited.

He still did not look up. "If we end up talking again, don't come to me like I can make it wild for you."

The line hit so precisely it left no defense around it.

"Okay," she said.

She stepped out into the wet walkway with the dry bag over one shoulder and the folded sheet tucked under her arm. Rain blew through the screen in a fine mist and freckled her forearms. From the rookery came the late, irritated squall of birds settling badly.

Her own room felt narrower than it had that morning, the camp mattress humped in the center, the air stale from being shut during rain. She set the dry bag on the bunk, took the annex badge from her pocket, and laid it on the folded sheet. The rusted clip caught one faded blue stripe. After a second she flipped the smiling photo facedown and left it there while the storm drains below the stilts gulped, choked, and tried again.


# Chapter 14

By the following dusk the camp had learned a new way to be polite.

Nobody told Mina to stay clear of the kitchen. Nobody had to. The main house carried sound too well for that kind of order to survive as an order; it would have become gossip before the sentence was finished. Instead the place had gone careful around her. A pot of rice sat on the stove with the lid tipped so steam could get out. Two fillets of jack were wrapped in foil on a tray, one gone, one waiting. Someone had moved her mug from the drainboard to the far end of the counter, beside the can of instant coffee nobody claimed unless they were furious or broke. Through the screen wall the spoonbills kept up their raw nesting racket in the darkening trees, and under that noise the stilts complained every time the wind leaned.

The argument from the night before had not stopped ringing just because everybody had run out of things to throw at it.

Tomas's silence had been the loudest part. He had not shouted after the first few ugly minutes. He had gotten worse than that. He had gone exact. He had named fuel, hours, and the shape of his own suspension as if he were reading his losses off a county form. He had named his body too, which had been harder to hear. Mina had stood there with the annex logbook on the table between them and understood too late that proof could strip skin faster than lies did. Rhea had not rescued her. Rhea had also not let Tomas turn the whole thing into a neat charge sheet with Mina as sole defendant. That had been its own kind of mercy, and it had not felt kind.

All day Mina had been given work that kept her useful and away from the guest-facing side of camp. She had scrubbed the bat-room sink with diluted bleach until her knuckles smelled like a public pool. She had carried two cracked bins of damp netting out to the wash line and back. She had tightened loose screws on the screened walkway with a driver whose handle had melted a little in some old summer and hardened again lopsided. They were manual things, things that could be finished. Her shoulders were heavy in the clean dumb way labor sometimes gave her, but underneath that sat the live wire she had earned.

She had just lifted the foil and decided she could make herself eat when Rhea tapped the screen doorframe with two knuckles.

Mina looked up too fast. The foil crinkled in her hand.

Rhea stood there with a board paddle under one arm and a dry bag slung across her chest. She had on one of Tomas's old county work shirts over black shorts, the gray fabric washed so thin at the elbows it had gone almost white. Mina knew the shirt because she had once pushed her hands up under it in the dark and felt the square of the stitched county patch against Rhea's ribs from the inside. Tonight the sleeves were rolled with Rhea's usual carelessness, one cuff tighter than the other. A crushed packet of saltines bulged out of the chest pocket. Rhea always kept something plain to eat in her field bag because bug spray on an empty stomach made her nauseous, and she refused to admit it was nausea. She called it chemistry.

"Low water in the west corridors," she said. "The recorder needs a clean run, and a motor would wreck it anyway. If you still know how to paddle, come with me."

Mina set the foil down. "You sure?"

Rhea's face did not soften, but it did something harder to refuse. It became plainer. "I didn't ask by accident."

Mina glanced past her toward the boardwalk. There was no engine noise, no county radio mutter, no Tomas moving around the mosquito dock with his keys hitting metal. Since the suspension, the camp had developed an absence where his airboat should have been, like a missing tooth a tongue could not stop checking.

"Does he know?" Mina asked.

"He knows I'm running bats." Rhea shifted the paddle in her hand. "He knows I asked for a second board."

That was not the same answer, and both of them knew it.

"All right," Mina said.

She did not trust herself to say anything more while Rhea was still standing in Tomas's shirt.

The board shed sat behind the bat lab, where the screened walkway gave up and the shell path took over. Mina changed fast in the little washroom, into long sleeves and old leggings with a seam rubbed shiny at the knee, then met Rhea outside with her hair dragged up wet from the sink. Mosquitoes found the water at her temples before she had the cap back on the repellent. The bat lab door was propped open with a brick. Inside, battery chargers glowed red and green on the counter. Damp netting and guano and rubbing alcohol made the room smell like a wound somebody had kept scientific on purpose.

Rhea was checking batteries against a notepad clipped to a Rite-in-the-Rain board. She wrote small, angular script that made even grocery lists look peer reviewed. Next to her sat the ultrasonic recorder in a foam-lined case, two headlamps with the white LEDs taped over and red filters snapped into place, a waterproof notebook, and a thermos with chipped green enamel.

"You ate?" Rhea asked without looking up.

"Enough."

Rhea slid the saltines out of her pocket and held them out. "Eat more than enough."

It would have been easier if she had sounded tender. Tenderness gave a person something obvious to flinch from. Rhea sounded like a scientist correcting a field error before it propagated through the data set. Mina took the packet and ate two broken crackers while Rhea checked the recorder one more time.

"We're not going near the spoonbill channels?" Mina asked.

"We're not hunting anything tonight." Rhea zipped the dry bag. "We're running transects and trying not to contaminate the file with wishful thinking."

Mina swallowed dry crumbs. "I didn't mean-"

"I know what you meant."

The shell path down to the launch bit through Mina's shoes. On the dark side of the main house, the camp felt smaller than it had with Tomas's airboat in the world. There was no prop cage looming, no fuel sheen, no sense that a bad idea could become motion in thirty seconds if the right man turned a key. The boards made them look almost harmless: two women carrying narrow white bodies under their arms, paddles clicking softly against shafts wrapped in bicycle tape to kill noise. A gecko watched from the railing, throat pulsing. Somewhere across the water a generator coughed itself steady.

They set in beside the mangrove fringe where the tide had dropped enough to expose black root knees. Mina stepped onto her board and felt the old small correction move through her ankles and hips, the body remembering before pride did. The water took her weight with a quiet slosh, then steadied. Rhea shoved off beside her with less visible effort than most people used to stand from a chair.

For the first hundred yards neither of them spoke. They let the boards find the creek's narrow center where the last light sat on the tannin surface like something thinking about silver and changing its mind. With no engine to push them, distance became personal again. Mina felt each yard in her shoulders. The paddle blade entered blackwater with a thick silk resistance and came out smelling of root rot and salt. Tiny fish skipped away from the boards with sounds like fingernails flicked against glass.

She had forgotten how much noise a motor erased. Without it the channels broke into layers: mosquito whine at ear level, mullet gulps in the dark, spoonbills in the farther rookery sounding less like birds than like a room full of people trying not to retch, and crabs ticking against submerged wood. Rhea's paddle dripped in an even metronome a few feet to Mina's left. Every time Mina changed sides, the board gave a soft hollow knock under her feet and sent a circle of tannin out through the reflected branches.

By the time they reached the first station, a low tunnel where the mangroves closed over the water and the air thickened with insects, Mina's triceps were beginning to feel the slower cost of everything Tomas had ever made look easy.

Rhea lifted two fingers without turning her head in the stop signal. Mina dropped to her knees and let the board drift. Rhea did the same. They clipped the boards together with a short line so they would not knock apart in the mild current, then killed the lamps and sat inside dark that was not complete because the water held a little sky and the sky held the afterimage of heat.

Rhea set the recorder between them, its indicator the faintest red bead under tape. She leaned close enough for Mina to smell coffee and DEET and the cool medicinal trace that always lived in the cotton at Rhea's wrists.

"Twenty minutes," she whispered. "Any emergence from the hollow snag on your side, note direction and count. Don't guess. If you lose one, you lose one."

Mina nodded.

Once she stopped trying to hear bats as a concept and started listening for disturbance, they arrived everywhere: tiny leather squeaks beyond ordinary hearing, translated through the recorder's monitor into dry needle-clicks, soft rushes of wing close to the water, and brief impossible changes in shape as something moved through the red-dark in front of her and then became branch again. Three came out of the snag within the first minute, then seven in a loose spill, then more from farther back in the roots where she had not even known a cavity sat.

Counting calmed her in a way thinking never had. She marked one body, then another. One dropped low enough to seam the water. Another broke upward and was gone. The notebook on her thigh went damp under her wrist. Beside her, Rhea made her own marks with the pencil braced against her thumb, never looking hurried, never correcting a movement twice.

When the first pass was done, Rhea touched Mina's knee and pointed farther inland. They unclipped, stood, and poled themselves deeper through a passage too narrow for anything wider than a board or a skiff with a patient fool at the stern.

The quiet changed scale. Airboats turned the mangroves into obstacles. Boards let the place keep its own shape. The roots arched over them in black braided masses that smelled of iodine and old mud. Bats crossed so near Mina heard the pressure of their wings more than the sound, quick compressions of air by her cheek. Twice she had to duck under branches slick with salt and spider silk. Once her board slid over something soft that rolled under the hull and vanished, and her whole body flashed to blue before her mind caught up and named it as a log.

She hated that the creek still knew how to use her. One false texture and she was ready to turn every living thing into a clue.

At the second station they crouched in the lee of a dead palm snag and let the recorder run. Rhea drank from the thermos and passed it over without asking. The coffee had gone milky and bitter. It tasted of chicory and the cap's rubber gasket. Mina drank anyway.

"You don't have to be decent to me tonight," she said softly.

Rhea kept looking into the branches. "That sounds exhausting."

"I mean it."

"I know you do."

Another bat slipped out of the mangroves on a line so clean it seemed drawn. Mina wrote it down and then said, because the dark was doing its job on her and because Rhea had brought her out here instead of leaving her to eat alone with her own shame, "He was right."

Rhea's pencil stopped moving.

"About what?" she asked.

"That I used him." Mina kept her voice low even though there was nobody to overhear but roots and birds and whatever things fed under roots and birds. "I can say I wanted him too. I did. I can say he knew what he was offering, and he did. None of that changes what part of my brain lit up first. He had fuel. He had routes. He had a county shirt and a key and a boat that made the law bend around the edges. I saw access before I saw a man."

Rhea took that in without looking at her. It was one of the things Mina had learned to crave most and fear most in her: the way she let a statement exist at full size before answering it.

"You saw a man too," Rhea said at last. "Don't do yourself the courtesy of simplification."

Mina gave a short ugly laugh. "That sounded nicer in my head than it deserved."

"It didn't sound nice."

There was no cruelty in it. Only calibration.

Mina rested the paddle shaft across her thighs. "It wasn't just him."

Rhea turned then, enough that Mina could see the pale strip of her cheekbone under the red lamp.

"I know," Rhea said.

Mina wished she had not opened the door if she was going to stop in it. The wish passed. There was no point being halfway honest with Rhea. Half honesty was just vanity with better lighting.

"Once you knew," Mina said, "once you knew enough to scare me and you still looked at me like that... I started wanting something from you that had nothing to do with fairness."

Rhea waited.

"Part of me thought if you wanted me after all of it, maybe that changed the shape of what I was." Mina kept her eyes on the notebook because looking at Rhea while saying it would have made her either theatrical or evasive, and she did not have the right to be either. "Like if the wife wanted me too, then I wasn't just the woman who fucked her husband for gas. I was something else. Chosen. Maybe even clean. That's ugly. I know it is."

The current nudged the clipped boards against one another with a rubber kiss.

Rhea exhaled once through her nose. "Yes," she said. "It is."

Mina let the word land. It hurt less than a lie would have.

Three bats burst from the hollow behind Rhea's shoulder, then another two. They both counted automatically. Mina wrote 5 NE. The act of writing steadied her hand enough to continue breathing like a person and not a machine left running.

"I didn't bring you out here to make you bleed for the pleasure of accuracy," Rhea said after a moment.

"Then why did you bring me?"

Rhea capped the thermos. "Because the file needed a second body who can still distinguish a bat from a wish. Because the boards are quieter than a boat. Because if I stayed on land tonight, everybody would keep pretending the only choices are courtroom or bed."

She adjusted the recorder gain with one precise thumb. "And because I wanted your company."

The last sentence went through Mina with more force than anything louder could have managed.

They ran the third station in near silence. A barred owl called once from deeper woods, flat and sour. Mosquitoes needled the backs of Mina's hands where repellent had worn thin. Sweat crawled down her spine under the damp shirt and cooled whenever the night moved. She kept the counts, kept the time stamps, kept her eyes on the exits Rhea assigned her. Work came first. Structure held her together; punishment would only have fed the shame. The difference mattered.

At a shell shelf no wider than a kitchen table, they slid the boards half onto mud and stepped off to stretch their legs. Mina's calves trembled in the sudden steadiness of land. The mud sucked at her soles through the thin water shoes. Rhea crouched by the dry bag and sorted batteries by touch. From this angle the old county shirt hung open enough at the throat for Mina to see a crescent mosquito bite high on her collarbone, angry and raised.

Rhea handed over the saltines again. Mina took one and watched her peel an alcohol wipe open with her teeth.

"Your hands," Rhea said.

Mina looked down. The waxed paddle grip had lifted two blisters at the base of her fingers, not dramatic, just white and mean.

"They'll live."

"So will an untreated fungal rash for longer than either of us wants." Rhea held out her hand. "Give me one."

Mina obeyed before pride could organize itself.

Rhea wiped the raw skin clean. The alcohol bit sharp. Mina hissed.

"I know," Rhea murmured, not unkindly. "Hold still."

She smoothed a strip of hydrocolloid over the blister with the flat of her thumb, pressing from center to edge as carefully as if she were fitting membrane over a specimen slide. Her fingers were cool from the night air and smelled faintly of guano and cheap hand soap. Mina could have made that into a seduction if she had wanted to be stupid. What it felt like instead was worse and better: care with no romance excuse attached.

"I'm trying not to ask for more than anybody can give me," Mina said.

Rhea snorted softly. "That sentence has too much advance credit in it."

Mina almost smiled. "All right. I'm trying to notice when I do it."

"That's better."

Rhea taped the second blister, then sat back on her heels. Behind her the blackwater reflected a ragged copy of the mangroves, roots reaching up and down at once.

"You're not wrong that desire changes the shape of things," Rhea said. "It does. It changes what we can bear to look at. It changes what we excuse. It changes how much stupidity feels briefly survivable."

"That sounds like a scientific term."

"It's adjacent to one."

Mina held the taped hand in her lap. The pad already made the skin feel less exposed, which was exactly the problem with being helped by Rhea: relief arrived before deserving it.

"But desire is not rescue," Rhea said.

The sentence settled between them with the same dry authority as a measurement.

Mina looked up.

Rhea met her eyes. "I wanted you. I want you now. That's real. It survived stress, novelty, even the erotic possibilities of shared contamination. And it won't carry either of us out of this. If I start treating it like a raft, I become cruel. If you do, you become dishonest."

Mina's mouth had gone dry in spite of the wet air.

"I know you aren't leaving him," she said.

"That's not the only thing I mean."

Mina waited.

"I'm not leaving my life for a cleaner story," Rhea said. "Not my marriage, not my work, not the ugly practical lattice of obligations that makes up most actual living. That wouldn't make us brave. It would make us dramatic. There's a difference."

The mangroves ticked with small creatures moving where they could not be seen.

"Some hours I'm furious with Tomas," Rhea went on. "Some hours I'm furious with you. Some hours I'm furious with myself for finding any of this beautiful enough to miss. None of those states absolves the others. I'm not interested in pretending you can replace him, or he can cancel you, or wanting one of you tells me what to do with the other. That's rescue-story thinking. It turns people into exits."

Mina stared at the dark patch on Rhea's shirt pocket where thermos coffee had leaked and dried in an uneven half-moon. It was easier than staring directly into the fact of being seen.

"Then what are we doing?" she asked.

Rhea glanced past her toward the creek, listening for the next pulse of wings. When she answered, her voice was quieter than before, less lecture than confession.

"Witness, if we can manage it."

Mina let out a breath she had not noticed she was saving.

Rhea rubbed one thumb over the rim of the recorder case, a small repetitive motion Mina had never seen her make in front of anyone else. "My job is full of forms of helplessness that have to stay precise. A colony gets sprayed or flooded or stressed out of a roost and there are days when all I can actually do is count who still comes out at dusk. People hear that and think it's a lesser task than rescue. It isn't lesser. It's the part that keeps the damage from being translated into rumor or public relations copy. Somebody has to look directly and say what happened."

She looked back at Mina. "That matters with people too."

The words hit lower than blame. Mina understood, suddenly and without relief, that witness required more of her than apology did. Apology could still be performance. Witness meant staying in the room after the pretty version died.

"You mean you can know what I did and still stay accurate about me," Mina said.

"Yes."

"And I don't get to turn that into sainthood."

"Correct."

The corner of Rhea's mouth moved. It was not quite a smile, but it was enough.

Mina sat down hard on the shell shelf before her knees could decide something embarrassing. The shells pressed through her wet shoes. She welcomed the sting.

"There was a minute last night," she said, "when Tomas was talking about the fuel and the hours and I thought, if you touched me right then, I would survive it. Which was already bad enough. Then I thought if you chose me in front of him, it would make the whole room arrange itself around that instead of the facts. That's the part I hate."

Rhea absorbed this with no visible flinch. "Thank you for not dressing it up."

"Don't thank me."

"I didn't say it was admirable."

Mina barked a laugh so sudden a heron farther down the shelf rustled once in protest.

Rhea's face changed then. Some of the hardness went out of it. She sat beside Mina on the shell shelf, knees up, forearms draped over them, close enough that their wet sleeves touched and stayed touching.

"I need to tell you something in return," she said.

Mina waited.

"Imogen asked for the raw coordinates from the spoonbill sighting file this morning. She didn't frame it as pressure, exactly. She framed it as prudence." Rhea picked a grain of shell off her shin. "I said the metadata were unstable because the file was corrupted. That's true enough to count as truth if I'm feeling cowardly. Less true if I'm feeling rigorous."

Mina turned her head. "You still have them."

"Approximately. Not to the meter."

"And you didn't give them to her."

"No."

The night seemed to tilt a little around that fact, not because Mina wanted possession of the eel back. She did not even know if possession was a meaningful category anymore, but because Rhea had chosen not to hand wonder and contamination over to the nearest institution just because institutions were hungry.

"Why are you telling me?" Mina asked.

Rhea looked mildly insulted by the question. "Because if I'm making a morally consequential decision in a swamp because desire has complicated my judgment, I'd like at least one person present who knows the full contamination profile."

Mina stared, then laughed again, softer this time. "That's the least romantic thing anybody has ever said to me."

"You say that like it's criticism."

"It might be praise."

Rhea bumped her shoulder once with her own. "I'm not offering you exoneration. I'm offering accuracy and involvement. If I decide to keep the location vague for now, I want you to know that's not me being dazzled by you. It's me refusing to let this place become an asset sheet before we understand how much harm is already braided into it."

Mina felt the old reflex rise, the one that wanted to promise usefulness fast, before anyone could change their mind. "I can stay out of it if that helps."

"That's not witness." Rhea's voice sharpened, then eased. "Mina. No vanishing trick. Not with me."

The use of her name, plain and unornamented, moved through her harder than the touch on her blisters had.

"All right," Mina said.

Rhea took a breath. "If this gets uglier, and it will, I need someone around me who won't start lying the second a cleaner narrative becomes available. Tomas can't do that right now, not about this, and I don't blame him for it. Imogen certainly can't. I'm asking whether that can be you."

It was the closest thing to trust Mina had been offered in years, and it arrived wrapped in caveats sharp enough to draw blood. That made it easier to believe.

"Yes," she said.

Then, because the creek and the heat and Rhea's precision had stripped her down to the part that could answer without theater, she added, "I don't know if I can do it perfectly. But yes."

Rhea nodded once, as if the answer had met a threshold she had set privately.

They sat for another moment listening to the dark tick and move around them. Then Rhea said, "Come here," in the tone of voice she used when handing somebody a specimen bag or correcting headlamp angle.

Mina turned. Rhea kissed her with one hand on the back of her neck and the other braced in shell grit by Mina's hip. The kiss landed with the blunt authority of a fact. Her mouth tasted of stale chicory coffee and salt and the faint sweetness of whatever hand cream she used only when her knuckles cracked badly enough to force the issue. Mina opened under it anyway, old hunger flaring so fast she had to grip the shell shelf to keep from leaning all the way over.

Rhea's braid brushed Mina's wrist. The county shirt smelled like mangrove water now, and underneath that, like warm skin. When Mina put her taped hand against Rhea's waist she felt the damp cotton cling and the hard line of the thermos cap digging through the pocket. Rhea made a low sound into her mouth, not helpless, not careful either. It was honest.

Then she pulled back just far enough to look at Mina in the red-thinned dark.

"This isn't a promise of departure," she said.

Mina nodded. Her lips felt salt-burned.

"I know."

"And it's not pity."

"I know that too."

Rhea studied her another beat, as if checking for slippage in the meaning. Satisfied, she kissed her once more, shorter, and stood.

"Good," she said. "We still have two stations."

It was so absurdly characteristic that Mina had to close her eyes and smile into her shoulder before getting up.

The last part of the route took them through water so narrow the boards brushed mangrove leaves on both sides. Bats were thick there, dropping out of the canopy and skimming the surface in quick dark loops that set the monitor ticking almost continuously. The work demanded enough of Mina that her mind stopped trying to turn every exchange into verdict. She watched emergence holes, counted passes, marked time. Once Rhea reached across the clipped space between boards and moved Mina's wrist two inches to angle the recorder away from insect buzz. The correction was practical. The heat it left behind in Mina's skin was not.

Near midnight a low thunderhead walked itself along the inland horizon, far enough away to show without yet arriving. The air changed before the weather did. It felt cooler on the forearms and heavier in the lungs. The mangrove leaves turned their paler undersides. Mina felt the shift in the water first, a subtle flattening as the night held still to hear what the sky planned next.

At the final station, bats poured from a dead gumbo-limbo split by lightning years ago, a ragged streaming knot that made both women abandon talk and just count. Forty-three on Mina's side in under six minutes. Thirty-one on Rhea's. Then smaller staggered exits from farther back in the cavity. The recorder's little hidden light glowed like a clot. By the time the flow thinned, Mina's notebook page had gone soft with humidity and pencil pressure.

Rhea exhaled and rolled her neck once. "That will do."

On the paddle back, they let the current help. The boards slid easier with the creek under them instead of against. Neither woman hurried. Without a motor there was no illusion of outrunning what waited on land, but there was also no prop cage shrieking them back into role. There was only the pull, the dip, the drip, the black lanes opening one by one between roots.

"Where is he sleeping?" Mina asked finally.

Rhea did not pretend not to know who she meant. "Half the night in our bed. Half the night on the porch when he gets tired of being angry in a horizontal position."

The sentence held affection and damage in proportions Mina could not calculate.

"I'm sorry," Mina said.

"I know."

"That wasn't me fishing."

"I know that too."

They rounded the last bend and the camp came into view in pieces: one dim bug bulb at the boardwalk, the main house windows yellowed by old screens, the bat lab rectangle dark except for the charger lights waiting inside. The place looked fragile again, just wood and mesh and debt over water, not the stage set it became when donors were present or a fight made everybody archetypal.

Rhea grounded her board and stepped off into shin-deep mud without complaint. Mina followed, wobbling once before the mud claimed her balance and held it. They hauled the boards up onto the shell lip and stood breathing in the mosquito-thick dark, not touching yet.

"I'm going back to the house," Rhea said.

Mina made herself nod before disappointment could turn adolescent in her face.

"All right."

Rhea set the paddle down and took Mina's jaw lightly in one hand, thumb rough at the corner of her mouth. "I said I'm going back to the house. I didn't say I want distance from you."

Mina looked at her.

"The recorder has to upload," Rhea said. "The files will take twenty minutes if the laptop behaves and an hour if it decides to be a university machine. I can stay for the upload."

It was such a Rhea offer that Mina almost laughed again. Intimacy, in Rhea's dialect, looked like twenty minutes under a progress bar with data as chaperone.

"Okay," she said, and heard how much it meant in that one small word.

Inside the bat lab the air had gone sour and warm from the day's sealed heat. Rhea switched on the red task light over the counter. It painted the room like a wound under skin. They moved around each other with the ease that survived even this: Mina laying out the notebook to dry, Rhea plugging the recorder cable into the laptop, both of them angling themselves automatically away from the place where the extension cord liked to spark if kicked.

The upload bar crawled.

Rhea sat on the edge of the counter and took off her glasses to clean them on the hem of the county shirt. Mina had never asked when Rhea had started needing them for screens. It felt like one of the private domestic facts a person earned by staying. She watched the careful circles Rhea rubbed on each lens, the small crease between her brows when a smear resisted.

"You should wash those bites before you sleep," Rhea said, putting the glasses back on. "The one on your ankle is already ugly."

"Romance again."

"I've got range."

Mina leaned against the opposite counter. The room hummed softly with chargers and laptop fan. Through the screen vent, the night outside kept breathing its wet black breath.

"If it comes apart worse," Mina said, "with Imogen, with the county, with whoever wants the eel to be either miracle or contamination exhibit, I can take my share out loud."

Rhea's gaze moved from the laptop to her face. "You'll have to. That's separate from this."

"I know."

"Good."

The upload reached sixty-two percent and stalled there long enough for both women to notice. Mina felt a childish panic at the thought of this one quiet thing getting corrupted too. Rhea slid off the counter, bent over the laptop, and tapped one key with efficient irritation. The bar moved again.

"There," she said.

Mina let out the breath before she could stop herself.

Rhea straightened and looked at her for a long second. Then she crossed the little room and rested her forehead against Mina's.

"When the facts start costing you," she said softly, "don't rearrange them to make yourself bearable."

The sentence went into Mina like a blade and like relief, both at once. It named the old trick more plainly than confession ever had: not lying outright, just shifting emphasis until she could stand her own outline. No donor-brochure innocence. No glamorous ruin either. Just annex logs and fuel theft and want mixed up with evidence; Tomas on the porch with his anger, Rhea in his shirt, Mina still wanting both witness and touch from the same woman. It did not let anybody out. It also did not ask them to disappear.

Mina put her good hand on the back of Rhea's neck. "I won't," she said. "If I start trying to sand it smooth, stop me."

The laptop chimed. The upload was complete.

Rhea kissed her once, brief and unspectacular and final in the way of things that intended to continue later without needing to advertise it. Then she stepped back, capped the thermos, and unhooked the county shirt from where it had caught on the corner of the recorder case.

"Sleep with ointment on those hands," she said.

"Yes, doctor."

"Not a doctor."

"You say that every time."

"Because it keeps being true."

Mina smiled despite herself. Rhea picked up the dry bag and headed for the door. At the threshold she looked back, braid dark down her shoulder, one hand already lifting to scratch at the mosquito bite on her collarbone now that nobody else was close enough to see her break discipline.

"Tomorrow," she said, "if anybody starts telling a prettier version than the one we have, come find me before you answer."

"I will."

Rhea nodded and was gone down the screened walk, her footfalls swallowed by the old wood almost immediately.

Mina stood in the red-lit room with the finished file on the laptop and the smell of chicory coffee still on her mouth. Outside, the spoonbills raised another burst of filthy, human-sounding noise from the rookery, and farther out in the mangroves the bats kept working the dark whether anybody counted them or not. The facts stayed dirty and fully hers. What the night had given her was narrower than rescue and much harder to misuse.

When she finally turned off the red light and stepped onto the walkway, the paddleboards below the bat lab still dripped creek water back into the black channel one patient drop at a time.


# Chapter 15

Under the bat lab, the paddleboards sat damp and creek-streaked when Beto came up the screened walk with the camp phone in one hand and a piece of toast in the other, chewing like whatever panic was in the receiver had interrupted breakfast rather than replaced it. He had blue pen across his knuckles again, torque numbers from the outboard manual he kept trying to memorize between dish duty and dock work, and one of the digits had smeared where he had wiped butter away with the heel of his hand.

"Imogen wants you and Dr. Acosta at the county dock," he said, voice full around the last bite. "Right now. She said right now twice, so I think she means it the bad way."

Rhea came out of the lab behind Mina with her braid half redone and a fresh alcohol wipe smell clinging to her wrists. They had been packing last night's bat cards into labeled sleeves, quiet work after the paddleboard survey, letting the tenderness of the evening keep its own shape without naming it too much. Mina still had black mangrove grit under one thumbnail and the ache in her shoulders from poling through root shade. For one drowsy hour she had believed the day might stay small.

"What happened?" Rhea asked.

Beto shrugged one shoulder. The oversized camp T-shirt slid down and showed the pale burn ladder on his wrist from the kitchen fryer back in May. "County radio. Something about positives. Something about a board member's nephew. She was already swearing before she hung up."

That narrowed it enough.

The walk to the mosquito dock took them through shell glare and wet heat so dense it seemed to press back. The camp's boardwalk gave under Mina's bare soles where the planks always sweated brine by midmorning. Beyond the mangrove fringe the air held the metal-flat taste of weather not yet willing to break. Mosquitoes found the backs of her knees even in full sun. Somewhere out in the rookery the spoonbills kept up their constant rough nursery racket, chicks begging, adults clattering, a whole colony behaving as if politics did not exist and could not arrive by county memo.

At the dock trailer Imogen was already inside with both hands on the edge of a scarred laminate table, wearing her reading glasses on the beaded chain she never let donors see. Her pinned-up silver hair had started to come loose on one side, which on Imogen counted as a public collapse. Across from her sat a county woman in a green district polo darkened under the arms, late thirties maybe, with a glittery kindergarten star sticker still attached to the corner of her clipboard. Mina noticed it because the rest of the room was all lock cages, faded safety placards, stale coffee, and the hard cold from an overworked wall unit. The sticker did not belong. That made it human enough to hurt.

"There they are," Imogen said without relief. "Marlene, this is Dr. Rhea Acosta. Mina is with camp operations."

With camp operations. The title shaved off the eel, BrightCurrent, every kind of trouble she actually carried. Mina took the demotion and stayed against the wall.

Marlene Velez stood to shake Rhea's hand first, then seemed to remember she had not offered one to Mina and corrected too late. She had the brisk, exhausted face of someone who had been explaining the same emergency to ten different angry people since dawn. "Thank you for coming fast. We have two presumptive arboviral cases on the mainland side, one pediatric, one elderly, plus horse positives north of the causeway. Commissioners want visible control before tonight's press scrum. State wants adult response in the low marsh blocks. We are reviewing sensitive habitat constraints before the district director signs off."

"Reviewing," Imogen said. "That's a generous verb."

On the far side of the room Tomas leaned against a file cabinet with his cap crushed in one hand. Mina had not known he would be there. His county shirt looked freshly pulled from a plastic drawer, too stiff in the shoulders from old detergent and not enough wear, and the ridge of beard along his jaw said he had shaved yesterday for no good reason and not gotten the benefit of it. He did not look at her right away. He was staring at the laminated map on the table, all the creek lines he knew better than most prayers.

Rhea moved in beside Imogen and read the map upside down with one palm flat on the paper to stop the curl. "What time window?"

"Tentative twenty-one hundred to zero-zero thirty," Marlene said. "ULV pass along the outer channels and the residential edge. We can keep the primary spray corridor outside the rookery buffer if conditions hold."

"If conditions hold," Rhea repeated. She had gone plainer already, which was how Mina knew she was angry. "The spoonbill chicks are at flush age in those interior trees. If your wind changes by even a little, you don't only get drift. You get panic. You get nest loss. You also get bat emergence right through your fog line if you run that late."

"We are aware of the bat survey."

"You're aware that I sent you a map," Rhea said. "That's not the same thing."

Imogen slid a permit packet across the table so hard the staple tore the top page. "Protected rookery. Active breeding. Existing written objections from this camp to any emergency adulticide within the mapped margin. I do not care what your commissioners want to look like on camera. If you hit those trees, I will hand the state your own route sheet."

Marlene put two fingers against the bridge of her nose, then lowered them carefully, as if even irritation needed to be managed on paper. "Ms. Price, I respect your position. I also have state health on my back, a county board calling every twenty minutes, and residents who do not give a damn about spoonbills if their kid is in ICU. We are trying to thread a needle here."

Mina knew that tone. It was the tone institutions used when they meant: your damage is regrettable but already budgeted.

Tomas finally looked up. For a second his eyes rested on Mina and then moved away to Rhea. "Who's running the air pass?"

"Contract bird out of Naples," Marlene said. "One helo. We need a local support boat inside because the outer channels shifted after the last blow and half my regular people know the maps better than the mud."

"Your regular people," Tomas said, "or the ones who haven't pissed you off lately?"

Imogen's gaze flicked to him. She had not known that part, or had not known it would be said in front of company.

Marlene's mouth tightened. "Your suspension is under audit review, not termination. Emergency assignment is separate."

The barb showed clean through the bait.

Rhea touched two fingers to the map margin and took one measured breath. "Fuel irregularities," she said, clipping each syllable as if she meant it for the record. "You suspended him on that and now you want his channels because the commissioner wants a show of control. That's not separate."

"It's payroll and weather," Tomas said. "Everything is separate until it isn't."

He said it dryly, but Mina heard the raw edge under it. He had been angry since the annex logbook came out on Imogen's table and turned every easy lie in the house sour, and the anger had settled into something denser than volume. It sat in him now like a weight under the breastbone. She hated that she could hear it and still think first of the rookery map, of the chicks hanging in those trees like unfinished thoughts.

Marlene tapped the map with a capped pen. "We can still modify. That's why you are here. Give me the highest-risk corridors. Fast."

So they did. Not because it would stop the spray, Mina realized almost immediately, but because refusal would let the district claim ignorance later. Rhea named bat emergence lines and the mangrove pockets where heat held longest. Imogen marked the spoonbill trees, the heron spillover, the donor platform as if public embarrassment were another protected species. Tomas, after a silence long enough to count as a choice, pointed with one blunt finger at the blackwater bends where drift would pool if the wind turned off the Gulf. Mina heard herself adding root height, channel pinch points, the old annex side current that sucked surface sheen inland on a falling tide. Nobody asked whether she should be speaking. Nobody thanked her either.

The map got better. The decision did not.

A radio crackled on Marlene's belt. A man's voice, too loud with distance, asked whether the director wanted the revised block notes before the county attorney came in. Marlene answered yes and no at once, then looked back at the table.

"Here is what I can do," she said. "We push the start later by thirty minutes to reduce direct day-roost exposure. We instruct the pilot to hold the interior line unless the support boat clears it. We maintain buffer as flagged. But the response window is being authorized. The director is signing in the next ten minutes."

Imogen gave a short laugh with no amusement in it. "You brought us in to help you draft cleaner blame."

"I brought you in because if I didn't, you'd say nobody consulted wildlife."

"Nobody did."

Rhea was already writing on the back of one of the district forms, hard enough to emboss the paper below. "You need a wind cutoff. Four knots and shifting inland, you abort."

"Noted."

"Not noted. Written."

Marlene took the paper, read it, and added it to the stack. "Written."

"And no pass over the interior canopy. None."

"If conditions allow."

Imogen's glasses swung on their chain when she straightened. "Say it plainly. You are authorizing a spray over a protected breeding site because your board is afraid of headlines."

Marlene met her eyes. Whatever softness the sticker suggested was gone. "I am authorizing emergency mosquito control because two people may die and my board would rather answer for dead birds than dead voters."

The cruelty of it was not in the sentence. The sentence was ordinary enough. The cruelty was in how familiar the arithmetic sounded. Choose your body count. Print the cleaner one.

On the table lay the final route sheet with its squares and initials, the language already doing its work: adult response, target block, low-volume application, non-target minimization. Mina had spent too many years around documents that turned living things into acceptable collateral. She could feel the old BrightCurrent part of her attention waking up, the part that scanned verbs before blood.

Marlene signed her name on the witness line while the director was still, somewhere else, about to sign the main authorization. That was how the disaster entered the room: not with engine noise, but with blue ink drying under air-conditioning.

When they stepped back into the heat, the cold of the trailer left a wet chill between Mina's shoulder blades. The dock boards burned through her sandals. Tomas stayed behind long enough to speak with Marlene at the gear cage, head bent, one hand on the latch. Mina did not need to hear the words to know a bargain was being offered in the language of usefulness.

Imogen pinched the bridge of her nose and said, "I need to notify the board and start an incident file before they pretend I agreed to any of this."

"I need the lab," Rhea said. "If they hit emergence, I want boxes ready and saline mixed."

Imogen looked at Mina. "Keep Beto off the water unless I say otherwise."

As if there were a version of this where bodies would stay abstract.

Mina glanced back toward the cage. Tomas had his cap on now. Marlene was handing him a radio and a laminated badge clipped to a retractable cord, the county giving him back just enough authority to be blamed with it later.

The spoonbills kept clattering out in the heat, blind to paperwork.

***

By late afternoon the camp had taken on the kind of false busyness people built when they could not stop the thing coming and refused to stand still waiting for it. Imogen was in the office dictating a formal objection into her laptop with the clipped voice she used for funders and enemies. Beto hauled extra crates from the generator shed, trying so hard to look useful that he almost missed the step down from the walkway. Rhea lined the bat lab counter with saline, towels, syringes without needles, nitrile gloves, all the small humiliating equipment of damage control.

Mina found Tomas at the county dock beside an airboat that was not his usual run boat, though the tiller had been wrapped in the same black tape all district equipment eventually acquired under his hands. A five-gallon mix tank sat strapped behind the driver's stand. A respirator hung from the rail. Two unopened boxes of marker flags sweated in the shade. Tomas was checking the fan cage bolts with a socket wrench he did not need for those bolts, only for something to do with his hands.

He had changed into long sleeves despite the heat and rolled them to the elbow anyway. One cuff carried a faint gray-green stain that had survived many washings. Mina knew the smell before she got close enough to confirm it: diesel, old spray residue, and the cheap bar soap from the camp showers.

"You don't have to do this," she said.

He did not jump. He had heard her coming on the shell. "That line worked better before noon."

"It still works."

"No. It sounds better than the truth. Different thing."

Rhea came down the path a few seconds later, not hurrying, which made her arrival feel more serious than panic would have. She had changed into field pants and tied her braid tight again. A pencil was tucked through it where another woman might have used a decorative pin. "Tell me what the assignment is."

Tomas set the wrench on the rail with more care than it deserved. "Support boat inside the outer line. Wind call, marker flags, route correction if the contract pilot starts freelancing. If things go clean, Marlene says it helps demonstrate cooperative conduct while the audit review stays open."

"Cooperative conduct," Mina said. "Jesus."

He gave her a look without humor. "That's what jobs sound like when they want you grateful."

Rhea stepped close enough to put one hand on the airboat rail between them. "If the wind shifts, you will be the first person with eyes on it. You need to refuse the interior pass. Not object. Refuse."

"And then what?"

"Then they write down that the district forced an unsafe application over an active rookery against field warning."

"You hear how much that sounds like a document and not a rent payment?"

The words dropped with no extra force at all, which chilled Mina more than shouting would have. Tomas loud could still turn back into joking or temper. Tomas quiet meant the sentence had already been lived with before it was spoken.

Mina moved around to the bow because she could not stand talking to his back. The shell path glittered under the low sun, white and mean. "They suspended you because your numbers got dirty. They bring you back for one ugly night, and suddenly you think they'll love you again."

"I don't need love. I need my badge not dead."

"You need your name not on this."

"My name's already on worse."

That landed where he meant it to. Her throat did not tighten; that would have been too generic for the heat. Instead the wet strap of her bra seemed to cut deeper into the skin under her shoulder blade, one more piece of material refusing to sit right. She looked at the mix tank behind him and imagined the route sheet from noon, the approved verbs, her own voice on the map helping refine the geometry of harm.

Rhea said, "The spoonbill chicks are edge-standing. If they flush, many of them don't know how to get back to center. They hit water, roots, each other. The bats will emerge through the aerosol because that is when they emerge. You know this."

"I know it," Tomas said. "I know it from before either of you had to tell me. That's not the part you're failing to explain."

He turned then, fully, and the tiredness on him had gone past metaphor. His lower eyelids were rubbed raw pink as if he had slept with his forearm across them. There was a nick at his jawline where the shave had gone wrong. He had bitten one thumbnail to a ragged crescent, which he only did when the money got bad enough to turn physical.

"The note on the skiff comes out in two days," he said. "The county health plan is what paid for the urgent care when I split my hand last year. Audit review turns into insubordination if I tell them no on emergency duty. Insubordination turns into done. Not suspended. Done. Marlene doesn't even have to say it that way. That's what it is."

Rhea's hand stayed on the rail. "I know exactly what it is."

"Do you?" he asked, and there was no grand cruelty in it, just a tired man's inability to leave a soft lie standing. "You've got grant money this month. She's got camp wages till Price finally throws her out for honesty or scandal or both. I've got a district that wants a body on a fan stand who knows these channels in the dark. That's the whole romance of it. I'm useful right when useful is ugly."

Mina said, "Don't make this about us wanting clean hands."

"No? Then what is it about."

She could have said the birds. The bats. The water. The eel surfacing later in poison. She could have said she did not want to watch him become evidence. All of it was true. None of it came out first.

"It's about them using your fear because they can," she said.

"That happens to be how work has always worked."

Rhea took her hand off the rail and tucked it under her opposite elbow, containing herself by inches. "Then hear me as a scientist, not as your wife and not as her anything. If you see the wind go inland, you stop the line. I don't care if somebody above you screams. I don't care if they fire you tomorrow morning. If you let that fog into the interior trees, I will spend the rest of this season documenting dead young from a damage pattern I warned them about in writing."

Tomas nodded once. "If I see the wind go inland, I call it."

"Call it and stop it are different."

"Rhea."

She held his gaze. Mina watched the two of them in the slant light and felt again what the slow paddle through the mangroves had left in her: intimacy without rescue, witness without delusion. Whatever this marriage was now, it still had room for this exact kind of devastating clarity.

Tomas reached for the wrench, then thought better of it. "I am not doing this because I think the county is right."

Low contraction on purpose. Emphasis. He heard it too, because he looked annoyed with his own mouth.

"I know," Rhea said.

"I'm doing it because if I vanish from that roster completely, they don't put me back on when the panic passes. They say the word reliability and that's the end of the sentence."

Mina stepped closer until she could smell the day's sweat dried in his collar. "Then let them call you unreliable for not poisoning a rookery."

His laugh came out small and ugly. "You say that like you haven't built half your life on taking the bad ride now and promising yourself you'll explain it clean later."

He had found the exact bruise and pressed.

She took it because she had earned it. But she did not move back.

"Maybe that's why I know the shape of it," she said.
