For a second he looked like he might touch her face or shove past her or do nothing at all. What he did was pick up the respirator and drape it around his neck.

"Stay upwind if you can," he said. "If you smell sweet instead of swamp, you're in it. Keep the kid out of the center channels unless birds start dropping. Then use towels, not bare hands. And wash everything twice after. Shoes too."

Instructions were the tenderness he had left to spend.

Rhea said, "Tomas."

He looked at her.

"If you can stop it, stop it."

He nodded again, once, and climbed onto the stand. The engine kicked after two tries and settled into a dirty growl that made the dock planks hum. He pushed off without flourish. The boat slid from the shade into white water and then around the bend toward the district shed, a county machine carrying a man who knew exactly what it could do.

Mina kept watching after he was gone, after the wake had already licked itself flat against the pilings.

"You love people in expensive ways," Rhea said quietly.

Mina looked at her. The sentence was not accusation exactly. It was diagnosis with weather in it.

"So does he," Mina said.

Rhea's mouth shifted, not quite agreement, not quite refusal. "Yes."

They walked back through the heat without touching. Above the mangroves the sky had gone the color of dirty brass, storm-light without storm, all pressure and no relief.

***

At dusk the camp sounded overprepared. Latches. Plastic bins. The slap of wet towels into buckets. Imogen on the office phone saying the words documented objection so often they lost grammar and became a pulse. The generator shed rattling under a load it did not want. From the bat lab came the high insect whine of chargers and Rhea's voice giving Beto instructions in careful, numbered pieces, the way she spoke when she needed someone young not to hear the fear underneath precision.

By full dark Mina was on the observation platform with a stack of milk crates, two towels, a headlamp set to red, and Beto vibrating beside her like exposed wire. He had changed into rain pants over his shorts, though the night was dry, and stuffed his pockets with nitrile gloves until he looked padded in the hips. A cheap saint medal flashed once at his throat when he bent to lift a crate, then disappeared under his shirt again. Mina had never seen him wear it before.

"My mom's going to say this is because people moved here and planted decorative bromeliads," he said. "She says all county policy comes down to new people wanting old bugs dead."

Below them the dark channels breathed rot, salt, and low tide. Farther out the rookery made its layered living noise: chicks rasping, adults landing heavy, occasional wing beats thudding through leaves so dense the birds themselves stayed mostly invisible. Mina knew where the trees opened just enough to show pale feathers when the moon rose. She knew which root tangles hid the blue if the blue chose to come. Tonight every piece of knowledge felt contaminated by advance warning.

"Your mom isn't wrong often," Mina said.

"No," Beto said. "That's one of her worst qualities."

He was trying. She loved him for trying.

At the edge of the platform Imogen stood with binoculars and a district radio Marlene had been too hurried to reclaim. She had traded field shirts for a yellow slicker she never fully buttoned, the old one with a torn cuff where a pelican had stabbed through years ago. She kept pushing her glasses back up with the heel of one hand and then forgetting they were on her head instead of her nose.

Rhea came up from the lab carrying two small animal carriers lined with dish towels printed with faded oranges. "If they start dropping live, bring them straight to the lab. Chicks, bats, whatever you can get without going under active rotor wash."

"That sounds like a clause written after somebody sued," Beto said.

"That's because it's essentially a clause written after somebody sued."

He huffed a laugh and then looked ashamed of it immediately.

Mina tested the wind with damp fingers. It moved from the Gulf side then stalled, uncertain. The night was so hot her shirt stuck under her breasts before the real work even started. Mosquitoes skinned the back of her neck. Somewhere toward the county channels an engine turned over once, died, turned again.

The first pass began as sound without shape. A far rotor thud under the insect chorus. Then another sound below it, flatter and meaner: an airboat fan finding pitch. The noises moved together through the dark in staggered alignment, one overhead, one lower in the channels, a machine duet designed by people who would never have to collect the result.

Imogen raised the binoculars. "Outer line," she said. "Still outside."

Mina saw the helicopter lights before she saw the body of it, red and white blinking over the mangrove edge. Farther downchannel a work light swept once across black water, caught a trunk, disappeared. The rookery sound changed by degrees into adults shifting in nests and a brittle burst of complaint from deeper in the trees, the whole colony listening harder than it could yet afford.

The helicopter made its run along the mapped edge. In the beam wash the aerosol barely looked like anything, just a temporary whitening in the dark, a thinness passing over thicker air. Then the smell hit half a minute later: sweet, oily, wrong. Not swamp, not salt, not any honest rot. Chemical citrus over hot metal.

Mina spat over the rail and tasted it anyway.

"Upwind," Tomas's voice cracked over the radio clipped to Imogen's slicker. "Hold that line. Hold it."

Imogen snatched the handset. "District support, this is Rookery Key. Your drift is carrying."

Static. Then Marlene, distant and flattened by transmission. "Reading acceptable. Continue monitor."

Rhea swore under her breath, rare enough that Beto's head jerked toward her. She was already looking out past the platform, toward the low black openings where the first bats should have started threading into the air. Mina saw one, then three, then twenty. Tiny bodies flicking above the mangrove skin, hunting because that was what twilight meant in their blood.

"This is exactly the hour I told them not to use," Rhea said.

The second pass came lower.

Whether the pilot misread the channel, or the wind took him, or somebody above Marlene decided close enough counted, Mina never knew. She only knew the rotor sound dropped until the platform boards shivered under her feet and the tops of the outer mangroves flattened like hands pushed down on a head. The aerosol line bent inward. Airboat fan roar surged from the side channel almost at the same time. Tomas's work light flashed once through leaves, too close.

Then the trees exploded.

Spoonbills came out of the canopy in a single violent lifting, too many bodies for the dark to hold. White and pink and raw gray chick-flesh, adults broad-winged and blind with panic, juveniles skidding into branches, nestlings pitched loose because the world had suddenly become wind and poison and noise. The sound did not resemble bird sound anymore. It was cloth ripping, a hundred wet coughs, branches beaten hard enough to sound like breakage. Mina saw one chick cartwheel through open air with its neck extended and no idea what flying was supposed to be. It hit a root arch and folded over it like dropped bait.

"Fuck," Beto said, and then louder, because once was not enough, "fuck, fuck."

Imogen was shouting into the radio. Rhea was shouting toward the lab for carriers no one could hear. Mina was already moving.

The stairs down from the platform were slick with old guano and fresh spray mist. She took the last three too fast and slammed her shoulder against the railing hard enough to leave splinters through her shirt. Beto hit the ground beside her with a crate and nearly lost it in the mud. The air below the platform tasted more chemical, heavier because it had nowhere to go. Above them the mangroves thrashed under rotor wash and fan blast, branches lashing one another while birds battered out through gaps too small to forgive them.

Another body fell. Then three.

Mina ran toward the nearest root line. The mud there sucked at her boots and released with obscene little kisses. A spoonbill chick lay on its side half in the water, old enough for pink to show under the fuzz but not old enough for dignity. One leg kicked straight out, then curled. Its crop had burst against something on the way down and half-digested fish pasted the white down under its beak. She wrapped it in a towel because Tomas had said towels and because bare hands would have felt like theft at that point. The chick was hotter than it should have been, heat trapped under chemical damp.

"Live one here," she shouted.

Beto answered from somewhere left of her. "I got two. No, one. One's bad."

Beto's "bad" was useless beside it. Mina found the second where he meant, hanging from a forked mangrove branch by one wing and opening and closing its beak on nothing. She had to climb two roots to reach it. When she touched the body, the wing had not snapped neat at the joint; flesh and skin dragged open in a wet red flap under the mix of blood and aerosol. The chick jerked once against her hand, a shocked, useless pulse.

She got it down. There was no saving it. There wasn't time for ceremony either. She laid it above the water line and marked the root with a strip of towel torn off in her teeth because they would need counts later, because even now part of her mind was inventory.

The helicopter banked back out over the dark, but the damage kept happening after the machine passed. Panicked adults circled, crashed, lifted again. Chicks leaned too far out of nests calling for parents already disoriented. Bats streaked through the suspended fog and dropped in small black commas onto the water, some floating, some still beating their wings until the tannin swallowed them.

Rhea splashed in from the boardwalk with another crate and a net bag. Her glasses were gone; she had tucked them somewhere safer or already lost them. "Anything breathing, lab first. If the eyes are sealed with residue, don't pry. Flush at the sink."

"How many carriers?" Mina asked.

"Not enough."

Tonight, shortage was the only honest number.

From the interior channel came the roar of an airboat cutting hard and then, abruptly, the ugly cough of an engine dropped to idle. A work light swung toward the shoreline. Tomas's voice, naked now without radio static because he was close enough, carried over the water.

"Pull him up. Pull the fucking bird up."

The helicopter did not immediately climb. Mina saw it skim the canopy again, rotor wash flattening a stand of buttonwood beyond the rookery edge. Then it jerked higher all at once, too late to count as mercy.

Tomas shoved the airboat into the mud at a slant and jumped off before the hull settled. He wore the respirator around his neck, not over his face, and whatever protective protocol the county had handed him had already failed at the level of human urgency. Spray glazed his sleeves. His eyes found Mina, then the towel bundle in her arms, then the root line littered with things too still to sort yet.

He came toward them through shin-deep blackwater. The airboat fan idled behind him, chewing the night to pieces even at low speed.

"Get back from the interior," he said. "Pilot's circling for confirmation."

Imogen reached them from the boardwalk at the same moment, yellow slicker flapping open. "You tell your pilot this site is under written protest and active casualty."

"I already told him," Tomas snapped. "He answers to the district and a contract."

Rhea thrust a carrier at him. "Then help, or get out of the wash."

For half a beat Mina thought he would refuse, not from malice but because refusal would keep the lines between roles intact. Instead he took the carrier. His gloved hand brushed the towel around the chick in her arms. He winced at the wet heat of it through fabric, as if the body burned.

"Jesus," he said softly.

"Yes," Imogen said. "Now earn the county payroll."

Imogen's words landed, but the root line finished the work. Mina watched his face lose the last defensive tightness and turn into something stripped and sick. Innocence had been gone long before tonight. This was the exact instant usefulness became guilt.

Above them another rain of bodies began, not from fresh rotor strike now but from nests destabilized by the earlier flush. Small fish too, silver flashing as they broke the surface and rolled belly-up in the drift film. The water near the roots wore a skin of rainbow under the headlamp glow.

"Beto," Mina shouted. "Left side, under the low fork."

"I see it."

He sloshed past carrying a live chick wrapped in his rain jacket. His face had gone so young with horror that the patchy mustache disappeared entirely. When he handed the bird to Rhea his hands kept moving afterward, opening and closing around nothing as if still trying to find the weight.

"It's breathing weird," he said.

"That's because its lungs aren't where they should be happy," Rhea said, and then, gentler because she heard herself, "Bring me the next one."

Mina turned toward a fluttering shape in the reeds and found not a bird but three bats knotted together by panic and spray, each one tiny enough to fit in her palm and furious enough to feel much larger. Their fur was slicked down. One had residue foamed at the nostrils. She used the towel corner to separate them while they bit at air. Rhea knelt in the mud beside her and took them one at a time with the deliberate hands she used for things that frightened her.

"Females," Rhea said. "Lactating."

The word hit like another body.

Tomas was behind them now, lifting fish and birds into separate crates because species still mattered even inside catastrophe. Over the radio at his belt Marlene kept asking for conditions. He did not answer the first time. On the second request he ripped the handset free.

"Conditions are dead juveniles in the root line," he said. Each word landed hard and level. "Conditions are bats down in active drift. Shut the pass."

Static. Then Marlene: "Copy non-target observations. Director requests completion of remaining outer block."

Tomas stared at the radio as if it had become an animal from the wrong century.

"No," he said.

The single syllable went nowhere big enough. It vanished into rotor noise, chicks screaming, Imogen swearing at nobody she could sue fast enough.

"Acosta," Marlene said, suddenly using the county voice, the one built for personnel records. "Complete your assignment."

Rhea looked at him.

Mina did too.

He did not answer for one second, two. Then the helicopter banked away for real, heading back toward the outer dark, either because someone else had finally made the same call or because the pilot had seen enough chaos not to want his own liability. Tomas let out a breath that shook the respirator against his chest. He clipped the radio back to his belt like it smelled bad.

"Help me move the live crate," he said.

There was no clean heroism in it. He had not stopped the first pass. He had maybe helped stop the next one. Both truths stayed.

They worked until counting stopped being possible in the field and became merely necessary later. Mina carried spoonbill chicks with legs bent wrong, with crops bruised black under the skin, with eyes sealed by residue. She found a little blue heron chick not meant to be here but here anyway, neck trembling against her wrist. She lifted mullet slick with spray and set them in a separate tub because fish kills changed reports. Beto gathered bodies with tears hanging off his jaw and no time to wipe them. Once he said "sorry" to a dead bat before putting it in a specimen bag, then looked furious that she had heard.

The noise at least gave the body something to brace against. The intervals were worse, when sound dropped away and the surviving chicks kept begging from the trees as if the adults would still return on schedule.

By the time true dark settled, the boardwalk stank of chemical mist, guano, fish blood, and the hot-plastic smell of headlamp batteries running low. Imogen had opened an incident notebook with her handwriting carved so deep through the paper it likely marked the pages below. Rhea's forearms were mottled with mud to the elbow. Tomas had spray drying on his sleeves in pale saltish tracks. The rookery itself sounded wrong now, patchy and startled instead of full.

Mina stood shin-deep at the edge of a pool under the platform and watched a spoonbill chick she had set aside finally stop moving. The water around her boots was studded with guano, leaves, and a drift of tiny white down that stuck to the sheen like ash.

This, she thought, was what institutions meant by manageable.

***

Near midnight the machines were gone, and the absence of them was almost as violent as the noise had been. The channels kept a long after-ring, as if metal had entered the mangroves and left their leaves remembering it. Somewhere offshore thunder muttered without committing to land. The air had gone heavier, not cleaner. Everything on Mina's skin felt coated.

The live chicks that still had a chance were in the bat lab under dim light, each in its own towel nest or shallow box. The room smelled of saline, guano, warmed feathers, and the sweet petroleum ghost of aerosol brought in on clothing. Rhea bent over the counter flushing one bird's eyes with a syringe while speaking into her recorder in a voice so controlled it seemed separated from the woman using it.

"Juvenile spoonbill, approximately fourteen to seventeen days," she said. "Respiratory distress. Right ulna fracture, likely impact. Residue visible on periocular tissue and nares. Collected zero-nine-forty-eight p.m., interior root margin."

The recorder clicked. She set it down, closed her eyes for half a second, then opened them and reached for the next syringe.

Imogen was in the office on speaker with somebody from regional wildlife, reciting numbers with the kind of exhausted formality that sounded like anger dressed for court. Beto had fallen asleep sitting upright against the outside wall with a specimen cooler beside him and his saint medal stuck to the damp hollow of his throat. One hand was still gloved. The other was tucked under his cheek like a child.

Mina could not stay inside any longer. The room's careful triage made her feel crazier than the chaos had. Outside, the boardwalk planks were slick with tracked mud and spray. The observation platform loomed above the dark like a bad idea built in lumber. She walked past it to the lower dock where they had started stacking the dead in lined fish boxes under loose lids because even grief needed containment against raccoons, heat, and paperwork.

Tomas was there hosing down the airboat he had brought back on district order after debrief, though the weak stream from the dock spigot only moved the residue around and made little poisonous rivers between the planks. He had stripped off the county overshirt and stood in a gray undershirt dark with water at the chest. His forearms looked scrubbed raw. The respirator lay on the dock beside him like a kicked muzzle.

He heard her and cut the hose.

"Rhea needs help in the lab," Mina said.

"I already gave her the counts I had."

"That isn't the same thing."

"No," he said. "It isn't."

Moonlight and dock light together showed what the night had done to his face. Spray had dried at the corners of his eyes. He had a streak of mud across one cheek he had missed on purpose or not cared enough to notice. The county badge was still clipped to his belt, plastic face turned inward against his thigh. It looked obscene there.

"Marlene says the director will write limited non-target impact unless state pushes for a site visit," he said.

Mina leaned one hand on a piling slick with old salt and fresh hose water. "Limited."

"Because the block got cut short and because they only count what gets documented. She's not wrong on procedure. She's rotten on meaning."

He set the hose down. Somewhere out in the dark a single adult spoonbill flew over late and alone, wings catching moonlight like torn paper. Mina watched it vanish toward the interior trees and felt the empty places under that flight more than the flight itself.

"Did you stop it?" she asked.

He understood the question exactly. She was not asking about the helicopter. She meant the larger thing.

"No," he said.

He left it naked of excuse, which hurt and helped in the same breath.

"I called drift after the first bend. They said continue monitor. I called active casualties after the second pass and got ordered to finish outer block. By then the damage was already in the trees." He rubbed the heel of one hand against his mouth. "I should've cut the line sooner. I kept thinking if I held the pilot off one more minute, if I talked clear enough on the radio, if I did it by steps, maybe I kept the job and the birds. That's a stupid man's dream."

Mina thought of documents again, of every stage-managed compromise she had ever accepted because total refusal seemed too expensive while partial resistance still let her imagine herself decent. The old annex logbook. The cooler. Tomas with fuel. Her own mouth on promises she had known were rotten at the core.

"It's not a stupid man's dream," she said. "It's the district's whole business model."

Something close to a laugh broke in him and failed.

He looked down at the fish boxes stacked three high. "There are more out there we didn't get. Tide will move them by morning."

"I know."

He nodded toward the far channel. "Marlene also said if state comes, I was reassigned temporary support and followed route command. Like she was already writing me into a sentence that keeps the district cleaner than me."

"She was."

"Yeah."

The hose dripped between them. On the dock, beside a fish box of dead juveniles not yet labeled, lay one of Beto's gloves turned inside out where he had stripped it off with his teeth. The cheap latex finger pointed toward the water like accusation.

Mina said, "You can take that badge off."

Tomas touched the clip but did not remove it. "Not yet."

Material reality reduced everything to timing. The county still paid. The paperwork was not finished. A man could know exactly what he had helped do and still need next week's deposit.

From the lab came a muffled clatter and Rhea's voice saying Beto's name in a tone sharp enough to wake the dead if any of them had been sleeping. Beto answered, groggy and ashamed, and Mina pictured him jerking upright against the wall, nineteen again instead of the old grief the night had tried to shove into him.

Tomas followed the sound with his eyes, then looked back at the water.

"How bad in there?" he asked.

"Bad enough that Rhea has stopped lying to herself about triage."

"Meaning."

"Meaning she's working on the ones that can still swallow."

He shut his eyes. The dock light made the wet lashes shine.

The channel below them carried a slow surface drift toward the rookery interior, a black mirror broken by leaves, by a silver fish turning once and not again, by chemical rainbow caught in the seams between current lines. Mina smelled mangrove rot under the spray for the first time since dusk. Honest stink coming back through the false sweetness. It should have felt like relief. Instead it made the contamination more intimate, as if the marsh had accepted the insult and was already being made to metabolize it.

Then the blue came up.

At first it barely registered: a thread under the surface, a seam of cold light moving through tannin the way a vein shows under dark skin when the lamp hits right. Mina saw it near the second piling and thought for one deranged instant that her eyes were still carrying afterimage from headlamps and radio LEDs. Then the thread curved. Thickened. Became a body.

"Tomas," she said.

He was already looking.

The eel rose slow through the poisoned skin of the water. Blue traveled along its flank in pulses, not lantern-bright but enough to stain the sheen around it with a ghost color no natural thing in these channels should have owned. It surfaced beneath a drift of white down and one dead mullet, lifting them both on the convex of its back for a second before they slid away. The head broke water just enough for Mina to see the blunt old intelligence of it, the mouth opening once at the air or the film or nothing she could name.

Beautiful was still the first word her body offered. Mina bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to raise blood and kept her hand flat on the slick piling.

The glow moved through runoff and spray beneath a dock where dead chicks waited in fish boxes and a county badge still hung from a tired man's belt. White down and rainbow film rode the curve of the eel's back, then slid loose when it dipped.

Tomas said, very quietly, "Look where it comes up now."

The eel rolled once under the piling shadow. Its light touched the underside of the dock and found the rivulets from the hose, the dripping chemical wash, the rainbow edges in the grain of the wood. Mina thought of the first time all three of them had watched blue move under the spoonbill frenzy, when even dread had still carried a charge close to joy. That version of the animal was gone whether the body was the same or not.

Behind them the lab door banged open. Rhea came down the walkway fast enough to rattle it, bareheaded now, braid half fallen apart, one glove still on and one hand bare. She took in their stillness and followed their gaze to the water.

The eel surfaced again, closer to the root line this time, weaving between two floating fish. Blue lit the undersides of leaves splashed low by tide. For a second the whole injured margin of the marsh wore the color.

Rhea stopped beside Mina. She smelled of saline and bird shit and the iron stink of stress sweat under repellent. "Of course," she said.

There was no awe in it, no disbelief either. The words landed like a field correction now that the clean story had finally died.

The eel slid forward through a clot of feathers. One spoonbill plume stuck along its back and rode there like a white scar before the water took it. Mina felt the sight enter her in the place obsession used to live and ruin the room forever.

No one reached for a camera.

No one said discovery.

From the lab a surviving chick gave a thin, stubborn cry. Beto answered with a soft "coming" that carried through the dark because at nineteen he still answered suffering like it might listen. Imogen's office voice rose again on the phone, now naming species and counts with the crisp fury of a woman preparing to be disbelieved. Over the water thunder moved closer. The storm would probably come too late to wash anything clean and just in time to spread it farther.

The eel turned under the surface, blue dwindling, then returned once more in a deeper channel pocket where the spray sheen had gathered thickest. There it hovered nearly vertical, head just below water, as if listening to the camp catalog its dead.

Mina stayed where she was, tongue worrying the cut she'd opened in her cheek. Whatever came next had to be said with the poison still on it. Above the dock, the spray kept drying tacky on the rails.

The light went down into blackwater and stayed there.


# Chapter 16

By morning the spray had dried tacky on the rails, a sweet chemical film that held whatever touched it: spoonbill feather dust, mosquito wings, gray ash from the generator stack, the soft down that had come off the dead chicks when Beto lifted them by the handful into lined fish boxes. Mina scrubbed her hands with the camp's orange mechanic soap until the skin in her palms looked peeled, but when she lifted them to her face she still got diesel, mangrove rot, and that pesticide sweetness underneath, as if the smell had moved into the small cracks around her nails during the night and found lease terms.

She had slept in pieces. Every time the stilts shifted under wind she had heard the impact again, not the spray run itself but the debris after it: wet little bodies on walkway planks, the slap of fish turning white-side up in the channel, Beto saying I got this one and then not sounding nineteen at all. Just before dawn she had stood by the sink in the dark and watched a spoonbill feather stuck to the drain pulse with the last of the rinse water. It had been almost beautiful, which made her want to break something.

Imogen sent for her at eight.

The main house office still carried last night's triage in a dozen petty ways. A stack of wet incident forms bowed on the desk where somebody had set them down with bloody gloves. The battery lantern on the file cabinet had burned a pale ring into the wood. One of the donor welcome baskets had been shoved under a chair and forgotten there, local citrus rolling loose among the brochures. Imogen stood at the open jalousie window in a field shirt she had buttoned wrong in her hurry, silver hair pinned up with the red golf pencil she used for guest counts. A smear of pink guano crossed one cuff. It looked less like filth than a stripe of war paint she had not had time to wipe away.

"Close the door," she said. "Please."

Mina closed it. The fan on the side table clicked once every turn, the blade housing bent from a storm three seasons ago. Imogen had a printed statement on the desk, two pages, single-spaced. Mina did not need to read more than the first lines to feel the shape of it.

Following last night's emergency mosquito-control action and the associated wildlife mortality event, Rookery Key Bird Camp is cooperating fully with reviewing authorities.

The words associated wildlife mortality event lay on the page like a pillow over a face.

"Who wrote this?" Mina asked.

"I did. Rhea corrected my wording on the section about the recorder. A board member corrected my wording on what not to say to donors." Imogen put both hands on the back of the chair and leaned into it, not sitting, as if the day had already denied her that much softness. "The Gulf Wildlife Board is sending an investigator by launch. A biotech-liability woman is piggybacking because somebody in Tallahassee heard the word luminous and saw a lawsuit. Elaine Porter is coming from the board because apparently no disaster is too ugly for oversight. If we do this cleanly, we might still keep the camp open and the rookery under people who know what it is."

Mina looked at the second page. Historic infrastructure material recovered during storm assessment may indicate a prior unauthorized release event connected to BrightCurrent legacy operations in the wetland system. There it was, flatter and safer than the thing itself. It said nothing about a cooler, nothing about Jonah Pike, nothing about the old blue photograph that had made her useful and then radioactive.

"You want me to say I found paperwork," Mina said.

"I want you to say what can actually help." Imogen's voice stayed low, too level to pass for calm. "The annex logbook matters. The badge matters. The current sighting matters. Your role in carrying a cooler for Jonah Pike years ago does not help the eel. It does not help the camp. It does not help the birds we still have alive on this island this morning."

Mina kept her fingers on the pages because the paper gave them something to do besides shake. Outside the open window a screen door banged once, then again harder. The sound lifted a picture into her head of Tomas coming up the steps after a night run, wet to the elbows, and she put it down before it had time to become longing.

"It helps explain why nobody should let me stand there as the discoverer," she said.

"You are not the discoverer. I am not asking you to be." Imogen finally sat, then stood again immediately as if the chair had been hot. "I am asking you not to confuse self-destruction with ethics. Those are different acts, whatever your generation likes to call bravery."

Mina gave a short laugh that had nothing in it. "My generation."

"Do not waste my time with tone. I have dead chicks in coolers and a donor who has already used the phrase communications opportunity in a text message this morning." Imogen pinched the bridge of her nose, then let her hand drop. "Listen to me all the way through. If you tell them about the cooler, if you reopen the photograph, if you make yourself the center of this, every person on that platform stops hearing corporate release and starts hearing disgraced guide lies again. They will build the whole day around whether you contaminated the evidence, whether you are confessing because you need absolution, whether the camp hired a known liability and therefore deserves whatever follows. BrightCurrent's old sins become your face. The county's spray becomes background noise. The eel becomes a scandal package instead of a protected animal."

Mina looked down at the statement again. There were phrases in Imogen's handwriting along the margin, grant-ready phrases, terms that could travel through a board call without making wealthy people put down their forks: legacy exposure, stewardship response, temporary access restriction, extraordinary event. Imogen had spent half her life learning how to make catastrophe legible to people with checkbooks. Mina could not even hate her for it. The camp's repair invoices were as real as the bodies in the freezer: twenty-seven thousand and change before hurricane inspection, plus Tomas's boat debt and her own monthly payment to the Gulf Wildlife Board, which had never once cared whether she ate before mailing them their money.

"You said yourself the donor story would start the minute they smelled money," Mina said. "If I let you flatten this, that's what they get. A miracle with some historic complexity. A contaminated wonder they can still brand."

Imogen's mouth tightened. "And if you do it your way, they get a woman they already know how to sacrifice. Do you imagine that protects anything? It gives them a culprit with a decent jawline and a prior record. It lets them package the past as one reckless employee, one charismatic older guide, one fallen field biologist, all neat enough to litigate. Meanwhile the camp goes under and the rookery gets managed by committee."

Mina pinched the damp paper until the top page bowed. The argument was not stupid. Jonah had always known the value of a story that narrowed blame to the person who could carry it prettiest. He had slipped it over Mina like a clean shirt years ago. She had worn it because it fit her hunger. Imogen was offering a different version of the same garment now: more respectable fabric, same trick in the seams.

"What are you actually asking me to leave out?" Mina said.

Imogen held her gaze. "The cooler. The fact that you cannot authenticate the old photograph as wild. Anything that turns this from present-tense evidence into a morality play about you."

"That isn't extra. That is the spine."

"No. It is the blood you want to smear on the frame because you think bleeding in public counts as honesty." The formal edges started to fray. "I defended you once. I took calls for you. I told people there was a difference between ambition and fraud, between bad judgment and malice. You know what I learned? Checks arrive when people can pretend the damage is already named, boxed, and far enough away to visit. This morning I have carcasses in freezers and inspectors on the water. I do not need you stepping forward to make yourself the exhibit too."

Mina let the papers go. They slid a little on the desk and stopped against a mug ring. She could feel sweat drying under her shirt in a salt crust left over from yesterday's heat and last night's fear. The office smelled of coffee gone bitter on the burner, damp paper, and the sharp ammoniac stink of guano baked into the boards below the window. Even here, inside bureaucracy, the place would not stop being animal.

"If I keep it clean," she said, "what happens next?"

Imogen answered too fast, which meant she had rehearsed. "We turn over the logbook through counsel, we separate the spray investigation from the legacy release, we freeze tourist access ourselves before the state does it badly, and we put out one statement that makes the camp look like the first competent witness on scene instead of the accidental landlord of a biotech mess. It buys me time. It buys Rhea permit cover. It keeps Tomas from being the only body they throw to the county. It might keep the eel from becoming a media feeding frenzy by lunchtime."

"And me?"

For the first time Imogen looked tired enough to be older than her posture. She lowered herself into the chair and stayed there. "If you stay on script, I can say you assisted in recovering evidence and acted appropriately once you understood what it was. I can keep you on through inspection at least. After that, I can try to move you into the back of the work, where nobody needs to point a donor at you. It is not redemption. Redemption is not on offer for any of us. But it is a life."

The offer loosened something mean and needy in her chest. A bunk through inspection. Wages another week, maybe two. A way not to get spit out onto the mainland with one duffel bag, a debt plan, and a fresh public disgrace to staple to the old one. The temptation in it was so ordinary she almost admired the precision. Nobody had to promise restoration. They only had to promise rent.

Her old visitor badge sat beside the logbook on the filing cabinet, sealed now in a clear evidence sleeve from the bat lab. The faded photo showed a younger version of her trying too hard not to look hungry. The plastic edge was clouded where floodwater had sat on it in the annex for years. She remembered slipping that badge into a pocket because Jonah had kissed her neck in the parking lot and told her nobody important ever obeyed every rule in the first pass. She had believed him because she had wanted the future fast. Want had always been easiest for other people to direct when it looked like work.

"If I take your version," Mina said quietly, "then the first true thing anybody hears today is still arranged around what will keep the lights on."

Imogen's chair scraped. "Of course it is. The lights matter. The roof matters. The permits matter. This absurd American appetite to pretend ethics happens somewhere above payroll is how places like this die."

"I know that."

"I do not think you do." She came around the desk now, close enough that Mina could see the broken capillaries around her nose from years of sun. "Because if you did, you would understand that there is no clean option left, only a ranking of harms. I am telling you which harm I can still manage."

Mina looked at the statement one last time. The pages had already begun to curl in the humidity. Somewhere under the office floor a pipe knocked. She thought of the eel rising through poisoned blackwater after the spray, blue along the body like somebody dragging a lit wire through oil. Nothing about the sight had felt clean. Nothing about it had felt marketable either until people on land began speaking around it.

"I won't give them a cleaner lie than the one that got me here," she said.

For a beat the room stayed very still except for the fan's bent click.

Imogen let out a breath through her nose. When she spoke again the professionalism came back so hard it sounded armored. "Then understand the consequence before you indulge yourself. If you go off script on that platform, you do it as yourself, not as a camp representative. Your contract ends the moment you make that choice."

Mina nodded. The nod felt small and irreversible.

"Fine," Imogen said. Her gaze dropped to the badge, the logbook, the printed statement, as if sorting which of them had done the damage. "Bring the originals. I would rather the state hear the worst from the person who can at least manage complete sentences."

She moved to the door and opened it for Mina as if dismissing a guest who had overstayed lunch.

"And Mina."

Mina turned.

Imogen's hand stayed on the knob. "Do not expect me to catch you in public."

The walkway outside lay bleached white in the sun. Chemical sheen flashed on the rail like varnish.

* * *

By eleven the observation platform had been rearranged into the kind of order people reached for when there was no order available. There were folding chairs in two rows. A card table held bottled water, legal pads, and a box of nitrile gloves somebody had set out by habit, as if whatever happened here next might still be solved by proper handling. The rookery beyond the platform sounded wrong. There were still spoonbills in the mangroves, still the raw hinge-squeal of adults landing and the occasional comic bark of a chick that had survived, but the noise came with holes in it now, blank spaces where whole small hungers had been yesterday.

Beto was the one lining the chairs, toeing each leg straight against the warped planks. He had changed into a camp T-shirt too big in the shoulders and kept an anatomy flashcard half tucked into the collar, forgotten there after breakfast cramming for the trade-school entrance exam he still pretended was a joke. One wrist showed the old kitchen burn white against his skin. He glanced at Mina, then at the evidence sleeve under her arm.

"You okay?" he asked, and because he was nineteen and the question was impossible, he added, "Stupid question."

"Not stupid," Mina said.

He nodded once, serious enough to age five years. "They moved the freezer to the shade. If anybody says the chick count sounds high, they can open it themselves."

That was Beto's detail, not the joke he might have made a month ago but the private line he had chosen to defend: the dead deserved witnesses who did not round down.

Tomas stood by the far rail under a patch of awning that did not throw enough shade to matter. He had shaved with bad concentration; a crescent of dark stubble still rode the angle under his jaw. The county fuel key was gone from his belt. In its place he kept turning a stainless hose clamp between his fingers, opening and closing the screw with his thumbnail until it clicked. He smelled faintly of detergent overlaid on engine grease, as if he had washed twice and lost to both substances.

When Mina stopped beside him, he kept looking out at the blackwater.

"You don't owe me a better outcome than the one you can stand," he said.

It was pure Tomas, stripped down to workman's language even now. He was not offering forgiveness or advice dressed as heroism.

"That's generous," she said.

"No, it isn't." The hose clamp clicked shut. "I've had enough of useful lies for one week."

Rhea came up from the bat lab carrying the recorder case against her hip, because she trusted even disaster less once it touched a shared table. Her braid had started to loosen, wisps dark with sweat around her temples. She had a stripe of blue Sharpie on the side of one thumb where she had been labeling contaminated sample bags, and the rubbing-alcohol smell from the lab rode ahead of her through the insecticide. She looked at Mina, at the logbook, then at the chairs.

"If you decide to say it, say it in the order that makes legal sense," she said. Her tone stayed flat enough to pass for technical instruction, which was how Rhea smuggled care into public space. "Act first. Documentation second. Interpretation third. Don't let them confuse the archive with the field evidence."

Mina almost smiled. "That's romantic."

"I'm not aiming for romance."

But the corner of Rhea's mouth moved, the barest acknowledgment of older bodies and worse rooms. Then it was gone. She took a chair on the side, set the recorder case at her feet, and folded her hands on her knee with the same economy she used before difficult grant calls.

The first launch came in low and too fast, flaring a wake against the pilings. The woman who stepped out first had a silver sea-turtle pin on her field vest and a cheap foam coffee cup wedged into the back pocket of her cargo pants. She moved carefully down the ramp because her left knee was wrapped under the khaki, the fabric stiff with brace straps. Her hair had given up on its bun hours ago. She was not polished, then, which felt like good news. Behind her came a smaller woman in office flats she already regretted, carrying a hard case and a legal pad with a bright pink grocery-store pen clipped to it. The pen had fake palm fronds floating in the plastic barrel. She kept twisting it as she looked around the camp, the way people did when they were translating real damage into a memo in their own heads.

The donor launch arrived a minute later with less competence and more sunscreen. Board treasurer Elaine Porter climbed out in linen trousers and expensive rain sandals, a paper hospital wristband still around one ankle because she had clearly forgotten it from some recent procedure or refused to treat her body as a thing requiring aesthetic continuity. She wore pearl studs anyway. The combination was so exactingly human that Mina trusted her less and more at once. Elaine had been sending Imogen money in ugly seasons for years. She also had the expression of a woman already deciding which version of a story her peers would find worthy of a gala table.

Introductions blurred. June Salcedo from the Gulf Wildlife Board. Mara Bix from BrightCurrent's insurer and liability review team, though she said the company's name the way people said mold in a rental house, with detachment meant to imply it belonged to somebody else. Elaine Porter from the board, here because the rest of the board had found excuses and she had not. Imogen performed the camp's side of the ritual with brutal grace, naming staff, thanking agencies, offering water nobody wanted.

The heat settled heavier as they took seats. Wind pushed chemical sweetness off the rail and then let it sit on everyone's skin again. June Salcedo opened her notebook and looked first not at Imogen but at the mangroves, counting sound the way field people did.

"Before we get into the bioluminescent report," she said, "I need a plain account of last night's spray event and current mortality numbers."

Imogen gave it to her cleanly: the time of county action, the wind shift, the preliminary count that was still rising, the access lanes closed to guests, the staff assisting with recovery. Her voice was polished enough to pass through microphones, but Mina could hear the grain underneath now, the scrape from not sleeping. She did not once look at Tomas while she described the spray. Tomas watched the hose clamp turn in his fingers and let her keep the lie of distance if not the lie of innocence.

Mara Bix took notes with the pink pen. Elaine Porter asked whether media had gotten wind of any of it. That was the donor question, quick as pulse. She wanted to know who knew, not what died.

"Not yet," Imogen said. "Which is one reason I asked everyone to come in person. We may have a narrow window to set responsible boundaries before rumor outruns fact."

There it was again, that phrase about responsible boundaries. Mina felt the sentence arrive the same way she had felt the script under her hand in the office, smooth enough to slide under skin.

Imogen nodded toward the evidence sleeve under Mina's arm. "During low-tide assessment of legacy infrastructure beneath the camp, Ms. Cordero recovered archival material from the abandoned BrightCurrent annex. It may indicate an unauthorized storm-release event in prior years. We also have partial contemporary audio and eyewitness testimony regarding an anomalous luminous eel in the rookery channels. The camp's position is that the current priority is habitat protection and evidence preservation."

The line had been built carefully, with enough truth to satisfy the room and enough omission to keep the frame intact.

June Salcedo turned to Mina. "Ms. Cordero, walk me through the recovery."

Imogen's eyes touched Mina's face for less than a second. It was not a plea now. It was a last warning.

Mina put the evidence sleeve and the logbook on the card table. The plastic ticked against the wood. Her palms were damp again. She dried them on her shorts and heard the bent fan in the office in her memory, that same stubborn click, as if the whole camp had become one machine with a failing part that still insisted on turning.

"I can do that," she said. "But if this is going into anybody's notebook, you need the part that makes me a bad witness before you decide whether I'm useful."

Elaine Porter frowned. "Imogen, is that necessary?"

Imogen did not answer. She held herself with the careful stillness of somebody carrying a full tray through turbulence, every muscle braced against the spill.

June Salcedo looked from one woman to the other and made a small gesture with her pen. "Go ahead."

Mina gripped the table edge. The rail tack under her fingers held a grit of dead insects.

"Years ago," she said, "I worked guide jobs around BrightCurrent facilities. I was sleeping with an older guide named Jonah Pike. He asked me to carry a cooler out for him. I did it."

The room did not explode. It tightened.

Mara Bix's pink pen stopped turning.

"Did you know what was in it?" June asked.

"Not when he put it in my hands. After that, yes. Fast." Mina swallowed. The taste in her mouth was soap and chemical drift and old shame, all of it practical. "I knew enough by the end of that day to understand I had moved something living that did not belong in a parking lot or a skiff or anywhere near a guide with a camera and a plan."

Elaine said, "I was told the old matter had already been adjudicated."

"Part of it was." Mina kept her eyes on June because June was the only one here being paid to hear ugly facts without converting them into funding language. "The rest of it lived in the gaps. The photograph that put my name everywhere and then ruined it later, the first luminous-eel image attached to me, I let people treat that photograph as proof of a wild find. I can't prove the animal in that image came from the creek. I can't prove it came from a tank. What I can prove is that I had already carried that cooler and I had no right to act like my hands were clean of the question."

The platform cinched tight around the silence; even the spoonbills beyond it sounded suddenly farther away.

Imogen's jaw set. Beto had stopped moving entirely, his hand still on the back of an empty chair.

June's pen moved once. "You represented the old photo as a wild specimen despite that uncertainty."

"Yes."

"In writing?"

"In interviews. In captions. In the way I failed to correct people because the failure was good for me." She gave a short breath that might have been a laugh in a better life. "I was not subtle about wanting what came with discovery."

No absolution arrived with saying it. The boards stayed hard under her sandals. Sweat kept sliding down her spine. Across from her, Elaine Porter's face settled into the polite blankness of the wealthy when disgust risked looking vulgar.

June said, "Why disclose it now?"

She wanted to say she had wanted the eel to save her and had wanted Rhea and Tomas to love her anyway, then watched chicks die under a spray somebody called management. Every version sounded vain when boiled down. Mina chose the smaller truth.

"Because this animal does not need another clean story told around it," she said. "And because the annex material changes what the current sighting means."

She slid the logbook toward June and set the old badge beside it. The badge photo flashed under plastic, younger Mina staring up with all her appetite still reading as promise.

"I found these in the intake tunnel under the camp at low tide," she said. "The logbook has maintenance notes, emergency cleanup language, release counts. This is not rumor or folklore. It is a storm evacuation. They dumped or dispersed engineered eels into the wetland system and wrote it down like they were clearing shelving before a flood."

Mara Bix reached for the book and then stopped when June did not.

"Release counts?" Mara asked.

"Marked as inventory adjustment in some places. Emergency dispersal in others." Mina could hear the pages in her head because she had read them by weak headlamp in air that tasted like rust and old water. "Enough animals that my one cooler does not explain what is in the creek now. My theft still matters. It does not explain the whole line."

Mara's face had the gray look of somebody hearing her week's work multiply. "Do you have photographs of the log entries before removal?"

"No. I took the original because the place floods and I wasn't leaving it there for another tide."

"Chain of custody is compromised," Mara murmured, half to herself.

Rhea spoke before Mina could. "It was already compromised by being underwater for years in an abandoned biotech annex beneath an active rookery." She kept her hands folded as she said it, voice measured, each clause set where she wanted it. "If you need a cleaner archive, build a time machine."

That was Rhea's way of choosing a side in public. She never reached for touch when exact contempt would do more work.

Elaine Porter leaned forward, sandal straps dark with sweat. "I am trying to understand the immediate practical implications. If this breaks as a revived fraud story tied to engineered contamination under our camp, private support will crater before any protection plan exists. Imogen, I assume there is a way to distinguish historic corporate behavior from present camp stewardship in whatever goes on the record."

The word stewardship spread across the platform with the same sweet burn as the spray.

Mina saw Imogen close her eyes for a beat. It was pain, not agreement.

June Salcedo answered before Mina could. "There will be no public language from this site until I finish the initial review. Ms. Bix, bag the logbook and badge. Ms. Porter, guest access to the channels is frozen effective now. Mr. Alvarez, I will need the mortality freezer after we finish with the spray questions. No one enters the restricted water or removes material from it without agency notice."

Elaine sat back as if struck, which was more satisfying than it should have been.

Mara Bix cleared her throat. "BrightCurrent no longer operates that annex. Liability pathways are not yet established. I need copies of every recording, every sighting note, and any coordinates associated with the current animal."

Rhea's eyes flicked once toward Mina, then back to Mara. "You will get the hydrophone file through the Board after it is logged. It is partial and corrupted. The witnesses can give you channel range, not a breeding site, and that is all we have."

June looked at Tomas. "For the record, identify yourself."

"Tomas Acosta. County mosquito district. Suspended airboat pilot, if we're being current."

Mina watched Elaine register the word suspended and file it under usable complication. Tomas saw it too. He smiled without humor.

"Then I want the spray parameters now," June said.

Tomas rolled the bent hose clamp once through his palm. "Altitude, wind, nozzle settings, route timing. I'll give you what I remember and the county log can cough up the rest."

The platform did not recover its earlier order after that. It had the feel of a net after something large thrashed through it. June took Tomas's spray numbers first, then asked for dates, names, exact recovery time, who else had handled the logbook, and where the badge had been found in relation to the tunnel entrance. Mina answered everything she could with field precision and none of the confidence she had once worn like weatherproofing. Noon sun crawled across the planks. Mosquitoes found the gap above Elaine's ankle strap and fed there until she slapped one hard enough to leave a blood smear. Beto brought out a second case of water and waited for June to follow him to the freezer. Rhea provided recorder details, contamination notes, and the general arc of the channels where they had witnessed blue movement under spoonbill activity, careful as promised with sequence and language. Mara bagged the logbook and badge in sterile sleeves that made the whole old crime look ridiculously domestic, like leftovers getting dated for a better refrigerator.

At one point June asked Mina, "Do you still possess any material connected to the original cooler incident?"

"No."

"Do you know where Jonah Pike is now?"

"Alive," Mina said. "Off this island. That's all I know for sure."

That was a lie of degree, not category. She knew the rough coast he had drifted toward, the kinds of charters he still sold, the age his face would be now. She did not offer it. One betrayal at a time had always been her limit, even at her worst.

When the questions slowed, Imogen took the opening they left behind.

Mina did not speak. She did not have to. The confession had already done what it came to do. The word discovery had left the platform an hour ago and had not come back. Nobody was talking about launch strategy now. They were talking about seizure authority, access freeze, state review, archived release language, mortality counts, liability, containment. The words were ugly, but they were useful.

Imogen stood. In the heat and stink and public ruin she somehow found the posture that had once made donors mistake exhaustion for command. She looked first at Elaine, then June, then finally at Mina.

"The camp will comply fully with access restrictions and evidence requests," she said. "We will also preserve our own records of last night's mortality and cooperate on the spray review. As for staff matters..." She stopped, and for the first time all day the polish cracked enough for feeling to show. What showed was injury, not softness. "As for staff matters, Ms. Cordero is no longer employed by Rookery Key Bird Camp as of this moment."

The sentence came off Imogen's tongue with the clipped force of paperwork already drafted.

Beto said, "Jesus," under his breath, then looked at Imogen like he was sorry for letting the word out and sorrier that it fit.

Imogen did not look at him. Her eyes stayed on Mina.

"Your contract was contingent on representing this camp in good faith," she said. Low contraction now, each word deliberately formal, because anger had pushed her into the register where she could keep standing. "You chose to use this meeting to disclose material that makes continued employment impossible. Turn in your staff key now. Keep the cot until morning and do not make another public scene on my walkway. If you need your final pay documented for the Board, I will have the paperwork ready by four."

It was cruel and not theatrical. That made it sting better. It was a firing shaped around payroll and keys, not destiny.

Mina felt the whole platform looking at her, not with one emotion but with a cluster of them that changed from face to face. Elaine's revulsion had relief braided through it now that the camp had cut her loose. Mara Bix looked almost grateful for the institutional distancing because it simplified her memo. June Salcedo looked tired, which Mina trusted more than sympathy. Beto looked furious in the helpless way of the young when adults choose procedure over mercy. Tomas's mouth flattened, and the hose clamp in his palm bent slightly where he had squeezed it. Rhea did not move at all, but the tendon at the side of her neck stood out hard as wire.

Mina reached for the lanyard at her throat and slid the staff key free of the ring. The cheap plastic tag had knocked against her sternum for weeks whenever she moved too fast. She had expected, stupidly, that handing over evidence would feel like sacrifice and handing over a key would feel administrative. The opposite was true. The logbook had always belonged to history once she found it. The staff key belonged to mornings: the kitchen door, the storage shed, the generator lock, the cheap authority of being allowed through first.

She set it on the table. The plastic clip left a wet print in the chemical film on the cloth.

"I'll clear the room in the morning," she said.

Imogen nodded once, accepting the key as if it were proof of something she had not wanted proved.

June cleared her throat. "For the record, employment status does not alter evidentiary relevance. Ms. Cordero, you may still be contacted for follow-up statements. No one associated with this site is to enter the restricted channels without authorization until we issue notice."

Tomas let out a breath through his nose at that. A laugh would have been too generous for the sentence.

Rhea picked up the recorder case. "Then issue it fast," she said. "The weather is not waiting for your notice."

June nodded, which in another room might have counted as respect.

People began standing because meetings ended that way even when nothing in them had ended. Chairs scraped. Water bottles rolled underfoot. Elaine was already on her phone by the time she reached the ramp, saying the words serious exposure in a voice meant to sound brave. Mara Bix kept her hard case hugged to her side like luggage through an airport she did not trust. June stayed behind long enough to ask Beto where the freezer was, and Beto, with a politeness so formal it bordered on insolence, said, "This way, ma'am," and led her down the steps.

That left Mina on the platform with Imogen, Tomas, and Rhea for one brief ugly pocket before the next task broke over all of them.

Imogen spoke first, quieter now, but no less hard. "I meant what I said this morning. You did not save us from anything simple."

"I know." Mina's voice felt scraped clean of performance. "That wasn't the point."

"That is exactly the problem with you."

Then Imogen went after the state investigator because that was her gift and punishment both: even wounded, she moved toward the paperwork.

Tomas looked at the bent hose clamp in his hand as if he had only just noticed what he had done to it. "Well," he said. "Now everybody's unemployed in spirit if not on paper."

It was almost a joke. It hurt like one.

Mina opened her mouth and closed it again. There was too much she could say to him and none of it would arrive unsoiled. He touched two fingers to the rail in a gesture so brief it might have been for balance, then headed down toward the dock where county trouble waited in a different uniform.

Rhea stayed.

Once the officials left, the platform kept only work sounds: the generator thrum below, June's voice, Beto opening the freezer lid. Somewhere out in the mangroves a spoonbill clattered into flight. The chemical film on the rail caught sunlight and turned it slick.

"You were legally sloppy in three places," Rhea said.

Mina barked a laugh despite herself. "Only three."

"I'm allowing for heat." Rhea adjusted her grip on the recorder case. Up close, the Sharpie stripe on her thumb had spread into her cuticle. "But the order held."

Act first, documentation second, interpretation third: Mina had followed it because Rhea had asked and because obedience to the right person, at the right late moment, could still feel like dignity instead of surrender.

"Did I just make your housing worse?" Mina asked.

Rhea did not pretend not to understand the question beneath the question. She looked out past the platform, toward the channels where no one was supposed to go now.

"Yes," she said. "Probably."

When Rhea turned back, her face held no recoil, no polite distance, none of the leaving Mina had already started to brace for.

"That does not make it wrong."

She touched Mina's forearm with two fingers, exactly once, in full view of nobody who mattered anymore, then took the recorder case and went down the steps toward the bat lab, toward contaminated files and grant trouble and a life that had not chosen ease either.

Mina stayed where she was after everyone else had found a direction. Heat climbed. Her job was gone. By evening her name would move through email chains and board texts and whatever shallow legal language people used when they wanted human mess to harden into categories. Some version of disgraced again would attach itself to her before the sun went down. That part was already done.

Below the platform, the blackwater shifted under mangrove shade, mirror-dark until a breeze wrinkled it. No blue showed, and no miracle announced itself on cue. The only thing the confession had changed immediately was the vocabulary on land. The eel was not a windfall now, or a savior, or a donor deck with a mascot. It had become evidence, contamination, protected risk, agency review. They were ugly names, but she could live with ugly names if they bought the creature one more week of not being turned into a logo or a specimen tray.

By the time the launch engines coughed back to life and carried the officials away from the platform, no one had called the eel a discovery in hours. What stayed behind was a thinner kind of noise: work, damage, official names. That, for now, was the nearest thing to protection Mina knew how to give.


# Chapter 17

The camp had gone quiet in the wrong places.

After spray night there were still spoonbills in the outer trees, still the wet iron racket at dawn when the surviving adults lifted and settled and fought over what nests were left, but the deep constant nursery noise had thinned so much that Mina kept waiting for it to rise again like weather and it never did. The silence had shape now. It pooled under the main house. It clung to the boardwalk where Beto had spent half the afternoon hosing guano, fish scales, and pesticide film out of the slats, his burned wrist wrapped in a strip of blue shop towel because he would not stop working long enough to bandage it properly. It sat inside the little staff room that had been hers until yesterday while she folded shirts into a dry bag that still smelled of diesel, wet rope, and the three of them.

Imogen had taken her staff key after the confession and done it in the voice she used for weather advisories, low and precise and furious enough not to need volume. Mina was out as an employee. Housing would be sorted in the morning. For one night she could keep the cot and not make another public scene on the walkway. That was as close to mercy as Imogen had come.

Mina had packed badly. The field pants went in first because they were closest at hand, then notebooks, then a braid of charging cords she probably did not need, then the old flashlight with the cracked tailcap, then nothing for a while because the room had begun to look too much like evidence. On the wall the pale clean rectangle where the camp schedule had hung made her think of tags on dead birds. Outside, rain moved through in a light hiss, not a storm yet, only the early-season kind that laid a skin over everything and left the wood sweating after.

When Rhea knocked, she did it with two bare knuckles and no apology.

"He's at the kayak rack," she said when Mina opened the screen. Her braid had half come loose at the nape. There was a black streak of Sharpie along the side of her thumb from labeling sample bags, and she smelled faintly of rubbing alcohol under the mangrove mud. "If we're going, it has to be now."

Mina looked past her toward the dark walkway. "Where are we going."

Rhea held her eye for a beat that would have counted as dry amusement on anybody else and counted as strain on her. "Don't waste my time with performative stupidity."

Mina almost smiled. She did not have much left in her for shame, but she had enough to feel the old quick sting of being read clean through. "You think Tomas wants me there."

"No," Rhea said. "I think he wants the thing itself there. That's different."

Rain ticked on the tin over the generator shed. Somewhere below the stilts, mullet snapped at the surface. Mina glanced back into the room at the open dry bag, at the rolled shirts, at the place where her camp polo had hung before she stripped the logo patch off and shoved it in the trash because she could not bear the soft little hypocrisy of folded institutional clothing. If the state people came in the morning, if county enforcement came with them, if BrightCurrent's lawyers had already started calling the right offices, tonight was the last night before the whole creek turned into a case file. She knew that because she had helped do it once before, years ago from the other side of the badge. First you named a thing. Then you narrowed its world to coordinates, chain-of-custody language, transport temperature, tissue viability. Care and capture wore the same gloves for a while.

"Do I need anything?" she asked.

"No containers," Rhea said. "No knives. No spare batteries unless they're already in the light. He was specific about that."

"Christ."

"Yes."

Mina reached for the flashlight anyway, then stopped with her hand over it. Rhea watched that decision and said nothing. The silence between them was not healed. It was only inhabited now. Mina left the flashlight on the shelf, zipped the dry bag shut, and followed Rhea out into the damp dark.

The screened walkway gave under their weight with the soft spring the whole camp had when the tide pushed high under the pilings. Rain had brought the water level up just enough that the lower mangrove roots no longer looked flayed, only exposed and ugly instead of skinned clean to the air the way they had two weeks ago. Through the gaps in the screen Mina could see the bird camp in pieces: the donor deck slick and empty; the kitchen light on, where Beto had likely fallen asleep over inventory again because fear made him talk more until it made him quiet all at once; the bat lab window lit blue-white around stacks of charging units and labeled bags; the dark square of Imogen's office, where somebody had probably already started drafting careful emails about cooperation and preservation and regrettable events.

At the bottom of the steps the air changed. Above the walkway the camp always smelled built, a mix of bleach, old lumber, frying oil, damp screens. Down here it opened into mud, salt, mangrove rot, and the sweet chemical edge that still came and went depending on the wind. Mina hated that smell more than the dead things. Dead things belonged somewhere. Chemical drift sat on top of the world like a lie.

Tomas was waiting by the old kayak rack where the guest boats were chained in season. He had a flat-bottomed skiff half in the water and half on the mud, the kind of cheap fiberglass thing people bought for canals and swore they would maintain. He had stripped it down to almost nothing: one pair of oars, a push pole with the grip taped over in black electrical tape, a short painter, a red-lensed headlamp clipped to the thwart, and a dented plastic bucket that held two water bottles and a coil of line. No net. No cooler. No baitwell. No engine bracket even.

His county shirt was gone. He wore a gray T-shirt darkened at the chest from rain and a pair of work shorts stiff with dried salt. The white rectangle from his suspended county ID badge had left a pale line on the sun-browned skin above his pocket. He was worrying at a split thumbnail with his teeth, not looking at either woman.

"You took your time," he said.

Rhea answered before Mina could. "She packed."

"What'd she pack for."

"Tomorrow," Rhea said.

That made him look up. Not at Mina first, but at Rhea, at the shape of that word. Tomorrow. The morning after consequences. The morning after his suspension stayed a suspension and Mina's firing stayed a firing and none of the three of them got to pretend the confession had burned anything clean.

Mina stepped closer to the skiff. "Where'd you find this."

"My cousin had it upside down behind his trailer with a wasp nest in it." Tomas gave the hull a shove and the skiff slid the last foot into blackwater with a sucking sound. "It floats. That's all I need."

"If county sees you in closed water again-"

"County can kiss my ass from shore tonight." He said it flat, without heat. That was worse than if he had barked. "Listen to me before we go. No phones out unless Rhea says. No light unless we have to. No touching anything if we find it. No taking anything even if it looks dead, loose, easy, obvious, God-given, or like the exact thing that might save your name. If we can't do that, we stay here."

The old impulse in Mina rose so fast it almost embarrassed her. It came up uglier than anger and faster. The need to say that she was not stupid, that she understood field protocol, that nobody had to talk to her like a thief who needed her hands watched every second.

She bit that off because she was a thief who needed her hands watched every second.

"Fine," she said.

Tomas kept his gaze on her a moment longer, gauging whether fine meant fine or only not-yet-arguing. Then he nodded toward the bow. "You up front. You know how to keep your weight quiet."

"That's romantic," Rhea said.

"You want romantic, we can all go back to bed mad."

The line landed dry enough that it might have been a joke in another month. Tonight it came down between them and stayed there. Rhea stepped into the skiff first with the grant-issued handheld GPS looped around her wrist and her phone sealed in a clear dry pouch against her thigh. Her field clothes were the same ones she wore on survey nights, soft from washing and patched at one knee, but she had changed out the camp sandals for old neoprene booties because she never trusted mud to stay where it belonged. Mina noticed stupid things under pressure. The frayed edge of Rhea's braid. The place where Tomas had rewrapped the push pole with tape starting from the middle instead of the end, which meant he had done it in the dark or angry or both.

Mina got in after her. Tomas shoved them off and climbed last, taking the stern with the pole. The skiff rocked once under the changed balance and then settled.

No engine meant no immediate authority. No propwash, no county racket, no hard smell of gasoline blooming behind them. For a few seconds the quiet felt wrong enough to make Mina's body search for the missing vibration. Then Tomas planted the pole in the mud, leaned into it with his broad mechanic's shoulders and old prop-scarred forearms, and the skiff slid forward as if the channel had accepted a condition.

They moved under the camp lights and out beyond them. Rain needled the surface and stitched their wake shut behind them almost as fast as it opened. The world narrowed to wet mangrove walls, the soft thunk of pole to hull, and the distant thin chaos of birds. Without engine noise Mina could hear everything else the creek had been trying to tell them for months: shrimp ticking in the roots, little fish worrying at the tannin film, the drip from leaves into water already full, the occasional cough of a night heron, Tomas's breath when the pole bit too deep and he had to wrench it free.

She had first wanted him for access. That had been the cleanest truth in the mess, and maybe because it was the cleanest it had felt easiest to survive. Then desire had made itself inconveniently real. Then tenderness. Then the ugly arithmetic had come in and named every favor for what it cost. She could still remember chapter and verse of the fight in the cabin after the annex logbook, the exact angle of Tomas's mouth when he asked if she had ever wanted him outside the use of his keys. She had not answered quickly enough. That had been answer enough.

Now he poled them through the dark as if the creek had always lived in his wrists. He knew where the bottom rose by the change in pressure through the fiberglass. He knew when to use the oar instead of the pole because a shell bank would ring. He knew which gaps in the roots were channels and which were traps. Mina hated and wanted the competence in him with equal force. Some nights she still woke with the memory of his hand warm over her lower belly, steadying her against an airboat gunwale. Other nights she woke hearing the spray rotors.

Rhea sat mid-skiff with one hand on the GPS and the other flat on the seat beside her to feel the shift of balance. Under strain she seemed to draw everything inward until even her silence had edges. When the bow nosed too close to an exposed root she laid two fingers on Mina's calf, just above the old prop scar, not as comfort but calibration. Weight left, the touch said. Mina shifted. The skiff cleared.

They went by the first ruined nesting stand without any of them speaking. Even in the dark the damage read. Branches that should have sagged under nest mass instead held ragged bundles like overturned baskets. One spoonbill carcass had snagged high enough that the rain had cleaned most of it down to pale ribs and a wet pink flap of wing. Lower, in the fork of a mangrove, a nest cup held nothing but spray-stuck down and a single chick leg no thicker than a pencil. Crabs had gotten into the roots. Mina heard them clicking.

The smell changed again and made the back of her tongue ache. Mud, guano, rot, then that medicinal bite laid over all of it. She had spent most of spray night helping Rhea rinse live birds with distilled water and crush ice in towels and bag dead ones with labels because once disaster became official it demanded handwriting. In the worst hour Tomas had not been there. He had been out in the county airboat under orders he should have refused and maybe could not have afforded to. By the time he staggered back in, the rookery already looked like a place after shelling. There were chemicals in his arm hair. She had wanted to hit him and hold him at once. She had done neither.

Tomas set the pole and let the skiff glide under a low branch. "Rhea."

"What."

"Tell her about the morning."

Rhea kept her eyes on the dark ahead. "State wildlife called twice after dinner. They want the hydrophone card and any route notes from the week before the spray event. County wants all private watercraft off the restricted channels until the mortality survey is complete. BrightCurrent's insurer has retained counsel. That part came through the university, which is a charming misuse of everyone involved."

"So they know enough to want the place," Mina said.

"They know enough to want anything that can be fenced and billed."

Tomas snorted once. "If they can call it a recoverable line, they'll strip these roots with dip nets and call it stewardship."

Rhea said, "Yes."

The word sat between the mangroves like a hook.

Mina looked down at the water sliding along the hull. Rain made dim silver crowns where it struck. Underneath, the black held its own depth, mirror-dark until the bow disturbed it and then full of brown veils, minnows, broken reflections. Months ago that black had felt like possibility. Tonight it looked like bruised skin.

"Then why are we going?" she asked.

Neither of them answered immediately. Tomas poled them through a narrow cut where the roots reached overhead and turned the rain into slow separate drops. Rhea lifted the GPS, checked the muted screen, then lowered it again without speaking.

It was Tomas who finally said, "Because I want to know what I helped nearly kill."

There was nothing grand in it. No plea for forgiveness. He said it with the flatness he used for fuel numbers and tide tables. The line struck harder for that.

Rhea looked at Mina then, not rescuing him, not rescuing her either. "And because not knowing won't protect it. Only the wrong people prefer ignorance."

Mina let that sit. She had spent years believing the opposite, or pretending to. If nobody could prove the original fish was tank-bred, if nobody could pin the old photo to the cooler run, if nobody could track her exact omissions, maybe ambiguity would protect her.
