Ambiguity protected institutions better than people. She knew that now. She had stood in front of donors and investigators and told the room what she had done, and the truth had not absolved her so much as pinned her properly to her own life. Maybe that was all truth ever did.

Still, one part of her kept grinding. If the line was breeding here, truly breeding, not only surfacing like a rumor under spoonbill frenzy but entering the creek's actual reproductive mess, then there was a fact in it big enough to reshape everything. Not save her. She knew better than that, or almost knew better. But big enough to make the whole scandal look different under the light. Big enough to say the animal had become more than BrightCurrent's trash and more than her theft and more than a donor brochure miracle. Big enough, maybe, to turn shame into expertise again.

That was the part of her Tomas did not trust, and he was right.

They traveled for what felt like an hour and was probably less. The channel widened, then pinched, then opened into a basin roofed by mangroves and one battered gumbo-limbo leaning white in the dark. Here the spoonbills sounded farther off, their colony noise broken and uneven. Rain thinned to mist. Mosquitoes found them the minute the air lost motion. Mina slapped one off the back of her hand and left a smear of her own blood.

Tomas rested the pole and took up an oar, feathering it so lightly the water barely noticed. "From here on, don't talk unless you need to."

Mina nearly asked from where on. Then she heard it.

A faint crackle came from under the roots ahead of them, like somebody crumpling cellophane underwater. She had heard it once before under the spoonbills at the height of breeding season and thought it was wings reflected through water. Tonight it came in intermittent little bursts, close enough to tighten the skin along her arms.

Tomas let the skiff drift. Rhea lowered the GPS entirely and closed her hand over it until the screen went dark.

The basin had been hit hard. One mangrove on the left side had gone gray at the leaves, not dead yet but failing from the tips inward. Dead fry ringed the sheltered water under it, so small their bodies read more as silver commas than fish. Farther back in the roots something larger had bloated and sunk half under. The smell was not strong enough to identify, only enough to register as sweet rot in wet heat. Above them an abandoned nest sagged open to rain. Feather dust had glued itself to the bark.

Vigil, Mina thought, and surprised herself with the word. Hunt belonged to appetite. Survey belonged to the report waiting outside the basin. Vigil was what people did beside damage when repair was no longer a clean lie but leaving was worse.

Tomas pointed with two fingers to a seam in the roots on the right where the water deepened under a tangle of exposed props. Mina leaned without thinking. The skiff gave a warning creak. Rhea's hand found her calf again, firmer this time.

The first light was so faint she thought it was strain in her own eyes. Then it gathered.

Blue, but not the hard electric ribbon of the first coil she had seen from Tomas's airboat months ago. This was milkier, internal, a soaked kind of glow rising through tannin as if the water itself had taken a bruise and started shining through it. It lived under the roots and not in open water. It thickened, faded, came back. Crackle again. Something moved.

Tomas took the pole and eased the skiff one yard closer, no more. They were tucked now in a pocket where three prop roots arched out of the bank and met again below the surface, making a cage. Inside it the water held still despite the rain.

Mina had spent enough years looking at spawn and fry and egg masses that her body knew before her mind did. The light was punctate, repeated, too scattered for one body alone.

She whispered, "Jesus."

Rhea did not answer. Her breath had gone shallower. Under pressure she often became plainer, the careful clauses burned off until only the load-bearing words remained.

Mina bent lower and saw them.

They were fixed along the underside of the roots in a clear mucous braid, dozens at first glance and then scores once her eyes adjusted, each egg no larger than a lentil, translucent enough to show the pale bead of living blue suspended within. Some glowed stronger than others. Some were only cloudy pearls with the light barely waking in them. Between the clusters, milky threads drifted and caught and drifted again. Neither algae nor any fish spawn she knew from these channels. The whole root pocket looked as if somebody had stitched stars into spit and anchored them where the tide could not tear them free.

And there, moving through them with impossible slowness, was the parent line. Not fully visible, never giving them the whole body at once, only the long narrow passing of it under the eggs, blue running under black skin, then gone behind the roots, then back again. The light in the eggs thickened when it passed. Mina could not tell whether the animal was guarding, tending, spawning again, or simply trapped in the same sheltered instinct the roots offered. BrightCurrent had made a sentinel species. The creek had made whatever this was.

She knew, with the nauseating totality of field certainty, that rumor, sighting, singular specimen were all behind them now. The line had entered the place.

All the air seemed to leave the basin at once.

Rhea reached slowly for the red-lensed headlamp, clicked it on once, and held the beam low across the water so it would not flash the whole channel. Under red the blue only deepened. The eggs remained their own light. Rhea was already watching like a scientist, counting, tracing attachment points, seeing how the roots had sheltered the clutch from current and spray alike.

"Depth maybe twenty centimeters in the pocket," she murmured before she could stop herself. "Attached along red mangrove prop roots. Adhesive matrix. No obvious fungal bloom. Repeated clusters extending-"

She cut the sentence off. Mina heard why a second later. Those were the first lines of a report. The first steps of a seizure.

Tomas said, very quiet, "Don't turn it into a shopping list."

Rhea swallowed. "I know."

The adult line moved again, closer this time. The water under the eggs swelled. Blue light slid over the root bark and onto Mina's wrist where she had braced herself on the bow. For one ugly reflexive second she wanted to scoop. Nothing more complicated than that. Cup both hands under one cluster, lift, run. There were no jars aboard, no cooler, no batteries to keep it stable, no way to make that act anything but theft, and her body wanted it anyway with a force that felt older than reason.

Tomas saw her shift and caught the front of her shirt in his fist.

He did not yank. He only held.

"No," he said.

The word went through her like a hook set clean.

She stayed bent over, heart knocking stupidly in the wet dark, aware of his hand in the damp cotton between her breasts and of Rhea beside her and of the blue eggs under the roots and of the part of herself that could still, after all this, imagine a specimen as salvation. Shame came not as heat but as cold water under the ribs.

"I know," she said.

"You do."

She nearly lied because lying had once been the fastest way to keep love from turning into contempt. Then she heard the crackle under the roots again, saw the adult line's light pass through the clutch like breath through a lantern, and the lie died where it started.

"Not enough," she said.

Tomas let go of her shirt. His fingers stayed curled after, as if they still held fabric. "That's honest, at least."

Rhea had narrowed to pure procedure. Mina looked over and saw that she had the handheld GPS back in her palm, screen on low brightness, a blinking pin marking the basin. Habit. Precision. Insurance. The kind of fact people used to pry a living place open.

"You marked it," Mina whispered.

"Of course I marked it." Rhea's voice stayed quiet, but now it had that deliberate edge exact wording gave her when she was angry enough to be careful. "I've spent two years teaching myself not to trust memory where a coordinate exists."

"If you keep that, they'll get it," Tomas said.

"If I don't keep it, I may lose my work."

There it was, plain as mangrove bark. Ethics, yes, but also money. Permit legitimacy. The university stipend that paid in monthly increments large enough to matter. The lodging it protected. The whole thin scaffolding of a field life that could vanish if she started choosing omission where a grant contract expected data. Rhea had never asked the world to confuse her with a saint.

Mina looked from the GPS to the roots and back. Blue light pulsed up through the eggs, patient as rot, patient as weather. She could already hear how the room would change if Rhea kept the coordinate. Investigators asking for full metadata. Imogen arguing for controlled access and donor leverage. County men in flat-bottomed skiffs with seine bags. Somebody from BrightCurrent pretending concern while pricing out legal exposure. Somebody else wanting embryos, tissue, survivorship rates, reclamation language. Everybody calling it care because ownership sounded ugly in public.

And beneath all that, the smaller dirtier thought that still wanted one clean thing for herself. One location. One undeniable fact. One point on a map that would outlast the stories people told about her.

She hated that thought for surviving. She hated it more because it was not fully wrong. The coordinate did matter. Exactness was what made knowledge usable. Exactness was how the world moved from rumor into public fact.

But she knew exactly what kind of public had its hands on this creek now.

"If you keep it," she said, and found her voice sounding flatter than she expected, "they won't stop at one clutch. They'll peel this whole bank open."

Rhea's mouth tightened. "I know what enforcement does."

"Do you know what donors do?" Tomas asked. Still quiet. That was his meanest register now, the one the audit had stripped down to almost nothing. "Do you know what county does when something gets named and budgeted. They don't arrive with reverence. They arrive with coolers and a reimbursement code."

"I know," Rhea said again, harder.

The eggs brightened all at once, independent of the headlamp. The adult line was there again, just under the foremost cluster, body curving through the root pocket in a long blue flare. This time Mina saw enough of the head to know it was not alone. A second slimmer body threaded in from deeper dark, dimmer but unmistakable, and crossed it once in the cramped water. The light rippled through the suspended clutch. There was no clean word for what the sight did to her. Wonder was too easy. Terror too narrow. It felt like being given proof and accusation in the same wet mouthful.

Rhea sucked in breath through her teeth. "There are two."

"Yeah," Tomas said.

Mina had not realized until then that he had seen this kind of shelter behavior before, or at least guessed it. She turned to look at him. Rain had darkened his lashes into spikes. His face in the red spill of the lamp looked carved down to fatigue and something harsher than grief. Penance, maybe, if penance could have calluses and tool debt and county suspension language folded into it.

"You knew this pocket," she said.

"I knew where the water stayed cold under roots after the spray. I knew where fish went that wanted hiding. Same rules for most living things." He kept his eyes on the clutch. "I didn't know this was here. I hoped."

"Why'd you bring us."

This time his answer came fast. "Because if I only told you, you'd still be able to say later that you didn't really see it. And if I only told Rhea, she'd write it down in the proper tense and then hate herself for it. I needed all three of us stuck with the same ugly fact."

The basin held them. Mosquitoes whined around their ears. Somewhere far off a spoonbill barked once and got no answer.

Mina thought of the first luminous eel photograph, the one that made her and wrecked her. The grainy blue body in a handled moment. The way everybody had argued afterward not over the animal exactly but over custody, provenance, whether she had the right to the image, whether the image had the right to exist, whether a thing glimpsed in the wild stayed wild once a human being framed it properly. She had spent years wanting the creature to testify that she had not lied, not all the way, not about wonder at least. Wanting the animal to stand up in court for her appetite.

The eggs under the roots did not care what she needed from them.

"Take one picture," the bad part of her said aloud before she could stop it. "No metadata. No flash. Just enough to know later we didn't invent this."

Neither Tomas nor Rhea answered right away, and in the pause she heard how naked the request was. Personal, not scientific or institutional. She wanted a relic.

Rhea turned to her first. "And where would you keep it."

"On my phone."

"Which they may seize."

"I could strip the location."

"From your memory too?" Rhea asked.

The question did not sound cruel. That made it worse.

Tomas gave a short breath that might have been a laugh if there had been any humor in him tonight. "If the picture is good, it's bait. If it's bad, it's still bait. All it has to do is make the right asshole think he's one boat ride away from owning a miracle."

Mina looked back at the eggs. The light had dropped to a gentler pulse. In the red dimness she could see some of the outer cluster had clouded over with silt, while the inner ones glowed clean, protected by the roots' angle from direct wash. Survivorship had a geometry. So did destruction. The spray had thinned the rookery, killed chicks, drifted into fish nurseries and bat corridors, and still this pocket kept working at life through the damage.

"Then what do we do," she asked, softer. "Just know it."

Rhea's face changed by fractions. The anger thinned. The precision stayed. "Not just know it. Keep it unhelpful."

"That sounds like cowardice."

"No," Rhea said. "Cowardice is handing a living place to institutions because evidence feels cleaner than responsibility."

Mina opened her mouth, shut it again.

Rhea lifted the GPS a little. Rain beaded on the screen. The blinking point sat there, innocent as any other point. A coordinate was one of the least dramatic things in the world until you understood how many kinds of violence could ride inside it.

"If I delete this," Rhea said, and now her voice had lost even the last of its dryness, gone plain the way it did when she was most exposed, "I'm doing it with both of you as witnesses. I'm not interested in later revision. And I'm not interested in one of you deciding six months from now that restraint would've been more convincing with a private backup."

"I didn't make one," Tomas said.

Rhea kept looking at Mina.

Mina could have lied. She had no coordinates, no hidden recorder, no second phone in a dry bag, no specimen bottle tucked in the waistband of her shorts. She could have said of course not and left it at that. Instead she heard herself say, "I thought about it before I came down the walkway."

Tomas looked at her sharply. Rhea did not.

"And now," Rhea said.

The basin seemed to wait with her.

Mina watched the blue clutch under the roots. Watched the second adult thread back through the dark and vanish. Watched the rain widen and then smooth the circles on the pocket water. Behind her eyes rose every stupid fantasy she had been feeding for months: the paper that would straighten her name back out, the interview where somebody finally asked a different question, the grant call, the relief of not having to flinch when strangers recognized her from the old scandal. Vindicated. The word felt lacquered and false in this water.

Blue kept pulsing through the eggs whether her name ever straightened out or not. The creek owed her no testimony. The blue line under the roots was only alive.

"Now I think if I carry one more thing out of this water," she said, "I stay exactly the same."

Tomas looked away first.

Rhea nodded once. Her thumb moved over the GPS controls with the ruthless competence of somebody deleting more than a point. Menu. Stored locations. Current mark. Confirm. The little screen asked if she was certain because machines loved certainty most where it mattered least.

She pressed yes.

Then she took out her phone, opened the offline map app she used for transects, and deleted the temporary pin there too. After that she turned off the location permission for the camera entirely, thumb sure despite the rain. Not procedural showmanship. A person making future temptation harder on purpose.

"There," she said.

Only the insects answered, and the crackle under the roots, and Tomas's breath, and Mina's own blood loud in her ears.

Something in her eased with painful slowness, the way a cramped muscle lets go one fiber at a time. Her hands stayed empty. The eggs kept burning blue in the root pocket as if nothing human had happened at all.

They stayed longer than she expected after the deletion. Leaving immediately would have turned the act into transaction again. So they held the skiff quiet under the mangroves and kept watch while the eggs pulsed and the adult line or lines moved in and out of the root pocket with slow impossible patience. Mina let herself memorize without cataloging: the angle of the prop roots, the blue thickening in the center clusters, the rain dripping from one dead twig into the lit water and breaking the image apart and letting it re-form. She would remember this basin for the rest of her life and never be able to prove any part of it to people who loved proof more than consequence.

Once, without looking at her, Tomas said, "I'm not taking another living thing out of these channels."

It was the closest he had come to apology.

Mina turned her head toward him. His hand rested on the pole grip, grease still embedded in the lines of his knuckles no matter how often he scrubbed. She knew what it had cost him to say the sentence with her present to hear it: the eel, the spray run, the fuel thefts, the whole ugly chain of wanting and money and county authority and bodies moved from where they belonged.

"I know," she said.

This time he let the words stand.

Rhea shifted on the bench and briefly pressed the back of two fingers to Mina's shoulder blade, right between wet shirt and skin. The touch was small enough to miss if you did not know them, and after months of sex and fighting and side-by-side fieldwork Mina knew exactly how intentional it was. It marked witness, not reassurance or promise.

When they finally backed out of the basin, Tomas did it inch by inch. The skiff scraped once on a submerged root and all three of them froze, but the clutch held, blue steady in the pocket water. Then the channel took them again. The light vanished behind mangrove bark so abruptly Mina could have believed she had imagined it if the cold in her chest had not changed shape.

The ride back was slower. Or maybe silence stretched it. Rain had stopped entirely. Without the need to hide their breath they could hear dawn beginning at the edge of the mangroves, not light yet but the first tentative waking calls from birds that still had enough life left for habit. Once a fish rolled close to the hull and flashed white belly. Once Tomas swore under his breath when the pole sank into muck to the tape grip and came up smelling of sulfur. Rhea tucked the dead GPS screen under her thigh like something buried.

Halfway back, Mina said, "Imogen will ask."

"Yes," Rhea said.

"What are you going to tell her."

Rhea took her time. "That there is reason to keep the restricted zone restricted. That damaged systems do not benefit from spectators. That any report I file will address contamination risk and post-spray reproductive instability in general terms unless compelled otherwise."

Tomas grunted. "You're getting lawyer in your old age."

"I'm getting practical."

"Same disease."

The line almost passed for tenderness. Mina let it.

"And if they compel otherwise?" she asked.

Rhea looked at the wet channel ahead. "Then they can compel me to say I saw enough to justify protection and not enough to map a harvest."

"That's not really true."

"No," Rhea said. "It's not."

Mina let that settle with the damp and the first bird calls. Rhea was about to sandbag her own report. Tomas had given up the last clean excuse he had. Mina, who had built half her misery on wanting the world to understand her properly, was being asked to choose obscurity on purpose.

By the time the camp lights reappeared through the trees, the east had gone from black to a thin rotten gray. The donor deck floated above the water like a stage set nobody wanted anymore. Somewhere under the kitchen windows a pot lid clanged. Beto, then, up too early because grief had ruined his sleep. Mina thought of him gathering fallen spoonbill chicks with both hands after spray night, speaking in clipped nearly formal sentences because something had hurt him past jokes. Childhood ended in stupid places.

Tomas grounded the skiff in mud below the kayak rack and held it steady while Rhea climbed out. Mina followed, boots sinking an inch in the soft bank. Her legs shook when she hit land, spent from an hour or two bent around the hardest fact of the summer.

Tomas hauled the skiff higher with one hard pull and turned it upside down against the rack. Rainwater sheeted out of the ribs. In daylight it would go back to looking like an abandoned cheap boat with a bad patch job, not the vessel that had carried them to the roots and back with their hands still empty.

"They're going to come hard at you," Mina said to Rhea.

"Probably."

"At both of you."

Tomas shrugged one shoulder. "They already did."

Plenty crowded up behind her teeth: his suspension, the spray, the way wanting had tangled itself around access until she could not separate debt from tenderness, the fact that love in this place never came clean. The dawn was too thin for speeches. The camp was waking. A truck would come down the shell road before long with agency plates and coolers and questions.

So she said the only sentence that felt earned.

"I won't tell anybody where."

Rhea met her eyes. "I know."

Tomas did not look at her, but he nodded once.

Mina lifted her dry bag from where she had left it by the steps. It felt lighter than it should have after a night like this, as if some rotten little hope had finally leaked out of it.
By full light Imogen would sort housing and call whatever spare corner the camp still had a practical solution; Mina could already hear the phrase in that weather-advisory voice.

Behind them, beyond the camp and the boardwalk and the office where official language would soon start trying to net the world again, the mangrove basin kept its own blue under the roots. Rain, chemical residue, crabs, tides: the line would live or fail among those and not inside any story she could sell about it or herself. Mina hitched the bag higher on her shoulder and kept walking toward the main house before the first truck came down the shell road.


# Chapter 18

Weeks later, something dripped against metal in the room next to Mina's with the patience of a clock, waking her before the generator came on. The early rains had stopped arriving as an event and settled into habit, and the camp had learned a different set of noises. In the dry weeks the stilts used to complain in thin sharp ticks, heat pulling pitch from the boards and shrinking every nail hole around them. Now the wood gave longer sounds. Water moved inside it. The main house let out slow damp groans before dawn, and the screened walkways snapped when wind shoved rain sideways through them, as if the whole place were flexing sore joints in its sleep.

Her room had been the linen closet once. Imogen had taken out half the shelves, left the rest, and called it a practical solution in the voice she used when practicality was covering for poverty. Mina slept under spare mosquito netting, on a narrow camp mattress that smelled faintly of bleach and old salt, with a coil of replacement screen spline hanging from a hook by the door and a stack of folded warning placards under the bed. On the wall above her duffel, clipped to a thumbtack, the latest receipt from the Gulf Wildlife Board curled at one corner where the air never dried. Payment received. Balance still ugly.

Rain had blown in through the outer screen overnight. The toes of her work shoes were wet. She pulled them on anyway and stepped into the hall in shorts and a camp shirt so worn the old logo had faded to a pink smear across her chest. The main house smelled of coffee left sitting on a burner too long, wet rope, and the medicinal bleach they used now on every railing that guests might touch after a morning of leaning into scopes and asking whether contamination transferred through skin.

The camp survived in halves. Cabin Three stayed shut because the south window would not stop leaking and because there were not enough clean mattresses to pretend otherwise. The observation platform was open again, but only on the inland side; the outer corner still carried new boards where Tomas had replaced a section that had gone soft under too many bodies and too much weather. The north rookery walk was limited access now, by permit and by Imogen's temper. A laminated sign hung where the old hand-painted spoonbill welcome had been: POST-SPRAY MONITORING AREA. CLOSED WITHOUT STAFF ESCORT. Disaster had stripped the camp of any noble story. What remained was explicit.

Beto was in the kitchen with one knee on a chair, balancing the way he always did in positions that made older people imagine ambulance sirens. He had a wet dish towel over one shoulder, a pencil behind one ear, and a grease-marked exam-prep workbook spread open next to the coffeepot. He was copying the diagram of a carburetor float bowl into a spiral notebook whose cover had been reinforced three different times with silver tape. The pages had gone soft at the corners from humidity and kitchen steam, but his handwriting stayed neat and blocky.

He looked up when she came in. "Coffee's mean this morning."

"Good. I don't need it to like me."

"That's mature." He slid the pot off the heat before it could scorch further. "Imogen wants the west walkway checked before the extension kids get here. Also Cabin Two has that smell again."

"Everything has that smell again."

"No, this is the dead gecko version."

He said it with the old fast clowning rhythm, but there was less waste in him than there had been in March. After spray night he had not stopped joking; he had started placing the jokes more carefully, like screws he could not afford to strip. Mina poured coffee into a chipped enamel mug and saw the little crescent burns along his wrist where the fry basket had kissed him earlier in the season. He caught her looking and rolled his sleeve down without embarrassment, already reading the next label on the diagram.

"You staying in here all morning with your true love?" she asked.

He tapped the workbook. "If I don't pass the practice section on fuel systems, some nineteen-year-old from Fort Myers whose uncle owns three lifts gets my future. So no. I'm helping you first and then I'm romancing internal combustion until lunch."

"You're disgusting."

"That's why the machines trust me."

Outside, the shell path had turned to a slick paste that sucked at boot soles. Rain still came down in a fine slant, not hard enough to justify quitting and not light enough to ignore. Mina carried a tool bucket in one hand and a roll of screen in the other while Beto lugged the aluminum ladder and kept up a running mutter about people who designed outdoor structures as if salt had not yet been invented. The mangroves beyond the cabins held water in all their leaf cups. Every few steps something let go and dropped onto their heads.

The spoonbills were fewer. That was the fact that never softened no matter how often she walked this path. Fewer, with nothing for grant language to polish into resilience. Dawn still caught pink flashes in the channels, and the rookery still had that rank guano sweetness under the rot, but spaces had opened in the noise. The colony no longer hit the body the way it had at peak breeding, like standing too close to an electrical substation made of feathers and appetite. There were mornings when Mina could pick out single calls where once there had been one living wall of racket. Loss had turned legible.

At the west walkway they found two split boards, a screen door that had pulled loose from its lower hinge, and a drift line of mangrove leaves blown in against the threshold. Mina knelt to gather screws from the bottom of the bucket. The wood was damp enough to take the imprint of her knee through her shorts. Beto had the ladder up and the loose hinge braced before she finished cursing at the stripped head on the first screw.

"Give me that," he said.

"I know how to use a drill."

"I know. That's why I'm worried."

She handed it over. He grinned without looking down, the pencil still tucked behind his ear, and worked the screw out slow enough not to chew the wood. He could never stay still in conversation, but with tools he became almost eerily polite. Every movement asked the material what it could stand.

From the main house came Imogen's donor voice, still alive if not exactly thriving.

"No, I would not call it a tour in the usual sense," she was saying. "If you prefer the usual sense, there are better places to spend your weekend."

Mina stood and looked back through the rain. Two women in university-logo ponchos stood on the porch with clipboards hugged to their ribs, the sort of graduate extension students who had enough funding for field credit and not enough funding for comfort. Imogen faced them in a faded khaki shirt buttoned too high against the weather, silver hair pinned up with what looked like a sharpened plant label because she could never find an actual clip when she needed one. She had one hand wrapped around a mug and the other gesturing toward the posted closure maps. Managerial, exact, unsentimental. The camp no longer sold miracle nights. It sold access to damage if you could behave.

The students went with Rhea toward the bat lab half an hour later, heads bowed under the rain. Rhea carried two sample coolers and a packet of preprinted labels tucked inside her shirt to keep them dry. Her braid was darkened almost black from the weather. One nitrile glove hung from the back pocket of her field pants, marked at the cuff with black Sharpie because the lab had ordered the wrong size again and she had taken to sorting her usable ones by hand. When she passed the walkway she did not slow, but her fingers brushed the damp back of Mina's knee where Mina still crouched by the door frame, a touch so brief it could have been balance if you did not know her. Mina knew the heat of it anyway.

"Battery shelf on the west recorder's dead," Rhea said to no one in particular and to Mina exactly. "If you're still alive after the walkway, I need a second pair of hands at low tide."

"I'm hard to kill."

"That hasn't always been your best trait."

The students glanced back, startled by the dryness in Rhea's voice, but she was already moving again, long stride through the rain, one cooler thumping gently against her leg. She smelled as she passed of wet canvas, rubbing alcohol, and the clean metal scent of batteries warming in plastic cases. Mina watched the line of her shoulders for a second longer than she needed to and made herself reach for the next screw.

Beto whistled under his breath. "That was romantic if you enjoy being assigned labor."

"You have work."

"I know. I'm watching how professionals flirt."

"Then watch somebody better."

By ten the rain thinned enough to become steam. The extension students had disappeared into the lab with Rhea. Beto went to deal with Cabin Two and its dead-gecko smell, taking his workbook with him because he would rather carry three extra pounds through humidity than lose an idle ten minutes. Mina hauled the split boards to the scrap pile and found Imogen on the porch rewriting the day's chalkboard schedule to account for another cancellation. The chalk had softened in the damp and kept breaking in her fingers.

Without looking up, Imogen said, "You missed a rot patch by the cistern stair."

"I'll get it after lunch."

"Get it before lunch if you can. The students from Gainesville will interpret a turned ankle as institutional failure."

"They're not wrong."

Imogen snorted once. On her it passed for laughter. She set the chalk down and rubbed moisture off the board with the flat of her palm, smearing half the letters into a white bruise. Up close she looked leaner than she had in spring. The months had taken flesh from her face and left the field lines deeper. She had started wearing her reading glasses on a blue cord around her neck because she was tired of hunting them, and the cord gave her the air of an exhausted headmistress at a school the county kept threatening to close.

"I've redone next week's staffing," she said. "If you are still willing."

Mina waited. Imogen never offered the useful clause first.

"I can put you on paper as utility and restricted guide." Imogen lifted the schedule and read from it as if the phrasing mattered legally, which with her it often did. "Boardwalk maintenance, kitchen overflow, low-impact day groups only, no donor dinners, no press, no night water, no independent data collection, and no representation of the camp in any conversation that includes the word discovery."

"Sounds glamorous."

"It is not glamorous. That is one of its advantages."

Mina leaned against the porch post. The wood was warm now under the damp surface. Somewhere behind the house a pump coughed, recovered, and kept going. Imogen looked at her over the top of the paper with the old sharpness still in place, the one that had probably once terrorized trustees and now mostly got used on vendors and men who said things like silver lining.

"The pay is worse," Imogen said. "The title is worse. If investigators come back through, which they may, I will not lie for you. If a guest recognizes you and wants the full disgusting story, I will not stage-manage that either. What I can offer is the bed you already occupy, wages that are bad but regular when the bookings hold, and work that needs doing. If you take it, you take it as it is."

The shame of that should have arrived hot. Earlier in the year it would have. She would have felt it in the back of her neck, in her teeth, in the frantic reaching part of her mind that wanted one clean proof big enough to make every smaller humiliation retroactive and irrelevant. Now it came quieter. A fact, not a wound she could theatricalize. Utility. Restricted guide. No discovery. The words sat on the porch between them like a bucket of fish scales, ugly and honest and not at all abstract.

"You could leave," Imogen said, softer than her usual setting. "I am not saying you should. I am saying I know what it looks like from the outside. Staying here after all this could read as masochism."

"Probably is, some days."

"That is not an answer."

No. Masochism was too neat a word for it. Mina looked past Imogen toward the wet walk to the bat lab, where Rhea had hung two nets to dry under the eaves and the mesh moved in the breeze like black lungs. Farther out, through the mangroves, she could just see the line of the closed monitoring zone and one white stake where floodwater had turned the mud inside out. Tomas had helped drive those stakes last month. He had not spoken much while doing it. He had hit each one square, shoulder and forearm working under a shirt with the county patch torn off so recently the outline of it still showed, a paler rectangle on the sleeve.

Some things stayed in their damaged shape because restoration had never been on offer. Tomas and Rhea still shared a history long enough to show up in reflexes: the thermos of over-sugared coffee he left at the lab door on survey mornings, the way she still reached automatically for the knot in his shoulder when he bent wrong lifting lumber, the private math by which they decided who slept at the house and who stayed in the lab when they could not stand the same room. Mina was inside that damaged arrangement sometimes and outside it more often. Rhea still came to her narrow room when she wanted to, usually late, smelling of swamp water and alcohol wipes and the last clean inch of skin inside her shirt. Tomas and Mina had not been in bed together since the week after the confession. He still handed her tools without looking dramatic about it. That was its own form of not abandoning her.

The life in front of her was cramped, compromised, and only partly chosen. Her penalty payment came due every month whether or not she achieved wisdom. People still used her name as shorthand for fraud in comment threads she had learned not to read. But the work here had stopped pretending to be anything except the work. Patch the screen. Escort the students. Tell the truth when people asked why the colony sounded thinner. Pick monofilament out of roots before dusk. Keep hands off what had survived.

Mina looked back at Imogen. "Put me on it."

Imogen did not move. "On what, specifically?"

"The ugly version."

"That is still not precise enough."

Despite herself Mina laughed once. "Fine. Put me on utility and restricted guide. Bad wages, bad title, all the dirty parts. I'm staying."

Imogen's mouth tightened, not displeased exactly, just refusing sentiment on principle. "Are you staying because there is nowhere else that will have you?"

The question landed cleanly. That was why Imogen asked things that way. No pretty exit route. Mina could have lied out of pride, but pride had already cost enough bodies to make lying feel cheap. She set her mug on the railing and thought about the closet room, the curled receipt above her duffel, the mangrove stakes, the smell of wet screen in her hands.

"Partly," she said. "And partly because I know where the weak boards are now. I know which channels are still throwing sick fish after a hard rain. I know how to keep people from loving this place in the stupid way."

Imogen considered that. "That is the first useful thing you have said to me before noon in months."

"Happy to improve."

"Do not ruin the streak."

Imogen handed her the softened clipboard. Mina signed on the line where the ink had bled a little from humidity. Her own name looked ordinary there, the loops feathered soft by damp. It tied her to a list of tasks that would recur next week and the week after if the stilts held.

From the dock came the hard metallic ring of a hammer striking bolt. Tomas was under the platform bracing a new support post, shirt already plastered to his back with sweat. He had tied a dish towel around his neck against the rub of salt and wood grit, an old habit from engine work that made him look more tired than he ever had when he still had county payroll. Beto was beside him now, reading off washer sizes from the open hardware box like a litany.

"Three-eighths stainless," Beto said.

"That's quarter-inch."

"No, it's spiritually three-eighths."

"Your spirit can go buy the right bolt."

Beto laughed and dug deeper in the box. Tomas looked up long enough to spot Mina on the porch with the clipboard in hand. His face did not do much; he had gotten quieter since the hearing notices started arriving. But there was still that dry, local lift at one corner of his mouth when he decided not to be mean.

"So that's it?" he called. "They got you sweeping up with the rest of us?"

"Promotion, actually."

"Hell of a ladder."

He said it without venom, which on him counted as tenderness. Rhea came back down the walk with the extension students trailing her, all three carrying sample racks. She stopped at the edge of the dock long enough to set a bottle of water by Tomas's boot. He tapped the toe of his work shoe against hers once in thanks, barely visible if you were not already looking. Mina was already looking. There was grief in that small competence and care in it too. Their history stood there in plain view, all habit and grief and practical care, still knowing the shape of each other's thirst.

One of the students, a narrow young woman with a rain hood big enough to swallow her face, stared at the posted closure map and then at Mina. Mina recognized the hesitation: the half-second when a person was sorting memory from internet scandal. The woman did not say the name out loud. She only asked, "Is the western blind still accessible if we're not collecting specimens?"

Mina took the question before Imogen could. "Only with staff. Tide's undercutting the approach."

"And the contamination risk?"

"Depends what you mean by risk."

The student blinked. Rhea's head turned slightly, listening.

Mina went on. "Don't put your hands in standing water if you can help it. Don't step off the marked boards. If you see a fish on its side, don't decide you suddenly need to rescue it barehanded. You'll be fine."

The answer came out blunt enough to rasp. The student nodded, relieved by plain talk. Mina felt Imogen's gaze touch her from the side like a level being checked. When she looked over, Imogen only said, "Before lunch, if you can," and tipped her chin toward the cistern stair.

Mina picked up the tool bucket again. The rain had stopped. Water kept falling from leaves anyway. She went where the rot was.

* * *

At low tide the camp's edges came into view the way old lies did, their cover washed thin. Roots rose black and knuckled out of the water. Broken oyster shell showed in the mud like teeth. Storm trash collected where the boardwalk shadows crossed the narrow cut on the north side, all of it braided together by line and grass and whatever else the water had decided not to carry farther.

Rhea needed the west recorder battery replaced before dark, but a section of monofilament had turned up in the roots below the restricted walk and Imogen wanted it out before something living found it first. So Mina went with a bucket, a knife, a coil of fresh flagging tape, and Beto, who had finished his workbook section and was insufferable from success.

"Ninety-one percent," he said for the third time as they stepped down the muddy access slope. "Which means if the real exam is fair and God has any respect for struggle, I'm basically licensed."

"That's not what licensed means."

"Mina, don't kill the dream."

"I'm adjusting the dream to code."

He carried the long-handled grabber in one hand and a headlamp wrapped with pink electrical tape in the other. The tape had originally been on a box of donated party supplies and had ended up in the tool drawer because camp life turned everything into repair material eventually. He had written TORQUE IS A LOVE LANGUAGE across the lamp strap in black marker. It sat on his forehead now like a bad vow.

The evening had that heavy washed feel the coast sometimes got after three days of rain, as if the air had been rung out and hung back up damp. Mosquitoes came anyway. They rose from the shadowed grass in gray whining clouds every time Mina's boots sank. She had learned to swat less. Swatting only broke the rhythm and made you feel attacked. Better to keep moving and let irritation turn to background.

The restricted walk ran above a side channel that had once mostly served guests with binoculars and false confidence. Now the planks were streaked with boot mud from sample crews, and bright tags marked the spots where floodwater had loosened the supports. Through gaps in the rail Mina could see the blackwater narrowed by mangrove knees and low branches. The tide had dropped enough that the current showed itself by pulling bubbles under the roots in long patient lines.

Beto stopped short by the second marker stake. "There."

The monofilament had wrapped itself around a cluster of low roots and snagged everything that touched it: eelgrass, a gull feather, one blue crab shell, a fist-sized wad of decomposed leaves. Mina crouched and reached down. The line cut cold and thin across her fingers even through the wet mud. She followed it back and found where it disappeared under a root, sawing in the current. Something had already fought it and gotten free. The bark was scraped pale where scales or shell had torn away.

"Hold the bucket lower," she said.

Beto obeyed. He talked more when afraid, more when embarrassed, more when bored. At tasks like this he got reverent. The bucket knocked softly against his knee.

"Rhea said the recorder on this side's been picking up weird click clusters again," he said after a while. "Not bat weird. Water weird."

"Everything's water weird right now."

"That's not science."

"No, that's experience."

She cut one loop, then another. The line loosened enough that she could pull a rank bouquet of marsh trash free in one wet fist. Mud ran down her forearm. Something tiny and alive darted from the mess back into the water. She shook her hand once and kept going.

Above them a pair of spoonbills crossed the opening between mangroves, late and oddly quiet, pink wings dimmed by the hour. The sight still hit her in the ribs, closer to recognition than guilt. Guilt had become too familiar to retain much glamour. It felt more like the knowledge of what did not come back because humans had paperwork for one urgency and not another. She imagined spray again sometimes when the air shifted wrong: the chemical sweetness under the diesel, the panicked flock noise driven flat by propwash, the soft impacts on boards. Memory did not fade. It just lost its sense of occasion and started living in the body like weather.

By the time they got the last of the line off the roots, Mina's knees were black with mud and the bucket held a mean little collection of evidence: hooks, nylon, bottle cap, two lengths of rusted leader, one child's glow-stick bracelet bleached colorless by water and sun.

"People are pigs," Beto said.

"People are mammals with opposable thumbs and bad impulse control."

"See, that's almost romance coming from you."

She stood and flexed her cramped hand. The tide had turned enough that the current no longer pulled straight out. It eddied now, indecisive. From farther back in the channel came the soft electronic chirp of the west recorder complaining on low battery. Rhea would be swearing at the time stamp already.

"Go swap that battery," Mina said. "I'll flag this support and be right there."

Beto took two steps, then paused. "You ever think about leaving and just not telling this place where you went?"

The question came so casual it nearly passed as idle talk. He kept his eyes on the recorder pole when he asked it, not on her. That was his serious voice now: aimed sideways so nobody could accuse him of wanting too much.

Mina tied a strip of orange flagging to the loosened support brace and cinched it tight with muddy fingers. "Sometimes."

"Yeah?"

"Sure."

"And?"

She looked up at the walk above them, at the patched boards, at the sign warning people back from the roots as if words had ever stopped hunger. "And then I think about learning a whole new set of bad decisions somewhere else."

Beto huffed a laugh. "That's inspiring as hell."

"You asked."

"I did." He adjusted the headlamp on his forehead. The pink tape had started peeling at one corner from sweat. "I'm still leaving for school if I can."

"Good."

"You didn't even pretend to be sad."

"I can be sad later. Go save the recorder."

He went, boots thudding hollow on the boards as he climbed back to the walk. Mina watched him reach the pole and start fumbling with the battery box, talking to himself under his breath as if the screws needed coaxing. He wanted out of the islands and he wanted to deserve out. That combination in a person always made her want to touch wood for luck.

She bent to the support brace and wrapped the orange tape once, twice. When she straightened, something blue moved under the far roots.

This time the blue came thin as wire, a narrow strip of color threading through the blackwater where the tide curled back on itself. Her phone caught under her palm inside her pocket before she realized she had reached for it. She dragged it halfway free, saw the mud on her own knuckles, and shoved it back.

Above her the battery door clicked open. Beto had gone still on the plank, one hand inside the recorder box, the other already lifting toward the switch on his headlamp.

Mina shook her head.

He let his hand fall and kept the lamp dark.

The blue slid deeper between the roots. Mina stepped sideways off the packed edge to lean around the brace, felt the bank skin over into soup under her boot, and rocked back before the mud could take her. The tape stuck to her wrist. Water tapped from the leaves in slow separate drops.

"You getting that battery or composing a poem?" she said, too low for the line to carry past him.

Beto snorted once, shaky, and seated the new pack. Plastic knocked against plastic. The recorder woke with a thin electronic chirp that sounded absurdly domestic in all that mud.

By then the color had thinned to a seam, then to nothing she could swear to from where she stood. Mina cut the flagging tape with her knife, cinched the brace tighter, and hauled the bucket up to the walk. Beto handed down the dead battery without looking at her for long.

"You saw it," he said.

"Enough."

He glanced toward the roots, then back to the screws. "You gonna tell her?"

Mina hooked the old battery by its strap. "I'll tell her the recorder stayed dead too long in a side cut after rain, and she should check the files before the clock drifts again."

That pulled the corner of his mouth up. "Sneaky."

"Accurate."

They started back with the bucket knocking her shin and the old battery cold in her hand. The boardwalk flexed under their weight. Below them the cut had gone dark except for rainwater sliding off leaves and the ordinary weak metal shine of evening on the tide. Something turned blue once farther in where the mangroves braided tight, too far in for boots and already gone by the time she looked full at it. Mina kept walking. Rhea still needed the cards downloaded before supper, and the north-side supports would want bolts by morning.
